# SIEMPRE SEGUNDO

*"He was born second. He died first."*

A multigenerational saga — **feature screenplay + companion novel, both titled *Siempre Segundo* ("Always Second")** — loosely inspired by the life of Frank Arellanes, the Californio pitcher from Santa Cruz who was among the first Mexican-American men to reach the major leagues — and the first Latino to play for the Boston Red Sox. The title is the irony the whole story turns on: a man the world kept calling *second,* who was — as the coda reveals — *siempre primero,* first all along. **Fiction. Names and events invented.** Our hero is **Cirilo Méndez**. A motivational small-town-Chicano-to-hero story, kept **period-real to teach history** — with a tragic twist and a victory deeper than any trophy.

---

## The thesis — *Siempre Segundo*

> **This is what the picture is about.** It is an argument for the dignity of the runner-up — that a life is measured not by the trophy you lift but by how you keep working when you are *always second*: second-class, second-looked-at, second-chosen, the second of your blood ever to reach the top. Cirilo Méndez spends thirty-six years second, working anyway, reaching anyway — and the one autumn he finally becomes **first**, it kills him. **The moment he is no longer second is the moment he dies.** And yet the real first — the one death can't take — is the root he planted and the people he lifted. *Keep working. Especially when you're second. Siempre.*

Three things the story turns on:

- **The win is real, and it's the trap.** He pitches the 1918 Boston Red Sox to a true World Series title — and the very crowd that finally lifts a Mexican kid onto its shoulders is the breath that carries the flu that fells him weeks later. Being first, being *embraced* at last, is exactly what exposes him.
- **It is the wound of the Mexican-American.** A people who had their world taken and were told to keep their heads down and keep working — made second-class on the very land that was first theirs. *Siempre segundo* is at once a virtue to live by and an injustice to rage against, and the film holds both without resolving them.
- **The curse, worked from the inside.** Don Aurelio carries an old, private belief: that since the land was taken, the Méndez family has been **cursed to second** — that to be first is forbidden them, and the world collects when one of them forgets it. Cirilo's death seems to prove the old man right. The coda flips it: the *true* first — the son, the name the town can't stop saying — is the first that endures, and that is how the curse is finally broken.

---

## Themes & the leather thread

**Leather is the visual through-line** — the single object the camera (and the prose) returns to across three centuries, the way we keep the thesis in frame:

- **Hides as wealth.** On the rancho, sun-dried cowhides are stacked like money — "the banknotes of California" — and the family tans its own leather on its own land.
- **Leather as wage.** After the land is taken, the same hands that owned the hide trade trim another man's hides for a Russian's wages at Volkov's tannery. The craft survives; the standing is gone.
- **Leather as escape.** A baseball is stitched cowhide; a glove is tanned leather. The boy's way out of the tannery is the same material that trapped his father — reshaped into a ball and a mitt.
- **Leather as legacy.** The film opens and closes on a single worn glove. The last image is that glove in the small hands of his son.

Underneath the leather: **dispossession & reinvention**, **the rooted treated as foreign**, the cruel arithmetic of **always second**, and **the body that betrays** — first the breaking labor, then the fever in the air.

---

## Principal characters

- **CIRILO MÉNDEZ** — our pitcher (b. 1882). Quiet, proud, watchful; a cannon for a right arm. Born on the tannery floor; raised on the boardwalk. The last hope of a fallen family. The Boston papers will not let him keep even his name.
- **DON AURELIO MÉNDEZ** — the abuelo. Last patriarch of a rancho that no longer exists. Keeper of the Mexican past, the family's conscience, and the old belief that the Méndez are cursed to second. Our prologue narrator.
- **ESTEBAN MÉNDEZ** — Cirilo's father. A hide-trimmer at Volkov's tannery. Wants his son safe and small; love expressed as fear.
- **DOLORES MÉNDEZ** — Cirilo's mother. The rosary, the language, the kitchen that holds the family together.
- **REBECCA WHITFIELD** — Anglo, wealthy; meets Cirilo at the beach (~17), then goes up to **Stanford** — which puts her in the grandstand the day Chuck's Stanford nine faces Cirilo's Santa Clara. The woman who crosses every line she was raised inside, and marries him.
- **CHARLEY "CHUCK" ASHBY III** — old San Francisco money, Rebecca's intended. Plays for **Stanford**. The embodiment of the class that inherited the family's land.
- **LUIS & ABEL MÉNDEZ** — Cirilo's older brothers; the meat market, the sandlot, the Santa Cruz Beachcombers.
- **ED BARROW** *(real)* — manager of the 1918 Red Sox; the man who finally gives the thirty-six-year-old his shot. *(He really managed that club, then went on to build the Yankee dynasty — a quiet irony to play.)*
- **BABE RUTH** *(real)* — Cirilo's 1918 teammate; pitcher-slugger, force of nature. Caught the flu *twice* in 1918 and lived. Cirilo catches it once and doesn't. The contrast does its own work.

---

## The Cast — deep backstories, the everyman lens & the secondary world

### The lens (Steinbeck, Dostoevsky)

The book and the script are told, wherever possible, from the eye level of the people who do the work — the tanner, the broken catcher, the widowed landlady, the barred Black ballplayer on the bench — because that is where the history actually lived. No one in it is a villain and no one is a saint; everyone is carrying something, and acting, mostly, out of fear or love or both at once, which are harder to tell apart than anyone admits. The Anglos who took the land are people, not weather; Chuck Ashby's gilded world is its own kind of prison, and we go inside it. The morality is grey on purpose. The interiority is the point — we are always closer to what a character tells themselves than to what they do, because the gap between the two is the whole subject.

### The principals, deepened

**Cirilo Méndez.** His silence is armor and also a wall, and it costs the people who love him; Rebecca and his mother both, at times, need him to speak, and he cannot, because he learned before he could read that the soft underside shown to a hostile world gets cut. He is not sure his ambition is a virtue. Late at night he wonders whether wanting to be first is the very pride his abuelo warned of — a vanity the world will punish — and whether his father was right, and safe-and-second is the only love a man like him is allowed. He wins, and feels guilty for what winning costs other men, and cannot stop.

**Rebecca Whitfield.** She loves him truly, and the love is tangled, in the honest way real love is tangled, with a willful need to be the author of her own life. There are nights in the cold flats when she wonders, with a shame she never speaks, whether she chose the man or the refusal — whether she would have loved him so fiercely if they had simply let her. Her mother died young; her father turned her into an ornament; she spent a girlhood being looked at and never once *seen*, which is why a bottle boy who looked at her like a person undid her in a single afternoon. Her growth is the discovery that she meant it after all — that the daily grind of the sacrifice is a harder and truer thing than the gesture of it, and that she is equal to it.

**Don Aurelio.** Keeper of the curse, and — in a way he cannot face — its author. He was young when the land went; he watched his own father die broken in the courthouse fight, and somewhere in that watching he made a vow of safety that hardened, over ninety years, into a doctrine of second. His guilt is enormous and unspoken: he taught his sons fear, watched Esteban shrink into the tannery and called it wisdom, and does not know, at the end, whether the curse is a truth he discovered or the excuse he built for his own failure to reach. He loves Cirilo, and is secretly terrified the boy will succeed and prove him a coward. The coda's reversal is his to earn.

**Esteban Méndez.** The father who loves in the only language the world left him: fear. He had a gift once, too — the fastest, the strongest, a boy who might have reached — and somewhere a reaching cost someone he loved, and he made himself small and has called it love ever since. He envies his son and is ashamed of the envy; he wants Cirilo safe partly to vindicate his own retreat. His fear-love is real love and real cowardice at once, and he knows it, and that knowledge is the ache he carries down to the tannery and back every single day.

**Dolores Méndez.** The silent strong one; the kitchen is her pulpit, and her faith is the family's spine, not its decoration. She came from even less than the rest of them, and she has buried two infants in the Santa Cruz ground and carries the grief without ever once displaying it. Her rebellions are quiet and total: she teaches the children the language; she keeps the rosary out when Esteban, frightened, wants it put away. She is hard toward Rebecca at first — the Anglo girl can only bring trouble — and the slow softening of that hardness is one of the book's quietest, truest arcs.

**Chuck Ashby III.** Not a villain — a wound. His father measured him his whole life against a dead elder brother and found him wanting, and Chuck inherited everything along with the private certainty that he deserved none of it. His feeling for Rebecca is the one true thing in a life of arrangements, which is exactly why losing her to the bottle boy curdles into something that lasts for decades. His cruelty is the cruelty of the insecure, never the strong. And in the box seat at the Series, for one unbearable afternoon, he understands precisely what he won and what he did not — and tells no one, ever, which is the most human thing about him.

**Abel & Luis Méndez.** Abel, the eldest, the wild one, was the better natural athlete and drank and brawled the gift away before it could cost him the way reaching costs; Cirilo's shadow-self, the road not taken, proof that the curse is sometimes just a man getting out of his own way before the world can. Luis, the middle son, took the meat-market life and the weight of the family when the eldest could not — steady, tired, half-resenting Cirilo's reaching and half-living through it.

### Ten lives along the road (the secondary world)

1. **Fyodor Volkov** — the Russian who owns the tannery (his people came with the otter hunters; Russia abandoned its California colony and left them). Not a monster: a hard, fair-in-his-fashion immigrant who clawed up in a town that called *him* a foreigner too, and who treats Esteban with a gruff, grudging, complicated near-respect even as he grows rich on his hands. He lets us smell the tannery — the lime, the bark, the bate — and see the cruel arithmetic by which one outsider is permitted to stand on the neck of another. *(Santa Cruz, the tannery.)*

2. **Father Aloysius Fagan, S.J.** — the Jesuit who finds Cirilo's arm. A genuine idealist who believes in the boy's soul, and a college man who needs to beat Stanford, and not at peace about which of the two is doing the recruiting. He wrestles his faith against the worldliness of it and mostly loses gracefully. He opens the door to the college — the Latin, the chapel bells, the class threshold. *(Santa Clara.)*

3. **Edward Purse** — the Beachcombers' barred Black star (real, and ruinous). Good enough to play for any nine in the county, made the *mascot* because the line would not bend, carrying a dignity so total it indicts the whole grandstand. His friendship with Cirilo is real and grey: there are days the sight of the Mexican *let onto the grass* is more than Purse can bear graciously, and a bitterness slips out, and is regretted, and is human. He is the book's conscience and its hardest mirror. *(The Beachcombers; the color line.)*

4. **"Deacon" Mulqueen** — Cirilo's first battery-mate, a broken old catcher in the Class D bushes who had three weeks in the big leagues in 1894 and has been drinking them ever since. He teaches the boy the road — the milk-run trains, the four-to-a-bed hotels, which towns will feed a brown ballplayer and which will not — and the long art of surviving a game that has already discarded you. Self-pitying, tender, ruined, and the truest friend Cirilo makes in the low minors. *(The bush leagues.)*

5. **Lim** — the Chinese café man by the depot who feeds the whole ball club at midnight, the brown one included, when the white café down the block will not. Marooned by the Exclusion Act — a wife he has not seen in twenty years on the far side of an ocean and a law — he answers the cruelest verdict the country ever put in writing with a hot plate and no questions: the one warm lit room that asks the barred nothing. Through him we get the Chinese Bay Area, and the deep clear solidarity of one sorted-out man for another. *(The valley; the back table.)*

6. **Aoife Doyle** — the Brooklyn landlady, an Irish widow with a gold-star flag in the window and a son in the French ground. Hard as a frozen stoop and unexpectedly, wrenchingly kind in the small hours; she lets Rebecca's rent slide the month the baby comes and would die before admitting it. Through her we get the tenement city — cabbage and coal smoke, church bells, war telegrams, the whole grieving immigrant Brooklyn the rosters were stripped to fill. *(Brooklyn.)*

7. **Nunzio Sabatini** — a Bay Area Italian, kiln-burned hands and a cannery wife, who plays winter ball with Cirilo and shares the particular fellowship of men the country has decided to insult in two different languages. He worships Ping Bodie — the real one, the Pezzolo boy who hit thirty over the fence and made the bleachers swallow their *wop* — and he carries the immigrant's whole argument with America inside one cheerful, exhausted body. He lets us see the Italian Bay Area: the kilns, the fishing wharf, the feast days. *(California; the immigrant labor world.)*

8. **Harry Hooper** *(real)* — the gentleman of the outfield, Hollister-born, who carried Cirilo's name in his pocket for years. Decent to the bone, and not without his own quiet guilt: he knows what the game does to men like Cirilo and Purse, and he benefits from a ladder he did not build and cannot quite bring himself to burn. The good man inside the unjust house, doing the one small right thing he can reach. *(The Red Sox; the bridge to Boston.)*

9. **Eleanor Ashby (née Vane)** — the woman Chuck marries instead of Rebecca. Poised, handsome, lonely, and far sharper than her husband knows; she married the chair at the club with her eyes open and has been quietly accounting the cost ever since. She sees Chuck's emptiness and her own with equal clarity and names neither aloud. Through her, Chuck's gilded world becomes a cage with people in it, rather than a wall to throw stones at — the only honest way to write the people who win. *(The Anglo upper class, from the inside.)*

10. **Danny Halloran** — the Boston sportswriter who hangs *El Cid* on Cirilo. Irish, two generations off the boat and the *No Irish Need Apply* signs, careless with a Mexican's name and genuinely, helplessly thrilled by the arm; he Anglicizes Cirilo half out of deadline laziness and half out of the recent-immigrant's instinct to climb by standing on the next man down. Not cruel, not kind — a man on a ladder, doing what the rung beneath his feet requires. Through him we get the press box, the bleachers, and the exact machinery by which a country renames the people it will not quite let in. *(Boston.)*

**And, deepened inside Chuck's world: Horace Whitfield** — Rebecca's father — is not old money but a self-made man terrified of falling back down, which is why his daughter's marriage does not anger him so much as *frighten* him: it threatens the respectability he bled a lifetime to buy. His cruelty, like Esteban's caution and Chuck's spite, is fear wearing a hard coat. Almost all of them are.

These come into the story not as a roster but as the rooms Cirilo passes through — each a place to stop and breathe and *smell*, each a life with its own weight, each a small window onto the history the textbooks flatten. The expansion pass weaves their pasts directly into the chapters: Mulqueen's three lost weeks in a freezing Class D depot; Purse's bitterness flaring and healing across a season of Sundays; Eleanor's clear-eyed loneliness threaded through the box-seat scene; Aoife Doyle's gold star turning the Brooklyn winter from backdrop into grief.

---

## Structure — three acts + prologue

**PROLOGUE — "California Banknotes" (1833–1882).** The Mexican golden age and its theft, told as Don Aurelio's memory: the secularization grant, the rancho at its height (vaqueros, the *matanza*, hides as money, the fandango, Branciforte rowdy across the river), the false promise of Guadalupe Hidalgo, the slow strangulation by the Land Act of 1851 and the drought, the fall into wage labor — ending on a match cut from a wet new hide to a newborn: Cirilo, 1882, born on the tannery floor.

**ACT I — "The Boardwalk" (~1900–1903).** Cirilo, eighteen, works the brand-new pleasure pier — labor in the leisure economy built on the land his family lost. The Whitfields summer at the beach; Rebecca and Cirilo collide. Chuck humiliates him; Cirilo answers with the only thing the Anglo world can't argue with — his arm. The attraction to Rebecca becomes perilous. A Santa Clara man sees the arm. Cirilo leaves the beach carrying the family's buried hope of rising again.

**ACT II — "The Rematch & The Long Wait" (~1901–1916).** Santa Clara as a class threshold. The hinge: the **Stanford–Santa Clara game** — Rebecca in the grandstand, Chuck at the plate, Cirilo on the mound; the boardwalk paid back with a strikeout, and Rebecca chooses. Then the cruelty of the long middle: a decade-plus of being passed over, the exotic "Spanish" spitballer who can't get his call to the bigs while lesser, paler men do. *Always second.* The act ends ~1917 with the call finally coming — and Cirilo and Rebecca marrying in the teeth of both their worlds.

**ACT III — "Siempre Primero" (1917–1918).** His first real shot at last — at thirty-six, the oldest man on the roster, on Ed Barrow's **Boston Red Sox** after the lean marginal Brooklyn years, in a clubhouse with Babe Ruth. On **January 1, 1918**, a son is born. Then the season of his life: in a year cut short by the wartime "Work or Fight" order, he leads the league and the press crowns him the people's ace, and he pitches Boston to the **1918 World Series championship** (begun Sept 5 — the earliest ever). For one autumn the man who was always second is *first*. Then the flu that is already loose in Boston — Camp Devens, the packed victory streets, the deadliest month in American history — takes him within weeks. He dies the moment he is no longer second; the root of life and the legacy he leaves are the only first that lasts.

*(History note: there was no Rookie of the Year, 1947, or MVP award in 1918 — so he is "crowned" by leading the league and by the press, not by a trophy. All the period facts above are real.)*

---

## The Frame — scene-by-scene (working index)

A living map of the whole picture: ✍️ = drafted in this document, ◻ = framed, still to write. The grind fills the ◻'s.

**PROLOGUE — California Banknotes (1833–1882)**
- ✍️ The remembered sweep (rancho → conquest → tannery → birth) — prose + compressed screenplay
- ✍️ The Fandango — full set-piece (the last good night; Cipriano's fiddle; Bray noting the creek)
- ✍️ The Hearing — the Land Commission dramatized (Hollister, Quint, the diseño; "robbed politely, with a receipt")

**ACT I — The Boardwalk (~1900–1903)**
- ✍️ The meeting (the ball-toss booth; Rebecca's laugh) — screenplay + prose
- ✍️ Home: the tannery household (Esteban's fear vs. the abuelo's defiance; the hands)
- ✍️ The secret courtship (the dark under the pier; the watchman's lamp)
- ✍️ The exhibition (Cirilo's arm goes public; strikes out the college boys; Chuck flails)
- ✍️ Chuck's first cruelty (the rope-and-a-tree threat; "you can't strike out a county")
- ✍️ The door opens (Coach Fagan of Santa Clara sees the arm)
- ✍️ Leaving the beach (the depot; her kid-leather glove as a token)

**ACT II — The Rematch & The Long Wait (1903–1917)**
- Seq 1 — ✍️ The Rematch (Stanford at Santa Clara; the strikeout; Rebecca stands)
- Seq 2 — ✍️ The Break (the Whitfield ultimatum; Rebecca chooses; Chuck curdles)
- Seq 3 — ✍️ The climb, level by level (bush leagues → Beachcombers → PCL Seals → Brooklyn) — prose + ✍️ the backstop lesson
  - ✍️ A Brooklyn "can't make rent" scene (the reserve clause; the winter hides; "we do not quit")
  - ✍️ A PCL triumph the bigs ignore (the man in the derby; "the right kind of half")
- Seq 4 — ✍️ The wedding (Holy Cross; two worlds; the curse warning)
- Seq 5 — ✍️ The Call (traded to Boston; bittersweet; Hooper's word; the war's empty rosters)

**ACT III — Siempre Primero (1918)**
- ✍️ The name they give him (the El Cid clubhouse; Ruth)
- ✍️ New Year's, 1918 — the son is born (first baby of the year; the glove in the cradle)
- ✍️ The season (leading the league; crowned "El Cid"; the pitching-craft montage; Hooper, Ruth, Barrow)
- ✍️ Chuck at the Series (the reappearance; everything-and-not-the-thing)
- ✍️ The last out (the real 1918 World Series; the strikeout; carried off; first — he catches the flu after, in the crowd)
- ✍️ What the crowd carried (the parade; the sick autumn air)
- ✍️ The room (the deathbed; the glove to the son)
- ✍️ The real victory, the curse broken (the coda; "throws like Méndez")
- ◻ Option: a 2020 COVID bookend (if we ever want the echo explicit)
- 📖 **The full novel is NOVELIZED** — Prologue + Acts I/II/III rendered as complete prose chapters (the novel sections below). Front to back.
- 🌱 **Grow pass complete (first pass):** every movement deepened with the full secondary cast and principal backstories, written through the Steinbeck/Dostoevsky everyman lens — Prologue (Don Joaquín, the Ohlone labor, Capt. Bray, Hollister & Quint), Act I (Nunzio, Pereira, Volkov, Toussaint, the family goodbye), Act II (Fagan, Mulqueen, Purse, Horace, Aoife Doyle), Act III (Halloran, Eleanor). Further passes can keep growing toward full novel length.

---

## PROLOGUE — *California Banknotes*

### Novel prose

Before this was a town for strangers — before the lights on the water and the laughing on the pier — it was ours. All of it. The old man said it the way other men said prayers, which is to say he said it until the words wore smooth and stopped meaning anything to anyone but him.

In the year the bells came down — 1833, when Mexico unlocked the mission and handed God's land back to men — a governor's pen made the Méndez family rancheros. The grant ran from the river to the redwood line, hills the color of a lion in August, and on it the family put cattle and a chapel and a low white house that held the cool like a cellar. They were *gente de razón*: people of reason, people of name, Mexicans and Catholics and, above all, owners.

The wealth came on the hoof and left on the hide. Each spring the vaqueros rode the cattle down out of the hills for the *matanza*, and the killing-ground ran red and then the hides went out to dry, staked in their hundreds to the ground like a field of dark windows. The Yankee ships stood off the cove and the Méndez men rowed the hides out and rowed back with silver and silk and iron, because a cowhide was as good as coin in those years — better; men called them *California banknotes*, and laughed, and meant it. And on the rancho, in a shed that smelled of oak bark and lime, the family tanned its own leather from its own herds on its own ground. Remember that. The leather was theirs before it was anyone's.

Across the San Lorenzo the pueblo of Branciforte gambled and brawled and drank — a town the Spanish had stocked, a lifetime before, with the contents of a Guadalajara jail — and on feast nights the guitars carried over the water to the rancho's own fandango, so that the sacred bank of the river and the profane bank of the river sang to each other in the dark. It seemed it would last forever. Nothing built on land ever does.

The thunder came from the north. A flag at Sonoma; a war; the stars-and-stripes run up over Monterey where the eagle of Mexico had flown that morning. And then, in '48, the paper that was supposed to save them: Guadalupe Hidalgo, which swore on the honor of the United States that the grants would be respected, that what was theirs would stay theirs. Don Aurelio's father believed it. That was his only crime, and it cost him everything.

For the conquest did not come with a sword. It came with a courthouse. The Land Act of 1851 made every Californio prove, in English, before a commission in a city three days' ride north, that the ground under his own chapel was his — and even the families who won bled to death paying the lawyers who won it for them. Squatters fenced the range. The cattle wandered and were not returned. Then the sky itself turned against them: the flood that drowned the valley, and after it the long drought of '64 that left the hills white with bone. They sold the rancho the way a man bleeds — not all at once, but a little at a time, until there is no more.

By the time Don Aurelio was an old man, the new Santa Cruz had risen on the grave of his: lime kilns smoking on the hill, the long wharf walking out into the bay, strangers' money everywhere and the family's name on nothing. The language went indoors. The rosary stayed out. And somewhere in those years the old man began to believe the thing he would never say aloud — that the family was *cursed* now, cursed to second, that the conquest had not only taken their land but their right to ever again be first at anything; and that the world would collect, hard, from any Méndez who forgot it.

And on a winter morning in 1882, his son Esteban walked down off the hill and through the gate of Volkov's tannery to do for a Russian's wages the one thing the Méndez had always known how to do — turn hide into leather — only now the hide was another man's, and the leather was another man's, and all that was left to the family was the knowing. *They left us our hands,* the old man would say. *So we gave them to the leather.*

That same morning, in the back room where the women waited, a child was born — wet and red and roaring, the way a new hide comes off the beam. They wiped him clean and the old man looked at the boy's hands, already big, already strong, and did not yet know what they would do. He only knew the family was not finished. Not while there was a Méndez with hands.

### Screenplay — the same movement, compressed

```
FADE IN:

EXT. MÉNDEZ RANCHO -- COASTAL HILLS, ALTA CALIFORNIA -- DAY (1840s)

Hills the color of a lion. No fences anywhere -- the land
just goes. A herd of cattle moves like a slow brown river.

DON AURELIO (V.O.)
(old, unhurried)
Before this was a town for strangers...
it was ours. All of it.

EXT. THE MATANZA -- KILLING GROUND -- DAY

HIDES -- hundreds of them -- staked to the earth to dry, dark
and gleaming, a field of black windows.

DON AURELIO (V.O.)
A cowhide was as good as coin. Men
called them California banknotes. And
they laughed. And they meant it.

INT. RANCHO TANNING SHED -- DAY

Oak bark, lime, the honest stink of the work. YOUNG ESTEBAN
(10) watches his father draw a knife along a hide.

DON AURELIO (V.O.)
Our leather. From our herds. On our
ground. Remember that.

INT. U.S. LAND COMMISSION, SAN FRANCISCO -- DAY (1850s)

A cold room. ANGLO COMMISSIONERS in English. DON AURELIO'S
FATHER lays a bundle of Mexican deeds on the table with
shaking hands. No one leans forward to read them.

DON AURELIO (V.O.)
The conquest did not come with a
sword. It came with a courthouse.

EXT. THE RANCHO -- DAY (1860s) -- DROUGHT

White bones in white grass. A SQUATTER'S FENCE cuts the old
range in two. A WEATHERED SIGN, new: another man's name.

DON AURELIO (V.O.)
We sold it the way a man bleeds. A
little at a time. Until there is no more.
(quieter)
And we learned the family's place.
Second. Always second.

EXT. KRON'S TANNERY, SANTA CRUZ -- WINTER MORNING (1882)

Smoke. The long wharf beyond. ESTEBAN MÉNDEZ (grown) walks
through the gate, takes up another man's knife, another
man's hide.

DON AURELIO (V.O.)
They left us our hands. So we gave
them to the leather.

INT. TANNERY BACK ROOM -- CONTINUOUS

A CRY. DOLORES holds a NEWBORN -- wet, red, roaring. DON
AURELIO takes the small hands in his. Already big. Already
strong.

DON AURELIO (V.O.)
The family was not finished. Not while
there was a Méndez with hands.

MATCH CUT TO:

A WET NEW HIDE coming off the beam -- then a BASEBALL,
white and new, dropping into a small brown palm.

TITLE OVER BLACK:  SIEMPRE SEGUNDO
```

---

## ACT I — *The Boardwalk* (1900)

### Screenplay — the meeting

```
EXT. SANTA CRUZ BEACH -- THE NEW PLEASURE PIER -- DAY (1900)

Fresh-cut planks, white paint, electric bulbs strung against
a blue sky. The leisure economy, newborn -- built on the very
ground the prologue mourned.

CIRILO MÉNDEZ (18) hauls a crate of bottles down the boards.
He does not look at the summer people. He has learned not to.

A BANNER: "TEST YOUR ARM -- 3 BALLS A NICKEL." Cirilo takes
his post behind a pyramid of iron milk-bottles.

CHUCK ASHBY III (20), white flannels, steers REBECCA WHITFIELD
(17) to the booth. She is already bored of him.

CHUCK
Watch. I rowed stroke at the club.

He throws three. Misses, misses, clips the pyramid -- it holds.

CHUCK (CONT'D)
(to Cirilo, not really to him)
Rigged. They weight the bottom row.
Aren't they weighted, boy?

Cirilo says nothing. He picks up one ball -- the stitched
leather, an old friend -- and without seeming to wind up at
all, throws.

CRACK. The pyramid does not fall over. It LEAVES, all six
bottles blown clean off the shelf.

Rebecca laughs -- a real one, surprised out of her.

REBECCA
Do it again.

CIRILO
(quiet)
Costs a nickel.

She holds his eye a beat too long. Chuck sees it.

REBECCA
(producing a coin)
Then here's a nickel.
```

### Novel prose — the same beat, interior

He had learned not to look at the summer people, which was a thing you learned the way you learned not to touch the lime vats — once, and never again. So he was looking at the bottles, and the worn ball in his hand, when the girl laughed.

It was not the laugh they used on the pier, the bright tin one the young ladies kept in their purses with their handkerchiefs. It came out of her sideways and surprised, as if he had knocked it loose along with the bottles. Cirilo had thrown a baseball through six iron bottles and felt nothing; one honest laugh from a stranger and his ears went hot.

The boy in the white flannels had felt it too, the shift in the air, the way a dog feels weather. *Aren't they weighted, boy.* Cirilo knew that voice. It was the voice that had bought the rancho at the courthouse and never had to raise itself. He had been answering it with his silence his whole life, because silence was the only thing they couldn't take and couldn't use against you. But the girl was holding out a nickel and looking at him as though he were a person and not a fixture of the pier, and the old man's voice was somewhere in him saying *not while there is a Méndez with hands* — and Cirilo understood, with the flat cold clarity of a thing already decided, that the silence was going to cost him more than it ever had.

"Then here's a nickel," she said.

He took it. Her glove was kid leather, soft as a thing his father might have made in another life, when leather still belonged to them. He gave her a ball, and tried not to think about whose hands had tanned the hide of her glove, and failed.

### Screenplay — the house in the tannery's shadow

```
INT. MÉNDEZ HOUSE, SANTA CRUZ -- NIGHT (1900)

Two rooms and a lean-to under the smoke of Volkov's tannery.
DOLORES works a pot; her rosary hangs by the stove. ESTEBAN
comes in gray with the day, his hands cracked and stained
dark to the wrist -- a tanner's hands, a thing that does not
wash off.

CIRILO (18) eats fast, boardwalk sawdust still on his collar.
LUIS and ABEL argue box scores. In the only good chair, DON
AURELIO watches the boy the way you watch weather coming.

ESTEBAN
(not looking up)
Volkov will take you on in the fall.
Steady work. A man can feed a family
on it.

CIRILO
(careful)
I have work, Papá. At the beach.

ESTEBAN
You have a season at the beach. Until
the summer people go home and the planks
belong to the gulls again.
(quieter -- the fear under it)
I want you somewhere they can't put you
out on a whim, mijo. That's all.

DON AURELIO
(from his chair)
You want him where you are. Safe. Small.
Second.

The table goes still. Esteban's jaw works.

ESTEBAN
Second is alive, viejo. I have buried
men who reached.

DON AURELIO
(to Cirilo, ignoring his son)
Show me the hand.

Cirilo crosses, holds out his right hand. The old man turns it
in the lamplight -- big, strong, and unmarked by the lime,
because this boy alone of them has kept his hands for
something else.

DON AURELIO (CONT'D)
Your great-grandfather tanned hides with
a hand like this and called himself a
king. Your father trims them for wages
and calls himself safe. You --
(he folds the boy's fingers into a fist)
-- you have the first hands in four
generations that don't smell of the
pit. Don't you dare give them back.
```

### Screenplay — the dark under the pier

```
EXT. THE BEACH BELOW THE PIER -- NIGHT (1900)

The boardwalk lights are off; the surf is the only sound.
CIRILO and REBECCA, half-hidden under the pilings -- in the
dark that is the only place a brown boy and a white girl can
stand together in this town without it costing blood.

REBECCA
My father thinks I'm asleep.

CIRILO
My father thinks I'm a fool.
(beat)
They're both right tonight.

She laughs -- the surprised one. He has started living for it.

REBECCA
Tell me something true. You never do.

CIRILO
(after a while)
My family owned this. Not the pier -- the
land. All of it, the river to the
redwoods, before there was a Santa Cruz
to own it.
(sand runs through his fingers)
Now I sell nickels on a plank built on
top of it. And I would rather be here,
in the dark, with you, than have had any
of it back.

She takes his hand -- the pitching hand, the unmarked one --
and holds it like a thing she has decided to keep.

REBECCA
Then we'll just have to take the rest of
it back ourselves.

A WHISTLE up on the boardwalk -- a NIGHT WATCHMAN's lamp
swinging. They freeze. The light sweeps the sand above their
heads and moves on. They breathe again. And the fear is its
own answer -- the thing neither of them will say: that this,
all of it, is borrowed, and the lenders are cruel.
```

### Screenplay — what it costs

```
EXT. THE PIER -- DAY (1901)

Cirilo hauls crates. CHUCK ASHBY III and two college FRIENDS
block the boards, easy and grinning, the way men are when they
outnumber.

CHUCK
The bottle boy. We were just talking
about you. About how you've been seen.
At night. With a young lady of our
acquaintance.

CIRILO
(setting the crate down, slow)
I don't know what you mean.

CHUCK
You know exactly what I mean. So I'll be
a friend and tell you how it goes out
here. A fellow like you, with a girl like
that -- there's no court. No judge. No arm
fast enough. There's a rope, and a tree,
and a crowd that goes home and sleeps
fine.
(close now, pleasant)
Throw all the strikes you like, Mendez.
You can't strike out a county.

He pats Cirilo's cheek -- once, lightly, an insult precisely
measured -- and strolls off laughing.

Cirilo stands very still. Picks the crate back up. His hands --
the good hands, the unmarked ones -- are shaking, and not from
the weight.
```

### Screenplay — the exhibition

```
EXT. BEACH FLATS BALLGROUND -- SUNDAY (1901)

A holiday crowd, parasols and straw hats, the boardwalk organ
carrying over the fence. A challenge game: the local nine
against a touring club of COLLEGE BOYS in crisp flannels --
Stanford men down for the weekend, CHUCK ASHBY III among them,
loose and certain.

Cirilo takes the ball for the locals. The college side laughs
-- the bottle boy from the pier, in spikes.

Then he throws.

The first college man swings at a fastball already in the
catcher's mitt. The second gets the wet one -- the SPITBALL --
that drops off the table and leaves him foolish in front of
his girl. The third is Chuck, who is not laughing now, who
grinds in and fouls and flails and finally stalks off
muttering that the ball is doctored.

In the little grandstand, a man in a dark Jesuit coat -- COACH
FAGAN of Santa Clara -- stops writing in his book and simply
watches. He has seen a thousand arms. He has not seen this.

After the last out he finds Cirilo at the fence.

FAGAN
Son. Have you given any thought to
college?

CIRILO
(plain)
We can't pay for college, Father.

FAGAN
(a small smile)
No. But Santa Clara can pay for an arm.
We play Stanford every spring.
(a glance at Chuck, sulking by the bus)
Wouldn't you like to do that again --
where it counts?

Off Cirilo: the door of his whole life swinging open on a
single sentence.
```

### Screenplay — the leaving

```
EXT. SANTA CRUZ DEPOT -- DAY (1901)

A small valise. CIRILO in his one good suit, the Santa Clara
letter in his pocket. REBECCA has come, against every rule, to
the edge of the platform -- no farther; she cannot be seen.

REBECCA
Santa Clara's an hour. That's nothing.
A morning's train.

CIRILO
It's a hundred years, and we both know
it. Your people, my people --

REBECCA
(fierce, low)
Then go and become a man so loud they
can't pretend not to hear your name. And
come back for me when you have.

She presses something into his hand: her KID-LEATHER GLOVE --
the soft one from the booth, from the day they met.

REBECCA (CONT'D)
So you remember whose hand you're playing
for.

The train calls. He closes his fist on the leather -- her
glove, made of the one thing his family ever truly owned -- and
boards, and does not look back, because if he looks back he
will not go.

Through the window the platform slides away. Rebecca stands in
the wrong place a moment too long, then makes herself turn, and
walk, into a life that has just become a waiting.
```

---

## ACT II — *The Rematch & The Long Wait* (1903–1917)

### The shape of the act — five sequences

1. **The Rematch (1903).** The Stanford–Santa Clara game below: Cirilo strikes out Chuck, and Rebecca stands and claps in a sea of cardinal red — choosing, in public, the wrong boy.
2. **The Break (1903–04).** Rebecca defies the Whitfields. They hand her an ultimatum; she takes the disinheriting. Chuck, humiliated twice now (the booth, the mound), turns from careless cruelty to real enmity — the kind that waits.
3. **The Climb That Stalls (1904–1915).** Cirilo becomes a star of the **Pacific Coast League** (Sacramento, the Seals), masters the **spitball** (legal then), and is, by any honest measure, big-league ready for a decade. The call never comes. Scouts admire and pass; the reasons are never said aloud. *Siempre segundo* — best in his league, second-class in his country. Years compress in a montage of seasons, exotic press names, and Don Aurelio's warnings not to reach.
4. **The Marriage in the Margins (~1916).** They wed at **Holy Cross**, in the teeth of both worlds (scene below). Rebecca, the heiress, lives the grind by choice — cheap rooms, barnstorming towns. A low season (an injury; the old temptation to quit). And **Chuck resurfaces** — prosperous, respectably married, appearing trackside to twist the knife: *"You could have had everything, Rebecca."*
5. **The War Opens the Door (1917).** America enters the war; the **"Work or Fight"** order and the draft strip the young arms off to France. Ed Barrow's depleted **Red Sox**, desperate for pitching, finally call the thirty-five-year-old. The bitter truth under the dream: he gets his shot because the white boys are in the trenches — and he takes it anyway, because you take it anyway. Rebecca is pregnant; the baby is due on New Year's. The train goes east. → **Act III.**

### Screenplay — Stanford at Santa Clara (Sequence 1)

```
EXT. SANTA CLARA COLLEGE -- BALL GROUND -- DAY (1903)

A college crowd. A score board: STANFORD vs. SANTA CLARA. The
Stanford side is louder, richer, sure of itself.

In the grandstand, among the cardinal red: REBECCA WHITFIELD, a
Stanford woman now. She did not expect to know anyone on the
field. She is wrong.

ON THE MOUND: CIRILO MÉNDEZ, 21, Santa Clara flannels gone
gray with dust.

AT THE PLATE: CHUCK ASHBY III, cardinal "S" on his chest,
tapping the corners like he owns them.

CHUCK
(grinning)
Well. The bottle boy.

Cirilo says nothing. He has always said nothing. He looks in
-- and THERE, in the red, is Rebecca, half-risen, a kid-leather
glove forgotten in her lap. Their eyes meet across four years.

CHUCK (CONT'D)
(to his bench, loud)
Three balls a nickel, fellas!

Laughter from the Stanford side. Cirilo sets. The first pitch
is past Chuck before the laugh is done. STRIKE ONE. The second
buckles his knees. STRIKE TWO.

Cirilo holds the ball a moment -- the stitched leather, an old
friend -- looks once at Rebecca, and throws the best thing he
owns.

STRIKE THREE. Chuck swings at air and nearly screws himself
into the dirt.

The Santa Clara side erupts. And Rebecca is on her feet, alone
in a sea of red, clapping -- for the wrong team, for the wrong
boy, in front of everyone she knows. She doesn't care.

CIRILO
(under his breath, to no one)
Costs a nickel.
```

### Screenplay — the break (Sequence 2)

```
INT. THE WHITFIELD HOUSE, NOB HILL -- DRAWING ROOM -- NIGHT
(1904)

Gaslight on good furniture. HORACE WHITFIELD, Rebecca's
father, stands at the mantel like a man addressing a board.
CHUCK ASHBY III waits by the window -- a suitor who has been
told he is owed something.

REBECCA stands in the center of the rug. Alone, the way she
is going to get used to standing.

HORACE
I have been more than patient. A
summer's foolishness with a -- a beach
attendant. I let it run, because these
things burn out.
(beat)
It has not burned out. So now I am
plain. You will not see the Mexican
again. Charley has been a gentleman
about all of it. More than you deserve.

CHUCK
(softly -- the knife)
He throws a baseball, Rebecca. That's
the whole of him. A strong arm and a
borrowed suit.

REBECCA
(to Chuck, almost gently)
And what's the whole of you, Chuck? Your
father's name, your father's money, your
father's chair at the club.
(beat)
At least his arm is his.

HORACE
If you walk out that door to him, you
walk out of this family. No allowance.
No name. No coming back when the romance
of being poor wears thin -- and it will
wear thin, faster than you can dream.

A long beat. Rebecca looks at the room she grew up in. The
good furniture. The warm, lit, comfortable cage of it.

REBECCA
You've confused two things, Father.
You think you're offering me everything.
(she takes nothing; lifts nothing)
You're offering me less than a man with
one borrowed suit already gave me on a
pier. He looked at me like a person.
You have never once looked at me like
anything but an arrangement.

She walks to the door. Turns back -- to Chuck this time. A
small last mercy, and a small last wound.

REBECCA (CONT'D)
Find someone who wants the chair at the
club, Chuck. They're easy to find.
They're the only people you've ever met.

She goes. The door clicks. HORACE stares at it. And in
CHUCK's face something curdles -- from wounded into the kind
of thing that waits.

CHUCK
(quiet, to the closed door)
We'll see how the person looks at you
in ten years. On a tannery wage.
```

She paid for it in full and on time. The allowance stopped; the name closed over her like water; the friends she had grown up beside learned, all at once, not to see her on the street. And Rebecca Whitfield, who had never in her life made a bed or counted a dollar twice, went to live in the margins of a country that had decided she no longer existed — and found, to the genuine astonishment of everyone who had warned her, that she had never once been happy until she did.

### Sequence 3 — The Long Wait: the climb, level by level (1904–1916)

*(Where the picture gets drunk on baseball — the smells, the rungs, the day-in-the-life — and where Don Aurelio, who has watched the game in California since the Gold Rush sandlots, becomes Cirilo's teacher and the audience's. Period-real throughout; the real figures are named.)*

He went out into organized ball the only way a kid like him could in those years — at the very bottom — and he climbed it a rung at a time, and the country let him climb exactly as high as it could stand him, and not one rung higher.

**The bush leagues — Class D, then C.** The floor of the professional game: valley towns baking under a white sky, cinder infields that took the skin off a slide, wooden grandstands leaning in the heat. A few dollars a month, four men to a hotel bed, the milk-run trains that stopped at every water tank between Stockton and nowhere, meal money that did not cover the meal. The *smell* of that life, which he would know in his sleep for the rest of it: wintergreen liniment and cut alfalfa, coal smoke and tobacco spit and the wet-dog reek of flannels that never fully dried. He learned the trade here by failing at it in front of nobody. And he learned the first hard law of the business, the one Don Aurelio made him repeat until it stopped making him angry — that **he did not own his own arm.** Under the **reserve clause**, the club held his contract for exactly as long as it pleased: it could sell him, bury him in Class D until he rotted, or cut him loose into nothing, and there was not one thing he or any other player, in the bushes or the big leagues, could do about it. *You are leather,* the old man told him, not unkindly. *They will buy you and sell you and never once ask.*

**Home — the Beachcombers, Beach Flats.** When the climb let him back to Santa Cruz it was to the real pro club, the **Beachcombers** of the Pacific States League, playing the ground behind the **Leibbrandt bath houses** — so close to the sand that the brine and the boardwalk barkers came over the outfield fence with the gulls (the same pier from Act I, watching him now from the other side of the rail). The **Daubenbiss brothers**, Ed and Charley of the old Soquel family, ran the nine with **Bill "Red Dog" Devereaux** of Oakland; a Sunday crowd came a thousand strong in its best clothes, and a local rhymer crowed after they beat the City: *Who killed the Bushnells? We — the Beachcombers — three-baggers and homers.* Cirilo's brothers Luis and Abel played beside him. And on the bench in street clothes sat **Edward Purse** — good enough to start for any club in the county, kept off the grass and called the team's *mascot*, because the color line would not let a Black man play, his heart breaking by inches in plain view every Sunday. Cirilo watched Purse and understood the thing no one ever said to his face: that there were lines drawn on this country no glove could carry you across, and that he himself stood with one foot on one of them — let onto the field only so long as the papers could keep calling him *Spanish.*

### Screenplay — the lesson behind the backstop

```
EXT. BEACH FLATS BALLGROUND, SANTA CRUZ -- LATE DAY

Empty after a game, the light going gold off the water. DON
AURELIO -- old, but the eyes miss nothing -- sits on an
overturned bucket behind the backstop. CIRILO (early 20s) on
the mound, sweating.

DON AURELIO
Before you throw -- read him. The feet
and the hands tell you everything the
man won't.

He lifts his chin at an imaginary batter.

DON AURELIO (CONT'D)
Stands deep in the box, back by the
catcher? He's buying time -- scared of
anything that bends. Bust him up high
with the fastball; he'll never get on
top of it. Crowds the plate? He can't
reach his own fists. Go in, tie him up.

CIRILO
And the hands.

DON AURELIO
White knuckles -- squeezing sawdust out
of the bat -- he's afraid, he wants to
KILL the ball. So don't give him a ball
to kill. Slow one, off the plate, let
his own fear swing for him. Loose hands,
easy grip -- that one's patient, that
one waits. Him you must throw strikes
to. Him you out-think.

Cirilo takes it in like scripture.

DON AURELIO (CONT'D)
And never show his eyes the same height
twice. High and hard, then slow and low.
The eyes load for the one -- the other's
already gone.

The old man stands, takes the ball, spits in his palm, works
it in with two fingers.

DON AURELIO (CONT'D)
And this. While they still let you.

He shows the wet grip -- the SPITBALL.

DON AURELIO (CONT'D)
Drops off the table like it died in the
air. They'll outlaw it -- a few years,
they say, and it's gone for good. So
learn it now and break their hearts with
it while you can.
(beat, quieter)
Everything they give us has a clock on
it, mijo. Throw fast.

He flips the ball back. Cirilo goes into his motion.
```

**The lessons the old man carried.** Don Aurelio had watched ball in California since men played it bare-handed on Gold Rush sandlots, and he doled out the game like a second catechism — sequencing, deception, the geometry of a hitter's fear — and he salted it with the real ones he'd seen come up. He'd watched **Hal Chase**, "Prince Hal," the Los Gatos boy, climb out of the Coast League and go east and go *crooked*, and he warned Cirilo that the game devours the ones who sell it. He'd known the Pezzolo family in the City and watched their boy **Ping Bodie** — a *wop* kid, they sneered, the way they'd sneer *greaser* at Cirilo — become the first man to hit thirty over a Coast League fence, in 1910, proof that an immigrant kid's bat could make the country choke its own slurs back down.

**The Coast League — the San Francisco Seals.** The highest rung he could reach and still be told no: the **Pacific Coast League**, graded Class A and then **Double-A from 1909**, the "third major league" — 180-game seasons in the soft coast light, the best ball west of the Mississippi, good enough that men turned down the East to stay in it. He starred there for years. He beat, to their faces, men who got the call he never got. The scouts came and admired and wrote one word in their books that he was never allowed to read, and they signed the paler arm. *Siempre segundo* — the best pitcher in the best league the East refused to call major. The one who got clear and stayed decent was **Harry Hooper**, the Hollister kid who'd played Soquel ball a ridge away, gone to Boston, made himself a star and a gentleman — and who, years on, would be the one to remember the old Coast spitballer and say the sentence that changed everything: *there's an arm out west nobody's using.*

**Brooklyn — the bottom of the top.** The call finally came, late, in his thirties — and it was to **Brooklyn**, Uncle Robbie's Robins, the National League's most dependable heartbreak, a ballclub that knew being second the exact way Cirilo did. The thing he'd chased fifteen years, and it nearly starved him. The reserve clause pinned a journeyman's pay to the floor; Brooklyn rent ate the floor; and with Rebecca beside him and a child on the way he stood in a major-league uniform and *still* could not make a month meet itself. So he did what his father did before him: in the winters he went home and back through the **tannery gate**, the championship-bound arm trimming another man's hides to cover the Brooklyn rent — leather to leather, the thread his great-grandfather started never breaking. Thirty-five years old, in the major leagues, and second-class still. He nearly quit. Rebecca would not let him. And then, in 1917, with the war stripping the rosters bare — *work or fight* — a Boston man bought the old spitballer's contract for almost nothing, the way you'd buy a used glove off a nail, on the word of a Hollister boy who never forgot the ridge he came from. Cirilo packed for the Red Sox not knowing he was packing for the only October that would ever matter. → **Act III.**

### Screenplay — the man in the derby

```
EXT. RECREATION PARK, SAN FRANCISCO -- DAY (1909)

A big Coast League crowd in the gray light. CIRILO -- a Seals
jersey, the best pitcher in the best league nobody east will
call major -- walks off the mound to a roar, having just
two-hit the Angels.

In a box, a MAJOR-LEAGUE SCOUT in a derby lowers his field
glasses to the BIRD DOG beside him, notebook open.

SCOUT
Best arm I've seen all year. Spitter
falls off a cliff. Reads hitters like a
cardsharp.

BIRD DOG
(pencil ready)
So we take him.

SCOUT
(a beat -- then, not even unkindly, just
certain)
He's thirty next year. And he's a... you
know. The crowds back east won't warm to
it. Ownership won't spend the ink.
(he folds the glasses)
Write down the kid in left. The blond
one. Half the ballplayer.
(beat)
But he's the right kind of half.

Down on the grass, Cirilo tips his cap to the crowd that loves
him -- the only crowd that's allowed to -- and does not know,
and will never be told, that a man in a derby just decided his
whole life in one sentence, for a reason no one will ever put
on paper.
```

### Screenplay — the wedding (Sequence 4)

```
INT. HOLY CROSS CHURCH, SANTA CRUZ -- DAY (1916)

Small. Half-empty -- and the half that's here has a fault line
straight down the center aisle.

On one side: the MÉNDEZ family -- DOLORES, ESTEBAN, the
brothers, DON AURELIO. Sunday clothes worn thin. Wary, not
joyous. They have learned that when the world notices them, it
is usually to take something.

On the other: almost no one. A single empty pew where the
WHITFIELDS would have sat. Rebecca's people did not come. Rebecca's
people are, as of a letter three weeks ago, no longer Rebecca's
people.

REBECCA (30s now), in a plain dress -- the gowns belonged to the
life she traded away -- walks the aisle alone. No father to
give her. She gives herself.

CIRILO waits at the front, hands too big for a borrowed suit.
The PRIEST speaks in Spanish, and Rebecca -- who has been
learning it in secret for a decade -- answers him in it.

EXT. HOLY CROSS CHURCH -- STEPS -- CONTINUOUS

DON AURELIO takes Cirilo's arm. Quiet. Not unkind. Afraid.

DON AURELIO
First the game. Now an Anglo man's
daughter. You keep reaching over your
head, mijo.
(beat)
Our family was not built to be first. I
have watched it cost us for ninety years.

CIRILO
(gentle, certain)
Then I'll be the one it stops costing.

The old man looks at this son who will not learn fear, and
says nothing -- because there is nothing to say to a man who
has decided to break a curse he refuses to believe in.

Behind them REBECCA laughs at something one of the brothers
said -- the real laugh, the one from the booth, sixteen years
old now and still surprising people out of themselves.
```

### Screenplay — the winter hides (Sequence 5)

```
INT. MÉNDEZ FLAT, BROOKLYN -- NIGHT (WINTER, ~1916)

A major-league uniform hangs on a nail. Below it, on the
table, the household accounts: a short stack of bills and a
shorter stack of dollars that will not reach across them.
CIRILO, in shirtsleeves, his pitching hands raw and stained
dark at the knuckles. Tanner's stains. REBECCA sees them.

REBECCA
You went back to the hides.

CIRILO
(not ashamed -- just tired)
A tannery in Newark takes on winter men.
No questions asked. They don't care what
a man's name is if his hands know the
work.
(a laugh with nothing in it)
I pitch in the major leagues, Rebecca,
and I can't make the rent on it. The club
owns my contract and pays me what it
pleases, and there's no other club I'm
allowed to ask. They call it the reserve
clause. My abuelo had a shorter word.

REBECCA
(quiet)
Leather.

CIRILO
Bought and sold and never asked. Four
generations, and the family business is
the same. Only now the hide is mine.

Rebecca takes his stained hands in her clean ones -- the
heiress's hands, that chose this -- and does not let go.

REBECCA
Then we work the winter. We wait for the
spring. We do not quit. Say it.

CIRILO
(the family creed, worn smooth)
We do not quit.
```

### Screenplay — the call (Sequence 5)

```
INT. MÉNDEZ FLAT, BROOKLYN -- NIGHT (WINTER 1917)

Cold-water rooms. REBECCA, pregnant, mends a sock by a weak
lamp. CIRILO (35) comes in stamping snow off his boots, a
TELEGRAM crushed in one fist, his face doing something it
doesn't have a name for.

REBECCA
What is it. Cirilo.

CIRILO
Boston bought my contract.
(he can hardly make the words real)
The Red Sox. The major leagues want a
thirty-five-year-old spitballer they can
have for the price of the train.

REBECCA
(rising, joy breaking over her)
Cirilo --

CIRILO
(stopping her, gently -- the truth has
to come with it)
They want me because the young ones are
in France. Half of Barrow's staff is in
a trench or under one. He's down to old
men and boys.
(beat)
Sixteen years I waited for the call.
And it comes because the country ran
out of white arms to send up.

Silence. The cruelty and the gift, sitting in the same cold
room.

REBECCA
(taking his frozen face in both hands)
Then listen. It does not matter why the
door opened. It matters that you walk
through it. You have waited your whole
life for a door to open at all.

He looks at her -- the heiress who chose this freezing flat.

CIRILO
Hooper put my name in. The Hollister
boy -- played Soquel ball one ridge from
my brothers. All these years, and the
man who remembered me is a kid from up
the road.

He lifts the worn GLOVE off the table -- his father's
leather, his great-grandfather's tanning. Turns it once.

CIRILO (CONT'D)
My abuelo will say it's too high. He'll
say the family was never meant to climb
this far.

REBECCA
(a hand on her belly)
Then we climb it for this one. Let the
curse take it up with a baby.

Off Cirilo -- almost smiling, plainly terrified, and going.

DON AURELIO (V.O.)
(a letter, faint, as the room dims)
Mijo. They call you to the very top now.
Be careful up there. The air is thin
where they let us stand -- and they have
never, not once, caught us when we fall.
```

---

## ACT III — *Siempre Primero* (1918) — THE CLIMAX

Sixteen years he waited. The boy from the tannery floor — passed over a hundred times, the wrong color for the call that kept going to lesser arms — finally stands in a big-league clubhouse at thirty-six, on **Ed Barrow's Boston Red Sox**, two lockers down from a wild twenty-three-year-old named **Babe Ruth**. On New Year's Day his son was born. And in a season cut to pieces by the war's "Work or Fight" order, the old man who was always second leads the whole league — and the Boston papers, who cannot be bothered to spell *Cirilo Méndez*, crown him anyway, under a name they invent for him.

### Screenplay — New Year's, 1918

```
INT. MÉNDEZ FLAT, BROOKLYN -- NIGHT (JANUARY 1, 1918)

The thinnest hour of the year. A midwife's basin, a lamp
turned low against the cold. REBECCA, spent and luminous,
holds a NEWBORN BOY. CIRILO (35), who has stared down major-
league bats without a flicker, stands shaking in the doorway.

REBECCA
(exhausted, shining)
First baby of the year, the midwife
says. Born on the one. Before anyone
else's.
(she finds his eyes)
First, Cirilo. Our boy is first at
something already.

Cirilo crosses and takes the small hands in his -- the very
gesture his abuelo made over him thirty-five years ago and
three thousand miles west, on a tannery floor.

CIRILO
(barely)
Already big. Already strong.

He lifts the worn GLOVE from the chair where it always sits --
his father's leather, his great-grandfather's tanning -- and
lays it in the cradle beside the boy, the way another man
might lay down a deed to land.

CIRILO (CONT'D)
There. Now you own the only thing we
never lost.
```

### The season (1918) — novel prose

He got his season the way a drowning man gets a plank: because the boat had already gone down. The war had pulled the young arms to France under *work or fight*, and the rosters that came north that spring were old men and boys and Cirilo Méndez — thirty-six, and finally, absurdly, getting the real shot the bigs had denied him for sixteen years.

And he was magnificent.

He pitched the way Don Aurelio had taught him behind a backstop in Beach Flats a lifetime before: reading a man's feet before he read his swing; busting the deep-standing hitters up and in where their fear could not reach; feeding the white-knuckle sluggers the slow wet one and letting their own greed swing for them; never showing a pair of eyes the same height twice. The spitball he'd carried up from the Coast League dropped off the table like it had died in the air, and the American League — in the last summer before they would outlaw the pitch forever — had no answer for a thirty-six-year-old who threw a dying ball with a surgeon's mind behind it.

He led them all. Most wins. Fewest runs allowed. The Boston writers, who could not be troubled to spell *Cirilo Méndez*, reached for something grand and handed him **EL CID** — and the bleachers took it up, and for one summer Fenway chanted a conqueror's name at a tanner's son and never once heard the joke folded inside it. **Harry Hooper**, the Hollister boy who'd put his name in, watched from right field and told anyone who would listen that the best arm in the league had been standing out west the whole time, unused, because no one had thought to look at a brown man twice. Even **Ruth** — twenty-three, enormous, already a legend and already, twice that year, knocked flat by the same fever loose in the camps — would drift over between his own starts just to watch the old man throw the thing nobody could hit.

**Barrow** rode him hard, and Cirilo let him, because sixteen years of *no* had taught him exactly what a *yes* was worth. By September the second-place country had itself a first-place team, and the man who had been second his whole life had, for the first time, a clear road to first — at the one thing the whole country had stopped to watch.

### Screenplay — the name they give him

```
INT. RED SOX CLUBHOUSE, FENWAY PARK -- DAY (SUMMER 1918)

CIRILO, 36, laces his cleats. A YOUNG REPORTER waves a paper
at him, delighted, meaning it as a gift.

REPORTER
You see what they're calling you? Page
one -- "EL CID CARVES UP THE TIGERS
AGAIN." You're a star, Mendez!

He drops the paper on the bench. The headline: a name that is
not Cirilo's, over a face that is.

BABE RUTH (23), unwrapping a sandwich two lockers down,
watches Cirilo not pick the paper up.

RUTH
They call me names too, pop.

CIRILO
(evenly)
They call you the Babe -- that's yours.
Me they made "El Cid." A Spanish
conqueror. They couldn't let a Mexican
be the hero, so they made me a Spaniard.
(beat)
There's a difference between a nickname
and a... correction.

Ruth thinks about that longer than you'd expect a man eating a
whole sandwich to think about anything. Then he nods, once,
like an umpire.

RUTH
(after a beat)
...Throw the one nobody hits today, Cid.

And Cirilo -- almost -- smiles.
```

### Screenplay — Chuck at the Series

```
EXT. THE BALLPARK -- WORLD SERIES -- A PRIVATE BOX -- DAY (1918)

Above the bunting, in the best seats money rents, CHUCK ASHBY
III -- older, heavier, prosperous. A banker's overcoat, a
banker's watch chain, and a wife on his arm who is poised and
handsome and is not Rebecca. He did exactly what he was always
going to do: he inherited the chair at the club.

Down in the cheap seats he finds them -- REBECCA, in a plain
coat, a BABY on her hip; and out on the mound, impossibly, the
beach attendant. The bottle boy. EL CID, the headlines say.

CHUCK'S WIFE
(following his stare)
Friends of yours?

CHUCK
(a beat too long)
I knew them. A long time ago.

On the field, Cirilo works. The whole park is on its feet.
Chuck does not rise. He watches the woman who chose the strong
arm and the borrowed suit watch her husband become the one
thing Chuck Ashby will never be in a hundred inherited years:
loved like that. In the open. By someone who had every other
choice and made this one.

He bought the best seat in the house. From it he is given a
clear, unobstructed, season-long view of the single thing his
money was never going to buy -- and he is not permitted, by
some cruelty of the geometry, to look away.

CHUCK'S WIFE
You're not cheering.

CHUCK
(barely)
No.

He keeps his seat while the city stands. A man who has
everything, watching, for one unbearable afternoon, the only
thing he ever wanted and never got -- in a ballpark he will
spend the rest of his life telling people he attended.
```

### Screenplay — the fever game (the real 1918 World Series)

```
EXT. COMISKEY PARK, CHICAGO -- WORLD SERIES -- DAY
(SEPTEMBER 1918)

A title card, plain and true:

   SEPTEMBER 1918. THE EARLIEST WORLD SERIES EVER PLAYED --
   THE SEASON CUT SHORT BY THE WAR. BOSTON vs. CHICAGO.

The stands are a strange sight: bunting, straw hats -- and,
scattered through the crowd, white gauze MASKS. The thing has
a name now in the camps. It does not yet have one here.

ON THE MOUND: CIRILO. Soaked through. Gray under the brown.
He has been fighting a chill since the third inning and
telling no one. The deciding game. Two out in the ninth. One
strike from the first championship of his life.

BARROW comes halfway out, reads his catcher's face, then
Cirilo's, and stops cold.

BARROW
You're done, Mendez. You're the color
of the ball. Let me bring the kid in.

Cirilo shakes his head. Once.

CIRILO
I've been second my whole life, skip.
(beat)
Not today.

Barrow holds his eyes a long moment -- then puts the ball back
in his hand and walks away, the way a man walks away from
something he'll think about for forty years.

In the box seats: REBECCA, the eight-month-old BOY on her hip in
a knit cap. The seat beside her is empty: DON AURELIO is three
thousand miles west, far too frail to have crossed a country,
sitting that very hour in his good chair in Santa Cruz -- and
afraid, even there, even blind to the field, because the old man
knows the family's law: we are not allowed to be first.

Cirilo looks in. The stitched leather, an old friend. He
breathes. He does not wind up big. He never did.

He throws the best thing he owns.

The world goes silent the way it does only in memory.

STRIKE THREE.

The park comes apart. The bench empties and reaches him the
way the sea reaches a rock, and lifts him -- the tannery boy,
the bottle boy, the man with the wrong name in the papers --
up onto their shoulders, above the whole roaring country, a
CHAMPION. First. For the first time in his life, and the first
time in his family's century: FIRST.

He finds Rebecca in the noise and holds the BALL up toward the
boy like a man showing a coin.

Down in the box, DON AURELIO does not cheer. He closes his
eyes. He is the only person in the park who is afraid.
```

### Screenplay — what the crowd carried

```
EXT. STREETS OF BOSTON -- NIGHT (DAYS LATER)

The city pours out to meet its champions. The first time
Boston has ever opened its arms to a Méndez, it does it forty
thousand strong, shoulder to shoulder, breathing the same wet
autumn air.

CIRILO rides it, dazed and happy, the BOY held up over his
head. For one night he is not second to anyone, and the whole
town loves him, and the whole town is sick and does not know
it yet.

DON AURELIO (V.O.)
He was first. In front of everyone, my
grandson was first.
(beat)
And I had spent my whole life learning
what it costs us to forget our place.

INSERT -- A NEWSPAPER, days on:
   CAMP DEVENS OVERWHELMED. INFLUENZA SWEEPS THE CITY.
   October, 1918 -- the deadliest month the country would
   ever know.
```

### Screenplay — the room

```
INT. MÉNDEZ FLAT, BOSTON -- NIGHT (OCTOBER 1918)

Small. Clean. A championship BALL on the sill beside a
guttering candle and the rosary from the prologue. The
influenza has done in four days what sixteen years of being
told no could not.

CIRILO in the bed, far away now. REBECCA at its edge, dry-eyed
past crying, the BOY asleep against her. AOIFE DOYLE -- the
Brooklyn landlady, come up on the train the day the word reached
her, because that is the only argument she knows -- in the
corner, lips moving over beads.

Down the hall a neighbor's radio-less quiet -- the whole
building is sick. (Ruth caught this twice, somewhere across
town, and lived. Cirilo caught it once.)

Cirilo's hand finds the old worn GLOVE on the blanket -- the
one stitched from leather his own father tanned. He turns it
once. Then he puts it not in Rebecca's hands but in the BOY'S --
folds the small fingers around it.

CIRILO
(barely)
Abuelo was right. There's a price...
for us being first.
(the ghost of a smile)
Worth it. Tell him it was worth it.

DON AURELIO
(broken, fierce)
No. No, mijo -- I was wrong. I was
wrong my whole life.

But Cirilo doesn't hear the last of it. His hand goes still on
the boy's hand on the glove.

REBECCA
(whisper, into the quiet)
You weren't second. Not for one day of
your life.

AOIFE DOYLE does not stop praying. And REBECCA, through her
tears, is looking at her son's small hands.
```

### Novel prose — the real victory, and the curse broken

They buried him in Santa Cruz, because Rebecca understood that a man belongs to the ground that made him even when that ground was stolen, and she carried him home on the train across the whole width of the country he was never quite allowed to belong to.

And the town came. That was the thing none of them expected — the town came. The tannery shut its gates for the afternoon, the first time it had ever shut them for a Mexican, and Esteban's old crew stood with their cracked leather hands folded. The fishermen came up from the wharf. The families came down out of the flats and in from the orchards, the people who spoke the language only indoors, and they stood in the open and spoke it, because the boy in the box had made it so that for one season the whole country had to learn to say a name like theirs — even the wrong version of it, even *Cy the Spaniard* — and say it with its hat off.

Don Aurelio stood at the grave on the edge of land that had once run from the river to the redwoods, all of it theirs, none of it theirs. He had believed his whole life that the family was cursed to second, and that his grandson had broken the law and paid for it, and standing there he understood at last that he had read the curse exactly backward.

What a trophy is: a cup in a glass case in a far city; the men who carried him grow old, then gone; the cup gathers the dust of strangers. Real, glorious, borrowed, brief — the way first always is in *their* world. Had that been his only first, the curse was real and it had won.

What a root is: the flats, summers later, boys at ball on the river ground where the rancho's cattle once came down to drink. A kid throws one nobody can hit; the others go quiet; somebody says it — *throws like Méndez* — and none of them ever saw the man pitch. They know only the story, and the story is enough: a tannery son off this dirt stood on the highest mound in the country and was first, and threw on through sixteen years of *no,* and the working was the man. The town that spent a hundred years forgetting the family can no longer stop saying the name.

That is the first that does not gather dust — the one the courthouse and the conquest and the fever could never staple a stranger's name to; the one the old man was too grief-blind to see had been theirs for the taking all along. The curse was never that a Méndez could not be first. It was the believing they couldn't.

They had hung *El Cid* on him for a joke and an erasure — a Mexican recast as a Spanish conqueror. They were more right than they knew: in the legend, the Cid's dead body, lashed upright in the saddle, led one last charge and won a battle after its own death. So did Cirilo Méndez.

CLOSE ON the boy on the river ground, his father's glove on his hand — the old family leather, gone soft through a third generation. The root. The legacy. The first that lasts.

LAST FRAME: the small hands, closing around the ball.

```
FADE OUT.
```

---

## _Siempre Segundo_ — Prologue, novelized (full chapters)

### Copyright

*Siempre Segundo*

**Copyright © 2026 Jacob J. Bolotin. All rights reserved.**

This is a work of fiction. Although inspired by historical figures and events — including the life of the ballplayer Frank Arellanes — the characters, names, dialogue, incidents, and events are either products of the author's imagination or are used fictitiously. Any resemblance to actual persons, living or dead, or to actual events is the product of dramatic license or is entirely coincidental.

No part of this work may be reproduced, distributed, or transmitted in any form or by any means, including photocopying, recording, or other electronic or mechanical methods, without the prior written permission of the copyright holder, except in the case of brief quotations embodied in critical reviews and certain other noncommercial uses permitted by copyright law.

### Dedication

*For Frank Arellanes — Santa Cruz's own, the real arm behind this invented one. The son of a Californio family, he pitched his way out of the Central Coast and onto the Boston Red Sox, where in 1909 he stepped into the great Cy Young's place in the rotation and won sixteen games. He was among the very first Mexican-American (Chicano) men to play in the major leagues, and the first Latino ever to wear a Boston uniform. This story is loosely his, and is offered in his honor.*

*And for all the others — the Awaswas and the Ohlone, the Californios and the conquered, the tanners and the kiln-men and the fishermen, the barred and the spent and the unrecorded — every soul across the long history of this coast who paid, in labor or in loss, for the complex beauty Santa Cruz wears so lightly in the sun today. The hands in the dirt were always theirs.*

### Chapter — The Hawk

She is aloft before the tourists, the way her kind has always gone before whatever people are newest to the coast — the red-tailed hawk, unmooring from a high Douglas fir in the Santa Cruz Mountains on the first thermal the sun pries off the ridge, paying her wings out toward the tin-bright shine of the bay. Below her the redwoods relinquish the slope the way they always have, down through the oak and the chaparral and the subdivisions now, the switchback roads, the high school, the water towers, the whole loud sprawl of a California beach town on a June morning; and she pays it no mind, for to a hawk a century is a season and a town a kind of weather. She is hunting. She is also, though she owns no word for it, bearing witness — as her bloodline has borne witness to this one curve of coast since before there was anyone upon it to be seen.

She comes out over the water at the point, by the lighthouse, where the swell wraps in and rears up into the long peeling walls of Steamer Lane; and there below her, in the cold glass of the morning surf, a young man drops down the face of one. Eighteen, perhaps, burnt brown by a whole summer of it — he pumps once and tucks up beneath the lip as the wave throws its hood over him, and for three entire seconds he is gone, sealed inside the spinning blue-green cathedral of it, the whole Pacific arched above his head — and then he is flung out the open end in a burst of spray and light, alive in the way only the young and the briefly perfect are ever permitted to be, and throws his head back and whoops, the cry going up thin and gull-thin to the hawk. His friends in the lineup hoot and beat the water; two girls on the cliff in the early sun watch him and pretend, with great care, that they are not watching; and the whole morning is eighteen years old, and salted, and gilded, and certain it will live forever. He will remember this wave when he is an old man. He does not know that yet. That is the one mercy of being him this morning.

She crosses the wharf — sea lions stacked and bellowing on the pilings beneath it, the first tourists nursing their coffees, and down on the wet sand a father and his small boy throwing a ball back and forth in the early light, the glove still too big for the boy's hand, the soft thock of the catch carrying up thin to the hawk — and the boardwalk unrolls below her, the old wooden boardwalk that has stood at the river's mouth more than a hundred years, and the Giant Dipper is already hauling its first cars up the long clattering lift. She watches a car breast the summit — a knot of kids wedged in together, a boy and a girl pressed shoulder to shoulder in the front seat, her hand already reaching for his arm — and tip over the lip and plunge, and the scream comes up off the whole train of them at once, that particular boardwalk scream that is terror and delight and being fifteen and turned loose for one summer afternoon. The girl seizes the boy's arm in earnest now, and neither of them will admit, after, that this was the moment; but it was, it always is — the drop is engineered for precisely this, to fling two strangers together hard enough that they forget, for one second, to pretend. Down on the planks the summer crowd drifts through the reek of funnel cake and saltwater taffy and sunscreen and the sea: adolescents in roving packs, tanned and loud and luminous, the girls armored in towels and inside jokes, the boys orbiting in studied indifference, every one of them fifteen and seventeen and beautiful in the way a person is beautiful only before being told what it costs — the whole golden machinery of an American summer turning at the rim of the continent.

And up on the cliff, and along the benches, and at the rail, the parents watch — and the hawk does not see them as they see themselves. The mother who worked a double every week of the spring to afford these three days, watching her daughter shriek down the Dipper and thinking *there — that — that is what it was all for.* The father priced half out of the town he was raised in, who wanted, more than he has wanted anything reasonable in years, to hand his children the exact careless gilded summer the boardwalk has been selling to families for a century, and scrimped, and brought them, and stands there now with a corn dog he cannot quite afford, watching them be happy, and is — for this one afternoon, for the span of one summer — given the thing he wanted. The boardwalk runs on that. It always has. It is an engine for the manufacture of the kind of day a family will tell itself about for the rest of its life.

The hawk drops lower, over the midway — the ring toss, the water race, and the old one, the oldest, the baseball throw: the stacked iron bottles, the kid behind the counter handing out the worn cloth balls. And here the bright morning snags, for a second, on something far older than the morning. The kid working the bottle game is poor. It shows in the shoes. A local boy on a summer job, brown, sixteen; and a knot of others has stopped at his booth, boys his own age but not his own kind, the kind whose summer is the boat and the club and the easy money, and they are not buying any throws. They work him over, lightly, grinning, in the way that has never needed a script: something about his accent, something about his shoes, one of them flicking a balled-up dollar at his chest and laughing when he flinches to catch it. The boy keeps his face still and his eyes at the level of their collars — the level the poor learn to keep — and waits for it to be over, the way the poor learn to wait. The hawk does not judge it.
