The hawk judges nothing; she only watches, as her bloodline has watched this coast since before the boardwalk, before the town, before any of it — and she has seen this small mean weather cross this same ground more often than the planks have been repainted, more summers than the people below have thought to count. It passes beneath her the way the tide passes, on a turning older and more patient than anything with a name, and she rides on.

She tilts and goes — back out over the water, the surfers, the cold bright bay — then banks inland, climbing, the town dwindling beneath her, the river drawing her up its ravine into the woods, the way her great-great-grandmother was drawn up the same ravine on a different morning.

And on the way up — where the river runs flat past the ballfields at Harvey West, in the green bottomland under the lime-kiln hills — she passes over a thing that has been happening on that ground, in one form or another, longer than the boardwalk has stood: a baseball game. Field 1 is dressed for a playoff this morning, every bell and whistle a small town can muster — the foul lines laid down fresh in lime, the little banners snapping on the backstop chain-link, the snack bar steaming, the scoreboard lit, a tinny PA reading out a lineup of nine-year-olds as though they were the '27 Yankees. The parents are folded into lawn chairs along the fence with their travel mugs and their held breath. And out on the mound, small in the middle of all that green geometry, a boy stands with the ball, and toes the rubber, and looks in for the sign — and for one held instant the whole bright morning belongs to him, the way it has belonged, on that exact patch of grass, to a hundred years of boys before him: the same chalked fairness, the same forty-six feet of nerve, the same green dream a poor coast keeps dreaming on its children no matter how often the dream is broken. The hawk has watched ball played on this ground since before it was a park, since it was a pasture, since it was nothing but the river's flat green floor; she has no word for it; she knows only the shape — the diamond, the small turning figures, the held breath — and she slides over it and on, up the ravine, leaving the boy on the mound to his one bright instant, which he will remember, like the surfer and his wave, when he is an old man.

She crosses a low rise above the river where a storage yard stands now — asphalt, steel roll-up doors, a chain-link fence, nothing, a lot no one looks at twice — and sets her wings and drops and takes the high limb of an old redwood that has somehow outlived the rest: the last great tree on a patch of ground that has been a parking lot, and a cannery, and a vacant field, and, longer ago than the asphalt can remember, a poor cluster of houses in the smoke of a tannery, where a family lived.

She folds her wings. She fixes the round gold eye on the ground below — the asphalt, the fence, the nothing — and holds her station over the exact patch of California where the Méndez house once stood, where a boy named for her once hauled water and looked up.

Nothing is there now to mark it. The asphalt does not remember the cannery it replaced, nor the field before the cannery, nor the low houses before the field, nor the tannery smoke that hung over them, nor the family that hauled its water across that dirt and argued and laughed and buried its dead in a language the lot has long since paved over. But the redwood remembers in the only way a tree remembers, which is to go on standing on the spot; and the hawk remembers in the only way her kind remembers, which is to come back, and come back, to the same branch over the same ground across more lifetimes than the people below have ever had names for.

She holds still on the branch, in the gold eye, in that long patience that does not trouble to tell a century from an afternoon; and the asphalt thins, and the fence with it, and the cannery and the field thin away behind them; and the smoke comes up again, low, over a close poor cluster of houses; and the morning is another morning, earlier and harder and still entirely ahead of itself; and down in the swept dirt of the yard a child too small for the bucket riding his hip stops, and feels a shadow cross him, and tips his face up to find it.

Nothing, yet, has been lost.

### Chapter — El Halcón

A hundred-some years before that bright loud summer morning — before the asphalt, before the Dipper, before the surfer in the barrel — the same warm wind worked the same ridge, and a hawk rode it down the same ravine toward the same bay: this one's great-great-grandmother, or near enough, over a country not yet smudged at the river's mouth by more than a single thin column of tannery smoke. She dropped the last of the height and set her wings and took a high bare limb of a live oak on the edge of a poor cluster of houses in the tannery's shadow, where the smell of the hides was, where the river met the work. She folded her wings. She fixed the round gold eye on the dirt yard below.

And below, a boy of four, hefting a water bucket built for a grown man, stops, and feels the shadow slide over him, and looks up — the way he will look up the whole of his life, the way that will frighten his grandfather and the country both — and sees her there against the white sky, immense and motionless, and does not run, and is not afraid, and lifts one hand to her as to someone he already knows.

His grandfather, watching from the doorway, sees the boy and the bird regard each other, and something very old turns over in the old man's chest. *El halcón,* Don Aurelio says under his breath, half a blessing and half a fear. *Mira, mijo. The hawk has come to look at you.* And from that morning it was the boy's name in the family — El Halcón, the hawk — the name his own people gave him out of love and the old knowledge, long before strangers far from here would take one look at a brown man who could throw and fix on him instead a borrowed, ill-fitting, alien name of their own choosing, and call the giving of it a compliment. He carried two names his whole life and answered, in his heart, only to the first: the one the hawk gave him, that meant *he belongs to the high air and the deep country and the people who watch the sky;* the one the women softened, when they wanted him near, to the smallest and the largest word there is.

*Mijo,* his mother called from the house. *Mijo — leave the bird and bring the water.*

### Chapter — The Mission

Before the Méndez had anything to lose, somebody else had already lost it to them; and Don Aurelio, who was an honest man in the particular way only the very old can afford to be, never once told the family's story without telling that part too — though it would have been easier, and more flattering, to leave it out.

Long before the Americans, before even the grant — before there was a Villa de Branciforte or a single adobe on the river — there were the people the Spanish called the Costanoans, the coast people, who had lived along that bay for longer than anyone could count: who had eaten from it and burned the meadows to tend it and buried their dead on the same hills the Méndez would one day climb to bury theirs. They had a word for themselves that was not Costanoan, that the Spanish never troubled to learn. And the Spanish came up the coast with the cross in one hand and the brand in the other, and built a mission — Santa Cruz, the Holy Cross — and gathered the coast people into it to save their souls and break their bodies, and called them neophytes, and worked them; and the measles and the smallpox and the simple crushing grief did the rest, so that within two generations the people who had owned that bay since before Rome were a remnant, a handful, a name in a baptismal register kept by a priest in a language not their own. And when Mexico threw off Spain and broke up the missions and handed the land out in great grants to its loyal men, the Méndez were among the men it was handed *to* — and the land it was handed *from* was the land of the coast people, who were given nothing, who were not even in the room, who were by then so far under the wheel that no one thought to count them as having been robbed at all.

*Remember that, mijo,* the old man would say, and his voice when he said it carried no self-pity and no self-excuse either, only the flat terrible honesty of a man reading the whole ledger. *When the Americans took our rancho, we cried that it was stolen, and we were right. But we were standing on it. We did not buy that land from anyone who had the right to sell it — the King of Spain gave it, and the King of Spain had it because his soldiers took it from people who are mostly in the ground now, with no grandsons left to climb a hill and pull the weeds and say their name. So when you feel the curse on us, the always-second, the being-pushed-down — do not let it make you innocent. We were pushed down by men who were once us. The wheel does not turn only against the Méndez. It turns. It has always only turned. And the only question a man gets to answer is not whether he was wronged — everyone was wronged, everyone did wrong, the whole coast is built on bones — but whether, when it is briefly his turn near the top of the wheel, he reaches down or he reaches up.* And then the old man would fall quiet a while, and look toward the hills where both the coast people and the Méndez children lay in their separate, unequal grounds, and say the thing that was the deepest root of everything he taught the boy: *We are not cursed because we are good and they are bad. There is no such story, not really. We are cursed the way everyone is cursed — by being part of it. The grace is not in being clean. Nobody is clean. The grace is in what you do with dirty hands.*

### Chapter — The Burn

The coast people set the world on fire every year, on purpose, with love — and it was the most careful tending that ground had ever known, or would know again until long after everyone who remembered how to do it was dead.

They burned the meadows in the dry season, low cool fires walked across the land in the evening when the wind lay down; and the fire was not destruction to them but its opposite, the central act of keeping. It cleared the brush before the brush could choke the oaks; it brought the deer to the tender green that rose after; it kept the meadows open and the seed-grasses generous; it gave the willow and the hazel the straight new shoots a woman needed to weave a basket tight enough to hold water. The land they had was not wilderness. That was the lie the newcomers told themselves to feel clean about taking it — that it was empty, untended, *wild,* a thing no one had made. It was made. It was the most made thing on the whole coast, made by ten thousand years of small wise fires, a garden the size of a country, tended so lightly that the conquerors could not even see it was a garden, and called it wilderness, and improved it into ruin.

For the Spanish, and the Americans after them, fire was the devil's own tool, and they forbade the burning — and the land, untended, did what untended land does: it thickened, and choked, and grew rank with the very fuel the small fires had always eaten, the brush piling up dry under the trees year on year, an account quietly building toward a reckoning. The careful annual grief of the cool walking fires was outlawed as savagery, in the name of order; and the order was a lie, because what it actually grew was not safety but the slow patient loading of a gun. Don Aurelio, who had seen the meadows in the last years anyone burned them and watched the hills grow sick and shaggy and dangerous in the decades after, understood the thing the coast people had known and the newcomers could not afford to learn — the same thing Dolores knew about grief, that she gave its Sunday hour and not one inch more: that there is a kind of fire that is tending, the small regular honest losses a living thing must take to stay alive; and that a people, or a family, or a land that forbids itself those small fires in the name of never letting anything burn is only saving it all up. The fire still comes. It always comes. And when it has been forbidden long enough, it comes all at once, and takes everything, and is called a tragedy — as though no one had spent forty tidy years building it.

### Chapter — The Whip

There is one night the coast people are remembered for doing more than enduring, and Don Aurelio told it the way he told everything — without making a hero of anyone, because, he said, the moment you turn a man into a hero you stop being able to see him.

In the autumn of 1812, at the mission on the hill above the river, there was a priest named Andrés Quintana, and there was a whip, and the whip was the whole of the trouble. The lash was the daily coin of the mission — the coast people had been gathered in to be saved and were kept in by it, and they bore it the way the conquered bear most things, in a silence their keepers mistook for consent. But Quintana had ordered a new one made, its ends fitted with twists of iron wire so that it bit past the skin and tore what was under it; and he had laid it across the back of a man named Donato until the iron had done its work, and let it be known there was more of the same to come. And in the quarters that night a thing took shape that the baptismal registers kept no column for: the people decided — the way water decides, at the last, to find the one crack in the dam — that they would not be opened with iron again, and that the only way to be sure of it was that the hand which held the whip should stop.

They used the surest summons there was, the one a priest could not refuse: a man was dying, down in the orchard, and needed the last rites. Quintana came — he believed, in his way, in the souls he was breaking — and they took him in the dark under the apple trees, and he asked them the question that the boy who would one day tell the story never forgot: *What have I done to you, my children, that you should kill me?* And one of them gave him the only answer there was, which was the whole indictment of three hundred years in a single plain sentence: *Because you made a whip with iron in it.* And then they killed him with their hands, and carried him back, and laid him in his bed to look as though God had taken him in his sleep; and for a while the registers believed it, because the registers believed whatever the priests wrote in them.

It was sixty-five years before the true account was set down, and when it came it came the way the true account in this country always comes — not from the men who kept the records but from a son. An old neophyte's boy named Lorenzo Asisara, gone gray, the mission a heap of melted adobe and the coast people all but gone from the earth, sat with a man taking down the old stories in 1877 and gave it back entire, exactly as his father had handed it to him: the iron whip, the dying-man's lie, the orchard, the question and the answer, the names. The friars had kept the registers, and the registers were a ruin in a drawer. The son had kept the story, and the story was alive in his mouth. And Don Aurelio — who worked leather his whole life, and whose grandson would one day make a name on a ball sewn from a hide — never finished telling it on his overturned bucket without his own hand drifting, once, unbidden, to the back of his own neck. Then he would get down off the bucket, the telling done, and go back to the yard where the hides waited under the same hill, and for the rest of that day he would say nothing more.

### Chapter — The Grant

Before this was a town for strangers — before the lights on the water and the laughing on the pier — it was ours. All of it. Don Aurelio would say it the way other men say prayers: he said it until the words wore smooth and stopped meaning anything to anyone but himself; and yet it was true, and the truth of it was the whole foundation the family stood on, long after the family had nothing else left to stand on at all.

In 1833 the young Mexican republic — hungry, idealistic, and broke — did a thing both generous and ruinous: it unlocked the missions, broke the friars' long hold on the coast, and handed God's land back to men. A governor's agent rode down from Monterey with a leather folio of grants, and one of them, when the dust settled, made Don Joaquín Méndez — Cirilo's great-great-grandfather, a blunt, devout, illiterate man who signed with a cross and wept doing it — a *ranchero.* The grant ran from the river to the redwood line, some leagues of hills the color of a lion in August, and Don Joaquín stood at the edge of it the first evening and could not speak, because no man in his family, as far back as the family could count, had ever owned anything a king could not take back on a whim.

They raised the low white adobe that held the cool like a cellar, and a chapel beside it, and ran the first cattle down into the bottoms, and for one brief generation the valley belonged, more or less, to the people who worked it. *Gente de razón,* they called themselves — people of reason, people of name — Mexicans and Catholics and, before and above all of it, owners.

But the hands that built the adobe and worked the cattle were not, most of them, Méndez hands, and this is the part of the story Don Aurelio told less often, and more quietly, and only when he was being honest — which old age made him more, and not less. The vaqueros and the diggers and the women at the Méndez fires were Ohlone, Awaswas people, the ones who had been at the mission before there was a Méndez grant to work, who came out of the broken mission with their old world gone and their new one already owned by men like Don Joaquín. The finest of the Méndez riders was one of them — a lean, silent man the family called Esteban, the *first* Esteban, the one Cirilo's father would be named for three generations on — who could gentle a wild horse with his breath and his hands, and who had been born, the mission books said, in a village whose name no one living could any longer pronounce. He worked the land the Méndez had been given. He owned none of it. The family that would spend the next hundred years being dispossessed had been handed its one brief kingdom, in part, on the back of a man more dispossessed than they would ever be.

It is a particular thing, to be born into a family that owned the ground beneath its own feet, and then to watch that ownership carried off across the length of a single life. It teaches a lesson the secure never have to learn — that everything is a loan, and that the lenders, when they come to collect, do not knock. But there is a harder lesson underneath it, the one Don Aurelio reached only at the very end: that his own people had, for one generation, been lenders too; that the wheel which ground them had ground others first beneath their own weight; and that the only hands in the whole long story that were ever truly clean were the hands of the people who had never been given anything to dirty them with.

### Chapter — California Banknotes

The wealth came on the hoof and left on the hide, and the whole bright machinery of it ran on blood and salt and the patience of men who owned nothing.

Each spring the vaqueros rode the cattle down out of the hills for the *matanza* — the great killing — and for a week the bottoms ran red and the gulls came inland in white storms and the work went on from dark to dark. The Ohlone riders did the killing and the Méndez men kept the count, and the women boiled the tallow in iron pots big enough to bathe a child in, and the hides went out to dry, staked in their hundreds to the earth like a field of dark windows turned up at the sky. It was filthy, holy, exhausting work, and the smell of it — tallow smoke and blood and salt and green hide — was the smell of money, exactly the way the tannery's stink would be the smell of money to the same family eighty years on, when the hides belonged to a Russian and the family only worked them.

For in those years a cowhide was as good as coin, and better. The Yankee ships came round the Horn and stood off the cove, and their captains — lean, courteous, devouring men, the polite leading edge of an empire that had not yet bothered to announce itself — traded silver and silk and Boston boots and bolts of cloth for the *cueros* the rancheros rowed out to them. *California banknotes,* the traders called the hides, and laughed when they said it, and meant it, and wrote down in their ledgers, in a language no ranchero could read, the exact future price of everything. One of them, a Captain Bray out of Boston who came three seasons running, ate at the Méndez table and admired the chapel and learned to dance the jota badly and with great good will, and who would live long enough to file, in 1852, one of the squatter claims that helped take the grant apart. He bore the family no ill will at all. That was the terrible thing about the men who took it: so few of them bore any ill will. They wanted the land the way water wants the downhill, and they came, and they were charming, and they stayed for supper — and then one morning the supper was theirs.

And on the rancho, in a shed that smelled of oak bark and lime, the family tanned its own leather, from its own herds, on its own ground. The leather was theirs before it was anyone's; the hands knew the work because the work had once been a kingship.

Across the San Lorenzo, the rough little pueblo of Branciforte gambled and brawled and drank — a town the Spanish crown had stocked, a lifetime before, with the swept-out contents of a Guadalajara jail, petty criminals for whom a stretch of empty California coast had been a sentence and a mercy at once — and on feast nights the guitars from the Méndez fandango carried over the black water to meet the fiddles and the cursing of the pueblo, so that the sacred bank of the river and the profane bank of the river sang to each other across the dark, and a boy lying awake in the white adobe could believe the whole arrangement of the world was permanent, and benevolent, and his.

It seemed it would last forever. It lasted about as long as one strong man's memory.

### Chapter — The Otters

Before they took the land, they took the otters; and the otters were the first lesson, the one nobody learned, in what that coast was going to be *for.*

The sea otter wears the densest fur in the animal world — near a million hairs to the square inch, for it has no blubber and must hold the cold sea off with pelt alone — and that fur, in the markets of Canton, was worth a small fortune, and so the otter was the first wealth of that coast to be noticed by the wider hungry world. Long before the hide ships, before the gold, before a single rancho was surveyed into squares, the fur hunters came — Russians and the Aleut men they pressed into the killing, Yankee and Spanish and English captains — and worked the kelp beds and the rocky points and the river mouths, and took the otters; took them by the thousand and the ten thousand; took them, with the terrible efficiency of a market that feels no cost it is not charged for, very nearly all the way down to none. Within a few decades the sea that had swarmed with them lay nearly empty of them, and the trade, having exhausted the thing it fed on, simply moved along the coast to the next bay and the next, the way such trades do, leaving silence behind in the kelp.

And the kelp itself paid for it, in the second lesson nobody learned: for the otters had eaten the urchins, and with the otters gone the urchins multiplied unchecked and mowed the great kelp forests down to bare rock, urchin barrens where a drowned green country had swayed — so that the kelp the hawk saw rocking offshore in Cirilo's boyhood was already a remnant of a remnant, the whole near-shore world quietly poorer, its richest weave unraveled, because of a fashion in a city on the far side of an ocean that would never see the cove it had emptied. This was the pattern, set on that coast before any human living there was dispossessed: find the most valuable thing, take all of it, take it for a distant market that is charged nothing for the damage, and move on. The otters were only the rehearsal. The hides were next, and then the redwoods, and then the lime, and then — for it is the same act exactly, only the cargo changes — the men in the kilns and the fields and the tannery, spent and taken and replaced, for markets that would never see the canyon or the culvert or the cost.

A few otters lived. A remnant hid in one cove down the wild coast and was overlooked, and held on, and a century on began, slowly, to come back along the shore.

### Chapter — The Fandango

There was one night Don Aurelio returned to all his life, though he had not been born in time for it; he had it from his father, who had it as a boy, and he told it so often and so well that it became, in the family, less a memory than a kind of scripture — the night of the great fandango, the high-water mark of everything, the last good year before any of them knew there had ever been a last good year.

It was the close of the *matanza,* the spring the hides ran to nine hundred, and Don Joaquín — the patriarch who had wept signing the grant — threw the whole rancho open. They roasted a steer and two pigs over pits dug that afternoon; they hung lanterns in the oaks; the women had been three days at the tamales and the women's mothers three days at the women. By dark the yard was full: Méndez cousins down from Branciforte, rancheros from three valleys, the vaqueros in their best with silver on their hatbands earned across a hard year — and the Ohlone families who worked the place gathered at the edge of the light, where the custom of the country kept them, near enough to the music to dance to it and far enough from the fire to remember whose fire it was.

A guitar started, and then another, and then old blind Cipriano's fiddle, and the jota went up into the oaks and the yard began to turn. And the finest dancer of the whole night — every account agreed, even the ones that wished otherwise — was the Ohlone vaquero the family called Esteban, the first Esteban, the one Cirilo's father would be named for three generations on: a man who could gentle a horse with his breath and who danced the way he rode, all stillness and sudden grace, his boots going like a drum on the packed earth while the rest of him stayed quiet as a held note. The Yankee was there too — Captain Bray, off the hide ship, three seasons a guest, learning the jota badly and laughing at himself and being loved for the laughing, a courteous, devouring man who admired the chapel and the dancing and the daughters, and who was, though no soul in that firelight could have known it, the polite advance scout of the very thing that would carry all of this off inside ten years. He danced with Don Joaquín's nieces. He toasted the old man's health in bad Spanish. And he noted, meaning nothing by it yet, the run of the creek and the line of the hills.

And a boy of nine — the boy who would grow into Don Aurelio's father, who would believe the treaty and lose the rancho and end with a measure of his mind gone — sat on the chapel step for half the night and watched it all: the turning yard, the leaping fire, his grandfather presiding like a king over a country that had not yet been told it was about to stop being a country. He carried that night intact through everything that came after, the way a man carries the one photograph he saved from a fire, and he handed it down to his son Aurelio, who handed it down the line — so that eighty years later, on a tannery floor and a Brooklyn ball field and a Boston deathbed, some buried Méndez cell still remembered, without knowing that it remembered, the smell of tallow smoke and crushed sage and the sound of a blind man's fiddle going up into the oaks on the last good night of the world that was theirs. It seemed it would last forever.

### Chapter — The Courthouse

The conquest, when it came, did not come with a sword. It came with a courthouse, which is worse, because a sword at least admits what it is.

The thunder came from the north first, in 1846: a flag run up by a mob of American settlers at Sonoma, with a bear on it that looked, the Californios said, like a badly drawn pig; then a war that was over almost before the rancho had heard it begun; then the stars and stripes raised over the customhouse at Monterey on a July morning where the eagle of Mexico had flown the day before. Don Joaquín was an old man by then. He rode up to see it, and came home and said nothing for three days, and then said only: *They have planted their flag. Now we will see whether they keep their word.*

In 1848 came the word, on paper, signed and sworn: the Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo, by which the United States, upon its honor, guaranteed to the conquered Californios the full enjoyment of their property — that what was theirs would remain theirs. Don Joaquín's son, Don Aurelio's own father, a gentler and more trusting man than the old ranchero, believed it entirely. That was his single crime, and it cost him the rancho, his health, and at the last a measure of his mind.

For the new country did not take the land at gunpoint. It took it by *process,* which is slower, and leaves no scar a man can point to. The Land Act of 1851 required every Californio to appear before a commission — seated in San Francisco, three hard days to the north — and prove, in English, with documents, before American lawyers, that the ground beneath his own chapel was lawfully his; and the burden of proof lay on the man who already held the land. For in the new country the land itself was no longer the wealth — the *paper* was the wealth; and the men who dealt in paper took their fee in the only thing a cash-poor ranchero had, which was the land the paper covered. So the Californios came to the law holding deeds in a dead king's hand, and won their cases, and walked out holding nothing — the title saved and the rancho gone in the same long breath, carried off by the very men who had saved it.

And then, as though the courthouse had not been enough, the sky turned. The great flood of the early sixties drowned the valley bottoms and the herds with them, and behind the flood came the long drought of '64, two years without rain, the hills gone white with the bones of cattle that had been California banknotes and were now not worth the cost of skinning. The cattle economy that had built the rancho died in a single dry season, up and down the whole coast, and the Méndez sold what the lawyers had left them the way a man bleeds — not all at once, but a parcel for a debt and a parcel for a winter, until there was no more to sell.

It was somewhere in those years that Don Aurelio, a young man watching his father fail, began to believe the thing he would carry the rest of his life and never quite say aloud: that the family was *cursed* now — cursed to second, that the conquest had taken not only the land but the right ever again to be first at anything — and that the world would collect, hard and without mercy and on its own schedule, from any Méndez fool enough to forget it. He was wrong. But he had earned the error honestly, in a paneled room in San Francisco, watching one decent man take four years to hand his family back a kingdom that another decent man had already quietly carried off in his briefcase.

### Chapter — The Hearing

And here is the other night the family carried, the black twin of the fandango: the day in San Francisco, ten years on, when they began to lose all of it in a paneled room.

Don Aurelio was fourteen, and he had come north with his father on the coastal steamer — three days of seasickness and dread — carrying a leather folio that held the whole of the family's claim to the earth it had lived on for twenty years: the Mexican grant with the governor's seal, and the *diseño,* the map, drawn in his grandfather's own hand, a thing of beauty and uselessness, with a sycamore for one corner and the bend of the creek for another and a cattle skull, carefully inked, to mark where the land met the sea. In the country that had made that map, every man who mattered knew the sycamore and the creek-bend and the skull. In the country that had replaced it, the map was a child's drawing, and the room they carried it into did not run on knowing the land. It ran on paper, and on English, and on time — of which it had an infinite supply and the Méndez had none.

The commissioner was a man named Hollister, gray and overworked and — Aurelio decided, across the four years it took — not a bad man: one who privately thought the whole proceeding a national disgrace, and who would in the end rule for the family; but who had four hundred such claims stacked on his docket and one ordinary mortal lifespan in which to hear them, and who therefore let each grind forward at the speed of cold honey, asking through an interpreter the same question a fourth and a fifth time, requiring a boundary witness who had to be fetched three hundred miles, adjourning, adjourning, adjourning. And the family's own attorney — a courtly San Franciscan named Quint, who took the case because there was a fee in it, and the fee, since the Méndez had no cash, was *land* — sat at the father's elbow through all of it being, by the standards of his profession, scrupulously fair: a quarter-league deeded over for the filing, a quarter-league for the first appeal, a quarter-league when the rival claim (entered, as it happened, by a genial retired sea-captain named Bray, who bore the family no ill will at all) had to be answered.

Aurelio sat on a hard bench against the wall and watched his father — who had believed the treaty — come slowly to understand, across four years, that he had been robbed without anyone in the room ever once doing a thing a sheriff could arrest them for; that the robbery *was* the process, not a break in it; that he would win, and be ruined by the winning, and that there was no man he could call out, no door he could kick, no single villain anywhere in the whole patient machine. It was the having-no-one-to-hate, Don Aurelio would say eighty years later, that broke his father's mind. A man can survive being robbed. It is being robbed *politely* — lawfully, by decent men following the rules, with a receipt — that there is no surviving.

They rode home down the coast with the title confirmed and the rancho three-quarters gone, and Don Joaquín met them at the landing, very old now, and read his son's face before a word was spoken, and only nodded — the way you nod at news you have been expecting for years. *They kept their word,* the old man said at last, looking out at the hills that were no longer theirs. *That was the cruelest thing they could have done. If they had only lied to us, we would at least have had something left to forgive.* And he turned and walked up toward the chapel, and that was the last anyone remembered him saying about it, ever.

### Chapter — The Culvert

What Esteban Méndez saw from the culvert when he was twelve years old, he never told his children, and they never asked — because a man's children learn early which doors in him do not open; but they lived their whole lives inside the house that day built, and so, in a way, they knew it without being told.

It was in the bad years, after the land was gone, when the family had fallen from the rancho to wage-work and the county had filled up with new men who did not know the Méndez had ever been anyone and would not have cared — years when a Mexican in that part of California was a thing it was safe to do something to, because the law that had taken the land had also, quietly, stopped counting Mexican grief as the kind that summoned consequences. Esteban had been fast then — the quickest boy in the flats, faster than any Anglo kid in the county — and a boy that fast draws a crowd, and a crowd draws the wrong kind of attention; and the attention, that day, did not fall on Esteban. It fell, the way it so often fell, on the one standing nearest him who was darker still: his friend Toussaint, a Black boy who could hit a ball into the next county, who answered for a crime that was really only the crime of having been admired alongside a fast boy the county had not yet decided how to fear — and answered for it with his life, off a low oak limb. And young Esteban, shoved down and held there by bigger hands that had the sense he did not yet have, had gone still as the stone in the mouth of a culvert under the road.

And from there, through a screen of weeds, in a culvert that smelled of mud and cold iron, twelve years old, he watched. He watched all of it and made no sound — because making a sound would have meant two of them in the road instead of one, and some animal arithmetic in him had already done that sum; and he was right, and it saved his life, and it cost him the rest of his life, both, in the same held breath. When the horses had gone he did not come out for a long time. When he came out he was the man he would be forever after: the gray careful man, the man who flinched at raised voices and loud joy alike, the man who wanted nothing from the world except to be beneath its notice, who taught his sons by example that the safest size for a Méndez to be was small, the safest sound none, the safest dream no dream at all. People who did not know took it for weakness, even cowardice. It was neither. It was a wound — perfectly rational, dressed every morning and never healed: a man who had learned at twelve, definitively, in a culvert, what the world did to those who stood up in the road — and to those who only stood beside them when they did, for it was his own quickness that had drawn the eyes that killed his friend — and who loved his children too much to want them ever to learn it the way he had. And the terrible thing — the thing Cirilo understood only as a grown man, with his own son coming, the thing that broke his heart finally and completely about his father — was that Esteban was not wrong. He was, in the plain bookkeeping of that country in those years, simply correct. To reach was to be aimed at. He had the evidence in his own eyes, screened by weeds, for the whole of his silent life. And he had spent that life trying to give his sons the one gift the culvert had given him, which he believed with his whole frightened loving heart was the only gift that would keep them alive: the instinct to get small, to get gone, to fold into the stone and make no sound. That Cirilo did the opposite — stood up in the road, in the daylight, on the mound, under the lights, where the whole country could see him reach — was the great defiance of his life and the great grief between him and his father; and both of them were right, which is the worst and truest kind of quarrel there is: the kind where love and fear want the same boy to do opposite things, and neither of them is wrong, and only the world that made the quarrel is.

### Chapter — The Tannery Floor

By the time Don Aurelio was an old man, the new Santa Cruz had risen entire on the grave of the old. Lime kilns smoked on the hills where the cattle had grazed; the long wharf walked out into the bay; the Powder Works thumped down the river and the tanneries steamed along the creek, and strangers' money was everywhere, and the family's name was on nothing at all. The language went indoors. The rosary stayed out — that one act of defiance, kept up in the window where the street could see it, the last unsold acre of the rancho.

And on a winter morning in 1882, Don Aurelio's son Esteban — named for the silent Ohlone vaquero who had gentled horses on land the family no longer owned — walked down off the hill and through the gate of Fyodor Volkov's tannery to do, for a Russian's wages, the one thing the Méndez had always known how to do: turn hide into leather. Only now the hide was another man's, and the leather was another man's, and the lime that had once tanned the family's own kingship into boots and harness only ate, slowly, at a hired man's hands. *They left us our hands,* the old man would say in the years after, to anyone who would sit still for it. *So we gave them to the leather.* It was the closest he ever came to telling the whole story, and most people thought it only an old man's saying, and let it pass.

That same morning, in the back room where the women waited and Dolores held the basin, a child was born — wet and red and roaring, the way a new hide comes off the beam. They wiped him clean, and the old man, ancient already and the last of them who still carried the kingdom whole in his memory, bent over the basket and took the small hands in his and turned them in the gray light coming through the tannery window. Already big. Already strong. And he did not yet know what they would do, only that whatever the courthouse and the conquest and the long bleeding century had carried off from this family, they had not, somehow, carried off *this* — the hands were unbroken, and they were the family's own, and no lawyer's briefcase and no squatter's fence and no commissioner's four-year patience had ever yet managed to carry a man's hands away from him while he lived.

The family was not finished. Not while there was a Méndez with hands.

And so the whole long story that follows — the boardwalk and the booth and the girl who laughed; the cinder fields and the long climb and the man in the derby; the cold rented rooms and the second-place years and everything still ahead of them and unguessed — all of it begins here, on a tannery floor, in the shadow of a stolen rancho, with a dying old man holding a newborn's hands up to a gray window and refusing, one last time, against ninety years of his own hard evidence, to believe that his family had been born to be second.

His name was Cirilo. Everything that mattered, in the end, was in the hands.

---

## _Siempre Segundo_ — Act I, novelized (full chapters)

### Chapter — Volkov

Fyodor Volkov owned the tannery that owned Esteban Méndez, and the truth of Fyodor Volkov — the inconvenient, shades-of-grey truth a simpler story would not trouble with — is that he was not a cruel man, was in some ways a decent one, and that it did not matter nearly as much as decency is supposed to.

He had come out of Russia in '52 with a trade in his hands and nothing else — a tanner's son out of the south, from somewhere past Odessa — who had crossed an ocean and a continent to reach a coast that had lived in his family's mouth since before he was born — and arrived to find, as the dispossessed Californios were finding in the same years from the other direction, that the golden country ran on a simpler principle than the broadsheets admitted: that it belonged to whoever got there in time to take it, and that *in time* meant after the Indians and the Mexicans had been cleared off it and before the next wave of hungry men arrived to compete. Volkov got there in time. He bought tannery land cheap — land that had been rancho land, Méndez land, a generation before, though he neither knew nor would have cared, having purchased it fair from the American who had purchased it foul — and he built, with genuine skill and genuine sweat, a genuinely good tannery, and worked beside his own men in the early years, and was proud of the work, and the pride was not entirely misplaced. He paid a wage. In the country of that time, paying a wage at all to the men the country had just robbed was, God help us, a kind of progress, and Fyodor Volkov knew it, and took for himself a quiet credit that was both real and obscene — the way so much of that country's decency was both at once.

For California was not a rumor to the Volkovs the way it was to the other newcomers; it was an old family wound. Fyodor's father had reached this coast a generation before any of them — a *promyshlennik,* a fur man of the Russian-American Company, one of the hunters who worked north of the bay out of the wooden fort the Russians raised at Ross, taking the sea otters by the thousand for the Tsar and the China trade: the very slaughter that had emptied the kelp before the first hide ship ever stood off the cove. The Volkovs, in other words, were among the first takers of that whole coast — the rehearsal, not the performance — and Fyodor was raised on his father's stories of it: the fort above the cold gray sea, the Aleut hunters in their skin boats, the otters, the cannon that never fired in anger, the brief strange Russian California that was meant to last forever and did not. Because the Tsar, an ocean away, looked at his ledger one year and judged the colony did not pay, and sold the fort to an American for cash and cattle and sailed his people home — and some of them would not go; some stayed on, the Volkovs among them, stranded subjects of an empire that had decided they were not worth the keeping. Fyodor grew up in Russia on the far side of that abandonment, the son of a man who had helped take a country and then been left behind by the very crown that took it; and he came back to California in '52 the way a man returns to a house his family was once evicted from — to make, this time, a holding no distant ledger could ever sell out from under him. He had learned, long before he reached the tannery, the same lesson Esteban learned in the culvert and Dolores learned on the hill, the one every outsider on that coast learned in the end: that whatever you love, the empire takes; that the safe course is to want quietly, hold low, and keep the precious thing out of the light. It was why, in the end, he could not bear to let his daughter shine.

He was good to Esteban, in the way of the time: he kept him on for forty years, gave him the better bench, learned his name and used it, and at Christmas pressed a coin into the hands of the Méndez children with a heavy Russian kindness that was perfectly sincere; and which is also to say that he paid Esteban a fraction of what the work was worth, never moved him past the floor, and would have been genuinely, woundedly astonished to be told that the warm coin and the long employment and the remembered name were only the velvet on a thing that was, underneath, simply the orderly transfer of a broken man's whole working life into another man's ledger. He did not see it. He was a good man as the word was priced in that country and that trade, and the price was low, and he paid it gladly and thought himself generous — and when Esteban's gifted boy threw a baseball in a way that drew Eastern scouts, Fyodor Volkov was, in his own front parlor, sincerely glad for the family, and said so, and meant it, and never connected the boy's long impossible climb to anything he himself had done or failed to do; for the tannery and the diamond were, to Fyodor Volkov, simply different countries, and he had no map that showed the road of hides running straight from his own cheap-bought land to the stitched ball in the boy's hand. He died respected, mourned, and solvent, a pillar of the small Russian Orthodox chapel, buried at last under a good stone in its ground across town from the small markers Dolores tended — and the leather he had spent his life turning out went on into the world and became, among ten thousand other things, the cover of a baseball and the pocket of a glove, and he never knew that either, and would not have understood why it mattered.

### Chapter — Anastasia

Fyodor Volkov kept two things out of the light above all others, and they were his wife and his daughter, and the keeping of them was the truest and the saddest thing about him.

His wife — Galina — had crossed with him and never once, in forty years, learned more of the new country's tongue than a shopkeeper required; she kept the tannery house in the old way, lit the lamp before the ikon in its corner, and watched the bright loud American street from behind a curtain she rarely drew, a woman who had left one country and never quite arrived in the next, living out her life in the narrow shadow her husband had decided was safe. And out of that shadowed house, into the California sun her mother feared, came their one child: Anastasia.

She was, simply, the most striking creature on the flats, and everyone who saw her said so, and Fyodor heard every one of them say it with a falling heart. Black hair, and green eyes — the wolf-green of the name she carried, Volkova, the she-wolf — a face that stopped men in the street and made the bluff girls go quiet and unkind; a beauty not of the soft safe kind but the arresting, dangerous kind that a poor immigrant's daughter is the least equipped of anyone to carry through the world unharmed. Fyodor looked at her and did not see, the way other men saw, a wonder. He saw a target. He had learned, across the long Volkov education in being left and being taken from, that the beautiful thing draws the empire's eye, and the empire's eye is never kind for long — and so he did the thing his whole bitter history had taught him, the very thing Esteban was doing with his sons in a different key down the road: he tried to keep her small. Schooled her at home. Kept her from the boardwalk, the dances, the bright exposed places. Dressed her plain. Told her, in Russian, in the lamp-lit house, the one commandment of his life — *stay out of the light, Nastya; the light is where they find you* — and could not see, frightened loving man that he was, that he was building with his own hands the very cage he most dreaded the world would build for her.

And the one crack in that cage, the window Fyodor could never quite wall up, was the boy who hauled water past the house. Cirilo and Anastasia had grown up across the tannery yard from each other, the worker's son and the owner's daughter, near enough in age and far enough from any other children that they became, before either had a word for it, a kind of brother and sister — the creek, the lime-yard, the long afternoons; she taught him Russian words he kept the rest of his life, he taught her the flats and the tide pools and how to put a stone through a tin can; and he was, of all the people in her walled small world, the only one who was simply, uncomplicatedly hers. He never looked at her the way the street looked at her. She was the one girl Cirilo Méndez never romanticized — for a man does not romanticize his sister — and that very fact made him the safest person she knew and, in time, the most dangerous; because a girl shut in a shadow will use the one unlocked window she has, and Cirilo, who could no more abide a cage than he could his own father's fear, was always, fatally, going to help her climb out of hers.

### Chapter — Marcella

Anastasia had one friend in the world who was not Cirilo, and that friend was the cure for very nearly everything Fyodor's shadow had done to her, and her name was Marcella Bregante; and where Anastasia had been raised to take up no light at all, Marcella had been raised, by an enormous and deafening family, to fill a room the way the sun fills a doorway.

The Bregantes were Genovese, off the Ligurian coast — from Riva Trigoso, a fishing village where the men had pulled sardines out of one blue sea for a thousand years before they crossed an ocean to pull them out of another — and they had come the way the Genovese came, one cousin sending the boat fare for the next, until half of Riva Trigoso seemed to have been carried whole into a few crowded blocks of the flats, the dialect and the saints and the recipes and the feuds all intact. Marcella's father and uncles fished the lampara fleet alongside Nunzio Sabatini's people; her mother and aunts and grandmother ran a kitchen that never closed and fed anyone who darkened the door, that turned out focaccia and farinata and a basil sauce ground in a marble mortar whose smell got into your hair and your dreams. And on the nights the men came in off the water singing — the old Genoese way, the *trallalero,* five and six of them with no instrument at all, weaving a high wild polyphony out of nothing but their own throats across the dark — the whole Bregante house spilled into the lane, and the wine came out, and the long tables, and nobody on God's earth stayed a stranger longer than it took to put a plate in their hands. It was the loudest, the warmest, the most crowded and least frightened world Anastasia had ever been permitted to stand inside. It was the exact opposite of her father's lamp-lit silence. And Marcella — brown-armed, fearless, quick to laugh from the belly — took one look at the beautiful lonely Russian girl who flinched from being seen, and decided, with the whole conviction of her people, that this simply would not stand: that what this one needed was to be dragged, daily and bodily, into the light, and fed.

So she dragged her. And the happiest hours of Anastasia Volkova's shadowed girlhood — the gold hours she would set, ever after, against the whole of her long traveled life and find the world hard pressed to better — were stolen in the Bregante kitchen, and on the Bregante boats, and in the singing crush of the Madonna's festa, where she was nobody's target and nobody's secret but only one more girl in the warm press of it, learning to gut a fish and swear in Ligurian and carry the women's part of a song and throw her head all the way back when she laughed, the way Marcella laughed, the way her own mother behind the curtain had never in her life been free enough to. The two of them down at the wharf in the dark, wrapped in one shawl, waiting for the fleet's lanterns to come in. The two of them with candles in the procession when the men shouldered the Madonna down to the water to bless the boats, the whole Italian colony singing at their backs and the sea itself seeming to lean in for the blessing. The two of them helpless with laughter in the corner of a wedding that ran three days and fed the entire flats. For a few bright years Marcella gave her the one thing all of Fyodor's love had taken and could never hand back: an ordinary, sunlit, deafening, garlic-smelling belonging — living proof, singing in the lane, that a poor immigrant family could answer the cold sorting country not with a shadow but with a feast, and win.

And here is the happiness that did not get taken, the happiness that outlasted all the grief the book of her life still had to spend: that bond did not break when everything else did. When Fyodor sent his daughter north, it was Marcella who wept hardest on the platform, and Marcella who wrote to her after, every single month, in a misspelled headlong joyful hand, for the rest of both their lives — through the correct cold marriage and the early widowing and the long bright decades when Anastasia at last walked out into the whole wide world she had been shut away from; so that wherever she went, Paris and Petersburg and the far hotels with the white railings over bluer water, a fat envelope was always catching up to her from a fishwife in Santa Cruz, stuffed with births and feast days and who had married whom and how the basil had come in that year — the one unbroken golden thread back to the first happiness of her life. And when Anastasia came home at the very last, decades on, the she-wolf in her furs, the first door she knocked at was not the Volkov house, which was gone, but the Bregante kitchen, which was not, older now and louder if anything, thick with Marcella's grandchildren, where an old fisherman's widow with flour to the elbow took the world-famous traveler's face in two floury hands and said, in the dialect, *sei tornata da noi — you came back to us,* and set another place, and poured the wine, as if not one hour had passed; because in the one country that ever wholly claimed Anastasia Volkova — the warm, loud, Ligurian country of the Bregante table — not one hour ever really had.

### Chapter — The Boardwalk

The town had a new face that summer, and it wore it down at the water.

Where for fifty years there had been only sand and the long wharf and the fishing smacks, the town had begun, plank by plank, to build itself a playground at the water's edge — the bath houses with their hot saltwater plunges, a pleasure pier of fresh-cut lumber and white paint reaching out over the surf, a bandstand at the foot of the planks: the whole bright new apparatus of a town that had decided to stop making its money from hides and lime and redwood and start making it from joy. They drove the pilings into the exact ground the Méndez cattle had once come down to drink; they built it, like everything in that town, on land that had been taken — and to build it they hired the sons of the people they had taken it from, and the Italians off the lime kilns, and the Portuguese off the whaleboats, and anyone at all with a strong back and no choice in the matter. Cirilo had driven pilings beside a cheerful, exhausted young Italian named Nunzio Sabatini, who sang while he worked, whose hands were already shiny-burned from a season at Cowell's kilns, and who told Cirilo that in this country a man like them could have anything in the world he wanted, anything, so long as he did not mind being hated for it. Nunzio meant it as a joke. It was the truest thing anyone said to Cirilo that whole year.

By midsummer the pier was open and Cirilo had been promoted, if that was the word, from the pilings up to the planks, and he worked the bright deck of the very thing his own hands had built, among people who would never wonder whose hands had built it. He was eighteen, and he had long since mastered the one essential skill of his position, which was not to look at the summer people. You learned it the way you learned not to touch the lime vats — once, and never again. The summer people were Anglo and moneyed and down from the City, and they moved through the world Cirilo swept as though it had been arranged for their convenience, which it had; and a brown boy who looked at them too long, or too directly, was asking for trouble that did not trouble itself to announce its arrival. So he kept his eyes on the work. He was looking, that afternoon, at a pyramid of iron milk-bottles and a worn baseball in his hand, behind a banner that read TEST YOUR ARM — THREE BALLS A NICKEL.

### Chapter — Three Balls a Nickel

It was Florence Pryor's idea — the cruelest small things so often were somebody's idea of fun — to stop at the booth and watch the Ashby boy win something and admire him for it, on a bright Tuesday with the band on the pier carrying its waltz out over the water and the whole lit length of the planks out promenading. Chuck Ashby was twenty-one that summer, three full years up on the bottle boy and home from his first year at Stanford with the loose certainty of it still on him like cologne; and he laid his nickel on the counter and took up the three worn cloth balls with the air of a man doing the booth a favor by gracing it.

The first ball took the top off the pyramid clean, and his friends made the noise you make, and he grinned. The second took two more and left them rocking, and Florence clapped her gloved hands, and down the rail Rebecca Whitfield watched without clapping. And then there were two bottles standing — the two low corners, the hard ones, the iron ones that sat their whole weight square on the shelf and did not care how good a man's year at Stanford had been — and Chuck Ashby set his jaw and threw his last ball as hard as a college arm could throw it, and caught the left one a glancing blow that spun it half around and sat it back down, upright, mocking, exactly where it had been. The two bottles stood. The little crowd went the particular silence a crowd goes when a man it had expected to win has, in front of everyone, not.

He could have laughed it off; a surer man would have. But Chuck Ashby had been raised in a cold grand house on a single commandment — that to be beaten, ever, at anything, even a nickel game, even for one instant, was to feel the warmth in that house switched off like a tap — and the not-winning rose in him as something nearer to panic than to temper, and it came out, the way it always came out in that set, as contempt. "It's rigged," he said, loud enough for the rail. "The whole booth's rigged — weighted bottoms, a dead ball — and the greaser's in on it." And he looked round for the laugh, and got it, and felt the warmth come back on.

Cirilo had heard the word more times than there were planks under his feet, and had long since learned to let it pass through him like weather. But the *rigged* he could not, for some reason, on that one afternoon, let lie — not for his own sake; for the booth's, for the honest stupid game's, for the one square foot of that whole stolen pier where the rule was the same rule for everyone. "It isn't rigged, sir," he said, low, his eyes level with the man's collar, which was the level the world allowed him. "The bottles are true. You only have to throw it low. And square." He said it without heat and without insolence, a plain correction, the one kind of honesty his position permitted him — and that, the maddening reasonableness of it, the dignity of a brown boy standing there simply and visibly *right,* was worse to Chuck Ashby than any insult could have been.

So when Cirilo turned to set the pyramid back up — turned his back, bent to the shelf, the way the work required of him a hundred times a day — Chuck Ashby picked the spun ball up off the planks and threw it, and hit him with it, square between the shoulder blades: not hard enough to call assault, and far too hard to call a joke. "You come up here and try it, then," he said, into the quiet. "Since you know so much. Come up here and try."

The planks went very still. Down the rail the flats had gathered without anyone deciding to — the Italians off the kilns, a knot of Mexican women, Nunzio Sabatini standing there on an afternoon's wage he could not spare — and they were still too, watching, knowing to the inch what it cost a brown boy to take a thing like that, and knowing to the inch what it would cost him far worse to answer it. Cirilo stood a moment with his back to all of them, the sting of the ball spreading between his shoulders, the chain he had worn his whole life gone suddenly very thin in his hands. Then he straightened, and he came around the end of the counter — which no bottle boy did, which was its own small trespass — and he set the whole pyramid up again himself, all six bottles square on the shelf, and stepped back to the line, and picked up a ball. He did not roll his shoulder or measure the distance or do any of the things a man does to buy himself a moment, because he did not need a moment. He threw it the way he had thrown the river stone at ten — without a windup, almost without seeming to try, a short whip of the wrist the eye could barely follow — and all six iron bottles went off the shelf together as if a horse had kicked them, and rang on the planks, and rolled, and lay still.

For a second nobody said anything at all. Then the flats came apart — Nunzio first, one joyful obscene bellow in Ligurian that made the women swat at him, and then the whole knot of them at once, laughing, calling out, a poor brown crowd granted for one afternoon the rarest thing the bright pier had to sell: the sight of one of their own being, plainly and publicly and beyond all argument, *better.* And into the middle of it Rebecca Whitfield stepped up to the counter — past Florence, past Chuck — and laid a nickel on the wood, and looked the bottle boy full in the face, which no young lady of the bluff was ever supposed to do, and said:

"Do it again."

"Costs a nickel," said Cirilo, and did not smile, and watched her hold his eyes a beat too long — longer than was safe, longer than was wise — and watched, just past her shoulder, Chuck Ashby watch her hold them: watched the young man discover, in that exact half-second, that the thing he had been raised to believe he owned had a mind of its own, and a preference, and that the preference was not him. She wore kid gloves, the dress kind, soft as a whispered thing, made of the one material his own father spent his life turning out for other people's hands; and she peeled the right one half off to set the coin in his palm, so that for an instant her bare fingers and the warm leather and the cold nickel were all in his hand at once. He gave her a ball. She would never throw it; they both knew the throwing was not the point. And Cirilo understood, with the flat cold clarity of a thing decided somewhere above his head and handed down to him already final, that the silence he had worn his whole life as the only armor the country had never learned to use against him had just, on a bright Tuesday, for the price of one nickel and one look, begun to cost him more than it had ever in eighteen years saved.

### Chapter — The Boardwalk People

The tourists saw the boardwalk. Cirilo, who worked it, saw the people who *were* the boardwalk — the great unseen immigrant crew who built the fun and ran the fun and cleaned up the fun and went home, after the bright crowds left, to rooms as poor as his own; and they were a country unto themselves, the boardwalk people, and they took the bottle boy in as one of their own, because that is what the used do for each other when the using stops at dusk.

There was old Brauer, the German who ran the flying horses — the old steam riding-gallery with its hand-carved animals, its brass ring, its wheezing band organ — and who tended those wooden horses with a watchmaker's tenderness and wept, privately, the day a splintered one was carted off, because he had come from a country that made beautiful things and had landed in one that mostly made money, and the horses were the only beauty his hands got to keep. There was the bathhouse man, and the woman who pulled the saltwater taffy in long shining ropes and had arms like a stevedore from a lifetime of pulling it; there was the barker at the diving-platform show and the girl who rode the diving horse off the high board into the tank twice a day for a wage that did not match the broken bones, smiling, always smiling, because the smile was the job; there were the freak-show performers, billed as monsters, gentle and bookish and bored between shows, who played cards with the bottle boy and treated him with the easy courtesy of people who knew exactly what it was to be stared at and sorted. And there was Pereira, the Portuguese night watchman, the widower who walked the dark planks till dawn because the rooms he went home to were louder in their silence than the whole Pacific — who never turned his lamp on the dark wedge under the pier where he knew two young people stood, because he had buried his own and had no wish left in him to ruin anyone.

They were the machinery of other people's golden summers, these people, and they knew it, and they were not bitter about it, mostly, which was the thing that moved Cirilo most and that he carried east: that you could spend your life being the unseen hands behind someone else's joy and still find, among the other unseen hands, a warmth and a courtesy and a card game and a kind of family — that the people the bright crowd looked straight through had quietly built, in the shadow of the very machine that used them, a small decent society of their own, with its own rules, the first of which was that you did not, ever, do to one of your own what the tourists did to all of you. The bottle boy threw a ball through six iron bottles for the rich kids' nickels by day. By night, in the off hours, Brauer let him ride the carousel alone, around and around on a hand-carved horse in the dark with the band organ off and only the sea for sound — a poor brown boy on a rich child's toy in the one hour it was his — and it was, he thought later, one of the kindest things anyone outside his blood ever did for him, and the man never mentioned it, because among the boardwalk people the kindness that mattered was the kind you did not bill for.

### Chapter — The House in the Tannery's Shadow

Fyodor Volkov's tannery sat where the creek met the flats, and you could find it in the dark with your nose: the green reek of the soaking pits, the lime that ate the hair off a hide and the skin off a careless hand, the oak-bark liquor steeping in its vats like the world's worst tea, and under all of it the sweet, rotten, animal smell of the thing itself — the smell of death being argued, slowly, into something a man could wear. Esteban Méndez had walked through its gate six mornings a week for the better part of thirty years, and he no longer smelled it.

So when the family gathered at night around the single lamp, the whole argument of Cirilo's life was already in the room before anyone spoke a word. His mother at the stove with her two graves and her undefeated God. His father gray with another man's hides and the friend he had watched die. Luis with his stone, Abel with his bottle, the old man in the good chair with his ninety years of evidence. And the boy, eighteen, with boardwalk sawdust on his collar and a girl's laugh still going off inside him like a struck bell, and hands — this was the thing, this was always the thing — hands that did not smell of the pit.

"Volkov will take you on in the fall," Esteban said one night, not looking up. He said it the way you offer a man a coat in a storm. "Steady work. A man can feed a family on it."

"I have work, Papá. At the beach."

"You have a season at the beach. Until the summer people go home and the planks belong to the gulls again." And then, lower, the old fear rising under the words like water under a floor: "I want you somewhere they can't put you out on a whim, mijo. Somewhere too small to aim at. That's all I want." And he meant Toussaint, though he would never say the name, and at the stove Dolores went still for a moment, because she could hear the dead boy in her husband's voice as plainly as if he had spoken it.

"You want him where you are," said Don Aurelio from his chair — not unkindly, which made it worse. "Safe. Small. Second."

The table went still. Esteban's jaw worked, and the thing he wanted to say — *I kept you all alive, old man, while you sat in that chair and dreamed about a rancho the lawyers ate* — he did not say, because he was a man who had buried his words a long time ago, beside everything else he had buried.

"Second is alive, viejo," was all he said. "I have buried men who reached." And only Dolores knew how literally he meant it.

The old man did not answer his son; he had learned, across a long life, that you do not win an argument with a frightened man by frightening him further. He looked at the boy instead. "Show me the hand," he said; and when Cirilo crossed and held out his right hand, the old man turned it in the lamplight — big, strong, unmarked by the lime, the only unbroken working hand in four generations of the family — and held it the way you hold a thing found again after you had given it up for lost.

"Your great-grandfather tanned hides with a hand like this and called himself a king," he said. "Your father trims them for a Russian's wage and calls himself safe — and he is not wrong to, God forgive me; I taught him to be safe, and the teaching kept him breathing, and I have hated myself for it every day for forty years." His thumb moved once over the boy's whole knuckles. "But you. You have the first hands in four generations that don't smell of the pit. Whatever else is true — and much of it is terrible, and I have seen most of it — that is also true. Don't you dare give them back."

And Esteban, who loved his son in the only language the world had ever permitted him to learn, looked at the floor and said nothing, because there is nothing to say to an old man who has decided, against ninety years of evidence and the cooling body of a boy named Toussaint, to hope. But under the table, where no one could see it, his cracked and stained and ruined hand had closed, slowly, into a fist — the old shape, the fast boy's shape, the shape he had forbidden himself for a lifetime — and then opened again, and lay flat against his knee, and was still.

### Chapter — Dolores

The men get the headstones and the box scores and the telegrams; the women get the kitchens — and the kitchens are where it is actually decided whether a family lives. Dolores Méndez kept the kitchen, and so Dolores Méndez kept the family, and almost no one ever noticed, which was exactly how she wanted it and exactly the injustice of it, both at once.

She had come from even less than the Méndez had fallen to: a laborer's daughter out of a Watsonville the history books do not trouble with, a girl who could not read and never learned, and who raised four readers anyway by the simple expedient of deciding that they would read and then making the world rearrange itself around the decision. She had married Esteban at sixteen, when he was already gray inside from the thing he had watched at the culvert, and she had spent forty years learning the exact dimensions of her husband's fear and building a life inside them — a life large enough to raise sons in, in the cramped room a frightened good man's caution allowed. She did it the way water does things: patiently, without announcement, finding the level, wearing the stone.

She had buried two children. A girl, Inés, of the diphtheria, at four; and a boy — little Joaquín, named for the old rancho king who had wept over the grant — of nothing anyone could name, at eleven months, in the same hard winter: two graves in the Catholic ground above the town that she climbed to every Sunday of her life, in every weather, and tended, and spoke to, and told no one what she said. The grief did not break her, because she did not permit grief the room to break things; she gave it its Sunday hour and its tended plot and not one inch more, and the other six days she cooked and washed and taught the language and held the line. People mistook this for hardness. It was the opposite of hardness. It was the most ferocious tenderness the whole story contains — a love so large and so disciplined that it had taught itself to keep functioning while carrying two small coffins, because the living children needed a mother whose hands did not shake; and so her hands did not shake, by an act of will repeated every single morning for forty years.

Her faith was the engine of it, and her faith was not the soft thing the word has since become. It was iron, and old, and entirely without illusion. She did not believe God would protect her children; she had two graves that disproved it, and she was no fool. She believed something harder and more useful: that you tend what is given for exactly as long as it is given, with your whole heart and no guarantees; that the tending is itself the prayer and itself the meaning; and that whether anyone is listening is not your business — your business is the pot, the language, the plot on the hill, and the rosary kept in the window where the street can see that this family has not disappeared and will not. That was the whole of her religion, and it would carry her, dry-eyed and unbroken, through everything the long years still meant to ask of her — and they would ask a great deal — because the one thing the world could never take from this woman, however much else it took, and it took much, was her refusal, on any morning of her life, to stop tending what was hers.

### Chapter — Mijo

There were, at the height of it, four boys who would answer to *mijo* in that house, and the confusion was a daily comedy the family never tired of and never resolved — because resolving it was never the point. The point was the running.

It went like this. Dolores, or the grandmother, or an aunt — it was always a woman; *mijo* was a woman's word, a word with a kitchen in it — would call from the house: *¡Mijo!* Just the one word, doing the whole work of a name. And from wherever they were — the woodpile, the creek, the roof they were forbidden to be on — would come the thunder of boys: Abel first, usually, being oldest and fastest; then Luis; then little Cirilo, the baby, tripping over his own feet to keep up; and as often as not a cousin or two from the Soto house next door, who answered to it on principle, on the sound theory that a call for *mijo* was a call that might, conceivably, end in food.

And then the argument, every time, the same beloved liturgy. *She called me.* — *She did not, she called ME.* — *You're not even her son, you're a Soto, go home.* — *Mijo means the littlest, and I'm the littlest, so mijo means ME* — this from Cirilo, who at five had already built an entire theology around being the baby of the house. And the women would stand in the doorway with their arms folded and let it run; let the boys shove and protest and stake their claims; because a mother of poor children in a hard country learns to bank the warm moments the way she banks anything scarce, against the lean times she knows are coming — and there is nothing warmer, nothing the lean times can never quite take back, than the sound of every boy you love each certain that he is the one you meant.

*Todos,* Dolores would say at last, when the comedy had run its course. *All of you are mijo. That is the trick of the word.* And she would cuff the nearest head with enormous tenderness and put the whole shouting herd of them to the one chore she had actually called one boy for — and they would do it together, grumbling, shoulder to shoulder, four boys and a word that belonged to all of them at once. Cirilo, who carried so few warm things east with him into the cold years that he could number them on his fingers, carried that one: the dirt yard, the thunder of feet, the indignant faces, his mother's folded arms and the laugh she was fighting to keep behind them, and the enormous fact, re-established daily and beyond appeal, that in that house there was always more than enough *mijo* to go around. He was El Halcón to the men — for the hawk, and the gift, and the danger that rode with both. But he was *mijo* to the women, along with his brothers, undifferentiated, interchangeable, beloved in a heap. And on the worst nights of his life, three thousand miles from that doorway, it was never the great name he reached for in the dark. It was the small one. It was the one that meant he had once belonged to a houseful of women who would have fought the whole world bare-handed for any boy who came running when they called.

### Chapter — The Poppies

For a few weeks each spring the dry hills above the town caught fire without burning — the California poppies opening all at once across the slopes, a gold so sudden and so total that the Spanish had called the flower *copa de oro,* the cup of gold, and had named the whole coast, some said, for the way the hills blazed with it in April; and the boy loved the poppies, and the poppies taught him, without his noticing, the shape of his own life.

For the gold did not last, and that was the point of it. The poppy is built for brevity: it throws everything it has into one brief radiant bloom and asks nothing of permanence, and it will not even give you the gold on demand — the flower closes its cup at night and stays shut all through the gray of fog and cloud, opening only to direct sun, so that on the overcast mornings that ruled that coast the hills held their fire furled and secret and gave it up only to the clear bright hours, which were few, and which the poppy did not waste. A few weeks. A few clear hours within those weeks.
