And then the gold went to seed and the hills browned for the long dry summer and you would not have known, looking at the tawny slopes in August, that they had blazed at all — except that the seed was in the ground, waiting, the way the chaparral's seed waited, for its hour to come round again.

He did not think about it as a boy beyond loving it; the meaning came later, the slow way the true meanings come — that the poppy does not mourn its brevity, because brevity was never the tragedy. The unopened cup is the tragedy: the gold furled and hoarded and never thrown to the sun, the bloom a frightened life is too careful ever to risk. To be made like the poppy is to be made to throw everything you have into one radiant opening and ask nothing of permanence — to blaze, full and total and unhidden, in whatever clear hour you are given, and then to trust the seed to the ground. And the boy who loved them, and could not have told you why, was taking in without knowing it the only ambition that would ever make sense of a life like his: not to last, but to bloom — to throw the gold open, once, where his own people could see it — and to leave the seed behind in the ground, waiting, the way the chaparral's seed waited, for its hour to come round again.

### Chapter — The Quail

There were California quail all through the brush above the houses, and the boy loved them, and only understood why when he was grown and far away and lonely in a manner the quail could have explained to him in a single morning if he had still been a boy to listen.

They moved as a covey, always, a soft gray crowd of them with the absurd little black topknots nodding as they ran, and they had a system, and the system was the whole of their genius and the boy's whole family in feathered miniature: while the covey fed in the open, scratching and murmuring, one bird — usually a cock — took the high post, a fence rail or a stump or a low bough, and stood sentinel, and watched the sky for exactly the thing the boy would be named for, the shadow of the hawk. And they talked. They talked the entire time, a constant low conversation, *chi-ca-go, chi-ca-go,* a call and an answer passing back and forth across the covey without pause — not because they had anything to say but because the calling *was* the saying; because as long as every bird could hear every other bird answer, no bird was lost, and the moment a voice failed to answer the whole covey knew, and froze, and the sentinel screamed his alarm and they burst up together in a roar of wings. They survived by never going quiet on each other. They survived by the answering.

That was his family — the constant low call-and-answer of a poor household keeping track of all its own, *¿dónde está? aquí estoy, here I am, I'm here,* the endless small unglamorous verbal touching by which the poor and the hunted keep from losing one another in the dark. He did not know, watching the quail at ten, that he was learning the thing that would very nearly unmake him, a lifetime on and a long way from any covey: that a bird alone, off on the deep-water road where no covey calls and no one answers and no sentinel watches your particular sky, is a bird in mortal danger, however strong its wings. In Brooklyn, in the cold, in the silence of a flat where no voice called *aquí estoy* back across the dark, he remembered the quail. He had been raised in the answering. The East had no answering in it. And a quail that cannot hear the covey will hold too still, too long, and forget that it was ever part of anything that watched the sky for it.

### Chapter — The Hill

The first thing his mother ever taught Cirilo about being a Méndez, she taught him on the hill, among the dead.

He was six, perhaps seven, the first time Dolores took him up — not into the living town but above it, to the Catholic ground where the graves looked down on the bay, where two small markers stood that he had not, until that morning, known were his to mourn. *This is your sister Inés,* his mother said, in the language she would speak only indoors, and here, in the open among the dead, where the street could not hear and it did not matter. *She was four. The diphtheria took her. And this is your brother Joaquín. He was a baby. He did not stay.* She did not weep — he would learn, growing up, that she had a place for the weeping and it was not here; here was for the tending — and she set the boy to pulling the weeds from around the markers with his small hands, and showed him how, and they worked together in the wind off the water; and she told him, in the old language, the thing that was the whole of her religion and the whole of the family's survival folded into one instruction a child could follow:

*We tend them, mijo, because they were ours, and because tending is how you say a thing was real. The world down there* — she meant the living town below, the Anglo town, the courthouse and the boardwalk and the men who had taken the rancho — *that world wants us to disappear. Not to die, exactly. To disappear. To stop having ever been here. And the only answer there is to that is this* — and she laid his small hand on his dead sister's name — *you tend. You keep the name. You say it out loud, and you teach your children to say it, and you do not let the wind or the weeds or the men down the hill take a single one of us off the face of the earth without a fight. That is what it is to be a Méndez. We are the ones who do not disappear.*

He did not fully understand it at six. He would spend the rest of his life understanding it, a little more each year — on grounds his mother could not yet imagine, in weathers she would never see — that everything he ever set against the long erasing patience of that town was only this: the boy on the hill, grown larger, pulling the weeds from around the family name so the world could not take it off the earth. His whole life would be his mother's lesson, played on bigger hills, before bigger crowds, in the same wind off the same water. He was the last of the family to understand it. She had been the first.

### Chapter — The River

In summer the San Lorenzo was the gentle green thing the brothers fished, low and clear over its stones; but the boy learned early not to trust its summer face, because every few winters the same river became a brown destroying god, and took back, in a night, whatever the flats had been foolish or poor enough to build in its way.

The rains would come down off the mountains for days, and the redwoods could only drink so much, and the creeks that fed the river all filled at once, and the San Lorenzo rose out of its bed and went looking for its old wider channels — the ones the town had paved and platted and pretended were streets — and it found them, every time, because a river remembers its floodplain long after the people who build on it have chosen to forget. It came through the flats brown and full of broken things, fence rails and chicken coops and somebody's table, climbing the doorsills in the dark while families carried what they could up to the higher ground and watched the water take the rest; and when it drew back down it left the mud and the stink and the ruin, and the flats people, who had no insurance and no higher ground to move to, shoveled out the mud and rebuilt in the exact same place, because the floodplain was the only ground the poor were allowed to have — the cheap wet dangerous bottomland nobody with a choice would take, which was therefore the land left for the people without a choice.

It taught the same lesson the tide pools taught and the steelhead taught and the whole coast kept teaching: that the forces which periodically wiped the poor were not malice, were not even attention, were simply the river being a river, the indifferent enormous engine going about its business of carrying the mountains to the sea; and that the people who lived in its path did so not from foolishness but from a poverty that had run out of other ground; and that they rebuilt, every time, in the mud, in the same doomed spot, because rebuilding was the only verb left to them — the same stubborn refusal the limpet made against the surf and Dolores made against the graves, the holding-on that is not stupidity but the only dignity available to those the world has assigned to the dangerous ground. The river gave them their water and their fish and their green summer hours, and every few winters it came and took its toll in mud and sometimes in lives, and went back down to the sea, and rose again as fog over the ridge, and fell again as rain, and came down again, world without end. And the flats shoveled out. And rebuilt. And stayed — because staying, on the ground you are given, however dangerous, however often it takes from you, is its own hard form of *we do not disappear.*

### Chapter — The Creek

The best hours of his boyhood, the ones he weighed everything against for the rest of his life and found everything wanting, were the hours the three brothers spent on the San Lorenzo, in the green shade where the creek ran under the alders, doing nothing the world would ever pay for or record.

They went up past the tannery and the smell of it, up where the water ran clean over the stones, and they fished — for the steelhead in the deep pools, and for the trout, with line their mother could ill spare and hooks they guarded like gold — but the fishing was only the excuse, the way the chore is only the excuse for the yard. The real thing was the three of them together in the cool: Abel, the oldest, the gifted one, who moved through the world with a grace that made you ache because you could already tell the world had no place to put it; and Luis, the middle one, who fell in at least once a trip and had to be hauled out spluttering and indignant; and Cirilo, the youngest, watching, always watching, the round gold eye of him already taking everything in. Abel could read the water the way Cirilo would learn to read a hitter — knew which pocket behind which rock a fish would hold in, could lay a line on the current soft as a landing fly — and when he hooked a big sea-run steelhead, fresh up from the salt and still bright with it, the three of them would lose their minds with joy, all the careful quiet gone, three boys hollering in the redwood shade with a silver fish flashing on the line.

And then Abel, who had a tenderness in him the world would later mistake for weakness and break him for, would more often than not work the hook free with his careful hands and hold the great fish a moment in the current, facing it upstream, letting the water run through its gills until the strength came back into it — and let it go. *Let it make the climb, hermanito,* he would say. *It didn't come all this way up from the sea to die in our pan.* And the fish would hang there a heartbeat, gathering itself, and then bolt on up the creek toward the high gravel; and Cirilo, ten years old in the green light, watched his brother give the climbing thing back its climb, and did not yet know that he was watching the truest and most generous act of a life that the country would not let amount to anything — Abel, who let the steelhead go because he understood the drive to get home up the current better than any of them, being himself a steelhead the falls would beat. Those hours cost nothing and earned nothing and were, Cirilo decided a lifetime later and a long way from that green shade, the richest he was ever given. Just the creek, and the shade, and his brothers, and a bright fish let go to make its climb. There is no box score for it. There never will be. It was the wealth the country could neither give them nor take away, and they had it in abundance, up the San Lorenzo.

### Chapter — The First Time He Threw

He was ten the first time anyone understood what was in his arm, and it frightened them, which was the correct response, and which he did not understand until much later.

It was a rock, not a ball — the family could not always spare a ball — a smooth river stone; and there was a tin can on a fence post a long way off, the kind of distance grown men missed, and Cirilo, ten years old and bored, picked up the stone and threw it without thinking and knocked the can spinning off the post on the first try, and then did it again, and again, until his brothers stopped their own game to watch. And Abel — Abel, the gifted one, the one who was *given,* who at twelve could already do things with a ball that drew crowds — Abel watched his little brother throw, and did not say *good arm,* and did not laugh, and did not challenge him to do it again. Abel went quiet: the particular quiet of a boy recognizing, in someone he loves, the thing that is going to hurt them — because Abel already knew, at twelve, having learned it the hard way, what a gift like that drew down on a brown boy in that county.

But it was the old man's face Cirilo remembered. Don Aurelio had been sitting in the shade, ancient even then, and had watched the whole thing — the stone, the can, the impossible accuracy of it, the casual murderous ease — and the boy looked over at his abuelo expecting the thing a boy expects, the pride, the smile, the *that's my grandson;* and instead found the old man regarding him with an expression he had no name for at ten and understood completely, a lifetime later and three thousand miles from that dirt yard, in the clear hard light the old man had warned of: fear and love and grief, all at once — the look of a man who has just watched his grandson hand him a thing that is both the family's only hope and its oldest danger, a man doing sums in his head that come out, every way he adds them, to a cost. *Put it down, mijo,* the old man said, very quietly, not unkindly. And then, contradicting himself, contradicting ninety years of his own hard-learned caution, in the same breath, because he could not help it, because hope is the thing in us that does not listen to evidence: *No. No — pick it up. Pick it up and throw it again. Let me see it once more, before I tell you to be careful with it the rest of your life.* And the boy threw the stone again and knocked the can spinning, and the old man closed his eyes — the first time, the first of many, the same closed eyes he would shut again, at the very last, rather than watch where the boy's gift would one day carry him — and Cirilo, ten years old, did not understand why his abuelo, having just seen him do a wonderful thing, looked for all the world like a man at a graveside. He understood it later. He understood it, in the end, completely: the gift and the danger were the same gift; they always had been; the old man had seen it in one throw of a river stone and had been right — and had told him to pick it up again anyway, because that too, the telling-him-to-pick-it-up-anyway knowing the cost, was love. Was, in fact, the whole of what the family had instead of safety. Was the only inheritance they had left to give.

### Chapter — The First Glove

The arm, once it was seen, had to be answered; and a family that had not been allowed to keep a single thing of its own in forty years answered it the only way that family had ever made anything that was truly theirs — out of leather, by hand, together.

For there was a cousin of Esteban's — Refugio, whom the children called Tío Refugio though he was nobody's uncle exactly, a cousin two turns back up the line toward the rancho years — who had come down out of the long fall by a gentler stair than the tannery: a *talabartero,* a saddle-and-harness man, with an awl and a half-window of a shop off the plaza, who mended the tack and traces and cinches of a county that still moved on horses. He was the last of the Méndez men whose hands were permitted to *finish* a thing — to take the leather in raw and hand it back whole and useful and, in its plain way, even beautiful — where Esteban only stripped and limed and scudded the hides and passed them on down the machine to be finished by strangers for the profit of a stranger; and the distance between the cousin who shaped the leather and the cousin who merely suffered it was the exact measure of everything the family had been brought down from, and they all felt it, and were too kind to say it where Esteban could hear. But they were proud of Refugio, quietly — the way you are proud of the one window a hard wind has left unbroken in a house it has otherwise had all its way with.

So when it reached him, by the headlong telegraph of aunts, that Esteban's littlest had knocked a tin can spinning off a fence post with a river stone at a distance grown men muffed, Tío Refugio put down the bridle he was stitching and said only, *Bring him to me.* He made no fuss of it. He sat the boy on a nail keg and took his measure the way he'd take a horse's, by eye and by hand, turning the small throwing hand over in his two scarred ones and pressing the palm and the finger-joints with a craftsman's frank attention; and then he went to the corner where he kept the offcuts — the bellies and the necks too small or too scarred for harness, the pieces a saddler saves and a poor saddler saves twice — and he chose. There were no store-bought gloves in that house, and would not have been the money for one if there had been; and the gloves of that year were poor crude articles in any case, scarcely more than a padded workman's mitten with a little rawhide lacing at the seams and no proper web to speak of, a thing a man wore to keep the sting out of his palm and not much more. So Refugio simply made one — the way his own grandfather had made whatever the family needed out of whatever the family had — cutting the pattern from memory and the breadth of his own hand, skiving the edges thin, working a pocket into the palm with a smooth stone and a great deal of spit and patience, lacing the fingers with thongs he cut himself.

But the part the boy carried, longer than he carried the glove, was that it was not made by one man. It was made by all of them. Dolores and the grandmother stitched where stitching was wanted, by lamplight, of an evening, the needle driven through the heavy leather with a thimble and a wince; and Don Aurelio, who could no longer hold an awl with the ruined courthouse hands but could still read a pocket truer than any living man, sat on his bucket and judged the depth of it and sent it back twice before he would pass it — *deeper, here, where the ball wants to die* — pouring into the shaping of a child's mitt the whole of a knowledge the high clean game had never let him spend on anything that mattered. So that the thing they put into the boy's hands one evening at the end of it was not, finally, a glove. It was the family's last surviving craft and the family's last surviving hope folded together into a single object small enough for a child to close his fist around: leather — the one material the Méndez had ever truly owned, that they had run cattle for on their own green hills and tanned now in another man's vats for another man's ledger — handed back to them for the space of a few evenings as a thing *they* made, with their own hands, out of love, for one of their own.

He slept with it the first night. He oiled it the way Refugio showed him, with his own hands, every Sunday of his boyhood. He outgrew it, in time, the way a boy outgrows everything but the few things that matter — and he never, as long as he lived, let it go; and where that small first glove went in the end, and what it came at last to say, and into whose keeping he would one day quietly leave it as the truest word he owned, is a thing the story has not yet earned the telling of. For now it is only a boy asleep with a glove his family made him, in a poor house in the tannery's shadow; and the leather smells of his uncle's bench and his grandmother's lamp and the lost green hills; it smells of *familia* — the one inheritance the country had left them, and the one thing it had never, with all its long taking, found a way to take.

### Chapter — Aunt Adeline

Rebecca's mother was dead — the fever, when Rebecca was eight, the careless fever that was not supposed to touch people who could afford doctors and took her anyway — and into the hole that left came Aunt Adeline, Horace's unmarried sister, to raise the girl and run the white house on the bluff; and Aunt Adeline loved Rebecca, in her starched and anxious way, and spent fifteen years doing her quiet best to make Rebecca's life turn out exactly as wrong as her own had.

But the first eight years had been warm, and the warmth had been real and not the hollow article the bluff so often passed off as the thing itself. Her mother — a soft, quick, unguarded woman the correct world had never entirely finished correcting — had delighted in her daughter plainly and out loud, had looked *into* the child and not merely at her, and had filled the white house with a laughter that Horace tolerated the way a careful man tolerates the one weather he secretly loves. For eight years Rebecca was seen, completely, by someone whose whole face changed to see her come into a room. Then the fever, which kept no ledger of who could afford a doctor, took the mother inside a week — and took with her the only person on the bluff who had ever known the difference between admiring a girl and knowing one. Rebecca would spend the next twenty years being admired. She remembered, the whole time, without quite being able to name it, that there had once been the other thing.

For Adeline had been a girl once, with her own summer and her own forbidden wanting — there had been a music master, decades gone, a poor and unsuitable man with beautiful hands, and a season when Adeline had felt the whole apparatus of her life crack open toward something real — and her family had managed her out of it, gently, reasonably, with a firmness she had been too well-raised to fight, and married the music master off to distance and married Adeline to nothing at all; and she had spent the long correct remainder of her life as the useful unmarried sister, the aunt, the manager of other people's households, with a small permanent grief where her own life ought to have been. And she had drawn from that grief the conclusion the wounded so often draw: not that the managing had been the crime, but that the *wanting* had been the danger — that safety was the only love that lasted, and that the great kindness you could do a girl was to school the wanting out of her before it could ruin her the way it had so nearly ruined Adeline. She set about saving Rebecca by making her safe, which she had decided meant making her correct.

So they went to war, daily, lovingly, over everything — and most visibly over the clothes. The bathing costume was too daring (an inch of ankle, a bared shoulder, *you are not parading down to that beach got up like a chorus girl, Rebecca, what would your poor mother*); the necklines wanted raising, the hems wanted lowering, the whole young body of the girl wanted covering and managing and keeping, because a body seen was a body wanting was a body in danger, and Adeline knew the road that started at a bared shoulder and where it ended, having walked the first steps of it herself a lifetime ago. And it was not only the clothes. It was the company — which families Rebecca might call on and which she mightn't, which girls were suitable and which were fast, which young men of which fortunes were worth a veranda afternoon (the Ashby boy, above all, the Ashby boy was worth any number of afternoons). It was the hours, the accomplishments, the piano practiced and the calls paid, the whole marriage-market schedule run with the rigor of a railway timetable, all of it pointed at the single station the line had always been pointed at: a correct and comfortable and loveless match, the only kind Adeline had been taught to believe a woman survived.

She was not a villain. That is the thing to hold about her. She was a frightened, wounded, genuinely loving woman trying to spare her niece her own exact fate by the only method she had ever been given, which was the very method that had ruined her — passing the wound forward, dressed as protection, the way the wounded so often do; and once, only once, brushing out Rebecca's hair on a summer night with the window open and the boardwalk music coming faint up the bluff, she came within a breath of telling the girl the truth, of saying *there was a man, once, with beautiful hands, and I let them talk me out of him, and I have been cold ever since, and I am so afraid you will be warm and I am so much more afraid you will be cold like me* — and did not; and the not-saying-it was its own small tragedy, one of the thousand uncarved tragedies stacked in that white house on the bluff, where everyone loved Rebecca and no one, not one of them, had the first idea how to let her be free.

### Chapter — The Girls

The boardwalk was where the girls got loose, for a few stolen golden hours, from the whole heavy apparatus of being turned into wives; and Rebecca's set went down to it every summer afternoon they could engineer, and the social warfare they waged on those sunny planks was every bit as intricate and as merciless as anything their fathers conducted in the banks. For these were the daughters of the bluff — the summer money up from the City, the bankers and railroad men and land lawyers whose fortunes sat, a generation deep now, on exactly the kind of taking that had emptied the Méndez rancho a county away; not one of the girls had ever been asked to notice that, and the war they waged in muslin on the planks was the same machine their fathers ran in oak-paneled rooms, only smaller, and sweeter, and louder.

It had a queen, the way these things always have a queen, and the queen was Florence Pryor — beautiful, quick, and armored in a confidence Rebecca half-envied and half-saw straight through — who kept the hierarchy and decided, by a tilt of her parasol, who was in the set that season and who had become, mysteriously, impossible. There was Cora Beck, soft and loyal and a little plain, who was Rebecca's actual friend, the one she told the true things to; and there were the others, the satellites, arranging and rearranging themselves around Florence in alliances and freeze-outs and coded little cruelties that were, when you stood back from them, the identical caste system the grown people ran, rehearsed in miniature by sixteen-year-olds in summer muslin — who walked with whom along the rail, who was asked to whose veranda, whose bathing costume was admired and whose was *talked about,* the whole pitiless sweet machinery of belonging. Florence had set her cap at the Ashby boy and his moneyed set, and measured every other girl against herself in beaux and frocks and accomplishments, and was, under the dazzle, as frightened as any of them of ending up on the wrong veranda; they all were; the fear was the engine the whole thing ran on, even as they shrieked down to the water with their arms linked, luminous and laughing, sixteen and free. And under all of it ran the autumn coming, and Stanford — for the bluff sent its daughters there now, an hour up the line, to be finished within reach of the right young men; Rebecca and Florence and half the set were bound for it the moment the warm planks closed, Stanford being where a girl like Rebecca was meant to go, and meant, in the going, to be settled for good.

And the freedom was real. Down past the bathhouses they went, into the daring costumes Aunt Adeline's horror had been left behind up the bluff to fret over, into the cold bright surprising sea — and there was the warmth after, the towels, the salt drying on the skin, the inside jokes, the sidelong watching of the boys who watched them back, the first electric ungovernable stirrings of being young and lovely on a summer coast with the whole apparatus out of sight. It was the best of their lives and some of them sensed it and most did not. Rebecca sensed it, and could not enjoy it, and that was her particular trouble: she was the most looked-at girl in the set and the least content, played the whole game beautifully and felt its hollowness in her teeth, went through the bright motions with a private starvation she had no name for — the motherless girl, looked at by everyone and into by no one, performing belonging on the warm planks while something in her quietly went hungry.

Which is why it landed the way it did — the thing none of the other girls carried out of that afternoon at all. They had stopped at the baseball booth that day, Florence's idea, to watch the Ashby boy win something and admire him for it; and instead they had watched him fail, and lose his temper, and call the game rigged, and throw a ball at a brown boy's back — and watched the brown boy come round the counter and clear the whole shelf with a single throw and answer Chuck's entire performance with nothing but the plain fact of being better; and then they had drifted on along the rail, bored already, because a rich boy losing a nickel game to a greaser was a thing the set put down quickly and did not think of again. But Rebecca did not put it down. She had seen, in the middle of it, the thing none of the others troubled to see: the bottle boy take Chuck's cruelty and his thrown ball without flinching, his face still and his eyes level, with a dignity that had clearly been practiced because it had clearly been needed — and then glance, just once, at her; and it was not the way the Ashby boy looked at her, which was the way you look at a prize you intend to win, and it was not the way the men on the verandas looked at her, which was the way you appraise a property; it was the way you look at a *person,* level and curious and entirely unimpressed by her dress or her name, as though he had seen the hungry thing inside the lovely thing and was simply, quietly, interested in it. It lasted a second. Florence was already pulling them away. And Rebecca walked on up the planks with her friends, laughing at the right places, and could not, for the rest of that strange unsettled afternoon, get the one look out from under her ribs — the first crack, though she did not know it yet, in an apparatus that had been built, with such love and such care and by so many frightened hands, to make absolutely certain she never felt exactly this.

### Chapter — Under the Canvas

It happened on the third afternoon, and it happened because of the weather — which is how the only unguarded minutes in a watched life usually arrive, handed to you by something that has no opinion about who is permitted to speak to whom.

A squall came up off the bay the way they do on a Santa Cruz afternoon, fast and gray and gone in twenty minutes, and it swept the pier of its promenaders in one shrieking, laughing scatter for the bathhouses and the awnings; and Rebecca, who had drifted near the booth again on the day's thin pretext, found the pretext and the crowd and her friends all snatched away at once by the rain, and stepped in under the booth's striped canvas because it was the nearest dry thing — and because she had wanted, for three afternoons, a reason. And then there was no crowd, and no Florence, and no thousand eyes: only the rain drumming on the canvas and the two of them under it, nearer than the daylight had ever once allowed, and for one improbable minute the bright pier had forgotten to watch them.

She did not know how to speak to a boy she was forbidden to speak to — she had spent her whole life being schooled in the elaborate grammar of the ones she was allowed — so she said the truest thing in her, which came out plainer than anything she had said all summer. "You could have thrown it at his head," she said. "The ball. Everyone saw he started it. And you didn't." He was quiet a moment, watching the rain and not her. "If I'd hit him," he said, "I'd be the one they remembered. Not him." And she understood it at once and completely — the whole brutal arithmetic of a life she had never had to total in her own — and was ashamed of how new it was to her, and did not look away. "What's your name?" she asked, and was startled by the asking, because she had realized in the same instant that she did not know it: that she had been calling him, in her own head, the thing the whole pier called him — the booth, the bottle boy, the job and not the person. He looked at her then: the level look, the one from the booth, the one that had got in under her ribs and stayed. "Cirilo," he said. And because no one of her kind had ever asked, he gave her the rest of it, slower, like handing over something breakable: "Cirilo Méndez." She said it back, and got it right the first time, which surprised them both — *Cirilo* — the Spanish of it sitting strange and soft and new in a bluff girl's mouth; and something moved in his face when she did, some small door whose whole size she would spend the next twenty years learning, because she had just done, without the first idea that she was doing it, the one thing his entire world had been built to deny him: she had said his name, his own true name, out loud, as if it were a name and not a slur and not a job.

The rain stopped as suddenly as it had started. Out on the planks the promenade was already re-forming, parasols shaking open, and somewhere in it Florence's voice was going up, calling her name — her own easy, correct, askable name — and Rebecca stepped back out from under the canvas into the daylight and the watching and the life that had every intention of keeping her, and did not say goodbye, because there was no safe word for it. But she had his name now. She carried it up the planks under her tongue like a coin she had stolen; and that night, alone, the window open and the surf coming up the bluff, she said it once into the dark — *Cirilo* — to find out what it felt like in her own mouth, the thing she was not allowed to want.

### Chapter — The Note

What the booth had started, and the stolen minute under the canvas had confirmed, somebody had to be reckless enough to finish — and it was never going to be the bottle boy, who had spent eighteen years learning that the surest way for a brown man to die young was to want something that lived up the hill. So it was Rebecca.

She had come back the next afternoon, and the one after, on thin engineered pretexts — a friend to be steered toward the rail near the booth, a parasol to be adjusted there a while — wordless and careful, letting him see only that she came, because she was not a fool and the bright pier had a thousand eyes. And then the squall had given them the one stolen minute under the canvas, and she had learned his name and said it, and the minute had quietly settled the question she had spent three afternoons pretending was still open. After that the wordless returns were not enough, for either of them, and she knew it — and knew, too, that the bottle boy could not be the one to risk the next step and live; that on the boardwalk the only move a brown man was allowed was to wait, and that whatever happened now would have to be hers to begin. So on the fourth afternoon, paying her nickel, she left in his palm along with the coin a folded scrap of paper no larger than a stamp, and was gone up the planks with her friends before his hand had closed on it.

He knew how this was done. That was the thing he turned over afterward, ashamed and not ashamed — that he knew the whole grammar of it secondhand, from Luis. His brother Luis was the charmer of the three, the one with the grin and the give-nothing-a-second-thought recklessness, who went over the bluff fences in the summer dark after the rich men's daughters the way other boys went after fruit: for the sport and the sweetness and the story, and who had so far, by a charm that was its own kind of luck, always come back over the fence laughing with his collar undone and his neck unbroken. Cirilo had watched him do it for years with a younger brother's tangle of awe and contempt, and had filed it away the way he filed everything, never imagining he would use it. Because Luis did it for nothing — an hour, a dare, a girl whose name he would half-forget by autumn. What Cirilo held in his closed hand on the planks was not nothing. It was the most dangerous thing a man his color could hold in that county, and he knew to the inch what it would cost if the wrong hand ever closed on it instead of his.

He opened it anyway. Four words, in a hand schooled by tutors and gone suddenly unsteady: *When the lights go out.* No place named — the place did not need naming; there was only one shadow in that whole lit town big enough to hide what they were about to do, and they both already knew where it was, the way you know a thing your whole body has decided before your mind is asked. He closed his hand around the note. He did not drop it in the bay, though his father's whole careful life was in his ear telling him to. The careful brother had just decided to do, for keeps and for love, the thing the reckless one did for sport — and to walk, with his eyes wide open, straight at the one line in that county that did not forgive.

### Chapter — Cora

Rebecca had one friend she told the true things to, and her name was Cora Beck — soft, loyal, a little plain, entirely without the queen's claws — and within a week of the booth Rebecca did the thing the lonely do when a feeling grows too large to carry alone: she gave a piece of it away, to the one person she trusted, in a corner she believed was private. On the bluff, no corner was private. That was the lesson she had not yet learned, and was about to.

They were on the Becks' veranda with the lemonade going warm, and Rebecca, who had held it through a squall and a sleepless night and the delivering of a note she could still not believe her own hand had folded, heard herself say it at last: that there was a boy at the booth, the one who had cleared the bottles, and that he had a name — *Cirilo* — and that he had looked at her, once, under the rain, the way no one on the whole bluff had ever looked at her, into her and not at her, and that she could not put it down. She said it low, to Cora alone, in the safest place she owned. And Cora — loyal Cora, who would not have carried it to a living soul, and never did — went pale, and took her friend's two hands, and said the frightened, loving, true thing that only a real friend says: *Rebecca. He is a Mexican, at a booth. Do you know what they would do — not to you; to him? You cannot. However it feels. You will get him killed and yourself ruined, and they will call it a mercy when they stop you.* And Rebecca heard it, and knew it for love and knew it for truth, and felt the wanting close over the truth anyway, the way water closes over a dropped stone.

But a veranda is not a vault, and the bluff was all one listening ear; and at the shaded end of the porch, behind the trellis and the bougainvillea, sat Florence Pryor, who had come round uninvited and stayed unannounced, and who heard all of it — the booth, the rain, the name — through the lattice, and went very still, and understood at once that she had been handed something of great value. For Florence had set her cap at the Ashby boy and his money since she was fourteen, and here at last was the rival who had only ever had to exist in order to outshine her, caught wanting a greaser at a nickel booth — caught *down,* for once, where Florence could reach her.

She did not gossip it idly down the rail. She was better than that, which is to say worse. She carried it in person to Chuck Ashby, on a shaded bench above the bathhouses, and gave it to him gently, as a kindness, as a thing a friend simply felt he ought to know — and watched his face while she did it, and got for her trouble exactly what she had come for: the warmth going out of him toward Rebecca, and something colder coming up in its place. Chuck said almost nothing. He thanked her, which Florence took for gratitude and which was not. He had half-known since the booth that the prize he was raised to believe he owned had a preference, and that the preference was not him; what Florence had handed him was the rest of it — a name, a place, a brown boy he could find at a booth any afternoon of the week. He looked out at the water a while. Then he rose, and went down the planks toward the booth, in no hurry at all, a young man in good flannels strolling out to have a look at a thing that belonged to him and had somehow got loose — and behind him Florence watched him go, and felt, under the clean bright pleasure of her triumph, the first cold finger of having set in motion a thing she did not know how to call back.

### Chapter — The Dark Under the Pier

They could only have each other in the dark, and so the dark became theirs.

When the boardwalk lights went out and the surf came up to fill the silence, they met under the pilings, in the black wedge of shadow beneath the pier — the one place in that whole bright town where a brown boy and a white girl could stand together without it costing blood. Her father believed she was asleep in the rented cottage up on the bluff. His father believed he was a fool, and was right, and was also afraid, which Cirilo did not yet understand were the same thing in his father. They were deceiving everyone who loved them, those nights, and neither of them could make themselves care — and that frightened Rebecca more than the dark did, because she had been raised to believe she was a careful person, and here was proof, nightly, that she was not.

She had come to him, in the beginning, out of something she was honest enough to mistrust in herself. She was motherless — her mother had died when Rebecca was eight, of the same careless fever that took the poor, except that it was not supposed to take people who could afford doctors, and had taken her anyway — and her father had answered the loss by converting his daughter, across a decade, into an ornament: a beautiful, accomplished, marriageable thing to be set out at the right tables and married, in the fullness of time, to the right name. She had spent eleven years being looked *at* — at her dress, her posture, her prospects — and never once, not by her father nor by Chuck Ashby nor by any of the right names at any of the right tables, looked *into.* And then a bottle boy on a pier had knocked six iron bottles off a shelf and turned and *seen* her — not the dress, not the prospects, but the person rattling around loose inside all of it — and the thing it broke open in her was so large and so sudden that she mistook it, at first, for simple rebellion, for the satisfying scandal of wanting the one boy on the beach she had been forbidden. It was only later, much later, in cold rooms three thousand miles to the east, that she would be certain it had been love. On the nights under the pier she was not yet certain, and she knew she was not certain, and she came anyway — which is either the bravest thing a careful person can do or the most reckless, and is generally both at once.

"Tell me something true," she said once. "You never do."

He let the sand run through his fingers a while before he answered. "My family owned this," he said at last. "Not the pier — the land. All of it, the river to the redwoods, before there was a Santa Cruz to own it. Now I sell nickels on a plank built on top of it." He looked at her in the dark. "And I would rather be here, like this, with you, than have had a single acre of it back."

She took his hand — the pitching hand, the unmarked one — and held it like a thing she had decided to keep, and told him they would simply have to take the rest of it back themselves; and just then a whistle came down off the boardwalk and a watchman's lamp swung out over the rail. They froze together in the black while the beam swept the sand above their heads. It was only old Pereira, the night man — a Portuguese widower who had buried a wife and three of his four children, and who walked the empty pier until dawn because the rooms he went home to were louder, in their silence, than the whole Pacific. He did not want to find anyone. He swept the lamp along out of habit and not out of zeal, and the beam passed over the dark wedge where they stood pressed together and breathing, and moved on, and Pereira shuffled away up the planks humming something low in a language neither of them knew — a man wholly uninterested in ruining two strangers, because the world had done enough ruining of its own to last him a lifetime.

They breathed again. But the fear was its own answer, the thing neither of them ever said aloud: that all of it — the dark, the nights, the held hand — was borrowed, and the lenders were cruel and patient and kept good books, and that somewhere a reckoning was being entered, in a ledger they would not be allowed to read until the day it was presented, in full, for payment.

### Chapter — The Carousel

There was one night that summer that Cirilo set against the whole rest of his life, and against which the whole rest of his life — everything the long years would bring him, every bright thing still ahead of him — came up, ever after, a little short: the night Rebecca rode the carousel with him in the dark.

Brauer knew. That was the thing about the boardwalk people: they saw everything and billed for none of it. The old German had caught the bottle boy and the bluff girl in the shadow under the pier more than once on his rounds and had said nothing, the way Pereira said nothing, because the boardwalk people had a country of their own and the first law of it was that you did not hand your own kind to the daylight. And on a night near the end of that first summer, with the crowds gone and the lights mostly down and the planks belonging to the gulls, Brauer threw the lever that woke the flying horses — the hand-carved animals, the painted manes, the brass ring on its arm — and started the band organ low, and then walked away to the far rail to watch the black water and grant them the one impossible thing the daylight would never give: an hour to be young together, in the open, like anyone.

They rode it alone, the two of them, in the dark, the great wheel of horses turning slow and the organ playing some sweet tinny waltz under the stars and the surf, Rebecca on a white horse with her hand out to the brass ring she kept just missing, laughing the real laugh, the sideways surprised one, not the bright tin purse-laugh of the bluff; and Cirilo on the black horse beside her, reaching out across the gap between the rising-falling horses to hold her hand whenever the painted animals brought them level, losing it, finding it again on the next turn — the whole courtship of them in one image, the reaching across a gap, the brief level holding, the loss, the patient finding-again on the next go-round. For one hour the line did not exist. For one hour there was no bluff and no flats, no Anglo and no Mexican, no Aunt Adeline and no Chuck Ashby and no county with a rope in it — only a boy and a girl and a turning wheel of wooden horses and a German at the far rail keeping their secret and the indifferent merciful sea. *This is what it would be,* Rebecca said once, quietly, on a turn, not looking at him. *If the world were different. This. Just this, going around.* And Cirilo, holding her hand for the length of one level pass before the horses rose and parted them again, said the truest and most dangerous thing he had ever said to anyone: *Then we'll have the hour. They can have the world. We'll take the hour.* And they rode the wheel until Brauer, gently, without a word, threw the lever and let it coast to still — and the hour ended, the way the hour always ends, and the world came back; but he had it, ever after, the black water and the band organ and her hand found and lost and found on the turning, the one hour the line let them steal, the hour he would reach for, in his heart, in all the hard far places that came after: *we'll take the hour.*

### Chapter — The Oldest Brother

The drinking did not come from nowhere, and the family knew exactly where it came from, because they had watched it before, in the old man, a generation up.

Don Aurelio had drunk, in the bad years — the years after the courthouse, after the rancho went, when there was nothing for a deposed Californio with ruined hands and useless dignity to do with the grief but pour it down a hole every night and hope to wake somewhere else. He had come out the far side of it eventually, dried himself around the time the grandchildren came and turned the wreck of himself into the stern watching man on the bucket; but the family remembered, the way families remember, in a lowered voice. And when the same shadow came down over Abel at seventeen, they knew it on sight — the inherited shape of a Méndez man drinking what the country had done to him.

For Abel had the gift, and the gift drew the eyes, and the eyes, in that county, drew the trouble. There was a Sunday he had hit three balls so far and so contemptuously over a club of Soquel and bluff boys that the thing curdled, the way it curdled, from a game into an affront; and after, in the dust by the wagons, a bluff boy named Roy Calder and three others backed him to a fence and explained to him in the reasonable voice, with the casual hands, what happened to a Mexican who made white men look like fools in front of their girls — and Abel, who had his grandfather's pride and not one ounce of his patience, broke Roy Calder's nose with one beautiful instinctive right hand before he could think better of it. It took Luis and two flats men to drag him off. It cost him three weeks in the county jail and a beating from the deputies on the way in, and it taught him at seventeen the lesson the culvert had taught his father: that the arm which made him magnificent was the exact thing that would get him killed, and that there was no safe way in that place to be as good as he was.

So he chose the unsafe way, and then he chose the bottle, and the two became a single habit. He could not stop being the best — it came out of the body whether he willed it or not — and he could not be the best without drawing the trouble, and he could not meet the trouble with his fists without the jail, and he could not bear any of it sober. But there was one use for the fighting the family could never quite condemn, because it was the last noble thing in the wreck of him: he spent it on his brothers. When the bluff boys came for Luis over some banker's daughter, it was Abel who came out of the dark and took the beating onto himself; when a boardwalk clerk shoved little Cirilo off the planks for blocking a doorway, it was Abel who put the clerk through a fruit stand and did another two weeks for it. He could not defend himself in any way that did not ruin him — but he could stand in front of his brothers, and he did, every time, drunk or sober, fists first: the gift turned from a glory into a weapon and from a weapon into a shield. And the jail record grew, and the drinking deepened, and Dolores lay awake, and the family said in the lowered voice, *like the old man, God help him — just like the old man,* and feared, correctly, for where it would end.

### Chapter — The Set

The rich boys had a cage too. It was a better-appointed cage than the one down in the flats — it had lawn tennis in it, and a yacht at the harbor, and a clear paved road to Stanford and the bank — but it was a cage, and the boys in it were as frightened as anyone down in the flats; and the cruelty on the boardwalk only makes its full sense from up on the bluff, seen from inside.

They were the sons of the men who had won: the bankers, the lumber barons, the land men whose fathers or grandfathers had been on the right side of the courthouse when the Méndez and the others were on the wrong. Chuck Ashby was their center of gravity — not the richest, but the surest, the one who never seemed to doubt, which in a boy that age is the rarest currency there is — and around him orbited the others: Roy Calder, who carried his broken nose like a grudge and a banner; Theo Pope, the lumber heir, soft and mean in the way of boys who suspect they are soft; and the rest of them, a loose bright cruel confederacy of seventeen-year-olds who owned the summer the way their fathers owned the town. And here is the thing the flats boys could not see from below: those boys were not free either. They were performing, every waking minute, an enormous and exhausting role — the role of effortless superiority — for an audience of their fathers, and their fathers' approval was a coin doled out only for winning, only for dominance, only for never showing the doubt or the softness or the fear that all of them carried and none of them could admit; and the performance had a price, and the price was that they could not afford to be kind, because kindness, in their cage, read as weakness, and weakness was the one unforgivable thing.

So when they worked over the bottle boy, the easy mark, it was not, mostly, about him at all. It was about each other. It was a display, a rehearsal of dominance staged for the watching girls and for the watching set and above all for the watching internalized fathers, each boy proving to the others and to himself that he belonged on the top of the heap by demonstrating, on a body it was safe to demonstrate on, that he could put another human being beneath him without a flicker of conscience. The bottle boy was not their enemy. The bottle boy was their *equipment* — the apparatus on which a rich boy proved his rank — which is, if you think about it, a worse thing to be than an enemy, and Cirilo felt the difference and never forgot it: that to be hated would at least have been to be *seen,* and what they did instead was use him, casually, as a thing, a rung, a way of talking to each other over his head about which of them was hardest. Chuck Ashby was the best at it, the most fluent, the one who could land the cut that made the others laugh and the girls go quiet — and Chuck Ashby, alone in his grand cold father's house at night, was also the most frightened of all of them, the one who had learned earliest and most thoroughly that in his family love was another word for winning, and that a boy who stopped winning, even for a moment, even out of mercy, would find the warmth switched off in the great house like a tap. He was not a monster. He was a boy being made into one, with great care, by people who called it raising him.

### Chapter — What It Costs

The reckoning came, the first installment of it, on the bright planks in the full light of a Tuesday afternoon.

Cirilo was hauling crates when Chuck Ashby and two of his college friends stepped into his path — easy, grinning, hands in their pockets, with the loose insolence of men who have never in their lives been outnumbered and so do not recognize that this, three on one, is precisely what outnumbering looks like from the other side. They had been talking about him, Chuck said. About how he'd been *seen.* At night. With a young lady of their mutual acquaintance.

"I don't know what you mean," Cirilo said, and set the crate down, slow, and kept his eyes at the level of the man's collar, which was the level the world permitted.

"You know exactly what I mean." And then Chuck Ashby, who was not, whatever else he was, a stupid man, told him how it went — pleasantly, conversationally, the way one man tells another the weather. Out here, for a fellow like Cirilo, with a girl like that, there was no court and no judge and no arm fast enough in the world. There was a rope, and a tree, and a crowd that went home afterward and slept the sleep of the just. He said it without heat, and that was the worst of it, because heat would have meant it was personal, and the absence of heat meant it was simply *true,* a fact of the country like the tide. He stepped in close. "Throw all the strikes you want, Mendez. You can't strike out a county." And he reached up and patted Cirilo's cheek — once, lightly, an insult measured with a jeweler's precision to be too small to answer and too large, ever, to forget — and turned and strolled off up the planks, laughing at something one of his friends had said, already, in his own mind, finished with the whole matter.

But he was not finished with it, and that was the thing Cirilo could not have known, standing there with his hands shaking. For Chuck had felt, in the half-second his palm rested on the other man's face, the muscle jump in Cirilo's jaw — the held thing, the leashed thing, the enormous and disciplined refusal — and somewhere under the easy cruelty a small cold voice had told Chuck the truth he would spend twenty years declining to hear: that the bottle boy had not answered the insult not because he *could* not but because he had *decided* not to; that the power in the transaction was not, in fact, entirely Chuck's; and that a man who can absorb a slap like that and merely stand there, deciding, is a far more frightening thing than a man who swings. Chuck walked away laughing to cover the fact that, for one instant, on a sunny pier, with two friends at his back and a whole county at his command, he had been afraid.

Cirilo stood very still. Then he picked the crate back up. His hands — the good hands, the unmarked ones, the first in four generations to smell of something other than the lime — were shaking, and not from the weight. He carried the crate into the cool dark of the storeroom and set it down and stood there a while; and he thought of his father, who came home gray every night and wanted only that his son be too small to aim at; and he understood at last, all the way down, the thing the old men had been trying to tell him in two different voices his whole life. The family was not merely cursed to second. It was that *reaching for first* — out loud, in the daylight, where they could see you do it — was a thing this country had ways of charging a man for that had nothing whatever to do with baseball. His father had paid that bill once, in a culvert, at twelve years old. Cirilo stood in the dark and felt the old fear settle over him like his father's own coat, two sizes too big and somehow still warm — and felt, underneath the fear and refusing it, the thing he had thrown that day without meaning to: the arm, the answer, the chain slipping its post. Both things lived in him at once. They would go on living in him at once for the rest of his days.

### Chapter — Don Aurelio's Game

The old man had loved baseball since before most of the country knew it had a name, and his loving it was its own long story, and a sad one under the joy, and it was the reason there was an overturned bucket behind every backstop the boy ever pitched in front of.

Don Aurelio had come up into his manhood in the Gold Rush years, when California filled overnight with men from everywhere, and the men brought their games, and the newest and brashest of them — the New York game, baseball — took to that raw place like fire to dry grass. He had watched it played on bare dirt by miners and sailors and clerks with their sleeves rolled, bare-handed in those first years, no gloves, the catcher standing well back and catching the thing on the bounce; he had seen the first San Francisco clubs form, the Eagles and the rest, grown men in earnest playing a children's pastime with a seriousness that puzzled their elders; and in 1869, a grown man with sons of his own by then, he had stood in a vast California crowd and watched the Cincinnati Red Stockings, the first openly all-salaried nine, the first true professionals, come west on the brand-new railroad and beat the local clubs senseless — and he had understood, watching those red-stockinged men move, that something was being born that would matter, that this game was going to be the country's own, the thing it would tell itself about itself. He fell in love. He memorized it. He gave it the part of his heart a man that age does not usually have left to give.

And it did not love him back — not the way he loved it — because by the time the game finished becoming the great American mirror, it had sorted itself the way everything American sorts itself, by money and by name; and a poor man, a Californio, a son of the conquered, could play it on the dirt for nothing and watch it from the cheap seats and love it with his whole soul, but the high clean professional version of it — the version with the salary and the train and the crowd — was being built behind a door that money and the right name kept, and he had neither. Don Aurelio learned that the way he learned the loss of the land — slowly, and completely, and without anyone in the room ever doing a thing he could name. So when the river-stone arm appeared in his own grandson two generations later, the old man's joy and his terror were the same thing, because he already knew the whole shape of it: that the boy had been given precisely enough gift to be broken against precisely the door that had broken the old man's own heart for forty years. He sat on the bucket and taught the boy everything anyway — every grip, every read, every trick the bare-handed years had taught him — pouring his entire ruined hopeless lifelong love of the game into the one Méndez who might, might, carry it through the door he himself had only ever pressed his face against. *Learn it all, mijo,* he said, and meant: *let one of us, finally, be too good for them to keep out.* It very nearly worked. That is the joy in it. It very nearly, and then exactly too late and at exactly the wrong cost, worked.

### Chapter — The Charmer

Luis was the beautiful one — not the most gifted, that was Abel; not the one with the arm, that was Cirilo; but the beautiful one, the charmer, the brother who walked into a room and quietly rearranged it — and what he did with that, in the boardwalk summers of his teens, was the single most dangerous thing a Méndez boy in that county could do: he made the rich men's daughters fall in love with him, and he fell in love with them back, every time, with his whole reckless heart.

They found him thrilling precisely because he was forbidden — the bluff girls, the bankers' and the lumber men's daughters, bored and chaperoned and curious, for whom a handsome boy from the flats with a grin like a struck match was the most exciting danger their safe summers had on offer. And Luis, who should have known better, who had his father's culvert and his brother's broken-knuckle lesson right there in front of him, could not help himself, because he was seventeen and on fire and the wanting was simply bigger than the fear. He went over the fences. He learned which bluff gardens had a gap in the hedge and which bathhouse hours the chaperones dozed; he crept onto the great dark lawns to stand under a lit window; he talked his way into dances he had no business at, in a borrowed collar he'd half-stolen the dime for, and got one waltz with a banker's daughter before someone placed his face and the music stopped. There was a girl named Maud Carrow — a whole summer of her — who loved the thrill of him the way you love a thing you fully intend to give back, and met him in the cypress shadows below her father's house, and wept real tears the night it ended, and married a Stanford man in the fall, and did not, so far as anyone ever learned, think of Luis Méndez again; and Luis, who had fallen for her completely, because he fell completely every time, his gift and his ruin both, carried that one a long while. He was not a cad. That was the heartbreak of him; a cad would have been safer. Luis actually loved them, the unreachable girls, really loved them, and got his heart broken on the bluff fences over and over for the crime of believing, each time, that this one would be different, that love might somehow be stronger than the line. It never was. The line always won.

And it cost him in blood, because the fathers and brothers of those girls did not take it lightly — a Mexican sniffing at their daughters — and Luis took his beatings for it, in alleys and behind bathhouses, the price of the line collected on his body; and it was Abel, more often than not, who came out of the dark to even the count and went to jail for it; and it was Dolores who lay awake over the pair of them, the drinker and the lover, fearing the rope for the one and the bottle for the other, having already buried two children and being unwilling, with the whole ferocity of her, to bury any more. *That boy is going to get himself killed over a girl who will not even remember his name,* she said of Luis — and she was very nearly right, and the nearness of it is the only reason there was a Luis left at all to become the family's spine.

For the boardwalk summers were only a prologue to the day Luis put it down. When Abel's drinking made it plain the oldest would not carry them, Luis — the charmer, the scandal, the fence-climber — folded the whole beautiful reckless wanting self of him into a butcher's apron, married a flats girl named Soledad who was kind and steady and his own, and never went over another fence as long as he lived. The wildness did not die; he buried it; there is a difference, and he felt the difference every day of the long useful unglorious life that followed.

### Chapter — Out of the Shadows

The summer Anastasia turned seventeen she stopped obeying the commandment, and Cirilo helped her break it, and what followed was the first time he watched a cage close on someone he loved at the hands of someone who loved her — which is the only way the worst cages ever close.

She wanted the boardwalk, the very thing her father had spent her life forbidding, and Cirilo took her — smuggled her down on the summer evenings among the swarm of flats kids, where one more girl drew no notice from the law and every notice from everyone else; because Anastasia in the boardwalk lights was an event. The bluff boys saw her — Roy Calder and that set, who kept a particular appetite for a beautiful girl they took to be beneath them and therefore fair game — and circled, and said the things such boys say, and it was Abel, twice, who came out of the dark and made them stop with his fists and paid for it with three weeks in the county jail. But the real danger was never the bluff boys. The real danger was Luis.

For Luis — the charmer, the brother who fell completely every time — fell completely for Anastasia; and she, shut her whole life in a shadow and handed at last a boy who was all light and grin and reckless tenderness, fell back. It was not a bluff-girl summer's thrill, the kind Luis collected and survived. It was the real thing, mutual and deep and wholly impossible — the tannery owner's daughter and the tannery worker's son, the Russian and the Mexican, two children of two frightened immigrant fathers who had each, separately, built the very same shadow to hide their families in. Cirilo saw it happen and was torn straight down the middle, because the two people inside it were his brother and his sister, and there was no side of the thing he could stand on that was not also a betrayal of someone he loved.

It ended the way these things ended, and the cruelty of how it ended was that no one in it was a villain. Fyodor found out — a tannery is a small world — and his fury was not, at the root, the bluff men's clean ugly prejudice; it was terror, the old Volkov terror, the certainty that his daughter entangled with a worker's family meant scandal and ruin and the light finding her at last, the exact fate he had walled her up to prevent. And he reached, frightened, for the one lever a frightened man of property holds: he told Esteban that if the thing did not stop, the Méndez were finished at his tannery — the bench, the wage, the forty years, the family's whole bread. He hated himself for it, in the Russian way, which is to say thoroughly, and aloud, and to no one's benefit. But he did it. And so Luis was handed the choice that would rehearse the rest of his life: the girl, or his family's survival. And Luis — who was not a coward, who was only a son in a poor house with everyone's rent riding on the boss's goodwill — gave her up. Buried it alive: the first of the two great buryings of his life, the dress rehearsal for the butcher's apron still years off. And Fyodor sent Anastasia north to Russian cousins in the city, to be kept safe in a deeper shadow and married, in time, correctly — caged at the last exactly as her father had always sworn the world would cage her, by the one man who loved her most.

Cirilo lost them both that summer — the sister to the northbound train, the brother to a silence that came over Luis after and never wholly lifted. And he learned the thing he would carry down to the pier with Rebecca and be unable, even then, to act on wisely: that the line between people is mostly built by love — by frightened, decent people walling off what they cannot bear to lose — and that being built by love makes it no less a wall, and no less cruel to the ones walled apart. He had seen it now from the inside. It did not stop him, when his own turn came, from walking straight at a wall of his own. Nothing ever stops the dreamers.

### Chapter — Sunday Ball

The flats had a baseball team the way the flats had a saint's day or a fishing fleet — not as a thing apart from the life but as the life itself, distilled and made playable on a Sunday — and the team was the Méndez boys and the Soto cousins and whatever else the lanes could muster, and it played, most Sundays of that last summer, on the hard flat ground behind the gasworks against a loose nine of bluff boys who came down to win and did not always.

It was a poor man's game in every particular. The bases were a feed sack, a flat stone, and a bare patch worn by a thousand sliding hips; the one good ball had been re-covered twice by Tío Refugio and was wound so tight and taped so often it came off the bat like a thrown brick; the gloves were Refugio's homemade ones or no gloves at all. But the ground did not care whose grandfather had won the courthouse and whose had lost it — that was the whole secret thing the flats loved about it, the thing they could never have said aloud — that for nine innings on a Sunday the only questions the world asked you were whether you could field the brick and hit it and run, and the answers did not once consult your name. Abel was the glory of them, when he was sober enough to play, hitting the taped ball halfway to the wharf; Luis played the way he did everything, beautifully and not quite seriously, one eye always on the rise where the girls were. And the girls were there — that was the gravity that pulled the whole bluff nine down to the gasworks in the first place — Rebecca's set arranged prettily on a blanket up the slope under their parasols, pretending the game was beneath them and not missing a pitch of it; and lower down, in among the flats, Anastasia where her father did not know she was, Marcella's arm run through hers, the two of them eating figs out of a Bregante apron and calling out in Russian and Ligurian at the plays.

And Cirilo did not play. That was the thing his brothers could not move him off, the summer he was eighteen: that the boy with the best arm on that whole stretch of coast would not take the ball in front of the bluff and the parasols, because the same gift that lived in his hand lived right next to a shyness so total it was nearly an affliction — the watcher's shyness, the bottle boy's downcast eyes carried off the pier and onto the diamond — and so he kept the book, and shagged the foul balls, and taught the change-up to the smallest Sotos, and would not, for all of Abel's cursing, pitch. He had decided, in the silent place where he decided everything, that to be looked at was to be a target, and he had survived eighteen years by being no one's.

And then came the Sunday after the booth. After the girl had laid her nickel on the wood and looked him in the face and said *do it again* — after he had been, for one cracking instant on the bright planks, *seen,* and not destroyed for it — something in the old arithmetic had shifted a single notch, and would not shift back. Abel was getting hammered that day; the bluff nine were laughing; Chuck Ashby himself had come down, twenty-one and home from Stanford and loose with it, and stood coaching at third for the bluff boys and calling things across the diamond the flats heard and pretended not to. And Rebecca was on the slope. And Cirilo set the scorebook down in the dirt, and walked out to the bare worn patch that served them for a mound, and held up his hand for the taped brick of a ball; and Abel, who had been begging him to do this exact thing for three years, went so still you would have thought he had been handed bad news, because Abel knew to the ounce what it cost and what it drew down. Cirilo threw four pitches to warm. Then he struck out the side on eleven pitches, the bluff bats coming off their shoulders at things already sitting in the catcher's hands; and the flats along the baseline came up off the dirt roaring, and Marcella screamed something magnificent in Ligurian, and Anastasia laughed the free laugh; and up on the slope a banker's daughter rose to her feet without quite deciding to, and had to be tugged back down onto the blanket by Florence Pryor, who had seen precisely where the girl was looking and did not care for it at all. Chuck Ashby stopped coaching third. He watched the brown boy he had patted on the cheek on a sunny pier do a thing to a baseball that no Stanford man on that field could do, in front of the same girl, and this time he did not say *rigged,* because there were no bottles to blame and no booth to blame and nothing at all to blame but the plain unanswerable fact of the arm; and he went home that evening with the first cold understanding that the thing his whole life had promised him — that a boy like that could be put beneath him and kept there with a word — was not, after all, going to be as simple as he had been raised to believe. The flats had a new king that Sunday, a quiet one, who had finally stepped out of the long shadow of his own shyness onto the one ground in the whole county where his name did not count against him; and they carried it home in the dark, singing, all down the lanes. And so, folding her parasol on the empty slope, did the banker's daughter — who said nothing to anyone, and smiled at nothing that anyone else could see.

### Chapter — The Exhibition

He had only one answer to all of it, and one bright Sunday in the new century he gave it in front of the whole town.

It was a challenge game, got up the way such games were — the local nine against a touring club of college boys down from the City for the weekend, Stanford men in crisp flannels with Chuck Ashby among them, loose and certain and already, in his own mind, the hero of the afternoon. A holiday crowd came out to the ground behind the Leibbrandt baths: parasols and straw boaters, the smell of frying smelt and spilled beer, the boardwalk organ carrying its waltzes over the outfield fence. Down the third-base line the people from the flats had gathered in their own loose knot — the Italians off the kilns, the Portuguese off the boats, the Mexican families who spoke the language only at home — and among them Nunzio Sabatini, who had taken an afternoon off he could not afford; and when Cirilo walked out to take the ball for the locals and the whole college bench began to laugh at the bottle boy from the pier come out in spikes, Nunzio rose and bellowed something joyful and obscene in Italian that made the women swat at him and the men grin.

Then Cirilo threw, and the laughing stopped.

The first college man swung at a fastball that was already in the catcher's mitt. The second got the wet one, the pitch his abuelo had pressed on him with a warning folded inside it — and it dropped off the table a foot in front of the plate and left him lunging at air, foolish, in front of his girl and his friends and the whole bright grandstand. And the third was Chuck Ashby, who was not laughing now, who dug in with his jaw set and fouled one back and flailed at two more and finally stalked off to the bench telling anyone who would listen that the ball was doctored — which it was, and which the rules of that year entirely permitted, and which gave him back not one shred of the thing he had just lost in front of the only people he had ever been taught to count. Down the third-base line the flats came apart with joy. Nunzio was weeping with laughter. It was, Cirilo would think much later, the first wholly happy hour of his life, and it was happy precisely because it was *witnessed* — because for one afternoon the thing he could do was larger than the thing he was, and the whole town had to sit in the sun and watch it be larger.

In the little grandstand, a spare man in a dark Jesuit coat had stopped writing in his book.

His name was Aloysius Fagan, and he had come to Santa Clara by way of a hard Limerick boyhood and a harder novitiate, and he carried inside his cassock two appetites he had never managed to reconcile and had stopped, by middle age, expecting to: a genuine and burning love of God and of the boys in his charge, and a frank, competitive, nearly worldly hunger to beat Stanford at baseball every spring — which he confessed as a sin twice a year and committed the rest of the time with his whole heart. He had seen a thousand arms. He had not seen this one. He watched Cirilo work the way another man might watch a sunrise, and he was honest enough even in the watching to admit he did not entirely know which of his two appetites had stood him out of his seat — and Jesuit enough to suspect it did not finally matter, that God was perfectly able to work His purposes through a baseball coach's vanity, having so little else, in this fallen country, to work with.

After the last out he came down to the fence.

"Son," he said. "Have you given any thought to college?"

"We can't pay for college, Father," Cirilo said.

"No," said Fagan, with a small dry smile that had Limerick still in it. "But Santa Clara can pay for an arm. We play Stanford every spring." He let his eyes drift, just once, to Chuck, sulking white-flanneled beside the charabanc that had carried the college boys down. "Wouldn't you like to do that again — where it counts?"

And the door of Cirilo Méndez's whole life swung open on a single sentence; and on the far side of it, only an hour up the line, was every last thing he had been told, all his life, in two languages and by people who loved him, that he was not permitted to want.

### Chapter — The Condor

There was a greater bird than the hawk over that coast once, the greatest in all of North America, and the coast people had held it sacred; and it was the hawk's dark twin, and it carried the one thing about death that everything else had only circled.

The California condor — near ten feet of wing tip to tip, a soaring black cross against the sky, riding the thermals so high and so still it seemed less a bird than a piece of the heavens deciding whether to descend — was a vulture, an eater of the dead, and the coast people did not despise it for that the way the newcomers would; they revered it, gave it a place in the oldest stories, because they understood the thing the condor does, which is the holiest and most necessary thing there is: it takes what has died and lifts it back into the sky.
