The deer that falls, the seal that washes up, the whale on the beach, the spent steelhead in the eddy — the condor finds them, and eats them, and turns the dead flesh into the highest flight in the hemisphere, so that the fallen thing does not merely rot but *rises,* is carried up on a great dark wing into the blue, recycled from death into the most magnificent soaring the continent could show. The hawk is the hunter, the spirit of the reaching life; the condor is its twin, the spirit of what the reaching life becomes — not an end but a lifting, the dead made wing.

The newcomers, of course, nearly killed it — shot it, poisoned it, let the lead of their bullets into the carcasses it fed on, brought the great sacred recycler of the coast down toward the same nothing they brought the otter and the seal and the redwood and the men, until only a remnant rode the far back-country thermals. But the boy, who never saw one — they were nearly gone from his stretch of coast by his boyhood — would have understood the condor at the end if anyone had told him the old stories, because the condor is the answer to the question his own death would pose, about what such a death is for. He died at the summit, spent, like the steelhead on the gravel; and the question is whether that is only an ending — only rot, the gift wasted and the man used up and dropped. And the condor says no. The condor says that what dies at the height does not merely fall; it is taken up, and carried, and turned into flight — that the spent father becomes the glove in the son's hand and the name in the flats' mouth and the gold in the seed in the ground, lifted out of death on a great dark wing into something higher than the living man could reach alone. The hawk had come for him at the start, to mark the reaching life. And the condor, the hawk's sacred twin, was the promise underneath the grief: that the dead, on this coast, in this family, are not dropped. They are lifted. They rise.

### Chapter — Año Nuevo

Up the wild coast north of town, at the point the Spanish had named for the new year, there was a thing the boy saw once and never forgot: the elephant seals, the great absurd magnificent monsters of that shore, hauled out in their hundreds on the sand — and they were, though he could not have known the whole of it then, another verse of the same song the otters sang, the song about being taken nearly all the way down to nothing and refusing, even so, to be quite finished off.

They are enormous, the bulls three tons and more, with the ridiculous pendulous noses that give them the name, roaring their challenges and rearing up to slam their great scarred chests together over the harems in a violence that shakes the beach — and they had been, within living memory, all but gone. The whalers and the sealers had hunted them for their oil the way the fur men had hunted the otters for their pelts, rendered the giants down into barrels of lamp-oil and lubricant, killed them on their own breeding beaches where they could not flee, and killed them and killed them until the species was believed extinct, gone, erased from the coast entirely — and then it turned out that a remnant had survived, a single tiny herd, a few dozen animals on one remote Mexican island that the killers had somehow overlooked, holding on, breeding, waiting; and from that handful, that overlooked unkilled remnant, the whole vast roaring recovery had slowly begun, until the beaches filled again with the impossible three-ton bulls and the harems and the weaned pups, a population brought back from the literal edge of nothing by a few survivors the slaughter had missed.

It was the otters again, and the basket-weaver again, and the chaparral again, and his own people again — the same coast teaching the same lesson in a different enormous shape: that the powerful will take a thing all the way down to what they believe is the end, render it into barrels for a distant market, and write it off as gone; and that *gone* is a verdict the powerful are wrong about more often than they will ever admit, because somewhere there is nearly always a remnant they overlooked, a handful holding on on the ground no one wanted, breeding in the dark, waiting — and that from that overlooked unkilled handful the whole roaring improbable return can come, given only time and the blind enormous engine that does not know how to quit. They had thought the elephant seals gone. They had thought the Californios broken. They were wrong about the seals. The boy looked at the impossible recovered monsters roaring on the beach and felt, without the words for it, that they might yet be wrong about his people too — that *erased* and *overlooked* are not the same word, and that the difference between them is the whole of hope.

### Chapter — How Dolores Got Her Rosary

The men of that family handed down leather, and the women handed down a rosary; and the rosary had come the longer way of the two, through more hands and more grief, and it was the women's whole inheritance and their whole defense, and Dolores carried it because her own mother had folded it into her hands on the night she died and told her, in the old tongue, to go on praying even when — *especially* when — it did no visible good at all.

It was a poor thing to look at, which was the first of its secrets: a string of olive-wood beads gone dark and smooth as river stones, the little cross worn nearly faceless, the whole of it humble enough that no squatter and no lawyer and no deputy had ever once thought it worth the taking — which was exactly why it had survived when the land and the cattle and the good silver had not. It had been carried north out of a dry rancho in Sonora by Dolores's grandmother, a woman who had walked away from one buried life with a child on her hip and this in her fist; and she had taught her daughter to pray it, who had taught Dolores; three generations of women's thumbs had worn those beads to glass, telling the same five mysteries over the same unchanging human freight — the men who did not come home, the children who did not live, the land that went, the fear that stayed. So that the rosary was not, to Dolores, a charm that asked God for things and was disappointed in Him. It was a rope. It was the thing you held while the flood went over you, knotted by the hands of women who had held it through their own floods and not let go; and the holding was the prayer, and the prayer was not *spare me* but *let me not let go.*

She had needed it. She had buried two children before Cirilo was old enough to know their faces — a fever took the one, a winter took the other, the ordinary murdering arithmetic of being poor in a hard country — and she had knelt at the little home altar she kept in the corner, the *altarcito* with its candle and its tin Virgin and its two small photographs, and run the worn beads through her fingers in the dark while the house slept: not because she believed the beads would give her children back, but because her mother and her grandmother had knelt exactly so over exactly this string of wood and had risen again in the morning and gone on, and Dolores meant to rise again in the morning and go on, and the rosary was how the women of her blood had always taught one another to do the one impossible necessary thing, which was to get up again. It was the women's version of the thing the men kept trying to teach the boys with the leather and the curse: how to be brought all the way down and not be finished. The men taught it as endurance and the women taught it as faith, and it was, underneath, the same lesson, the only lesson that family had left to give. And Dolores already knew, telling her beads in the dark over the two small graves, which of her living she thought would have the hardest road and would need the rope the most; and she had already decided — long before the morning ever came that he would climb onto the northbound train and leave them — that when it did, she would press the worn beads into his pitching hand and send the women's whole long unbroken refusal up the line with him, out past the safe inshore line and into the deep water she herself could not follow him into.

### Chapter — The Whales

Twice a year the gray whales went by, close in along that coast, and the whole town knew it the way it knew the seasons of the fish and the fog: the spouts standing up off the water like little gray trees that grew and vanished, the long dark backs rolling, the oldest road on the planet running invisible just offshore.

They were going where they had always gone — down in the winter to the warm lagoons of Baja to bear their young, up in the spring to the cold rich Arctic to feed — the longest migration of any creature that draws breath, ten thousand miles a year, swum by an animal navigating a featureless gray country by means no one could name. And in the spring they came back north past Santa Cruz mothers-and-calves, the great cows keeping their young tucked close to shore, away from the deep water where the killers waited, teaching them the road. That was the part that held the boy at the rail: the calf did not know the way. It had never made the journey. The route lived in the mother, and she swam it with her child pressed to her flank for ten thousand miles, holding it to the safe inshore line, breaching once in a while as if to say *watch, this is how, remember this point, remember the taste of this water* — handing the whole long road forward to a creature that would one day swim it alone, and teach it in turn to a calf of its own, the map living nowhere but in the passing-down, the same as the monarchs, the same as the family, the same as everything that survives at all.

Cirilo watched the spouts go north the spring before he left for good — the season the cows and calves came through — and felt the thing he had no words for at the rail and only understood later: that he was about to swim out into the deep water his own mother could not follow him into, off the safe inshore line, out where the killers were; that no one could press him to their flank for the part of the road he was about to take; that this was simply what the calf must finally do, leave the teaching mother and swim the dangerous open by the map she had put in him, and either make it or not. *Go, mijo,* his mother would say, when the day came, dry-eyed until he had turned away. She had swum him as far up the safe line as she could. The deep water was his. The whales went by, north, mother and child, the oldest road on earth, and then they were past, and the sea closed gray and enormous over where they had been, keeping its counsel, as it had over the otter hunters and the hide ships and would over him.

### Chapter — The Dreamer

Everyone else in the family had been taught, by the culvert and the courthouse, to be afraid — and Cirilo was the one who was not; and that was not courage, exactly, and it was not foolishness either. It was that he was a romantic, a dreamer, the single incurable believer the Méndez had produced in four broken generations, and the dreaming was the thing that kept him alive to a future the rest of them had quietly given up on.

His father had made himself small. His grandfather had named the curse and bowed to it. Abel drowned his gift on purpose and Luis buried his alive — each of them, in his own way, had made a separate peace with the fear, had agreed to want less so the wanting could not be used against them. Cirilo would not. From the time he was small he was reaching for something just past the edge of what a Méndez was permitted to want, and he reached not in spite of the fear but straight through the middle of it, because the dream in front of him was always, to him, more real than the danger around him — which is the whole definition of a romantic, and the reason romantics get broken, and the reason the breaking, in the end, never quite proves them wrong.

His life ran up a ladder of dreams, each rung larger and more impossible than the last. As a small boy it was the hawk — and he did not want to watch the hawk, he wanted to *be* it, to live in the high clean air where the sorting could not reach, free and seeing and answerable to no one. Then it was the rancho, the lost kingdom: a boy who had never laid eyes on it dreaming of winning it back — not with money, which the family would never have, but with *justice,* by becoming so undeniably good at a thing that the system which had stripped them would be shamed into handing it back, forced at last to call a Méndez first. Then it was Rebecca — the most impossible dream of them all, forbidden by every lesson his brothers' broken hearts had taught him, a bluff girl behind a hedge a flats boy was not allowed even to look at — and he loved her anyway, dreamed her anyway, because the impossibility of a thing had never in his life been a reason not to want it. And then it was the diamond: the baseball hero's life, the green field where the lines were chalked fair and a man was weighed by what he could do and not by what he was — the one stretch of ground in the whole sorting country where his romantic's ideal, that it should be *fair,* that a man should rise on his merits, that equality might be more than a word, was not a daydream at all but the printed rule. He fell in love with baseball because baseball was his politics made playable: the world he wished for, drawn in lime, sixty feet six inches at a time.

And under all of it lay the plainest thing, the floor beneath every soaring story he told himself, and the truest and most defenseless part of him: he did not, finally, want to be a hawk, or a landed don, or even the grand alien name that strangers would one day try to fix on him from far away. Beneath the hawk and the rancho and all the rest of it, what Cirilo Méndez wanted — the entire reaching length of his life — was the smallest and most ordinary thing a man can want. A wife who saw him. A child who would carry the name. A roof that was his own, and a door he did not have to stand afraid behind. Every grand impossible dream he ever chased was only the long way around to that plain one, the romantic's enormous detour back toward his own kitchen table.

### Chapter — The Tamalada

Because a Méndez did not leave for anywhere without the family first feeding him past the point of reason, the last thing they did before they let him go was make tamales — a *tamalada,* the whole clan packed into the small kitchen and the yard behind it, three generations and the Soto cousins and anyone who drifted by, because a tamalada was not a meal you cooked so much as a thing the family did to itself, together, in self-defense against a parting none of them would say aloud.

It ran on the women, and it ran for two days. The dried corn had been simmered with a lump of *cal,* the lime, until the skins slipped — *nixtamal,* the old word, older than Spanish — and then ground on the stone *metate* that had come north with the same grandmother as the rosary, Dolores and the aunts taking turns on their knees at it, leaning their whole weight into the *mano* until the wet *masa* came off the stone smooth as clay; and the lard was beaten into it by hand, a fistful at a time, until a woman could drop a pinch of the dough into a cup of water and it floated, which was how you knew it was ready, the only way anyone knew, no clock in it anywhere, only the grandmother's thumb and the floating. The *chile colorado* for the pork was its own long afternoon — the dried red chiles stemmed and seeded and toasted on the *comal* until the whole kitchen breathed fire and everyone coughed and laughed and blamed everyone else, then soaked soft and ground back down to a dark red velvet and cooked into the shredded meat until the house smelled of nothing else. And the corn husks, the *hojas,* soaked pliable in a bucket, waited in their soft wet stack for the assembly.

And here the women did the thing they did every single time, the thing the young men dreaded and secretly lived for: they conscripted the boys. There was no escaping it; a Méndez male who tried to slip off to the yard was hauled back by an aunt and sat down at the long table with a soaked husk in front of him and shown, for the hundredth time and as though for the first, how to spread the masa thin and even with the back of a spoon — *thin, mijo, thin, you are not plastering a wall* — and where to lay the line of red pork, and how to fold the husk over on itself and turn the empty bottom up and stand the little parcel upright in the great steaming *olla* with the others, packed close as churchgoers, to cook over the low fire for hours while the smell drove the whole flats half-mad with wanting. Abel folded his crooked and ate two of the filling raw and was threatened with a spoon. Luis flirted with the masa so outrageously that even Dolores had to laugh. And Cirilo, who would be gone in two days up a line none of them could follow, spread his masa with a care that had nothing to do with tamales, because his hands had understood before his heart would let it that this was the last of these he would stand inside for a long while, and perhaps — though he shoved the thought down where he shoved everything — the last like this, exactly this, the whole clan loud and whole and in one room, that he would ever have; and so he wanted to do it slowly, and well, and to keep the smell.

They ate at dark, too many in too small a room, the tamales steaming when you peeled the husk back, the grandmother weeping a little and pretending it was the chile. And when the plates were cleared, Tío Refugio stood up — which the talkers all hushed for, because Refugio rarely stood to speak — and set a bundle of soft cloth on the table in front of Cirilo, and did not make a speech, because the family was not a speech-making family. It was a glove. A new one; a man's glove this time, cut from the best whole piece of hide in the shop and not from an offcut, the pocket worked deep and true the way Don Aurelio liked it, the lacing tight, a real web stitched in between the thumb and the first finger — a glove fit to be seen on a green college field an hour up the line, where the sons of the men who had taken everything would be watching to see the Mexican fail. *The little one stays small,* Refugio said — which was as near as he would come to saying that he knew the boy still had the first glove, the child's one, and meant him to keep it. *This one's grown, like you. Don't let them tell you whose it is.* And the family, who had nothing, who had been left nothing, gave the boy the only thing they had ever truly owned, twice over — leather, made by their own hands, with their whole hearts stitched into it — and sent him off up the line carrying both: the small first glove of the child he had been, and the new one for the man they were betting, against all the evidence and with everything they had, that the country would finally have to let him become.

### Chapter — The Night Before

He went to her the night before he left, because there are things a man cannot say from a train window, and because the thing he had to tell her would bend the shape of both their lives, and he was not coward enough — or perhaps only not wise enough — to let her hear it first from the gossip of the bluff.

They met where they always met, in the black wedge of shadow under the pier with the surf coming up to fill the silences, and he told her: that a priest in a black coat had watched him throw, and that there was a place waiting for him at the old college an hour up the line, paid, on nothing but the strength of his arm; that he meant to take it; that he was leaving in the morning. And Rebecca Whitfield, who had rehearsed a hundred brave things to say if ever this day came, said not one of them. She said, "Don't go."

It surprised them both. She heard it come out of her small and bare and frightened, stripped of every accomplishment that eleven years of being made into an ornament had armored her with, and she could not call it back, and found, in the end, that she did not want to. "Don't go," she said again. "Stay. Be the bottle boy. Let me come down to the pier all summer and pay my nickel and let it be this. I know what you think is up there. I was raised on those men, Cirilo — I have sat at their tables my whole life — and I will tell you the thing they will never tell you: the higher you climb, the *harder* they hold the line, not the softer. Down here, whole, you are nobody anybody covets. But go up there and make yourself into a thing they have to look at, and they will take you from me a rung at a time and call it your success — and the world will help them, and call it your due." She was crying now, without leave, the motherless girl who had learned before she was nine exactly what it was to have the thing she loved carried off by a careless world that never even noticed it was taking. "I would rather have the bottle boy whole," she said, "than a great man three thousand miles away."

And Cirilo, who loved her past all sense and knew to the inch that every word of it was true, told her the one truth that was truer. "Look at us," he said, gently, in the dark, where it was the only place the country would let them stand. "Look at where we have to do this. I'm not going to stay down here so they'll let me keep you in a shadow under a pier. I'm going up there so that one day I can stand with you in the daylight and there won't be a man in this country with the standing to tell me no — because I'll have made the one thing they cannot argue with, which is to be so plainly the best at a thing that they have to say my name out loud whether it kills them or not." He took her two hands. "I don't want to be a hawk," he said — and she did not know what he meant, and he did not entirely explain it. "I don't want the rancho back, or any of it, not really, not underneath. I want what my father has and can't keep safe, and what your father has and doesn't deserve. I want a *family.* A wife who sees me. A child to carry the name forward. A roof over the two of them that is mine, with a door I don't have to stand afraid behind. That's the whole of it, Rebecca — that's the entire size of the dream, under all the loud parts of it. Every big impossible thing I'm about to go chasing is only the long road back to that small one: a kitchen table, with you at it, and ours around it. And there's no short road to it for a man like me. There's only the long one, up the line. And I have to start up it in the morning."

She did not answer, because there was no answer; he was right and she was right and the rightness of them both was the exact shape of the tragedy, and they stood a while holding it between them the way you hold a thing too hot to keep and too dear to drop. And when the watchman's lamp came swinging along the rail and it was time, and he had to go, he did a thing she did not understand until he was already gone. He crouched in the dark, and set down on the dry sand where the lamp would not find it the old glove — the small one, the first one, the one Tío Refugio had cut and his grandmother had stitched and his grandfather had judged twice from the bucket, the one he had slept with as a boy and outgrown and never in his life let go — and he stood it carefully upright against a piling, in her keeping, and said nothing about it at all. He did not say *hold this for me.* He did not say *I'll come back for it.* He let the leather say the thing his pride and his fear would not let his mouth shape: that a man does not leave the truest thing his family ever made him in a girl's keeping unless he means, with the whole of his life, to come back for it. And then he climbed up out of the dark and was gone up the planks toward the morning, and the depot, and the train.

She saw. Of course she saw — she had been watching his hands the whole while, the pitching hand, the unmarked one, and she watched it set the glove down and knew to the bottom of her exactly what the setting-down meant. And she did not run after him. She could have; the planks were empty; she could have caught him at the rail and pressed it back into his hands and told him she understood, told him she would wait, said at last the brave thing she had failed to say. She did not. She let him go, and let the unspoken thing stay unspoken between them, and stood alone in the dark a long time with the small outgrown glove held in her two hands — the leather still faintly carrying the smell of a poor family's lamp and a saddler's bench and the oil a boy had worked into it every Sunday of a childhood she had not been there to share: holding, without yet having the courage to say it even to herself, the first thing she had ever been given by anyone who wanted nothing from her at all except to be, one day, in the daylight, her *family.* And somewhere up the dark planks a train was already waiting to make a sixteen-year farewell out of a one-night goodbye; and the waiting that would be the whole long middle of her life had — though she stood very still, and said nothing, and let the lamp sweep past her into the dark — already, quietly, begun.

### Chapter — The Leaving

He left from the little depot in his one good suit — Dolores had turned the collar and pressed it twice the night before, saying nothing, which was how she said the most — with a college letter buttoned into his breast pocket and a valise that did not need to be large.

The family came to the end of the lane but not onto the platform, because the platform belonged to the other Santa Cruz, the one with the money and the timetables, and they had learned where their welcome ended as surely as they had learned where every other line was drawn. Luis shook his hand hard, said something about writing, and looked away — his resentment and his love so thoroughly braided by now that even he could no longer have told you which was which. Abel, already a little drunk at nine in the morning, hugged him too long and whispered, with the terrible clear sight of the ruined, *don't come back like me* — and then laughed it off and was gone up the lane before Cirilo could answer. Dolores took his face in both hands and looked at him a long while and gave him the language one last time, low and fast, a blessing older than the mission; and then she pressed into his pitching hand the worn olive-wood rosary that had come north in her own grandmother's fist out of Sonora, and folded his fingers down over it, and said only, low, *no lo sueltes, mijo* — don't let go — meaning the beads, and meaning all of it; and let him go. And Esteban stood a little apart, gray already though the day was young, his ruined hands working at the brim of his hat; and when his son came to him he could not, at first, find anything to say at all, because everything he wanted to say was *don't,* and he had decided in the night, watching Dolores press the collar, that he would not say it. So instead he said, roughly, "They will try to make you small up there. In a different way than here. A politer way." And then, against his whole life, against the culvert and the oak limb and forty years of being careful, he reached out and folded his son's pitching hand — his mother's beads already shut inside it — into a fist inside his own two stained ones, the old man's gesture, the gesture he had watched his own father make and had sworn in his fear never to make himself; and he held it a moment, and let it go, and turned and walked up the lane after his sons without looking back, because if he looked back his son would see his face.

Rebecca came to the platform itself, against every rule, to the very edge of it and no farther, because she could not afford to be seen, and they both knew it, and the knowing was part of the goodbye. She told him Santa Clara was only an hour, a morning's train — as though either of them believed for a single second that distance was the thing that kept them apart.

"It's a hundred years," he said, "and we both know it. Your people, my people —"

"Then go," she said, fierce and low, the tears standing in her eyes and refusing, by force of will, to fall where the platform could see them. "Go and become a man so loud they cannot pretend not to hear your name. And come back for me when you have."

She pressed something into his hand: the kid-leather glove, the soft one, the one she had worn the afternoon at the booth. "So you remember," she said, "whose hand you're playing for." The train called down the line. He closed his fist around the leather — her glove, made of the one material his family had ever truly owned, tanned God knew where by God knew whose stolen hands — and he stepped up into the car, and he did not look back, because he had learned that very morning from his father, without either of them ever saying it, that there are partings a man can only survive by refusing to witness them.

Through the window the platform slid away. Rebecca stood in the wrong place a moment too long — a beat past safety, a beat that was its own small act of defiance — and then made herself turn, and walk, alone, into a life that had just, in the space of one train whistle, quietly become a waiting.

And as the train pulled out of the valley and swung north, a red-tailed hawk came up off the ridge on the morning wind and ran with it a little way — out over the bright water and the drying nets and the hills going gold — holding station off the car window the way the first one had hung over a dirt yard eighteen years before, the morning the old man whispered that it had come to look at the boy. Dolores saw it from the lane, and stopped, and did not say aloud the thing she knew: that it had come to see him out, and that it would go with him only to the edge of the country that was theirs, and not one wingbeat farther. It tilted, at the last, on a held wing. Then it slid back south toward the hill and the bay, and let the train carry him on alone into the clear hard country that kept no such birds.

And the boy on the train rode north toward the colleges and the cathedrals and the sons of the men who had taken everything, with a white girl's glove shut in one fist and his abuelo's voice in one ear and his father's two warnings in the other, and a silence in him that had finally, after eighteen years, hardened all the way down into a purpose: he would climb, and he would not stop climbing, and he would make them say the name. Whatever it cost — and it would cost him, in the end, everything, though he could not yet know that, and would not have stepped off the train if he had — he would not, ever, for any price they named, agree to be second to the men who had decided he had been born it.

---

## _Siempre Segundo_ — Act II, novelized (full chapters)

### Chapter — The Chaparral

On the dry sun-facing slopes, where the redwoods could not hold and the meadows gave out, grew the chaparral — the tough gray-green scrub the comfortable never look at twice, the chamise and the ceanothus and the manzanita twisted like old arthritis — and it was the most stubborn and unkillable thing on the whole coast, the boy's people rendered into a single hardy plant.

It asked for nothing. It lived on the steep poor ground no gentler plant would fight for, in the long rainless heat, its little leathery leaves turned edge-on to the sun to spend no more water than it had to, its roots going down through cracked rock farther than the plant stood tall — a thing built entirely for endurance, for the long dry second-place seasons, for surviving on exactly what the better ground did not want. And it had a secret the redwoods did not have and did not need: it had made its peace with fire. The chaparral burns — it is built to burn, oily and ready — and where a redwood meets fire as a wound, the chaparral meets it as a door, because many of its seeds will not open, will not even consider living, until fire has passed over them; they lie in the dirt for years, for decades, waiting, and it is only the heat and the char and the smoke of the thing that destroys the parent that finally cracks them awake, so that the burned black slope nobody mourns is, by the next spring, greener and more alive and more crowded with new ceanothus than it had been in twenty unburned years.

There was the whole hard hope of his people in it, and Cirilo half-knew it as a boy and knew it completely at the end. That the ones assigned the poor steep dry ground learn to spend nothing and root deep and turn edge-on to the heat and endure the long seasons the favored never have to; and that the very fire that comes through and blackens everything and is mourned as catastrophe is also, for the ones built to wait in the dirt, the thing that finally cracks the future awake — that the burning and the renewal are not opposites but the same event, that a people can be charred to the black ground and be, in the seed, in the waiting unkilled seed, more ready to come up green than they had ever been; *we do not disappear,* said the chaparral, in the only way a plant can say anything, which is by being unkillable on the ground nobody else will have — burn us and we seed, cut us and we crown back from the root, and come the spring after the worst fire you can set, look at the black slope you wrote off, and count the green.

### Chapter — The Threshold

He went up to the College of Santa Clara on the strength of one sentence from a man in a black coat, and he spent his first month there learning that a door opening is not at all the same thing as being welcome on the far side of it.

The college was Jesuit and old — the oldest college in California, raised on the grounds of a mission older than the state and the conquest both — and it was full, that autumn, of the sons of the men who had taken his family's land. Some of them he could even have named, had he wished to: a Bray, descended from the Boston captain who had danced the jota at the rancho and then filed on it; a boy whose grandfather had bought a Méndez quarter-league off the lawyer Quint for the price of a good horse. They were courteous to him in the way the secure are courteous to the gifted: for he was, by the end of that first autumn, the best arm the college had — the ace they sent at Stanford and the University every spring, the boy the alumni came out to watch — and that bought him a real place at the long table. But it was a poor boy's place, down at the foot of it, and he felt the distance every day, and the distance was not cruelty so much as the plain unbridgeable fact of money: the others had it without ever having had to think about it, and he had none, and no arm on earth closed that gap at dinner, whatever it closed on the field. He wore his one good suit until it shone at the elbows. He learned which fountains and which staircases and which pews were, by an arithmetic no one had ever written down but every soul in the place could perform without error, *his.* He learned to construe Cicero and to serve at Mass and to hold his knife as the others held theirs — all of it the way he had learned everything: in silence, watching, giving nothing back that could be used.

Father Fagan watched over him, in his fashion, which was a complicated fashion. The Jesuit was proud of him the way a man is proud of a fine tool he has found and sharpened, and fond of him the way a man grows fond of anything he has bet on; and there were nights, walking the cloister, when Fagan examined his conscience on the matter and did not love what he found there — that he had brought a poor tannery boy into a house full of the sons of wealth, and could not always tell whether he had opened a door for him or only walked him up to a glass one — and that he, Fagan, was the man who had done it. He took the boy's confession on Saturdays. He never quite found the courage to make his own to the boy.

Cirilo did not mind the edge of the table, or told himself he did not, which at nineteen is very nearly the same thing. He had not come for the welcome. He had come because Rebecca Whitfield was an hour up the line at Stanford, close enough that on clear nights he fancied he could feel her there like heat off a wall; and because his abuelo had folded his fingers into a fist on a tannery floor and forbidden him to give the hands back; and because somewhere under the silence he had worn like a second suit his whole life, a thing had finally hardened all the way into a plan, cold and clear and patient as a winter creek. He would make himself so good that they would have no choice but to say his name — and with a name they had been made to say, he would go and get her, and give her a life worth what it would cost her to take him. He confessed the pride of it to Father Fagan twice, and was absolved twice, and kept it anyway — because some sins a man is not actually sorry for, and both he and the old priest knew it, and that shared knowledge was the nearest thing to friendship the house allowed them.

### Chapter — The Mission Nine

And here the book changes hands, the way his life did; for at the College of Santa Clara, an hour up the line and a whole country away from the boardwalk, Cirilo Méndez walked for the first time in his life into a place where the thing he was *known* for was a thing he could *do.*

The college played the best ball in the state and had for thirty years — the old mission school, the Jesuits, who had taken up the American game with the same competitive devotion they brought to Latin and to souls, and who fielded a nine that went at Stanford and the University every spring as though salvation rode on the result; and a boy who could throw the way Cirilo threw was, on that one ground, not a problem to be managed but an asset to be prized. The difference nearly undid him those first weeks — the sheer unfamiliar weight of being *wanted.* He had come up out of a world where his gift was a danger to be hidden, and here were grown men in cassocks and flannels who looked at the very same gift and saw a thing to be polished and shown off and beaten Stanford with; and it took him a month to stop flinching at the wanting, the way a beaten dog flinches at a lifted hand even when the hand is holding out food.

The diamond itself he loved the way a man loves a country he has been homesick for his whole life without ever knowing its name. He loved the morning smell of it, the cut grass and the wet leather and the sharp green sting of the liniment the trainer worked into the older men's arms; he loved the particular sounds of it, the flat crack of ash on the dead ball, the *thock* of it into the pocket of the glove his family had made him, the chatter and the spikes loud on the board floor of the cage; he loved the dawn practices when the valley fog was still down in the orchards and the only souls awake in all the world were the ballplayers and the birds. For it was a different country up there, inland, and he set himself to learn its animals the way he had learned his coast's — not the hawk and the otter and the steelhead of the cold gray shore he came from, but the valley's own: the meadowlarks that sang off the outfield fence at first light, the jackrabbits that broke from the foul ground in long crazy zigzags, the red-tailed hawks — those followed him; those were everywhere — riding the warm valley thermals over miles of orchard. They called it the Valley of Heart's Delight, the whole broad floor of it gone to prune and apricot and cherry, and in the spring the college played its games in a sea of blossom that snowed white petals across the infield on the wind, the sweetest-smelling ballground in creation. And Cirilo, the coast boy, learned that his own Santa Cruz lay just there, over the dark wall of the mountains to the west — the two old mission towns raised by the same brown and Indian hands within a few years of each other, joined now by the rail line that climbed up through the redwoods and over the hill, so that the very lumber that built the valley's crates for its fruit came down out of his own mountains, and the line he had ridden up was the same line that carried his coast's redwood to the valley's orchards: two halves of one taken country, and him shuttling between them with a glove and a secret.

And he had, for the first time in his life, a friend made out of baseball — a catcher: a broad, freckled, scholarship Irish boy named Brick Sullivan, up from the railroad yards of the city, as poor as Cirilo and as out of place among the gentlemen's sons, who caught him that first autumn and discovered inside a week what every good catcher discovers about a great arm, which is that it belongs, a little, to him. For a battery is a kind of marriage, the only one the rough game allows — two men who learn to think one thought across sixty feet six inches without a word said aloud — and Brick Sullivan, who had nothing and no name and a squat body built for nothing on God's earth but crouching in the dust and taking foul tips on the bare hand, gave Cirilo the one thing the whole genteel college withheld: an equal. A partner. A man who needed him and was needed back, who called him *Cy* because he could not be troubled with the rest of it and meant it with affection, and who would, in the long cold years still ahead, be one of the few from the early days who never forgot the arm, or the man it was bolted to. They were the two outsiders at the far edge of the long table, the Mexican and the mick; and they did the thing outsiders do, which is to make, out of the very game that used them both, a country of two, and hold it.

### Chapter — The Examination

There was a professor at Santa Clara, a layman named Pruett who taught rhetoric and resented having to teach it to the likes of Cirilo, and who found, in the oral examinations, his chance.

The orals were public — that was the cruelty built into them — conducted before the assembled class, a boy standing alone at the front construing Latin and defending propositions while his fellows watched and the professor probed. For most of the gentlemen's sons it was a formality, a genial hazing they had been bred for since the cradle, the Latin half-learned at good schools and the confidence wholly inherited. Pruett ran Cirilo's examination differently. He ran it long, and he ran it hard, doubling back, setting traps in the ablative, putting to the scholarship boy the questions he put to no one else, with a thin courteous smile that invited the room to enjoy what was coming — the bottle boy exposed at last as the fraud a man like Pruett needed him to be, because if the tannery's son could construe Cicero as well as the banker's son, then something Pruett had built his whole small life upon was not true, and Pruett would sooner break a boy in front of a class than let that be so.

Cirilo construed the Cicero. He had spent four hundred nights on it, in the silence, by a borrowed lamp, the way he had spent four hundred afternoons on the spitball — because he had understood from the first week that for him there would be no formality, no genial pass, no inherited confidence to coast upon; that he would be examined harder than any gentleman in the room and would therefore have to be *better,* simply to be permitted to be equal. And so he was better. He answered the traps. He found the verb. And when Pruett, furious now beneath the courtesy, abandoned rhetoric altogether for open baiting — asking the boy whether a charity scholar, a tannery hand's son, did not think his talents might be better spent in the trades that had bred him than in wasting a gentleman's Latin — the room went still; and Cirilo, who had taken the slap on the pier and not swung, looked at the professor a long moment and answered, in Latin, quietly, with a line of the very Cicero they had been construing: the observation that it is the mark of a small mind to mistake the accident of birth for the measure of a man. Which was not, in fact, anywhere in the passage. Which Cirilo had composed on the spot, in correct Ciceronian Latin, and attributed to Cicero — gambling everything on the wager that Pruett, for all his airs, did not know the text half so well as the boy who had spent four hundred nights inside it. And Pruett, caught, uncertain, not daring to challenge a citation he could not place, went red, and nodded stiffly, and ended the examination; and the gentlemen's sons, who were not bad fellows and who loved a winner the way the young always do, broke into a startled applause the professor could not stop. Father Fagan heard of it within the hour. He laughed until he had to sit down — and then went to the chapel, troubled, and confessed that he had laughed, and admitted to God that he was not, in truth, entirely sorry, which was the same thing he was forever having to confess about that boy, and the same thing God, he suspected, was forever declining to mind.

### Chapter — The Steelhead

The steelhead lived the whole shape of Cirilo Méndez's life ten thousand times over before he was born to live it once.

It begins in the gravel of the upper creek, in the cold clean water under the redwoods, a fish no longer than a finger. And then the engine takes it — the same engine that drives the hawk and the spawning grass and every reaching thing on that coast — and it goes *down:* down the ravine the hawk flew down, down the San Lorenzo to the lagoon and out through the bar into the enormous salt, and there it grows, and silvers, and becomes a strong and beautiful thing in the open sea, ranging far, answerable to no one. It could stay. Nothing in the ocean compels it home. But the engine is patient and absolute, and one season it turns the silver fish around and points it back at the single creek out of all the creeks on that coast, the one it came from, and says: *up.*

And the fish climbs. Against the whole falling weight of the water it climbs — up the riffles, hurling itself at the falls again and again, battering its silver sides bloody on the rock, resting in the pools to gather what is left of itself and then climbing again, driven by a thing it did not choose and cannot refuse: to reach the exact gravel it was born in, and spawn, and make more of its kind. The current is always against it. That is the design. The climb is *into* the current, by definition, every inch of the way up — and most years most of them do not make it. The otter takes them, or the heron, or the drought leaves the creek too thin, or they simply spend the last of themselves on one fall a single leap too high and turn over, silver, in an eddy, and feed the same forest that made the fog that fed the creek they died climbing. The ones that fail are not less driven than the ones that arrive. They are only the ones the current happened to beat. Abel was a steelhead. Edward Purse was a steelhead — the strongest in the creek, turned back at a falls that men had built across the water for no reason but that they could. The river is full of them, the strong ones the current beat, and the river does not grieve them; and that is the river's mercy and the river's crime at once, and they are the same thing, as they always are.

But some make it. Some claw the whole of themselves up the river against the falling weight of the world and reach the high gravel and spawn — and here the steelhead tells the last of Cirilo's life, the part he could not yet know: that the climb spends you; that many a fish, having dragged its whole self up the river and given the gravel its only purpose, simply dies there, at the top, in the cold clean water it was born in, having arrived, having won, having made more of its kind — finished by the very reaching that completed it. The triumph and the dying are not two events. They are one event, seen from its two ends. The fish does not know it is heroic; it is only obeying the engine, which is another word for love, which is another word for the refusal to not-continue. And it climbs, into the current, and wins, and dies of the winning, and leaves the gravel thick with small finned things the length of a finger, that will go down to the sea in their turn and feel, one day, the same absolute summons to come home against the current and reach and spawn and be spent — *siempre,* the river says: second to nothing in this world but the engine itself, which made the river and the fish and the falls and the long doomed beautiful climb all at once, and called it good.

### Chapter — The Craft

What his grandfather had given him on the overturned bucket was a wild and natural thing — an arm, a few bare-handed tricks, a river-stone accuracy — and what the long valley afternoons made of it across the next years was a *craft;* and the making of the craft was the first thing in Cirilo Méndez's whole life that was wholly and cleanly fair, and he gave himself to it the way a drowning man gives himself to air.

For pitching, in that dead-ball age, was not the thing of pure speed it would one day become. The ball itself was soft, kept in play until it was brown and lopsided and soft as a plum, and a man who lived by velocity alone got found out the second time through the order. It was a game of deception and command, of the long chess underneath the brief violence — and that suited the watcher in him exactly, the boy who had survived his whole life by reading rooms he was forbidden to look at directly. He learned to command the ball to the corners, low and on the black, the way the old man had drummed it into him on the bucket — *never the same height twice, mijo, and never the height they want* — and to change the hitter's eye, a fastball up under the chin and then the slow ball off the identical arm-swing, so that the man's whole body committed to a heater that never came and he buckled at a thing floating in at half the speed. He learned the spitball, which the rules still allowed and would for twenty years yet — two fingers laid flat on the wetted leather so the ball left his hand with no spin at all and fell off the table a foot in front of the plate, the pitch his abuelo had pressed on him as a boy. He learned to scuff and to shine; to read a hitter's feet and his grip and the set of his hands and know, before ever he came to the windup, whether the man was sitting dead-red on the fastball or guessing, and to throw him the other thing. And he began, in those years, to work at the hardest pitch of them all — the one a great New York pitcher was just then making famous clear across the country, the *fadeaway,* the ball thrown with the wrist turned in and the arm pronated against its own nature, breaking the wrong way, away from a right-hander's bat and into the reach of no glove that expected it. It cost him to learn it, the unnatural inward twist of it aching in the elbow on the cold mornings; and he learned it anyway, because the whole of his life had already taught him that the things worth having all cost precisely that — an ache in the joint and a price paid in the dark — and were had on those terms or not at all.

But the deepest thing he learned in those years was the thing the diamond taught him about the world, which was that the diamond was a lie. A beautiful one — the most beautiful he ever knew — but a lie. For sixty feet six inches the country told a truth it told nowhere else on its whole broad surface: that a man was exactly as good as what he could do; that the lines were chalked fair; that merit was real and visible and past all argument; that if you threw the ball by them they had to write it down in the honest book. He had fallen in love with baseball for precisely this — because it was his politics made playable, the fair world he believed in drawn in lime on the grass — and he was good enough, and getting better every valley spring, to let himself begin to believe that the diamond's promise might be true off the diamond too; might leak out past the foul lines into the country at large; might mean that a man who made himself undeniable between the chalk would be, at the last, undeniable everywhere. It was the one thing his cold clear watcher's eye never quite let him see straight, the single blind spot in a man who saw nearly everything: that the fairness stopped at the chalk. That the same country which would, on the field, be forced to enter his strikeouts in its honest book kept also a second book, off the field, with a different arithmetic in it and a door at the end of it — and that he was building his whole magnificent climb on the faith that the fair lime lines ran clear to the edges of the world, when in truth they stopped exactly where the grass did, and there the long grass of the real country began, where no one anywhere was chalking anything fair.

### Chapter — The Bird Dog

There were men who made their living in the gap between what the diamond promised and what the country delivered, and one of them was up in the Santa Clara grandstand the spring Cirilo found his fadeaway, with a flask and a dog-eared notebook and an eye that had been appraising ballplayers since before the boy was born; and his name was Vance Greevy, and he scouted the West for the Coast League, and he is worth the knowing because he saw the whole of the tragedy plainly, years before it finished, and set it down in three words and could do nothing on God's earth about it.

The Coast League was the best ball west of the Mississippi, good enough that men called it a third major league, and the Eastern clubs sent their bird dogs out to comb a whole coast of it for talent before the next man's bird dog got there first. Greevy was a bird dog — a finder — paid a pittance and his train fare to sit in the cold grandstands of college games and mining-town games and Sunday games and turn up the arms and the bats early. He was not a sentimental man; sentiment was bad for the work. He sat that afternoon beside a hard practical Coast League manager named Brennan, down to look at a Stanford shortstop everyone was talking up, and the two of them watched the Santa Clara Mexican kid strike out the side on a fadeaway that broke like it had hit a wall in the air; and Brennan said the thing managers say — *Christ, where'd the Jesuits dig that up?* — and Greevy did not answer for a while, because he was doing the sum he had done a thousand times and hated every single time, and it came out the way it always came out. *Best arm on the field,* he said at last, and spat, and wrote three words under the boy's name in the dog-eared book; and the three words were: *can't use him.* Not *can't pitch.* Not *won't last.* Can't use him — not *can't,* in truth, but *won't:* because the book Greevy scouted for kept a second column no one ever printed, the column where a club totted up not the arm but the gate, and a poor Mexican junkballer with a tannery behind him and a name no marquee would carry came out, every time, on the wrong side of the only arithmetic the clubs finally ran. Too foreign to sell. Too old to develop. Too cheap a story to spend the ink on, whatever he did to a baseball. Vance Greevy, who knew an arm when God set one in front of him, knew that this one had been born on the wrong side of the till.

And here is why he is worth the knowing: he did not forget. That was Greevy's particular small decency and his particular small torment both — that he was good enough at the only thing he did to know exactly what he was looking at, and powerless enough inside the machine he served to do nothing with the knowing but carry it. He kept the name. He underlined it, season after season, as the Santa Clara kid went out into the bush leagues and the Beach Flats clubs and up at last into the Coast League itself, and beat to their faces the arms that got the calls he never would; and Greevy ground his teeth in the cold grandstands and signed the usable men, because the usable men were what the machine would take, and kept the kid's name underlined in book after book like a debt the game owed and would not pay. Whether it would ever be paid — and in what late hour, and at what price — was not a thing a bird dog could know or alter; and he could not, for all his underlining, move it a single inch toward the light. He took a long pull from the flask and felt, not for the first time and not for the last, like a man paid to keep the gate of a house he was ashamed of. And the white petals came down across the green infield on the wind, and the unsellable arm threw another one nobody could touch, and Vance Greevy wrote it all faithfully down in a book that no one who could have changed a thing would ever trouble to read.

### Chapter — The Rematch

It came, the way these things do, on an ordinary afternoon the body remembers forever.

Stanford came down to Santa Clara on a bright Saturday in the heart of the spring, when the whole Valley of Heart's Delight stood in blossom and the orchards snowed white petals across the infield on the warm wind. The rivalry filled the wooden stands and lined the foul ropes three deep — these were the two best college nines in the state, and had hated each other gladly for thirty years — and the Santa Clara faithful had come to see one thing above all the rest: their ace.

For that was what Cirilo Méndez had become, in two seasons up the line. Not the arm they tolerated at the foot of the table but the *ace* — the one the old grads drove down from the City to watch, the one the student body chanted for, the reason a Santa Clara man could walk into any room in the valley that spring with his chin up. He had beaten Stanford the year before and no one on either side had forgotten it. He warmed in the petal-snow with his cap pulled low, and the stands said his name, and for once in his life the saying of it was a clean and uncomplicated thing.

He pitched that afternoon the way the old man had drummed into him off the bucket, and the cardinal nine could do nothing with it. He read their feet and changed their church and never showed a pair of eyes the same height twice; he ran the dying ball in fat and watched the Stanford bats swing over the ghost of it; inning after inning their hitters walked back to the bench muttering, and the petals came down, and the home crowd climbed toward the particular ecstasy of a town watching a rival humbled by one of its own. He did not look at the stands. He had long since learned not to look at stands. He kept his eyes on the stitched leather and the catcher's mitt and the one fair country, sixty feet six inches long, where for nine innings he was nobody's bottle boy and everybody's ace.

He did not know she was there. She had not meant to be. She had come down from Stanford with the set — with Florence and the whole cardinal-red crowd, to a ballgame she had no intention of watching, the way one goes where one's set goes — and she had been laughing at something when the Santa Clara pitcher came out to warm, and the laugh had died in her throat, because she knew that motion. She had watched it clear six iron bottles off a shelf on a summer pier two years and a lifetime before. She sat very still in her wrong-colored seats and watched the bottle boy strike out the flower of Stanford one after another, and felt the old lurch under her ribs, and could not, for the life of her, look away.

And then, late in the game, his turn came round to face the one man at the plate she knew best in the world. Chuck Ashby dug in with an *S* on his chest and tapped the corners as though he had bought them, and called it down the line to his bench, loud enough for the red seats and for the brown boy on the mound: "The bottle boy. Three balls a nickel, fellas." And the Stanford side laughed, the easy laugh of people who have never had to wonder whether the laughter is about them.

Cirilo looked in. And there, half-risen from her seat in the sea of red, a kid-leather glove forgotten in her lap, was Rebecca. Their eyes met across two years and the whole width of everything that stood between them. He set. The first pitch was past Ashby before the laugh had finished — strike one, hard and in, where the fear lived. The second was the dying ball; it came in waist-high and full of promise and fell off the table at the last instant, and Ashby's knees buckled and the Stanford bench went silent. Strike two. He held the ball a moment — the stitched leather, an old friend — and looked once more, against every rule he had ever set himself, at the woman in the wrong-colored seats; and then he threw the best thing he owned.

Strike three. Ashby swung at air and nearly screwed himself into the dirt.

The Santa Clara side came up off the boards roaring, but Cirilo did not hear it, because Rebecca was on her feet — alone, in a sea of cardinal red, clapping for the wrong team and the wrong boy in front of every person she had been raised among, and not caring who saw. *Costs a nickel,* he said to no one, under his breath. And up in the stands a woman who had everything to lose looked down at a man who had nothing, and chose, in public, out loud, and for good.

### Chapter — An Hour Up the Line

After that spring afternoon, Stanford was an hour up the line in a way it had not been before — an hour with her at the end of it — and for the rest of his years at the college Cirilo Méndez crossed that hour the way other men cross themselves: furtively, devoutly, and at the peril of his soul as the world around him reckoned souls.

They worked out a geography of the forbidden. There was a grove of old redwoods on the back edge of the Stanford lands, out past where the gentlemen rode — a remnant stand the loggers had somehow missed, a few ancient trees keeping their own fog and their own dark under them at the foot of the hills — where a Santa Clara boy who came up on the milk train and walked the last two miles in the dark could meet a Stanford girl who had told her housemother she was at her devotions — and in a sense was. They had perhaps two hours, once a fortnight, when his games and her chaperones and the train schedule and the moon all conspired to allow it, and they spent those two hours talking, mostly — which is the thing no one believes about the young and the forbidden: that what they were starved for, even more than the other, was simply to be *known* by one other person in a world that had agreed to know them only as types.

She told him about her mother, the careless fever, the long conversion into an ornament. He told her — slowly, across many fortnights, because he had never told a living soul — about the rancho, and the courthouse, and his father's silence, and the boy named Toussaint his father had watched die from a culvert, which he had pieced together over the years out of things half-said and never finished. He told her things in that grove he would not tell her again until they had been married a decade, and some he never told her again at all. And Rebecca, who had been raised among people who used candor as a currency and conversation as a kind of fencing, discovered in the redwood dark the disorienting experience of being told the truth by someone who wanted nothing from the telling except to set it down, once, in front of a person who would not use it. It was, she understood much later, the actual beginning of the marriage — not the wedding, years off, in the half-empty church, but those stolen fortnights in the trees, where two people each profoundly alone in their separate prisons taught each other the slow foreign language of not being.

They were nearly caught twice. Once a pair of riders passed close enough that Cirilo pressed her back against a redwood with his hand over her own mouth and his heart going like a fist on a door, and they stood like that, breathing each other's breath, until the hooves faded — and afterward neither could speak, because they had both felt the same thing in the same instant, which was not fear exactly but the cold clear arithmetic of what discovery would cost. And it was not the same arithmetic for the two of them, and they both knew it. If they were caught, Rebecca faced disgrace, a ruined season, a swift correcting marriage to someone like Chuck. Cirilo faced worse, and they both knew that too. They never once spoke of the difference. It sat between them in the dark like a third person, and it was, in the end, the truest measure both of what she was risking and of what he was — and of the fact that she climbed up to the grove anyway, every fortnight, and so did he.

### Chapter — The Cliff House

Once, and only once, in all those guarded fortnights, they did a reckless thing: they stole a whole day, and spent it in the one place on the whole coast big enough to swallow two people who were not allowed to be a pair — San Francisco, the gray indifferent City, where nobody knew either of their faces and nobody, in all that crowd, could have cared.

She told her housemother she was calling on an aunt; he had no game that Saturday and a borrowed two dollars; and they met not in the dark of the grove but in the broad gray morning under the great clock of the Ferry Building, among a thousand strangers pouring off the boats. That was the gift the City gave them, the gift no other ground ever had: they were nobody's scandal there. They were two more young people among the multitude, and they could walk side by side down Market Street in the open daylight, and did, dizzy with the plainness of it.

They rode the cars all the way out — past the last houses and the blowing sand lots and into Golden Gate Park, green and impossible where there had been only dunes a generation before, past the glass Conservatory and the great windmill turning at the western edge — and came out at the end of the land, at the ocean, where the Cliff House stood up on its rock like a wedding cake: eight stories of white Victorian gingerbread hung out over the surf, the gulls wheeling, the sea lions barking down on Seal Rocks. They had clam chowder they could not really afford at a little counter with the spray on the glass. They went down into Sutro's vast glass palace and watched the swimmers in the great green tanks under the iron-and-glass roof, the whole improbable monument a rich man had built to the plain idea that ordinary people deserved somewhere grand to be. They walked the long flat reach of Ocean Beach with their shoes in their hands and the Pacific coming in gray and cold and endless, and the wind took her hat, and Cirilo ran it down the wet sand like an outfielder under a fly and brought it back to her, and she laughed the laugh — the real one, the surprised one — out loud, with no one on the whole shining shore to tell her not to.

And the wonder of it, the thing they both understood without saying, was how *ordinary* it all was, and how they had never once been let near the ordinary. Other couples did this every Sunday and thought nothing of it — walked, ate, laughed, sat close on a streetcar with a girl's head tipped onto a boy's shoulder. For them it was contraband. They had had the dark under the pier and the dark of the grove, the furtive and the stolen and the afraid; and now, for one gray ordinary glorious day, they had the daylight, the boring and beautiful daylight, and were doing nothing on God's earth that anyone anywhere should ever have had to hide. It was, Rebecca thought, the most romantic day of her life precisely because there was nothing secret left in it at all.

They rode the last ferry back across the bay in the dark, the City's lights coming up behind them and the water black and wide, standing at the rail not quite touching, because the boat had thinned and a face or two might now be a face that knew them. The foghorns started up out on the bay, calling and answering across the dark the way they had called over the whole of his childhood on the other shore. He did not say the soft thing; he never could; but he stood close enough that the cold did not reach her, and she took it for what it was. They had stolen one whole day. The world was welcome to the rest of them. And they carried that gray ordinary San Francisco Saturday between them, both of them, intact, for the rest of their lives — the boring, beautiful, unhidden daylight they had been allowed exactly once and never forgot; so that on the hard days afterward, the cold-room days, the days the world was back at its old work of keeping them apart and keeping them down, one or the other of them had only to say *Ocean Beach,* and the whole gray glorious ordinary day came back entire, and held.

### Chapter — The Break

Her father gave her a choice the way men like her father give choices, which is to say he did not give her one at all.

It was a drawing room on Nob Hill, gaslight on good furniture, and Horace Whitfield stood at the mantel and addressed his daughter as though she were a board that had voted unwisely. A summer's foolishness, he said. A beach attendant. He had let it run because these things burn out. It had not burned out, and so now he would be plain: she would not see the Mexican again. Charley — Chuck, waiting by the window with the wounded patience of a man who believes he is owed something — had been a gentleman about all of it. More than she deserved.

"He throws a baseball, Rebecca," Chuck said softly, the knife slid in gently. "That's the whole of him. A strong arm and a borrowed suit."

"And what's the whole of you, Chuck?" she said, almost kindly. "Your father's name, your father's money, your father's chair at the club." A beat. "At least his arm is his."

Her father read her the terms like a sentence: walk out the door to the Mexican and she walked out of the family. No allowance, no name, no coming back when the romance of being poor wore thin — and it would wear thin, faster than she could dream. Rebecca looked around the warm, lit, comfortable cage of the room she had grown up in, and understood that she was being offered everything, and that everything was less than a man with one borrowed suit had already given her on a pier when he looked at her like a person and not an arrangement.

What she could not see, in her fury — and would understand only years later, when her own son was born into nothing — was that her father was not, in that moment, cruel so much as *terrified.* Horace Whitfield was not old money. He had come to San Francisco at seventeen with a dead father's debts and a cardboard valise, and clawed his way up through ship chandlery and railroad freight and a great deal he never afterward discussed, and the respectability he had bought with thirty years of that clawing was, to him, not a luxury but a seawall — the one thin thing standing between his family and the roaring cold water he had only just escaped. His daughter marrying a tannery hand, a Mexican, a *nobody,* was not breaking his heart; it was breaching the wall, and he could feel the water coming through it already. He was a frightened man bailing, and frightened men bailing will throw anything at all over the side, even a daughter, to keep the boat one more hour above the line. He never put it to himself that way. He told himself, that night and ever after, that he was protecting her. The most dangerous men of that time and that place were, nearly every one of them, protecting someone.

She walked to the door. She turned back once — to Chuck, because her father had gone somewhere past reaching and Chuck could still be wounded: "Find someone who wants the chair at the club. They're easy to find. They're the only people you've ever met." And then she went — down the wide curving stair she had come down a thousand times to a thousand dinners, past the long pier glass that had checked her appearance ten thousand mornings, and she did not check it now, and she did not look back; out the heavy door and down off the hill with a single valise and nothing of the house's in it, into the life her father had promised her would be a ruin. It would be fourteen years before she climbed that stair again.

She paid for it in full and on time. The allowance stopped. The name closed over her like water. The friends she had grown up beside learned, all at once, not to see her on the street. And the heiress who had never made a bed or counted a dollar twice went to live in the margins of a country that had decided she no longer existed — and discovered, to the genuine astonishment of everyone who had warned her, that she had never once been happy until she did.

### Chapter — The Game

Underneath everything, it was always baseball. It had been baseball since the river stone and the tin can, since the overturned bucket behind the backstop, and it would be baseball at the grave; the game ran under all of it — the fog, the leather, the long dispossession — never truly absent, because the game was the shape it all took. And there is a reason the shape was baseball, and the reason is the truest thing there is to say about America.

Baseball is the only game built on failure. A great hitter fails seven times in ten and is called great for it. The season is a hundred and fifty games long and mostly made of losing; you cannot win it in a night or carry it on one afternoon's heroics; it rewards not the sprinter but the man who can fail all summer and keep his hands quiet and come back tomorrow with the same face. It is a pastoral game — a green field, open space, country distances, and no clock anywhere on it — set down in the middle of the most industrial, time-driven, whistle-blown century the world had yet built; and the country fell in love with it precisely because it was the thing the country felt itself losing: a green, slow, forgiving space where the clock did not run and a man got another at-bat tomorrow no matter how badly today had gone. The boy dreams it. The grown man grinds it. The old man, at the end, finally understands what it had been quietly telling him the whole time.

And here is why it is the perfect vessel for this particular life — this *siempre segundo:* because baseball, alone among the games, is honest about how much of any life is spent second. You lose most days. You finish behind most years. The long season is an apprenticeship in not-winning, in falling short, in the daily ordinary dignity of the runner-up — and then once, if you are very good and very lucky and you have kept working through all the second-place summers without quitting, you are first, for one October, and it is everything, and it is brief, and it does not erase the years of coming up short; it *crowns* them. That is the game. That is also the man. That is also the country he came up out of — striving, second, and told to be grateful for the standing. The diamond did not merely host Cirilo's life. It explained it. It was the one place in America that took the exact shape of his soul and handed it back to him with rules and a scoreboard, where the lying was legal and the failing was honorable and the long second-place grind was the entire point — and the one bright first-place morning, when at last it came, was worth dying for, which, in the literal event, it turned out to be.

Don Aurelio knew it on the bucket. Cirilo knew it on the mound. And it would be known again one day, down the same blood, by hands not yet born — one family, generation after generation, reading the same green book, the book that says: *you will be second most of your life; keep your hands quiet; come back tomorrow; the season is long; and once, just once, if you refuse to quit, you will be first, and it will be the finest and the most terrible thing that ever happens to you. Play anyway. Siempre. Play the next pitch.*

### Chapter — The Bottom of the Ladder

To get to the top you start at the bottom, and the bottom of professional baseball in those years was a circuit of valley towns baking under a white sky. Cirilo Méndez went out into it the only way a kid like him could: at the very floor, a rung at a time, and never one rung faster than the country could stand him.

He learned the trade by failing at it in front of nobody. Class D, and then C — cinder infields that took the skin off a slide, wooden grandstands leaning in the heat, a few dollars a month, four men to a hotel bed, the milk-run trains that stopped at every water tank between Stockton and nowhere. He learned the smell of that life so deeply he would know it in his sleep for the rest of it: wintergreen liniment and cut alfalfa, coal smoke and tobacco spit and the wet-dog reek of flannels that never fully dried. And he learned the first hard law of the business — that he did not own his own arm. Under the reserve clause the club held his contract for as long as it pleased; it could sell him, bury him, or cut him loose into nothing. *You are leather,* the old man told him, not unkindly. *They will buy you and sell you and never once ask.*

His first battery-mate in the Class D bushes was a ruin of a man named Deacon Mulqueen, a catcher gone to seed at thirty-eight, with a gut and a thirst and a left hand missing the top of one finger to a foul tip in a town whose name he could no longer reliably produce. Mulqueen had had three weeks in the big leagues in 1894 — three weeks, eleven at-bats, one ringing double off a pitcher whose name he could recite in his sleep though he had long since forgotten his own mother's birthday — and he had spent the fourteen years since drinking those three weeks slowly, sip by sip, the way a miser spends a fortune he knows he will never replace. He taught Cirilo everything the abuelo's catechism had left out: which boardinghouses would take a brown ballplayer and which would set the dog on him; how to sleep sitting up on a night train and step off it able to throw; how to ice an arm with creek water and a flour sack; how to eat when the meal money ran dry on a Tuesday and payday was Friday. He was self-pitying and unreliable and frequently drunk and he wept easily, and he was, in that first hard year, the truest friend Cirilo had — and when he died, in a charity ward in Sacramento, of the thing that kills men who have decided to die, Cirilo was the only ballplayer who came, and he came a hundred miles to do it, and he understood, standing at the cheap grave, that the game he loved was also a machine for producing exactly this, and that loving it did not require him to pretend otherwise.

When the climb let him home, it was to the Beachcombers, his county's real pro club, playing the ground behind the Leibbrandt bath houses in Beach Flats, so close to the sand that the brine and the boardwalk barkers came over the fence with the gulls. His brothers Luis and Abel played beside him. And on the bench, in street clothes, sat Edward Purse — good enough to start for any nine in the county, kept off the grass and called the team's *mascot* because the color line would not let a Black man play, his heart breaking by inches in plain view every Sunday. Cirilo watched Purse and understood the thing no one ever said to his face: that there were lines on this country no glove could carry a man across, and that he himself stood with one foot on one of them, let onto the field only so long as the papers could keep calling him Spanish.

There was a Sunday — Cirilo carried it the rest of his life — when he had thrown a two-hit shutout and the flats crowd was chanting his name, and he came off the grass high on it and found Purse at the end of the bench not looking at him, and said, foolishly, something about how Purse would have caught that last man easy.
