And Purse turned and looked at him with a face like a shut door and said, very quietly, *Don't.* Just that. *Don't you stand there in your spikes — that they let you wear — and hand me your pity in my street clothes.* And then, a moment later, because he was a generous man and not a small one, and because the look on Cirilo's face was the look of a boy shown a wall he had not known he was standing on: *It's not your doing, kid. You didn't draw the line. But don't ask me to be glad about exactly where they drew it.* They were friends after that — real friends — but it was a friendship with a wall down the middle of it that neither of them had built and neither could take down, and the honesty of leaving the wall standing, of neither pretending it away nor blaming the other for it, was the truest thing in it.

And through all of it, behind every backstop, on an overturned bucket, sat Don Aurelio, who had watched the game in California since men played it bare-handed on Gold Rush sandlots, and who doled it out like a second catechism. *Read the feet before the swing, mijo. The deep stander is buying time; bust him up and in where his fear can't reach. Watch the hands on the bat — white knuckles is a frightened man, give him the slow one and let his own greed swing for him. Never show his eyes the same height twice.* And this — he would spit in his palm and work the ball — *this, while they still let you. It drops off the table like it died in the air, and they'll outlaw it in a few years, so learn it now and break their hearts with it while you can. Everything they give us has a clock on it, mijo. Throw fast.*

### Chapter — The Road

There was a town in the valley — Cirilo never afterward said which, because by the end there had been too many to keep straight, and because it was every town — where the only café open after the night game would not serve him.

It was past eleven, and the club had come in dead on its feet off a doubleheader, and the café by the depot had a lamp lit and a man behind the counter; and the ballplayers filed in out of a heat that did not break even at night that time of year in that country, and the counterman looked at Cirilo once — the way they looked, the quick flat appraisal that took in the brown and rendered its verdict before the eyes had finished moving — and said, not unkindly, the way they said it when they meant to be reasonable about it, that he could not serve the Mexican, but the rest of the boys were welcome. And the boys, who were nineteen and twenty and starving and not bad fellows, did the thing that nineteen-and-twenty-year-old starving not-bad fellows do, which is the worst thing, which is nothing: they shuffled, and studied their feet, and one or two of them muttered, and not one of them walked out — because they were hungry, and a man has to eat, and it was not, after all, *their* fight, and tomorrow there was another game.

Deacon Mulqueen was forty and fat and finished and three weeks of glory behind him forever, and he stood in the doorway of that café and looked at the counterman, and then at the boys finding their seats, and then at Cirilo standing alone in the heat outside the screen door, and he said, in the carrying voice of a man who had spent his life behind a plate making himself heard over a crowd: *Well, then. I guess none of us are hungry.* And he did not move, and he did not stop looking at the boys, one by one, until — shamed, sullen, grumbling, nineteen years old and learning something they had not come in to learn — they got back up, every one of them, and filed back out into the night; and the whole club walked a mile and a half to the only other place with a light on, a Chinese place by the tracks run by a man named Lim who had his own long acquaintance with the quick flat verdict, and who fed all of them, the brown one included, without a word, at a back table, for cash, at midnight. Mulqueen got drunk that night, drunker than usual, and wept, as he often did, and said something maudlin and self-pitying that Cirilo did not trouble to remember. But he remembered the doorway. He remembered the carrying voice. *I guess none of us are hungry.* It was the first time in his life, outside his own family, that anyone had stood in a doorway for him — and it had been a ruined drunk with a missing fingertip and nothing left to lose by it, who did it anyway. And Cirilo understood, walking the mile and a half to Lim's through the valley dark, that this too was the game: not the diamond, but this — the long road and the closed doors and the rare, unlooked-for, unreasonable man who stands in the doorway — and that he would spend the rest of his life trying to be, for someone, the thing Deacon Mulqueen had been for him that night, and would not always manage it, and would never stop trying.

### Chapter — The Powder Works

Up the San Lorenzo, in one of the loveliest folds of the redwood canyon, in the green hush where the creek ran cold and the big trees combed the fog, men manufactured the means of blowing the world apart; and the boy never could hold the two things in his head at once — the beauty of the place and the business of it — which was, his grandfather said, exactly the point, and exactly the country.

The California Powder Works made black powder, and then the new and far more terrible dynamite, in a scatter of buildings spaced carefully apart along the creek — spaced apart because they blew up. Not if; when. The work was making the stuff that broke the mountains open for the mines and the railroads and the quarries, the violence that built the modern world, and it was done by the men such work is always done by — Chinese laborers above all, and immigrants of every poor stripe — in a canyon so beautiful it could break your heart, beside a creek full of steelhead, under trees a thousand years old, and every so often a mill would go up with a sound they heard in town and felt in their teeth, and there would be a funeral, or there would be nothing to bury, and the powder works would hire more men by the end of the week, because there were always more men, that was the one thing the country could be relied upon to provide.

Cirilo walked up there once as a boy and stood looking at it, the neat deadly buildings in the glorious canyon, the cold creek going by indifferent, the warning flags, and felt the particular vertigo that was becoming the steady condition of his understanding: that the same hands and the same canyon and the same country could hold, with no apparent difficulty, the redwood and the dynamite, the steelhead and the blast, the fog-soft beauty and the spaced-apart buildings full of death; that there was no contradiction the country could not absorb so long as the absorbing paid; that loveliness was no protection against use, and never had been — the otters had been beautiful, the trees were beautiful, the men singing on the fishing fleet were beautiful, his own gift was a beautiful thing — and beauty bought you exactly nothing, the canyon would be lovely and the mill would blow and the creek would run cold over the stones either way, while up the slope the redwoods stood and drank the fog and declined, with their whole two thousand years, to have an opinion about any of it.

### Chapter — The Lime Kilns

Above the town, back in the canyons where the redwoods were, Henry Cowell turned mountains into money; and the men who did the turning coughed their lives out in white dust and were replaced; and Cirilo worked a season among them and never forgot the particular hell of it — a hell with no villain in it, only a price.

The lime came out of the hills as rock, and to make it into the lime that built San Francisco it had to be burned, and to burn it you needed fire, day and night, for days at a stretch, and to feed the fire you needed wood — and so the great redwoods, trees that had stood since before the Mission, since before the coast people, some of them, a thousand years and two thousand, the oldest living things any of those men would ever touch, came down by the hundred and went into the kilns as cordwood, to cook rock, to make the lime that raised the banks and hotels of a city that would never think of the canyon it had eaten. Cirilo saw one of them felled, once, up where the choppers worked — a tree that had been a sapling when Christ was still a rumor, brought down in a single working day by two men with a saw and a wedge, standing a moment after the last cut as if considering, and then coming down through its smaller neighbors with a sound he felt in his chest from a quarter mile off, to lie there and be bucked into stove-lengths before the sap had stopped running. There was a lesson in it the old man did not even have to point out: that the country burned its oldest and grandest things to make its cheapest and most useful ones, and called it progress, and was not wrong that it was useful, and was not innocent that it was a burning. It never knew what it had, that country; could not see the worth of a thing until the thing was already on the ground, bucked into logs, and by then had taught itself, very thoroughly, not to look. Cirilo fed those kilns one summer between ball seasons when the meal money ran short, and breathed the dust that hung in the canyon and never settled — the dust that got into Nunzio Sabatini's chest that same summer and never came out — and he understood why the men who worked the kilns were mostly Italians and Portuguese and Mexicans and Chinese: men the country had decided were the kind you *spend,* the way you spend cordwood, to make something worth more than themselves.

He worked beside a kiln-man named Sciaroni, a Genovese with a wife and a row of children in a company shack, who had the cough already and knew what it was and what it meant and went up the canyon every dawn anyway, because the alternative was that the children did not eat — and a man chooses his children over his own lungs every single time, which is not heroism, the old man said, but is simply what a father is, and which the country relied upon, absolutely, the way it relied on the tide: that there would always be a Sciaroni who would trade his lungs for his children's bread, and that the trade could therefore be priced cheap, because the seller was never in any position to refuse it. Sciaroni died the next winter, the way kiln-men died, drowning slowly in the open air; and the kiln did not pause; and another Genovese with another row of children took his place at the same fire for the same wage; and the lime went down the mountain and built another wall of a city that did not know his name and would not have spelled it right if it had. Cirilo carried Sciaroni the way he carried Purse and Mulqueen and all of them — not as a martyr, because the man would have spat at the word, but as a fact, an entry in the true ledger he kept against the false one the country published: that the grand things are built of spent men, that the spending is arranged so the spent cannot say no, and that the only honest thing a man who got out of the canyon could do was refuse, ever after, to pretend he did not know what the white dust on the city's banks was actually made of.

### Chapter — Nunzio

Nunzio Sabatini drove pilings beside Cirilo the summer the boardwalk went up, and sang while he did it, and told Cirilo the truest thing anyone told him that year — that a man like them could have anything in this country so long as he did not mind being hated for it — and then Nunzio went on and lived the whole of that sentence, cheerfully, to the end.

He never got out the way Cirilo nearly did. There was no arm in Nunzio, no gift the country would pay to watch; there was only a strong back and a good voice and an enormous uncomplicated heart, and he spent all of it where men like him spent themselves — at Cowell's lime kilns until the heat and the dust finished his lungs for finer work, then on the Monterey wharf hauling sardines, then, when his back went, at a fruit stand he and his wife ran on a corner of the flats, selling other men's harvests at a markup of pennies. He married a girl named Concetta off the same hill his people came from, and filled a small house with children — seven who lived, two who did not, the ordinary arithmetic of the poor in those years — and he was, by every measure the world keeps, a failure and a nobody, a wop who burned his lungs out for wages and died owing the grocer; and he was, by every measure that is real, one of the happiest and most successful men on that whole coast, because he had wanted exactly what he had, and loved it without reservation, and never mistaken the country's contempt for the truth about himself.

He followed Cirilo's whole career like a man following a saint. He could not always afford the trolley to the games and went anyway; he could not read the Eastern box scores and had his eldest read them to him at the fruit stand, and wept at the good ones, openly, among the oranges — an old man crying over a line of agate type about a friend three thousand miles away. He believed in that arm the way he believed in the saints — without evidence, past all reason, and to the last — and he said to Concetta, more than once, across the years, the thing that was the whole faith of the flats distilled into one cheerful exhausted man: *He's one of ours, Cetta. Whatever they let him be and whatever they don't, whatever finally comes of it — he is one of ours, and we do not forget our own. As long as there's one of us left on this coast to remember him, that boy is not gone.* And he meant it the way the flats meant everything, fiercely and well in advance of the proof — a truer monument than anything anyone would ever think to carve, and it cost nothing, and it would last, in the flats, in the mouths of Nunzio Sabatini's seven children and their children, longer than any bronze the rich men buy.

### Chapter — Lim

The café by the depot that fed the whole ball club at midnight — the brown one included, when the white café down the block would not — was run by a man named Lim, and Lim deserves his own chapter, because Lim's people had been handed the cruelest verdict the country ever put in writing, and Lim answered it, every night, with a hot plate and no questions, which is the only answer to cruelty that has ever once worked.

The Chinese had built the hard parts — the railroad's killing grades, the levees, the reclaimed delta land — and the country had thanked them, in 1882, with the Chinese Exclusion Act: the one and only time the United States ever barred an entire people by name, slammed the door and locked it and threw the bolt, so that men like Lim were marooned, unable to bring wives or families, unable to become citizens, a bachelor society of the deliberately stranded, allowed to stay and work and never, ever, to belong. They were pushed out of the mines and the good trades into the narrow channels left open to the barred — the laundries, the kitchens, the cafés — and they made those narrow channels pay by working harder than any free man would, and were hated for the working the way the otters were hunted for the fur, for being too good at the only thing they were permitted to do. Lim ran his café eighteen hours a day. He had a wife he had not seen in twenty years on the far side of an ocean and a law. He knew the quick flat appraising verdict that took in the yellow and rendered judgment before the eyes finished moving — knew it more totally than even Cirilo did, because the law that hated the Chinese was written down, signed, official, where the law that hated the Mexican was only everywhere.

And so, when the ball club came in dead off a doubleheader and the depot café turned the brown man away, it was Lim who fed them all, every one, at a back table, for cash, at midnight, without a word and without a flicker — not from sentiment, which he could not afford, but from the deep clear solidarity of one barred man for another, the recognition across the back table of someone else the country had sorted out. He had been burned out once already, in San Francisco, in '06 — had watched the ash of his whole Chinatown world while the city fathers debated relocating the survivors off the valuable ground — and had come down the coast and started again, because starting again was the only verb the barred were left, the same as the flats rebuilding in the floodplain, the same as the family on the hill. Cirilo never forgot Lim. On the long road through the valley dark to the only lit door that would have him, he understood that he and Lim were the same man in two different skins, sorted by two different prejudices into the same back table — and that the back table, the one warm lit place that asked no questions, run by someone the country had also thrown away, was the truest dining room in America, and the only one he was ever fully welcome in, and that he would carry the debt of it the rest of his life, and pay it forward where he could, in the currency Lim had taught him: the hot plate, no questions, for whoever the front room would not seat.

### Chapter — The Sardine Fleet

The bay fed people. The people it fed were mostly Italians. And the fleet that did the feeding went out at night, so that the boy's whole boyhood had, underneath the smell of the tannery and the lime, a second smell that came up off the water at dawn: fish, and salt, and tar, and the particular green reek of a good catch — the smell of the bay being generous.

They came from Genoa and from Sicily, the fishermen, Nunzio's people, and at first they fished the old way, in lateen-rigged feluccas with the one triangular sail, slipping out past the wharf in the dark and setting the lampara nets in a great encircling sweep around the silver schools the moon brought up — sardines and anchovies in their millions, the bay boiling with them some nights, the nets coming up so heavy with silver that the men sang hauling them, sang in dialect across the black water, the way Nunzio sang driving pilings, because there is a kind of poor man who answers hard work with song and that coast was full of them. The catch came in at dawn to the wharf and the canneries, and there the generosity stopped: for the sea gave the fish freely and the cannery bought them cheap and sold them dear, and the fishermen who took the actual risk on the actual black water for the actual silver got the smallest cut of it, the way the men in the dirt always got the smallest cut of what the dirt produced — the same arithmetic as the kilns, the same as the fields, the same as the tannery, only wetter.

But the bay itself was honest, and the work was beautiful, and the boy never forgot the sight of the fleet coming home in the gray dawn light low in the water with the night's catch, the gulls in a screaming cloud behind each boat, the men standing easy at the tillers, tired and singing — a thing of such plain hard grace that it made him understand, young, the difference between the work and the wage; between what the fishermen *did,* which was magnificent, and what they were *paid,* which was an insult; and that the country had a genius for arranging that the most magnificent labor be done by the people it valued least, and for keeping the two facts — the magnificence and the contempt — carefully in separate rooms so that no one had to look at them together. He looked at them together. He had been taught to, on the hill, in the yard, up the canyons. And he saw in the singing Italian fleet the same thing he saw everywhere on that coast: people doing something far finer than the price the world had put on them, and knowing it, and singing anyway, across the dark water, going out for the silver the moon brought up.

### Chapter — Saturday Night

Six days the flats belonged to the kilns and the canneries and the tannery. Saturday night the flats belonged to itself — and that, not Sunday's ballgame and not even Sunday's Mass, was when the neighborhood remembered what it was alive for.

It started in the kitchens. The women had been at it since afternoon, and the smell came out the open doors and down the dirt lanes and pulled the whole block together the way the fog pulled the trees — beans and chiles and the good lard smell of tortillas off the comal, and from the Sabatini place next door the garlic and tomato of Concetta's gravy, and from the Portuguese end of the lane the linguiça spitting in a pan: three countries' suppers cooking within smelling distance of one another, and nobody pretending, on a Saturday night, that they were not all the same hungry. And then somebody's guitar — there was always a guitar. Nunzio could not read a note and could play anything, and he would settle on a crate in the lane with the instrument against his big chest and start something low, and old Cipriano would find his fiddle, and inside the half hour the lane was a party no one had called and no one could have stopped.

They danced in the dirt. The old men sat against the wall and argued, with total conviction and no information, about baseball and the price of fish and which of them had been the greater terror with the ladies forty years and one ocean ago — *you, Beppe? you couldn't have caught a girl standing still* — while the young ones spun in the lantern light and the littlest children ran underfoot drunk on being up past bedtime, and the food went hand to hand down the block with no accounting of whose pot it had left. Abel, a ruin six days out of seven, was on Saturday night the most alive man in the county; he danced the way he fielded, all careless grace, and the girls knew it and he knew they knew it, and for a few hours the gift was only a joy and not yet a wound. Luis ate three plates and laughed with his whole body. And the boy Cirilo sat on the step taking it in with his round gold watching eye, handed a tortilla folded warm around beans by a mother who would not, on this one night of the seven, tell him it was time for bed — and was, though it would take him thirty years and three thousand miles to know it, as rich as he would ever be.

Nobody on that lane owned a thing the county counted. They owned this. And this — the garlic and the lard and the guitar, the arguing old men and the spinning girls and the children up too late, the three poor suppers shared down one dirt block in the lantern light — was not the thin consolation of people who had nothing else. It was the thing itself. It was what the kilns and the canneries and the long week were, if any of them had stopped to think it, *for.* On Sunday they would line the foul lines and watch one of their own throw a baseball; on Monday the whistle would blow and take them all back. But Saturday night was Saturday night. The lane sang. The boy watched. And the fog came down over all of it the same as it came down over the bluff, and kept, for that one night, no accounts at all.

### Chapter — The Flats Boys

The Beachcombers played Sundays at the ground behind the Leibbrandt bath houses, and on Sunday the whole flats came — and for a few hours on the one day the kilns and the canneries and the tannery let their people go, the poorest neighborhood in the county got to be, out loud, in the sun, the thing it never got to be the other six days: proud, whole, and joyful.

They came on foot, all of them, the Mexican and Italian and Portuguese and Chinese families who would not have been welcome together anywhere else in that town, and they spread along the foul lines and up the little rise, the old men with their memories of sandlot ball and their loud certain opinions, the mothers with the food, the courting couples pretending to watch the game, and the boys — always the boys, a swarm of them, ragged and barefoot and electric, chasing the foul balls for the nickel a returned ball earned and worshipping, with the total uncritical worship that only a poor boy has to give, the local nine. They knew every Beachcomber's stance and stat and story. They argued them like scripture. And when Cirilo Méndez took the mound — the flats' own, one of theirs, a tannery kid throwing a ball past men from clubs that would not have let him in the front gate — the boys lost their minds, and the men stood, and for nine innings the flats was not a place the county used and forgot but a place with a *champion,* a name, a Sunday king of its own.

There was a boy named Chito Soto, a cousin off the next lot, eight years old and made entirely of want, who appointed himself Cirilo's shadow that whole last flats summer — fetched his water, guarded his glove, threw a rag ball against the tannery wall for hours in a windup that was a worshipful clumsy copy of Cirilo's own — and Cirilo, who remembered being exactly that boy in exactly that posture watching Abel, gave him what the old men had given him: the time, the catechism, *read the feet, mijo, never the same height twice.* He knew what most of these boys did not yet know and he would not be the one to tell them: that the kilns were waiting, and the fields, and that the gift, in this county, drew the danger; that most of the swarm chasing foul balls in the gold evening light would spend their lives spent, like their fathers, for someone else's distant want. The dream was mostly going to lose. He knew that. And he taught Chito Soto the change-up anyway, in the long Sunday light, because the dream losing did not make it not worth dreaming — because for one afternoon a week the flats had a king and the king had been a barefoot boy here once too, and that relay, that handing-forward of *you could be the one,* was the truest wealth the neighborhood owned, and it cost nothing, and the county could not tax it or tan it or take it, and the boys carried it home in the dark, singing, every Sunday, all that long gold summer.

### Chapter — A Sunday at Beach Flats

There is no understanding the man Cirilo became without a Sunday at Beach Flats, because it was there, on a diamond behind the bathhouses with the surf for an outfield wall, that he learned what the game could be when it was allowed to be only itself.

The Beachcombers played their home dates on a scrappy field the Daubenbiss brothers — Ed and Charley, of the old Soquel pioneer stock, who ran the club on civic pride and a shoestring — had talked the town into letting them keep, hard by the Leibbrandt baths, so close to the water that a long foul into right rolled down onto the sand among the bathers, and a boy in a striped costume would fetch it while the game waited, good-natured, for him to. Bill Devereaux managed — "Red Dog," a barrel of an Oakland man who chewed and hollered and loved his ballplayers like a hard father — and on a good Sunday a thousand people came, which in that town was a multitude: the merchants in their straw boaters; the young ladies in their summer best, the romances of the season conducted in the stands as openly as the game on the field; the flats families down the right-field line; the whole loud democratic carnival of an American town at play. The boardwalk organ came over the fence. Somebody always sold tamales. And when the Beachcombers beat the City club a local rhymester would have a verse in the Monday paper — *Who killed the Bushnells? We, the Beachcombers — three-baggers and homers* — and the town would say it to one another on the street for a week, delighted with itself.

Cirilo pitched, and his brothers played behind him — Luis steady at first, Abel brilliant and unreliable in center, a whole inning sometimes a thing of careless beauty and the next a thing he had plainly poured out of a bottle the night before — and for nine innings on a Sunday the Méndez family was not a fallen house in a stolen town but simply the local nine, *theirs,* cheered by name. Nunzio came when the kilns let him, with a wife and a swelling brood, and bellowed his obscene Italian encouragement, and wept when they won and wept when they lost, an uncomplicated man in a complicated country. It was as close to belonging as the place ever let any of them come.

And at the end of the bench, as ever, Edward Purse kept the score book in a copperplate hand finer than any banker's; he warmed the pitchers; he knew more baseball than Devereaux and Cirilo and both Daubenbiss brothers put together, and everyone, Purse included, knew it. Once — only once — a visiting club came up a man short, some flu or some fistfight, and for ten minutes in the bright Sunday air it was actually proposed, half in earnest, that Purse fill in, just for the day, just this once; and the whole field went quiet while a thousand people in their Sunday best decided, without a vote, without a single word, by the simple gravity of what the country was, that it would not happen. And it did not happen. And Purse picked the score book back up and licked his pencil and entered the next batter in his beautiful hand, and his face did not change at all, which was somehow the worst of it. Cirilo, watching from the mound, understood that he was being shown the exact dimensions of his own permission — that the same town now chanting his name would, the day it decided he had become a Mexican again instead of a ballplayer, fall just as silent and just as unanimous, and pick up its own pencil. He threw the rest of that game in a cold fury, a two-hit shutout, and did not enjoy a second of it; and he and Purse rode the trolley home afterward in a silence that was, for once, entirely shared.

### Chapter — The Bluff Girl

In San Francisco no one knew her, and so the city took her in easily. In Santa Cruz everyone knew exactly who she was, and so the flats made her earn it — which she came, in time, to be glad of, because a thing earned among people who owe you nothing is worth more than a thing handed to you by people who were paid to give it.

When the climb brought Cirilo home to pitch for the Beachcombers, it brought Rebecca to the flats; and the flats knew her on sight, or knew her kind — a Whitfield, a bluff girl, summer money, the daughter of exactly the sort of man whose lawyers had emptied the ranchos and bought the canyons and built the bathhouses on the ground their own grandparents had grazed. They did not trust her, and why would they. They had watched bluff girls come slumming on the boardwalk their whole lives, daring for a summer and then gone home up the hill to marry a Chuck Ashby; and here was one who had fastened herself to one of their own, and the flats waited, with the long patience of the poor, for her to prove the thing they expected, which was that she would not last.

She lasted. That was the whole of her argument, and she could only make it the slow way. She came to the Sunday games in a plain dress with the gowns long sold, and sat in the flats' part of the stands and never once drifted toward a better seat; she learned the children's names and did not get them wrong; she carried a dish down to the lane suppers and did not apologize for its being a poor dish; she scrubbed her own floor in a flat the bluff would not have kept a maid in, and the flats women, who missed nothing, saw the new red across her knuckles and revised, by a degree a season, the verdict they had been so certain of. The fishermen's wives and the tannery women came round first — they knew earned hands when they saw them — and then the men, and then the children, who had settled the matter the afternoon she mended a torn kite no one had asked her to touch.

Only Dolores held out, up on her hill, watching the banker's daughter the way she watched everything the country had ever offered her family: for the hook in it, for the day it would turn out to have a price. That wariness would outlast the Beachcombers and the Seals and the better part of a decade, and would not finally give way until a freezing Brooklyn morning years off, over a pot of beans a new wife was ruining with the grim determination of a woman who meant to learn or starve her husband trying. But the flats themselves — the wide poor unforgetting flats that had every reason on earth to shut their door on a Whitfield — opened it, not all the way and not fast, but truly; and Rebecca, who had been adored her whole girlhood by people on the bluff who never once looked into her, discovered that she would far rather be slowly and grudgingly and genuinely taken in by the flats than ever again be easily admired by the hill. The hard belonging was the only kind that was real. She had starved for it her whole life, and she got it at last in the one square of the county her father would sooner have died than let her set foot in.

### Chapter — The Basket

Don Aurelio had said the coast people were mostly in the ground now, with no grandsons left — and he was almost right, which is a different thing from right, and Cirilo learned the difference from an old woman who sold baskets by the wharf.

Her name, the name the priests had given her people, was González; and her name before that, the one she would tell you only if you sat a long time and bought a basket and did not hurry her, was a word in a language perhaps a dozen living mouths could still shape — Awaswas, the tongue of the coast people of that particular bay, going out of the world one funeral at a time. She was very old, older than Don Aurelio, old enough to have been a child when the Mission lands were broken up and her people scattered to the ranchos as laborers — to the Méndez among others, which neither she nor Cirilo ever said aloud, though both of them knew it, and the not-saying-it was its own kind of conversation. She wove baskets the old way, the way her grandmother had taught her in the language no one spoke, baskets so tight they held water, and she sold them to tourists on the wharf as curiosities — quaint things, *Indian baskets,* a dying craft to set on a shelf — and she took their dollars with a face that gave nothing, and went home, and wove another, and taught the weave to one great-niece who half-listened; because that was the whole of the war she had left to fight: one more basket, one more attempt at the great-niece, one more day of not being finished off.

Cirilo bought baskets from her for years. He could not always afford it and did it anyway, the way he could not afford Lim or the old woman and did it anyway, recognizing in her the exact thing his mother did on the hill, the exact thing he himself was doing on the mound under a borrowed name — the refusal, the tending, the *we do not disappear* carried on past all reason by people who had every reason to quit and did not. They never spoke of the rancho. But once, near the end of her life, she looked at him a long time — the brown ballplayer the papers called Spanish, called by a name that was not his own, called everything but what he was — and she said, in English, slowly, the only thing she ever said to him that was not about baskets: *They take the name first. You know this. Your people know this. They take the name, and they give you their name, and they wait for you to forget. My grandmother told me: do not forget, and do not let them see you remember. Smile, and take the dollar, and weave the basket, and teach the child, and do not forget.* And she pressed a small basket into his hands, not for sale — the tightest she had ever woven — and would not take his money for it; and he kept it all his life; and it sat, at the very end, on a windowsill in a cold Brooklyn flat beside a ball glove and a rosary: three small objects, three peoples, three different languages of the same single sentence, which was — *we were here, and we tended what was ours, and you did not erase us, and the proof is that you are reading this at all.*

### Chapter — Abel

If the family had a ghost while it was still living, it was Abel.

He was the oldest brother, and he had been born with the thing Cirilo had to work for — the gift, the raw and careless gift, a body that did what the game asked before the mind had finished asking it. The old men of the flats, the ones who had watched ball since the sandlot days, said it plainly and often, to Abel's face and within Cirilo's hearing, which was its own slow poison: *that one, the oldest Méndez, he's the natural. The little one works. But Abel — Abel was given.* He could hit a ball farther at sixteen than Cirilo ever would; he ran down everything in the outfield; he threw with a long whippy ease that made Cirilo's hard-earned mechanics look like exactly what they were, which was earned. And he drank it all into the ground before he turned thirty, methodically, almost on purpose — and the family told itself for years that the drink had ruined the gift, when the truth, which only Cirilo ever quite let himself see, ran the other way: the gift had ruined Abel, because the gift had drawn the notice, and the notice, for a brown boy in that county, was the thing that got you hurt.

Abel had learned that at fifteen, the way Cirilo learned it later on the pier — except that Abel had not had a girl's laugh or an abuelo's fist or a plan to set against it. He had only the gift and the fear and no language to hold both, and so he did the thing a frightened boy does with a gift that frightens him: he got there first. Before the world could take his arm on the world's terms — before some Chuck Ashby could decide that a Mexican who hit the ball that far had gotten above himself — Abel took it himself, with a bottle, on his own schedule, so that when the ruin came it would at least be a ruin he had chosen. He was, in the family's private theology, the proof of the curse. Cirilo came, late, to understand him as something harder and truer than that: not a man the curse had struck down, but a man who had read the curse correctly and decided, rationally, in his way, to disarm it by failing on purpose — because a brown man who fails hurts only himself, and a brown man who succeeds, out loud, hurts everyone who loves him. It was the most logical thing in the world. It was also a slow suicide. Both things. Always, in this family, both things.

There was one night Cirilo carried the rest of his life. He had come home from a good Beachcombers Sunday, still warm with the crowd, and found Abel on the back step in the dark with a bottle, and he sat down beside him without a word; and after a while Abel said, not looking at him, in a voice with no self-pity in it at all, only a terrible clarity: *You're going to make it, hermano. You've got the thing I never had. Not the arm — I had the arm. The other thing. The thing that lets you take the slap on the pier and not swing. I'd have swung. I'd have swung the first day, and they'd have hung me for it, and that is why God gave the arm to the wrong brother.* And then he laughed, and drank, and would not say another serious word, and was dead inside the decade; and Cirilo paid for the stone, and had carved on it, over the family's objection that it was prideful, the one true thing — ABEL MÉNDEZ · HE WAS GIVEN — because somebody, Cirilo thought, standing at the grave with his own arm still sound and his own road still open before him, ought to say it in stone where the county could read it: that the boy had been given everything, and that being given everything, in that place, in those years, had been the precise thing that killed him.

### Chapter — Luis

And if Abel was the family's ghost, Luis was its spine — which is a harder and less romantic thing to be, and gets no headstone worth the carving.

He was the middle brother, but the load of the eldest came to rest on him anyway, because the eldest could not bear it: Abel, who by birth should have carried them, was busy drinking and brawling his gift into the ground, and so the whole weight of a fallen family slid down past him to Luis, and Luis, from the time he could carry a quarter of beef, carried it, and never set it down. He had been wild once himself — wilder than careful Cirilo ever dared to be, the charmer of the three, the one who went over the bluff fences in the dark after the rich men's daughters — and he put every bit of it down, all at once, the year he understood that no one else in that house was going to carry the family if he did not. He had a gift too — smaller than Abel's, smaller than Cirilo's, but real, a steady reliable line-drive bat and soft hands at first base — and he folded it, along with the wildness and the charm and the bluff-girl summers, without drama and without being asked, into a butcher's apron at the meat market on Pacific Avenue, because somebody had to bring home the thing that was certain while the other two chased the thing that was bright. He married at twenty, had children at twenty-one, and bought, on a butcher's credit, the small ordinary respectability the family had not known since the rancho: a house with a number on it, a pew with their name penciled in the book, a future you could see the shape of. It was the least glorious life any of the three brothers led, and by a wide margin the most useful — and useful, in this world, is the thing nobody writes the song about.

He carried his resentment of Cirilo the way a careful man carries a knife: folded, pocketed, brought out only for work, and never, he told himself, for malice. He loved his brother. He also knew that he, Luis, had been just good enough to wonder, all his life, what might have happened had *he* been the one the family could afford to gamble on — and that no one had ever once asked him to make the sacrifice he had made, which meant no one had ever thanked him for it, which meant it did not, in the family's accounting, quite count as a sacrifice at all; it was just Luis, being Luis, being there. When Cirilo's name began to travel, Luis cut every clipping and pasted them into a ledger book from the shop and told no one; and when a customer at the counter said *say, Méndez, any relation to the ballplayer?* he would say, gruffly, weighing the chops, *my brother* — and feel two things so tangled he could not have separated them with his own boning knife: a pride so fierce it frightened him, and a grief, small and clean and permanent, for the line drive he never got to find out whether he could hit. Both things. He wrapped the chops and made change and went home to the house with the number on it, which his brother — the king of the family, the man whose name the country would one day learn to say — never had, and never would.

### Chapter — Soledad

Luis married Soledad the year after he buried Anastasia, and her name meant *solitude,* and the cruelty and the grace of her whole life was that she knew, going in, that she was marrying a man whose heart was in the ground of someone else — and married him anyway, and built, on that unpromising foundation, one of the few marriages in the whole long story that simply, quietly, worked.

She was a flats girl, plain where Anastasia had been a flame, steady where the bluff girls were bright — the kind of woman the world overlooks precisely because she will never give it the trouble that gets a woman noticed. She had watched the whole Anastasia summer from the edge of it, the way the plain girl always watches the beautiful one, and she had seen Luis broken by it; and she understood, with a clarity that would have undone a more romantic woman, exactly what she was being offered when Luis — sobered, grieving, suddenly intent on the safe useful life — turned to her. She was being offered second place in a man's heart, permanently: the steady wife of a buried wildness. A great many women would have refused it, or taken it and spent a life punishing him for it. Soledad did neither. She looked at the bargain clear-eyed — a good steady man who would never quite be all the way hers — and decided that a real half of a real man, given honestly and worked at daily, was worth more than the whole of some lesser one; and she took it, and never once, in forty years, threw the buried thing in his face.

That was her genius, and it went, like most women's genius in that time and place, entirely unrecorded. She made the house with the number on it a home. She raised the children. She stood between Luis and his resentment of Cirilo and softened it, so that the brothers stayed brothers. She knew the silence in him, the folded-away self, and she did not try to dig it up; she simply built a life warm enough that the silence had somewhere to rest. She was no saint and no martyr — she had her own sharp tongue and her own buried wants and her own private grief at being the steady second choice, and on the hard nights she felt the solitude her name had promised her. But she had chosen, with open eyes, to love a man more than he could quite love her back, and to make of that lopsided thing a marriage; and she turned out to be right that it was worth it, because the daily tending built, across decades, a thing as real as any grand passion and far more durable: a kitchen, a table, a family that held.

And when Anastasia came back at the end — the she-wolf in her furs, to release Luis from the old guilt — Soledad did the last and largest thing of her quiet life: she was glad. She had every reason on earth to dread that beautiful ghost walking back into her husband's shop, and instead she set another place at the table and poured the tea and sat with the two of them while the wound she had lived beside her whole marriage was finally, gently closed — because she had understood, long before either of them, that you cannot make a man whole by guarding his cage, only by loving him free of it; and she had spent forty years loving Luis toward the very peace that Anastasia, in the end, was the one who could deliver. The solitude in her name was real. So was the love. Soledad, plainest of them all, carried both the most gracefully of any of them.

### Chapter — The Black Diamond

There was another pitcher, in those same years, who shared more with Cirilo than a craft — who shared the very name — and whom the country buried so completely that Cirilo knew him only as a rumor carried up from the south: José Méndez, the Cuban, whom they called *El Diamante Negro,* the Black Diamond.

In Havana, in 1908 and 1909, this other Méndez had done the thing that should have ended every argument forever: he pitched against touring major-league clubs — the Cincinnati Reds among them, big-league men down on a winter barnstorm — and beat them, shut them down, made the finest white professionals in the world look like schoolboys, until men who had watched both said plainly that José Méndez was as fine an arm as drew breath anywhere on the earth. And it bought him nothing in the country to the north, because José Méndez was a Black Cuban, and the color line did not bend for genius; it had never bent for genius; not bending for genius was the one thing it had been built to do. He would spend his life in the only leagues that would take him — the Cuban winters, the Negro circuits, the Kansas City Monarchs in their time — magnificent, and barred, and known to the wider world, when at all, as hearsay.

Cirilo heard the name and felt the floor tilt under him, because he understood at once, in his body, the thing the name laid bare: that he and José Méndez were very nearly the same man — the same surname, near enough the same arm, the same gift drawn down on the same wrong side of the same line — and that the entire daylight between them, the whole of it, the difference between Cirilo on a major-league mound and José Méndez shut out of organized ball for life, was a single shade of skin and the lucky lie of a word. The papers had decided to call Cirilo *Spanish.* That was all. That one ruling — Spanish, not Mexican; light enough, framed right — was the hairline crack he had slipped through; and José Méndez, a shade darker and an island farther and impossible to call anything but what he plainly was, had not slipped through it. There was no justice in the difference. There was no logic in it. There was only the line, and which side of its arbitrary, murdering, hair-fine border a man happened to be born on. And Cirilo, who had spent his whole life believing himself on the wrong side of every line there was, understood — hearing of the Black Diamond — that there was a wrong side even of his wrong side: a man with his own name and his own gift standing in a deeper dark, every bit as undeniable and far more thoroughly erased. He never met him. He carried him anyway, the other Méndez, the better arm the country chose to swear it could not see — carried him the way he carried Purse, as an entry in the true ledger: that the line he had barely cleared had, a hand's breadth past him, swallowed a man whole.

### Chapter — Purse

Edward Purse was the finest ballplayer Cirilo Méndez ever stood beside, and that is the first thing to say about him, because it is the thing the record never will. He caught everything; he threw out runners from his knees; he read a hitter the way Cirilo read one and framed a pitch so the umpire handed you the corner; and he had, on top of the hands, a baseball mind that the Daubenbiss brothers and Red Dog Devereaux and every visiting manager who watched him take a practice quietly agreed was the best in the county — a mind to manage with, to build a club around. Everyone who ever saw him play knew exactly what he was. And not one inch of it was ever entered in a record that counted, because Edward Purse was a Black man, and the one line in all of American baseball that did not bend — the line that shut the Black player out absolutely, where it let a light-enough Mexican who could be called Spanish slip through — was drawn all the way around him, with no gate in it anywhere. He kept the Beachcombers' books eleven seasons in a copperplate hand finer than any banker's, and warmed their pitchers, and was called the *mascot;* and when the club folded he was forty and out of even the bench he had been allowed. There was no late triumph for him, no door that opened, no scout who came around at last to the obvious. The line held to the end of his life. But the line is the smaller fact, and the country's, not his: the larger fact, the true one, is that he was magnificent, and that a few hundred people on that coast were lucky enough to have seen it.

What he did instead was this. He took the beautiful copperplate hand that had kept the score books and got work as a porter on the Southern Pacific; and then, because he was the kind of man he was, he started keeping other books — for a colored ballclub he helped get up in the years the barnstorming Black teams came west, an Oakland nine that played the white semipros on the off-Sundays when the parks could be had cheap. He managed them and taught them all the baseball he had stored across eleven years of not being allowed to spend any of it, and some of his boys were better than anyone the Beachcombers ever fielded, and a few of them, a generation on, would be among the men hammering at the closed door from the outside until it finally, decades after Purse himself was in the ground, came off its hinges. He did not live to see Robinson. He died in 1931, of a winter chest, in Oakland, respected in his own world and invisible in the larger one; and the Oakland paper that the Black community read ran a long and loving obituary, and the Oakland paper that the white community read ran nothing at all — because as far as that paper and that country were concerned, Edward Purse had never existed, had never crouched behind a plate finer than any man in the county, had never been kept off the grass, year on year, by a town that could not say so out loud and did not have to.

Cirilo wrote to him to the end — the two of them, the let-on and the kept-off, the friendship with the wall down the middle that neither could remove — and Purse wrote back, in that copperplate hand, dry funny letters about his boys and their progress and the slow education of the world. The last letter Cirilo ever had from him, in the summer of 1918, said only, after the baseball news: *They are saying your name in Boston, I read. Even the wrong one. It is a start, Méndez. Somebody has to make them practice saying it. You go on and make them practice.* Cirilo kept that letter folded against his heart and carried it with him through everything still ahead of him, the good and the ill alike; and Rebecca, who would come in time to read it, understood from it for the first time the whole shape of what her husband had been carrying, and why, and for whom, all those years. It stayed among the few true things he kept — one of the truest things anyone ever said to him, written in a hand the country had tried, and failed, and would go on failing, to erase.

### Chapter — The Yard

To understand what the game took from him and what it gave him, you have to stand inside one ordinary working day of it, dawn to dark, and smell it — because the smell is where the truth of a man's life's work always hides.

It started before light. The milk-run train, or the long walk to the park with his spikes knotted over his shoulder, the morning fog still down so that the grandstand loomed up out of nothing as he came. The smells were the same at every level, Class D to the Coast, and he could have found a ballyard blind: wet flannel that never fully dried, wintergreen liniment, cut grass and the dust under it, neat's-foot oil worked into leather, pipe tobacco and chaw, the green copper smell of old sweat in a wool cap. He loved it the way you love a language you are fluent in. He warmed the arm slowly — the old man had taught him that, *the arm is money, mijo, you do not spend the first of it on showing off* — long easy tosses, lengthening, until the shoulder ran warm and loose and the ball began to do what he told it.

And then the work, which was thinking, disguised as throwing. People believed pitching was the arm. The arm was the smallest part. Pitching was *reading* — the whole catechism Don Aurelio had spat into him off an overturned bucket since he was six. Read the feet before the swing: a man who steps in the bucket is afraid of the inside pitch, so live there, bust him in and up where the fear is, then let him chase the one that dies away off the plate. Read the hands on the bat: white knuckles is a man squeezing, a frightened greedy man, so slow his timing and let his own want swing him out in front of it. Read the eyes, but never trust them, because a good hitter lies with his eyes. Never show him the same height twice. Change the church he's praying in every pitch. And the ball itself — he wet two fingers and worked the seam, the spitball still legal in those years, still honest work, a pitch that came in fat and waist-high and then fell off the table at the last instant like something that had died in the air, the pitch the old man loved best and warned him they would outlaw soon. Mulqueen crouched behind the plate calling for it, his ruined hands setting the target a hair off so the umpire would not see how much it moved.

The games were long in the heat, nine innings of a chess match no one in the bleachers knew was being played, the whole thing conducted in a private grammar of grip and glance and the angle of a foot. He was the best in any yard he stood in, most days, and the strange grace of it — the thing the long doomed obscurity gave him that the famous ones never got — was that he learned to do the work perfectly for nobody. For a flats crowd of a few hundred. For Mulqueen. For the old man on the bucket. For the work itself, which does not care who is watching, which pays out the same clean private wage of *done it right* whether the East ever hears of it or not. They would not call his league major. But the craft he practiced in it was as deep and exact and beautiful as anything done by any man they put on a card back East, and he knew it, and Mulqueen knew it, and the old man knew it, and on the good days that was enough.

### Chapter — The Best League They Wouldn't Call Major

He climbed until he reached the highest rung a man like him could reach and still be told no, and there he stopped, and stayed, for years.

The Pacific Coast League was the best baseball west of the Mississippi and very nearly the best anywhere — a hundred and eighty games a season in the soft coast light, big crowds at Recreation Park, a brand of ball so good that men turned down the East to stay in it. They graded it Class A, and then Double-A, and called it, when they were generous, the third major league; and the generosity was the whole point, for it was major in everything but permission. Cirilo starred there for years. He beat, to their faces, men who got the call he never got.

He learned why, in the end, though no one ever said it to his face — and it was the same verdict Vance Greevy had set down in three words under his name in a Santa Clara grandstand years before, the same sum every Coast League box ran on him every Sunday: not the arm. The gate. He was thirty, and a junk-baller — no fastball for a poster, no name the papers back east would trouble to learn, a tannery story that was no story at all; the crowds wanted youth and a man they could chant and a face for the card, and ownership would not spend the ink on an old fog-league spitballer it would only have to explain. So the scouts admired him, and signed the legible younger man, and went home, and had forgotten by supper that they had decided anything — and that forgetting, not any malice, was the whole horror of it: that the sentence which ended a great man's one chance cost the men who passed it not a single night's sleep, then or ever.

The years went by like that. He led the league and the league pretended not to notice. He grew older in the one place that wanted him, and the door east stayed shut.

### Chapter — The Outlaw League

There was a year and a half, near the end of his time in the fog, when a door he had long since stopped believing in cracked open from a direction nobody expected — and then slammed, the way they all slammed, and left him the last hard lesson the business had to teach.

In 1914 a third major league declared itself. The Federal League, they called it: built fast on rich men's money, eight clubs in eight cities, and dangerous to the men who ran the game for one reason above all — it refused to honor the reserve clause. The whole of organized baseball, the Nationals and the Americans and the high minors that fed them, ran on that single chain: the clause that bound a player to one club for as long as the club cared to keep him, that let them buy and sell and bury a man with no say of his own — the thing the old man had named the first day off the bucket, *you are leather, mijo; they will buy you and sell you and never once ask.* And here, suddenly, were the Federals, raiding the rosters, waving real money at men who had never in their lives been allowed to hear an honest offer, throwing up new ballyards in a single winter — one of them on the north side of Chicago, a tidy steel-and-concrete park that would outlive by a hundred years and more the outlaw league that built it. For two seasons the ballplayers of America held the one thing the reserve clause existed to deny them: a second buyer, and therefore, for the first time, a price.

The interest reached Cirilo the way such things always reached him — sideways, and late, and through a man who only half meant it. A Federal club out west let it be known they might have a place for an old Coast League spitballer who could still make a ball die: not because they had grown enlightened, but because they were short of arms, and he was cheap, and available, and the war in Europe had not yet begun draining the young ones. And for a few weeks Cirilo Méndez, past thirty now, let himself believe the outlaw league might be the door — precisely because it was an outlaw, because the men who would not bend the rules to let him in were exactly the men the Federals had been built to defy. And then it folded. It had burned through its backers' money faster than it could earn any, and the frightened established leagues had bought some of its owners off and sued the rest, and at the end of 1915 the whole brave illegal thing collapsed into a settlement that enriched a handful of men and freed not one player; and the reserve clause closed back over the game like water over a dropped stone, and would not be cracked again for sixty years. The door the Federals pried had been real. Cirilo had felt the cold draft of the open world come through it. And then it shut, and the lesson it left was the same one the man in the derby had left him: that the men who own things will burn a rival to the ground and call the ashes order, and that the one brief season in which labor holds a little leverage is always, always followed by the long patient restoration of the way things were.

### Chapter — The Offer

It was put to him once, plainly, by a man who meant it as a kindness — which is the only way that particular offer is ever really made, and the reason it cuts the way it does.

The man was a Coast League manager named Dorgan, who had handled ballplayers thirty years and liked Cirilo about as well as he liked anyone, and who took him aside one evening after a game, the way you take a man aside to tell him something for his own good. *You've got the best arm in this league and you're going to rot in it,* Dorgan said, *and I can't stand to watch it, so I'm going to tell you the thing nobody else will say to your face.* And he said it. That the bigs would take an arm like that tomorrow — that he'd made calls, that there was real interest — except for the one thing, which Dorgan named by waving a hand at the whole of him. *But here's what you don't know, son, because you came up too poor to learn it: it's fixable.* Be Spanish. Castilian. A don's grandson — which, Dorgan pointed out, he by God actually was. Lose the Mexican. Say the name with a different music, or let the writers say it for him; never mention the tannery; let them make him *exotic* instead of cheap. *It isn't even a lie. Half the "Spanish" gentlemen in this state were greasers a generation back who learned to hold a fork different. Nobody's asking you to be ashamed of your mother. They're asking you to be* — he hunted for the word — *sensible. One vowel. A ticket east. That's the whole price.*

Cirilo did not argue; he had learned long ago that you do not argue with a man who is being kind to you, because it only embarrasses you both. He looked at the dark coming down over the outfield and thought, the way he always thought in the worst moments, of his mother — of Dolores on the hill in the wind with her hand on a dead child's name, teaching a six-year-old the one thing the family had instead of safety: *we keep the name, we say it out loud, we do not disappear.* And here was the door he had wanted since he was old enough to want anything, swung half open at last — and the only key to it was to do, softly and sensibly and for a train ticket, the exact thing his mother had spent her whole life refusing to do: to disappear, dressed up as arriving. To say the name quiet. *I'm obliged to you,* he said at last, and meant it. *You're the first man in this league to tell me the truth to my face.* And then: *But my mother can't read, Mr. Dorgan, and she keeps a name on a hill that the people who took everything else from us could never take, because she would not let them. I am not going to be the one who finally hands it over. Not for the train. Not for any of it.* Dorgan looked at him a long moment, and nodded, and was sorry, and never raised it again; and Cirilo went back to the league that would not call itself major and threw, for years, the finest ball in the West for a few hundred souls — having turned down, with his eyes wide open, the one thing he had ever wanted, to keep the one thing he could not give away.

### Chapter — The Wedding

They married at Holy Cross in 1916 — late, and quiet, and years after Rebecca had already given up everything a wedding is supposed to risk — in the teeth of both their worlds; and the church told the whole story without anyone saying a word.

It was half-empty, and the half that was there had a fault line straight down the center aisle. On one side sat the Méndez family — Dolores and Esteban, the brothers, Don Aurelio — in Sunday clothes worn thin, wary rather than joyful, because they had learned that when the world noticed them it was usually to take something. On the other: almost no one, and a single empty pew where the Whitfields would have sat. Rebecca's people had not come. Rebecca's people were, as of a letter some years old now, no longer Rebecca's people.

She walked the aisle alone, in a plain dress — the gowns had belonged to the life she had traded away — because there was no father to give her, and so she gave herself, which she would have told you, had you asked, she had been doing her whole life anyway. The priest spoke in Spanish, and Rebecca, who had been learning it in secret for a decade, answered him in it.

Dolores had not wanted this wedding, and on the day, at Holy Cross, she still did not; she stood on her side of the fault line with her face composed and her heart withheld, because she had buried two children and survived a stolen century by trusting nothing the Anglo world ever offered, and a banker's daughter marrying her son looked to her, for a long time, like precisely the kind of gift this country gave her people — the kind with a hook in it, the kind you paid for later and forever. She had watched the girl for two years for the hook. It would be months yet — in a freezing Brooklyn kitchen, the morning she found the banker's daughter ruining a pot of beans with the grim determination of a woman who had never been taught to cook and meant to learn now or starve her husband trying — before something in the old woman's chest that had been clenched for two years quietly let go, and she crossed the kitchen and took the spoon, not unkindly, and said the first warm thing she ever said to her: *Not like that. Like this.* But that was months off. On the wedding day Dolores only watched the girl walk the aisle alone and give herself because there was no one to give her, and answer the priest in hard-won Spanish, and she filed it away, the way she filed everything, to be weighed later, when the world had shown its hand.

Afterward, on the steps, Don Aurelio took his grandson's arm — quiet, not unkind, afraid. *First the game. Now an Anglo man's daughter. You keep reaching over your head, mijo. Our family was not built to be first; I have watched it cost us for ninety years.* And Cirilo, gentle and certain, said the only thing there was to say to a curse he refused to believe in: *Then I'll be the one it stops costing.*

And behind them, in the thin October light, Rebecca laughed at something one of the brothers had said — the real laugh, the surprised one, the one from the booth, sixteen years old now and still knocking itself loose out of people who had forgotten how.

### Chapter — The Coast

He gave the best years of his arm to the Coast, and the Coast loved him, and the country went on pretending he did not exist.

The San Francisco of the San Francisco Seals was a city of fog and appetite, and for the better part of a decade it made Cirilo Méndez one of its gods — out at Recreation Park, down on the workingman's flat of the city, where for two hours on a foggy Sunday a pitcher who could make a baseball die in the air was not a Mexican at all but the best arm in the West.

He was, by any honest reckoning of arms, one of the dozen best pitchers alive, and everyone who watched Coast ball knew it; and the East did not care, because the East had decided the Coast was a minor league — decided it partly to keep its own salaries down, and partly because the men who ran the East could not quite imagine that the best baseball in America might be played by Italians and Mexicans in the fog at the wrong end of the continent. He won twenty-eight games one year. He threw both ends of a September doubleheader, both shutouts, because the club asked him to and the reserve clause made the asking a formality. The fog crowned him its own king, over and over, in the only coronation the country would allow him — and not one ripple of it ever carried a single foot past the bay.

And it was wonderful, and it was a cage, and Cirilo came to understand across those years the particular cruelty of being beloved exactly as far as the fog reached and not one foot farther. He learned to spot the Eastern scouts by their hats, and to feel nothing when they left — which was its own kind of dying, the slow administrative kind, the kind with no funeral. He would lie awake on the road, in some Coast-League boardinghouse with the foghorns calling out on the bay, thirty, then thirty-two, then thirty-four, the best man in the best league no one would call major; and he would think of Rebecca three towns west in whatever cold rented room was theirs that month — the banker's daughter who had walked out of a mansion to keep house for him on a journeyman's pay — and of the promise he had made her on a pier a lifetime before, that he would make them say his name. And he would understand, with the foghorns answering on the water, that he was running out of the one thing a pitcher cannot buy more of at any price; that the door east might never open at all; and that a man could pour his whole gift into a game and a city for a decade and still be handed nothing he could lay at his wife's feet but a hatful of other men's silver dollars and a line in a record no one east of the Rockies would ever trouble to read — nothing that could give her back a single inch of the world she had given up to stand in the cold beside him.

### Chapter — Recreation Park

And yet — because the account is never only the ache, and a marriage in the margins keeps a second set of books the sorrow never sees — on the Sundays the Seals played at home, Rebecca was in the stands, and happier there than she had ever once been in a box in her life.

Recreation Park stood at Fifteenth and Valencia, down in the flat workingman's heart of the Mission, a ramshackle wooden bowl that held fifteen thousand when the fog let them come; and on a good Sunday it filled with the whole laboring city at its ease — the longshoremen and the teamsters and the clerks, the Italians and the Irish and the Portuguese and the Mexicans, a city's worth of people who had also been told, in one language or another, to know their place, and who came to Rec Park precisely because for two hours on a Sunday the only place that counted was sixty feet six inches from a man with a bat. There was a section down the right-field line the regulars called the Booze Cage, where seventy-five cents bought a seat and two drinks and a deviled-egg sandwich and by the seventh inning the loudest, most profane, most knowledgeable opinions in California baseball; and that, of all the seats in her life, was the one Rebecca Whitfield came to love.

She had been raised to a box and a parasol and a careful distance from exactly such people. She found, to her own lasting astonishment, that she preferred the Cage: the fishwife two rows down who saved her a place and taught her to keep score on the back of a card; the longshoremen who argued the squeeze play like theologians and who, once they had placed her — *the pitcher's wife, the one that came to all of them* — treated a banker's runaway daughter with a rough courtly tenderness the gentlemen of the hill had never once managed. Down there it did not matter in the least what a person had given up. It mattered only whose you were; and she was Méndez's, the quiet one's, the king of the fog's, and that made her theirs, and they closed around her on the cold Sundays like a second family that asked her nothing and kept her warm.

And she watched him pitch. She knew the game now — had taught herself, the way she taught herself everything she could no longer afford a teacher for — and she could read a count and a defense and the long slow chess of it, could see the enormous something in what looked to the untrained eye like nothing happening at all. On the mound in the fog her husband was not a brown curiosity or a meal ticket or a man the East declined to call; he was simply the best pitcher in the West, doing the one thing the country had ever let him be undeniably good at, in front of the one crowd that loved him with no asterisk anywhere in it. She watched the Booze Cage carry him off on its shoulders, roaring his name into the fog, the silver dollars ringing on the infield grass; and she did not feel, as a Whitfield would once have felt, that she had married down. She understood, in the cheap seats, in the cold, among the people her father had spent his life climbing away from, that she had after all her careful upbringing finally married *in.*

They rode the streetcar home together in the gray evening, two of the poorest people in the city, the cheers still in their ears; and somewhere along Mission Street she put her head on his shoulder, in the open, on a public streetcar full of strangers, the way she had not quite dared to on the ferry back from Ocean Beach all those years before — and he let it lie there, and did not stiffen, and the conductor did not look twice, because in that car and that crowd and that grand indifferent city they were nobody worth a second glance: which was the whole of what the two of them had wanted, and had never once been given, anywhere else on earth.

### Chapter — The Year the Earth Moved

He was with the Seals the spring the city tried to end, and what Cirilo Méndez remembered of the eighteenth of April, 1906, was not the shaking — the shaking was over in a minute — but the three days of fire after it, and what the fire taught him about which lives a city counts.

It came a little after five in the morning, and he was in a rooming house south of the Slot when the floor became the sea — the whole building groaning and walking on its foundations, plaster coming down like snow, the awful sound he never afterward had words for: the sound of solid things discovering they had never been solid. He got into the street with the rest of them, men in their nightshirts standing in a road that rolled, and watched a brick cornice come off a building across the way and take a man he had nodded to the day before — just take him — the way the culvert had taken his father's friend, the way the world took people: without warning and without malice and without the slightest interest in whether they had been good. By full light the fires had started, from the broken gas mains and the toppled stoves; and the water mains that should have fought them were broken too; and the city began, methodically, over three days, to burn itself down to the bay.

And here was the thing he carried, the thing that was not in the newspapers' telling: the fire did not burn democratically, and the saving of the city was not done democratically either. He watched soldiers dynamite the houses of the poor to make firebreaks for the districts of the rich, and call it engineering. He watched Chinatown — the Chinatown that had fed him and every other unwanted man at back tables for cash at midnight, Lim's whole world — burn to nothing; and then watched the city fathers, with the ash still warm, convene to discuss whether the Chinese might now be permanently relocated out to Hunters Point, off the valuable ground their burned quarter had inconveniently occupied — the disaster reframed before the embers cooled as an opportunity to finish a clearance the law had not quite managed. He watched the relief lines, and saw how a brown man, or a yellow man, or a man with the wrong English went to the back of them — even there, even in the ashes, even with the whole city leveled to one common ruin that should have, for one moment, made everyone simply a person who needed bread. *I thought,* he told Rebecca once, years later, trying to explain why the quake had marked him more than any game, *that when the ground itself shook us all down to nothing, we would at least be nothing together. And we were not. They sorted us in the rubble. They sorted us in the relief lines with the city still on fire. That was when I stopped believing the sorting was an accident. A thing you keep doing even in an earthquake is not an accident. It is what you are.* He played ball again that summer, in a half-wrecked league, in towns full of refugees, because the game went on the way everything goes on, because men needed somewhere to put their eyes for two hours that was not the ruin — and he understood, that summer, that this too was part of what the game was *for,* and was almost ashamed of how much it helped.

### Chapter — The Plague

There was a plague in San Francisco in those years, and the city learned almost nothing from it, which is the whole reason it is worth the telling.

In the spring of 1900, in a basement in Chinatown, a Chinese man named Wong Chut King was found dead of the thing that had once emptied medieval Europe: the bubonic plague, *la peste,* the Black Death itself, carried across the Pacific in the holds of the ships that carried everything else. And the city did the thing the powerful do first, which is to deny that the thing is happening at all, because the thing happening is bad for business. The governor of California himself — a man named Gage, with the railroads and the merchants at his back — stood up and called it a lie, a slander on the fair name of the state, an invention of meddling federal doctors; forbade the spending of a dollar of state money to fight it; leaned on the papers to bury it. For the truth was expensive. The truth would quarantine the wharves and frighten the Eastern investors and slow the golden machine, and so the truth was, by the considered policy of reasonable men, not permitted to be true.

What *was* permitted was to blame the Chinese. The city threw its rope of quarantine around Chinatown alone — around the people, not the rats; around the color, not the contagion — penned twenty-five thousand human beings inside it overnight and let the white blocks on every side go free, as though the plague carried a map and consulted it, as though the bacillus could be made to honor the same lines the city drew with everything else. They talked of burning the quarter to the ground. They talked of shipping its people out to a camp on an island. Lim had a cousin in those blocks, and Lim — feeding a ball club at midnight a hundred miles south — followed it in the Chinese papers with the flat unsurprised attention of a man watching the country do, to his people, the precise thing it had set down against them in writing eighteen years before: decide that they were the carriers of whatever it feared, and act accordingly, and call it public health.

The plague was real, and it killed, quietly, for years, while the governor called it a fiction; and it took a change of governors and a patient federal doctor and a long war on the actual rats before the city would admit what it had and end it. And then the earthquake threw the rats out of their broken burrows and scattered them through a ruined city, and the plague came back, in 1907, into the white neighborhoods this time — and *now,* with the right people dying, the money was found, the rats were hunted, the thing was named and fought and beaten inside a year.
