Cirilo was in the city for that second coming of it, a Coast League man, and he watched San Francisco discover its urgency at the exact moment the dying crossed the color line, and he filed it where he filed the relief lines and the dynamited firebreaks, in the true ledger: that a plague, like a fire, like a flood, is only one more question a country asks itself, and that the answer is always the same answer, and is always about whom it has privately decided it can afford to lose. The city sprayed the rats and reopened the wharves and got back to the business of forgetting, which it was very good at — and forgetting, where a plague is concerned, is only the patient building of the welcome the next one will be given.

### Chapter — Matty

There was a standard, in those years, against which every pitcher in America privately measured himself, and his name was Christy Mathewson; and Cirilo measured too — from three thousand miles off, by the agate type, in a fog the great man's light never reached.

In the autumn of 1905, while Cirilo was a young man grinding the valley bushes, Mathewson did a thing in the World Series that pitchers still speak of in lowered voices: three complete games in six days, and not a single run scored against him in any of them — three shutouts, a feat so clean and so total it seemed less a performance than a sermon. And Mathewson was the man the country had been waiting to love: tall and fair and college-bred, a Bucknell man who would not pitch on Sundays, the *Christian Gentleman,* photographed in his high white collar, the hero America wished all its heroes to be. He threw a pitch he called the fadeaway — a ball that broke the wrong way, in against a right-hander, the way water might be coaxed to run uphill — and Cirilo, who had never seen him throw and never would, taught himself that pitch off a paragraph in a newspaper, against a barn wall, through two winters of fog, until he owned it.

He admired Matty without envy, which is a rare and difficult thing, and the admiration showed him the exact dimensions of his own cage — because Mathewson had the craft, the reading and the control and the lovely deceiving stuff, and Cirilo had it too, every degree of it, the fadeaway included. The difference between them was never on the mound. The difference was that Mathewson was the man the country had already decided to love — tall and fair and college-bred, a name that fit a headline, a face that sold a magazine — and was therefore made, out loud and on the cover, into the thing he was; while Cirilo was a poor tannery-bred junkballer whose name the papers would consent to say only if they could make it sound Spanish, a whole continent from any cover, the kind of man the machinery never troubles to make into anything at all. Same craft. Same fadeaway. One of them a national saint, the other a fog-league rumor — and the whole gulf between those two lives drawn not on the rubber but in the ledger: in who could be sold and who could not, who was bred for the cover and who for the bushes, by men who never had to throw a pitch in their lives.

And here is the part that turned even his admiration to something sadder and truer: that the gentleman, too, could be broken, and that the gulf between the saint and the secret was the country's own invention, which the country was the only thing in creation that bothered to honor. Mathewson would go to the war in 1918, and in a gas drill in France his great lungs would be scarred, and he would come home diminished and die slowly of what it left in him, at forty-five — the clean white celebrated hero, the man on the cover, the Christian Gentleman in his high collar, proven at the last as mortal and as spendable as any poor arm in any valley bush. There were forces loose in that century that kept no box scores and honored no collars, that could not have told a Bucknell man from a fog-league rumor because to them the distinction the country had spent itself to maintain was no distinction at all. Same craft. Same fadeaway. Same fragile borrowed lungs. The line between him and Matty was real and cruel and absolute, and drawn, every inch of it, by men — and the things that were not men, and were coming, would honor none of it.

### Chapter — Merkle

There was a name that went through the bush leagues that autumn the way a cold goes through a bunkhouse, and the name was Merkle, and Cirilo read the whole sad arithmetic of it off a week-old paper in a valley town and understood it more completely, he thought, than the men who had written it down.

The bare facts took some untangling, and were, once untangled, the whole horror of it. It was a September afternoon in New York, the Giants and the Cubs neck and neck at the top of the league and the season very nearly riding on the one game. The score was tied, last of the ninth, two men out, a Giant runner dancing off third base and a nineteen-year-old Giant named Fred Merkle on first. The batter lined a clean single into the outfield; the runner on third trotted home with what every soul in the Polo Grounds took for the winning run; and the crowd, certain it was over, came pouring out of the stands and onto the field in their thousands. And young Merkle, the runner who had been on first, did the thing every player in America did a hundred times a summer: seeing the run cross the plate and the game plainly won, he peeled off short of second base and cut for the clubhouse through the spilling crowd, because it had never once mattered — because no one, in the whole careless history of the game to that hour, had ever been made to pay for it. Only this once a Cub named Johnny Evers stood on second screaming for the ball, and got hold of one — maybe the actual game ball and maybe not; no one would ever be sure, and it would turn out not to matter — and stepped on the bag and appealed to the umpire. And here was the rule that hanged the boy: a runner forced toward the next base can be put out simply by a fielder touching that base with the ball; and a run does not count if the third out of the inning is made that way, on a force. Merkle had been forced toward second and had never reached it. So the umpire called him out there — the third out — and the winning run that had already crossed the plate was wiped back off the board, and the game that everyone had gone home calling a Giants victory was, in the cold letter of the law, no victory at all but a tie, played out to its tie in front of an empty diamond because the field could not be cleared and the light was going. The two clubs finished the whole long season dead even because of it, and the pennant came down to a single makeup game on those same grounds a fortnight later, and the Cubs won that one and took the flag — by that one uncounted run, the run a boy had cost by failing to touch a base that no one, in forty years of the careless game, had ever once been asked to touch. And the next morning the country, which needs its catastrophes to belong to a single nameable man the way it always needs that, reached past the thousand players who had done the identical thing unpunished and laid the entire pennant, the entire season, the entire weight of a city's grief on the back of one teenager, and gave him the name it would make him wear to his grave and past it: *Bonehead.* Bonehead Merkle. The boy who lost a pennant by not touching a base that every man alive had been failing to touch for forty years.

And Cirilo, nineteen himself near enough, grinding the bushes a continent away, felt the thing settle into him cold and clear: that the box score is not the truth, and the headline is not the truth, and a man can be unmade entire by a paragraph written by someone who never had to stand where he stood — that the same printing presses that anointed Mathewson a saint could brand a decent ordinary boy a fool forever, and that there was no court of appeal, because the type dries and the morgue files it and the thing becomes, simply, what happened, truer in the record than it ever was on the field. Merkle would play thirteen more good years and field his position cleanly and live a long quiet decent life, and none of it would ever once outrun the name; he would be Bonehead in the obituaries of men who had not been born when he played. *That,* Cirilo thought, was the cage the famous lived in that the fog had at least spared him: out where no one wrote him down, no one could write him down *wrong.* He envied Matty the cover and the collar. He did not envy Merkle one single thing — and he filed the lesson where he kept the old man's warnings, against the day, which he did not yet know was coming, when a writer named Halloran would reach past the whole true difficult man he was and find, instead, the word with the best music in it, and print *that,* and make it stick.

### Chapter — The Benefit

And then, the same season the country was still calling Merkle a fool, it did a thing that showed Cirilo the other face of the game — the warm one, the one that nearly broke his heart with wanting — and showed him, in the same stroke, exactly where the warmth stopped.

A pitcher named Addie Joss had died that spring, suddenly, at thirty-one — a Cleveland man, beloved, who had thrown a perfect game and then been carried off inside a fortnight by a fever in his brain, leaving a young widow and two small children and not much else, because the game paid even its saints like hired hands. And the players did a thing that had never quite been done: they crossed the lines. The best men in the league — Cobb and Speaker and the Big Train himself, stars off every rival roster, men who spent the season trying to beat each other's brains out on the grass — came to Cleveland on an off day in July and put on one uniform and played an exhibition against Joss's old club, and the crowd poured in, and the gate went whole and entire to the widow: thirteen thousand dollars, a fortune, the game reaching into its own pocket to catch the family of one of its dead before they hit the ground. Cirilo read it in the bushes and felt his throat go tight. *That,* he thought — that was the thing under all of it, the reason a man gave his one body to the foolish beautiful game: that it was, when one of its own went down, a brotherhood; that the standings stopped at the edge of a grave; that these hard competing men would lay down the war for an afternoon and stand together over a widow and a couple of fatherless kids and say, with their bodies and their gate receipts, *you are ours, and we have you.*

And then the second thought came, the cold one, the one his whole life had trained him to catch on the heel of the first: *not you.* The brotherhood was real and it was glorious and it had a fence around it the exact shape of the country. They would cross every rivalry in the league for Addie Joss's widow; they would not have crossed the street for José Méndez's, the black Cuban genius they would not let into the same league to begin with — and they would not, he knew without having to be told, pass any hat for a brown widow and a half-Méndez boy, not in Cleveland and not anywhere, because to be inside the brotherhood that buried its own with such tenderness you had first to be counted a man, and the counting was done at the door, in the daylight, by the same hands that wrote the headlines. He wanted in. He would have given an arm to be the kind of player the league would bury like that, gathered up and held. And he understood, reading the glorious thing by lamplight a continent away, that the warmth and the fence were not two things but one thing — that the same circle that made the brotherhood so beautiful was the line that left him outside it in the cold; and he folded that knowledge in beside the others, and did not let it sour him, and went out the next day and threw, for a few hundred souls who would never pass a hat for anyone, the most honest baseball in America.

### Chapter — The Arsenal

He had four pitches by the end, and he knew each of them the way a carpenter knows the four tools he has worn to the shape of his own hand, and the philosophy that bound them was the dead-ball era's whole hard wisdom and the old man's too: that power is the cheapest thing in baseball, and deception the dearest, and that a pitcher who lives by speed is a young fool spending capital he cannot replace, while a pitcher who lives by *lying* — by making the ball do one thing while the hitter's eyes swear it is doing another — can pitch into his forties and break hearts the whole way.

The fastball came first and came free, the inheritance, the thing in the arm the river stone had announced when he was ten; but he learned early not to trust it, not to fall in love with it, to use it the way you use a hard true word in a quiet sentence — rarely, and only to make the soft ones land. The curve he built by labor, hours against a barn wall, until it broke down and away off a right-hander's bat like water going around a stone. The change-up was his favorite cruelty: the exact same arm, the exact same furious motion, the hitter's whole body committing to a fastball that never came, the ball arriving a long slow beat late while the man's swing died in the air ahead of it — a pitch that does not overpower a hitter but humiliates him, uses his own eagerness against him, lets his greed do the work, *let his own want swing him out in front of it,* the old man on the bucket. And then the spitball, legal in those years and honest, the seam loaded just so, the pitch that came in fat and waist-high and full of promise and then fell off the table at the last instant like something that had given up the ghost in flight; the pitch Don Aurelio loved best and made him learn young, *because they'll outlaw it, mijo — they outlaw everything we're good at, sooner or later.*

Four pitches, and behind them the one idea that made them an arsenal instead of a grab-bag: never let the hitter's eyes tell him the truth. Change his church every pitch — the eye level, the speed, the break — so that he stood up there praying to a god who kept moving. It was, Cirilo came to feel, the only place in his whole life where he was permitted to be exactly as intelligent and exactly as deceptive and exactly as dangerous as he actually was, with no one able to dock him for it or sort him out of it. On the mound the lying was *legal.* On the mound a brown man could outsmart a white one sixty feet away in front of a paying crowd and be applauded for it. They would not let him reach for first base in any other room in that country. But they handed him a ball and a rubber and sixty feet six inches, and inside that small exact rectangle the only thing that counted was whether the hitter could touch what he threw — and the hitter, most days, could not. It was the one square of America that was fair, and he loved it the way a man loves the single window in a cell.

### Chapter — Three Finger

Of all the men he read about in the agate type and never met, there was one whose story Cirilo Méndez kept the way you keep a coin from a country you have never been to but somehow know is home; and the man's name was Mordecai Brown, and the country knew him better by the wound: Three Finger Brown.

He had been a poor boy on an Indiana farm, and at seven he had got his hand caught in a feed shredder — the kind of machine that ate the fingers of poor farm children all over America in those years and was never blamed for it — and it took most of his index finger and crooked the rest into a permanent ruined claw, and that, in the ordinary run of things, should have been the end of any thought of a boy like that ever amounting to anything with his hands. Only it was not the end. It was the beginning. Because the mangled hand, when it grew into a man's and closed around a baseball, gripped the thing in a way no whole hand could — put a spin on it, a wicked dropping tumble, that no sound-fingered pitcher in the world could imitate however he tried; and Mordecai Brown rode the ruin of his hand all the way up out of the Indiana coal mines and into the major leagues and stood there, year on year, and beat Christy Mathewson himself head to head more often than the saint could stand, with a pitch that existed in all the world only because a machine had once torn a poor boy's hand half off and the boy had been too stubborn or too desperate to let it mean what everyone said it meant.

And Cirilo, reading it, felt the old family law rise up off the page in a stranger's shape, plain as his grandfather's voice off the bucket: that for people made the way he was made, the wound and the gift are not two things, they are one thing, the same object turned to the light from two sides — that you do not get the dying ball without the dead rancho, the arm without the exile, the music without the maiming; that the world takes something irreplaceable from the poor and the dark and the broken, takes it early and takes it for keeps, and then, every so often, in one stubborn soul out of ten thousand, the very place where the thing was torn away grows back wrong and grows back deadly, and the man learns to win with the scar. Three Finger Brown had been handed a ruined hand and a coal mine and had thrown the ruin itself, the actual wound, sixty feet six inches and into Cooperstown. Don Aurelio had been handed a stolen country and a tannery and had pressed the loss, the actual loss, into a boy's fingers behind a backstop until it came out as the finest soft stuff in California. *There's a price,* the old man had said. Brown's hand was the price, made visible, carried out in the open where a whole nation could see it and cheer it and still never understand that the thing they were cheering was a child's mutilation that had refused to stay a tragedy. Cirilo understood it. He carried his own where it did not show. And he never threw the dying ball again without thinking, somewhere under the thinking, of the Indiana boy and the shredder and the claw that conquered the saint — *the wound is the weapon,* the whole secret bitter creed of everyone the country had ever broken and then, bewildered, applauded.

### Chapter — The Battery

A pitcher and his catcher know each other the way almost no two people are permitted to — better than most marriages, certainly better than most brothers — because the catcher is the one human being on the field who faces the pitcher, who sees what the hitter sees, who shares the secret of every single pitch before it is thrown and wears the bruises of the ones that get away; and the first man ever to know Cirilo Méndez that way, more nearly than any woman or any brother yet had, was a ruin of a catcher in the Class-D bushes named Mulqueen.

He was a drunk and mostly finished, caught now in the low minors only because no one else would crouch in that heat for that money, and he taught the boy the thing that outlasted him and every league above him: that the battery was a conversation, not a command; that the catcher's job was to know the pitcher better than the pitcher knew himself, to call for the change-up the instant before the pitcher's own doubt could talk him out of it, to set the target a hair off so the umpire would never see how much the dying ball moved, to take the foul tips on his already-broken hands without a sound so the hitter would never know he'd been fooled. Mulqueen caught him for three Class-D summers and loved him without ever once saying so, the way men of that kind and that era loved, through the work, through the crouch, through the small profane praise — *that's the one, you old greaser, that's the one, throw me that one again* — and Cirilo learned from him that to pitch was to trust, completely, a single other man's hands and eyes and nerve, and that the trust, once built, was a closer thing than blood. He carried that lesson up out of the bushes and never set it down; and when, much later and much farther on, the time came to trust a second man that completely, it was Mulqueen's grammar he reached for, Mulqueen's target he asked the new man to learn.

### Chapter — Two of a Kind

The other great star the San Francisco fog produced in those years was a thick, joyful, prodigiously strong Italian kid off Telegraph Hill named Francesco Pezzolo, who played under the name Ping Bodie because *Pezzolo* would not fit in a box score and *Bodie* would — and who, in 1910, hit thirty home runs over the Coast League fences, the first man ever to do it, in an age when thirty home runs was very nearly a supernatural number, and made the whole bay forget, for a season, to call him a wop.

He and Cirilo were not close — Bodie was loud and Cirilo was quiet, and their paths crossed only when the Seals played wherever Bodie was — but they were two of a kind in the way the era made certain men two of a kind: foreign-named, an Italian off Telegraph Hill and a Mexican off the Santa Cruz flats, each of whom had learned to play the game beautifully in a country that found his right name too much trouble to print. They sat out a rained-out afternoon once under a leaking grandstand roof in Sacramento, two of the best ballplayers in the West watching the puddles spread on the tarp, and said little. What the real Bodie made of any of it the record does not keep, and this story will not put words in his mouth; the situation itself needed none, and was already being said, steadily, by the rain on the canvas, to both of them.

Bodie got his call east; the big leagues took him in 1911. And it was not the size of the gift that parted them — Cirilo was, by any honest reckoning of the two, the finer player. It was that Bodie was *sellable,* and Cirilo was not. Bodie hit home runs, and home runs put bodies in the seats and a face on the cardboard; he had already filed his own name down to the one blunt syllable a headline could carry; he was young and loud and legible, a thing the machine knew exactly how to price and move. Cirilo threw a junk pitch that made nothing fly, carried a name no marquee would set in type, and had refused — when it was put to him plainly — to file that name into something that would; and he was past thirty now, with the one thing a pitcher cannot restock running low in his arm. The door that let Ping Bodie through had never, in truth, been bolted against Cirilo by any rule of the game — lighter, sellable men with foreign names slipped through it often enough, and a Mexican who let the writers call him Spanish could slip through it too. It was only ever opened for the men the game could put on a poster; and an aging, foreign-named spitballer who would not repackage himself was not, by the one cold arithmetic the clubs finally ran, a man worth the ink. There was nothing to say about it that the rain was not already saying, steadily, on the tarp, for both of them: *the game will buy what it can sell. The rest of you it leaves out in the weather.*

### Chapter — The Dying Ball

Every pitcher has a pitch that is the truest expression of who he is, and Cirilo Méndez's was the spitball — the dying ball, the wet one — and it was no accident that the pitch which defined him was a pitch the rulebook was already planning to take away.

He had it from his abuelo, who had it from God knew where: the Gold Rush sandlots, the bare-handed men who played the game before there were rules enough to break. You wet two fingers; you gripped the ball just so, off-center; and you threw it hard, with a fastball's arm — and the moisture killed the spin, and a ball with no spin does not obey the air, it betrays it; it sails in flat and true, and then, in the last ten feet, having no spin to hold it up, it simply gives up and *dies* — drops off the table, falls out from under the bat like a thing that has decided, mid-flight, to stop pretending. It was legal in those years; every pitcher who could throw it did. Cirilo threw it better than almost anyone alive, because he threw it the way his abuelo had taught him — with a mind behind it, setting it up, hiding it, saving it, never showing the same eye level twice.

But the old man had folded a warning into the gift, the day he first showed it behind the Beach Flats backstop, and Cirilo carried the warning as long as he carried the pitch: *They will outlaw it. A few years, they say, and it's gone. So learn it now and break their hearts with it while you can. Everything they give us has a clock on it, mijo. Throw fast.* And the old man was right, as he was right about most of the hard things and wrong only about the one that mattered: in 1920 the rule-makers banned the spitball — grandfathering a handful of established big-league names to throw it to the end of their careers, and outlawing it for everyone else — and Cirilo Méndez was not on the grandfathered list, because to be on the list you had to have been *seen,* counted, recorded, allowed; and he had spent his best years throwing it in a fog the East had decided did not count. They would let a handful of made men keep the pitch to the end of their careers, and take it from everyone the record had never troubled to write down — but the dying ball was his, wholly his, the one thing in the whole game that no clock and no committee and no Eastern ledger could quite get its hands on. The pitch with the clock on it. The pitch that was the truest picture of his whole people's whole history — thrown flat and hard and true into a hostile air, holding its line as long as it could, and then, having been given nothing to hold it up, falling. But falling, always, exactly where he aimed it. Falling on purpose. Falling like a decision. Dying, when it had to die, precisely where it would do the most good.

### Chapter — A Masterpiece

There was one afternoon in the fog when Cirilo Méndez pitched the best game of his life, and no one east of the Rockies will ever know it happened — and that sentence is the whole of what this country did to men like him, written small.

It was a Sunday at Recreation Park, the Seals against Los Angeles, and from the first pitch he had the thing that comes to a pitcher maybe a dozen times in a career if he is lucky and never at all if he is not: the day when the ball does exactly what the mind asks before the mind has finished asking it, when the catcher's mitt seems to pull the pitch into it, when the hitters walk back to the bench already beaten and knowing it. He did not walk a man. He did not let a man reach. Twenty-seven up and twenty-seven down — a perfect game, the rarest thing in baseball — thrown in the fog at the wrong end of the continent, in a league the East had decided did not count, in front of fifteen thousand people who would remember it the rest of their lives and not one sportswriter who could make the country remember it at all. The Booze Cage came apart. They threw the silver dollars. They carried him off. It was, by any measure of the thing itself, one of the great games anyone ever pitched, and it survives today in no record book that matters — because the record books that matter were kept by the men who had decided the fog did not count, and they were not watching, and would not have written it down if they had been.

He sat in the clubhouse afterward, thirty-three years old, perfect, beloved, and unwanted, and walked all the way into a thing he had been circling for years: that there was no game good enough. That was the cruel gift the perfect game gave him. He had believed, somewhere under the silence, that if he were only good enough — if he were *perfect,* if he left them no possible reason — the door would have to open. And he had just been perfect, as perfect as the game permits a man to be, and the door had not so much as rattled, because the door had never been about how good he was. It had been about *what* he was, and no number of strikeouts would ever change what he was; a man could throw a perfect game every Sunday for the rest of his life and still die a Mexican in a country that had already decided what that meant. He went home to Rebecca that night and she saw his face and said, *you won — you did something today,* and he said yes, and for a while could say no more, and then he said the truest and most desolate thing he ever said to her: *I was perfect today, and it wasn't enough, and now I know it is never going to be enough — and I think that's the day a man either quits, or decides to do it anyway, for some reason that isn't them.* And she waited. And he said: *I'm going to do it anyway.* And that — not the perfect game, which the world forgot, but the kitchen afterward, where a man looked the whole cruelty of his life dead in the face and chose to keep pitching into it — was the real masterpiece, the one no fog and no record book and no country could ever take from him, because he threw it where only she would ever see.

### Chapter — His Mother's Hands

He came home when he could, across the PCL years, and the homecomings blurred into one in his memory afterward, all but the hands — his mother's hands, which he watched the way other men watch the sea, because they were the one fixed thing in a life another man's word moved from town to town.

Dolores's hands were not beautiful and never had been; they were a working woman's hands, thick-knuckled, burn-scarred from forty years of stoves, the nails worn short, the skin gone to a leather of its own — for there was more than one kind of tanning in that family, and hers was the slow kind, done by the work itself, upon the worker. He would sit in her kitchen, a grown man, a ballplayer the fog threw silver dollars at, and watch those hands roll tortillas and tell beads and mend his brothers' shirts and never stop moving; and he would think a thing he never said, because it would have embarrassed them both: that the whole country kept careful records of what men's hands did — his strikeouts, his wins, the box scores, the bronze — and kept no record at all of what these hands had done, which was *everything,* which was to hold the entire family up over the water for forty years by main strength, unpaid, unwatched, unrecorded, uncheered. There was no Hall of Fame for his mother's hands. There was no agate type. There was only the family, still alive, still speaking the language, still climbing the hill on Sundays — which was the whole of her monument, and which she would have told you, had you been foolish enough to call it a sacrifice, was not a monument and not a sacrifice but simply *Tuesday;* and Wednesday; and all the other days; the doing of the next needed thing, and then the next.

She asked him once, on one of those visits, not looking up from the masa — the only time she ever asked him anything about the deeper matter: *Are you happy out there, mijo? In the fog? With the men who will not call you up?* And Cirilo, who lied to everyone, who had lied to the whole grandstand his whole life with his silence, found he could not lie to his mother's hands, and told her the truth. *No, Mamá. Not happy. But — right. I am doing the right thing with what I was given, even though it costs, even though they won't call me. That is not happy. But I think it might be better than happy. I think it might be the thing happy is only the cheap version of.* And Dolores nodded, and went on with the masa, and said the thing he carried to Boston and to the end — the benediction of a woman who had been doing exactly that her whole unrecorded life: *Yes. That is the thing. Happy is for people who were given easy lives. We were given this one. Do the right thing with it, and let them keep their happy. The right thing lasts. I have buried half of what I love, and I am telling you: only the right thing lasts.* And she handed him a warm tortilla, because the talk was over and the boy needed feeding, and that was that.

### Chapter — The Last Time Home

He did not know, the morning he left Santa Cruz after the wedding, how much of what he was leaving he was seeing for the last time. A man never does. You say your goodbyes to a place believing it will keep — believing you will be back to find it, and them, as you left them — which is the small mercy and the great cruelty of the way a life is built.

The town had changed past his childhood and went on changing. The boardwalk he had swept as a boy was a vast electric thing now — the great casino and the carousel and a million bulbs, the leisure-machine all but fully built at last on the ground his family's cattle had grazed — and the flats had filled and the kilns had slowed and the wharf had lengthened, and the new people did not know there had ever been old people, which is how a town forgets: not by deciding to, but simply by adding newcomers faster than it keeps rememberers. He walked it that last morning with Rebecca on his arm — his wife now, in the open, in daylight, on the very planks where once he had not been allowed even to look at her — and the small triumph of that was real, and was not nothing, and he let himself have it.

He had said his goodbyes the night before. Luis, gruff, with a clipping-book under his arm he was too proud to show and too proud not to mention. His mother, who held his face the way she always had, and gave him the language, and did not let him see her weep until he had turned away. And the old man — Don Aurelio, ninety now and more, gone to paper and bone in the good chair, his eyes the only young thing left in him — who took Cirilo's pitching hand in his two cold ones, the old gesture, the hundredth time, and held it, and said in a voice gone to a whisper: *I will not see you again, mijo. No — don't. We are too old for lies, you and I. I will not see you again. And I have been wrong, I think, about the family, about the curse; I have begun to believe I had it backward my whole life, and I am too near the end now to work out how — so it will fall to you to show me, after, whichever of us is watching, and from wherever we end up watching from.* And he had smiled, the old man, with his three remaining teeth, and let the hand go, and said the last thing he ever said to his grandson, the thing Cirilo carried onto the train and across the country and up onto the mound in October and down into the bed at the end: *Throw fast, Cirilo. Everything they give us has a clock on it. You always did listen to that part. Throw fast.*

And Cirilo went down to the depot — the same little depot, sixteen years on — and boarded the train east; and this time Rebecca boarded it with him, openly, his wife, and the town that had drawn every line in the world around them watched them go and could no longer do a single thing about it. And as the platform slid away he made himself, this once, look back — because he was not a frightened boy any longer, and because some partings a man has earned the right to witness — and he held the look until the bend in the track carried it all away: the boardwalk and the wharf and the hills and the chapel-that-wasn't and the entire stolen, beloved, unforgetting town, gone around the curve and out of his sight for good.

### Chapter — Watsonville

Dolores came from Watsonville, from the Pajaro Valley over the hill where the apple country was, and Cirilo went back there sometimes to see his mother's people, and watched, across the years, a thing happen in those fields that taught him the dispossession he had been raised on was not a single event in the past but a wheel that never stopped turning — only changed who it ground.

The valley was the richest dirt in the county, black bottomland that grew apples and berries and lettuce in a profusion that made men rich — other men, always, the men who owned it; never the men whose hands were in it. When Dolores was a girl the hands in it had been Mexican and Chinese; by the time Cirilo's own hair was going gray the hands were largely Japanese — whole families of them, who did the thing the country never expected its stoop-laborers to do and never forgave them for doing: they were good at it, they saved, and some of them began, quietly, to lease and then to buy small pieces of the black dirt for themselves, to stop being hands and start being owners. And Cirilo watched the country answer that, in 1913, with a law — the Alien Land Law, which forbade men who could not become citizens (and the law had already, separately, made certain the Japanese never could) from owning farmland at all. It was done in the open, in the statute books, with no culvert and no rope, by reasonable men in a capital — and it was the same act exactly as the one that had taken the Méndez rancho sixty years before: the law arriving, after the work was done, to make sure the right people kept the dirt. Only the names had changed.

He stood at the edge of a Pajaro Valley field one afternoon, an aging ballplayer home for a few days, and watched a Japanese family work down the rows the way his mother's people had worked them, the way the coast people had been worked before that, the way someone would be worked after — and he felt the whole thing at once: the entire turning wheel of that golden valley, every wave of hungry hopeful people poured onto the same black dirt and used up and legislated back down the moment they reached for the dirt itself. He did not romanticize it. He knew the Japanese farmers and his mother's Mexican people were not natural allies; that the country was expert at setting each new wave against the last; that the very Mexicans ground by the Land Act of '51 could be found, some of them, resenting the Japanese of 1913, as if the wheel could be appeased by helping it turn. But he saw the wheel. That was the gift the culvert and the courthouse and the Mission and the kilns had finally given him — the only inheritance the dispossession left that was worth having: he saw the wheel plainly, all of it, and could no longer be recruited to push it; and he meant to teach his children, whenever they came, to look at the hands in the dirt and never mind the name on the deed — because the hands in the dirt, whoever they happened to be this time, were always, in the end, his own.

### Chapter — The Comet

In the spring of 1910 the whole world looked up at the same thing at once, which it almost never does; for a comet had come back the way comets come back — on a schedule older than any country and indifferent to all of them — and for a few weeks the long human business of sorting stopped, not because men had grown wise but because men had grown afraid.

It was Halley's, the famous one, that returns about every seventy-five years, so that a man sees it once if he is lucky and never twice; and that spring the Earth was to pass clean through the comet's tail, and an astronomer announced that the tail carried a poison gas called cyanogen, and the cheap papers did the arithmetic of fear and printed it, and a good part of the human race spent the night of the eighteenth of May convinced the very air was about to be poisoned out from under them. People sealed their windows with rags and prayer. Charlatans sold *comet pills* and comet umbrellas to the credulous and the terrified. Down in the Mexico that was just then beginning to tear itself apart, some read the thing in the sky as the omen of the blood already coming. And the old writer Mark Twain, who had been born under that same comet seventy-five years before and had said for years that he expected to go out with it, did exactly that — died the day after it rounded the sun, keeping his strange appointment with the light.

Cirilo watched it from a Coast League town, lying out past the last streetlamp the way he had lain out as a boy above the bay, and felt the old double thing the night sky had always given him — how small he was, how completely he belonged — sharpened now by the spectacle of a whole frightened world looking up at the same cold fire at once. The comet did not care that the papers feared it. It did not know there was a Mexico coming apart beneath it, or a country down there sorting its people into the ones whose names it would learn to say and the ones it would not. It had crossed this same sky before there was any of that — over the coast people and the Spanish and the rancho and the tannery, on its long indifferent round — and it would cross again in seventy-five years over whatever the coast had become and whoever was left to look up, and would not ask them, either, who had finished first and who had finished second. He found he was not afraid of it, the way the window-sealers were afraid. He had learned young what it took the comet to teach the rest of them for one night: that the great indifferent things — the otter, the gray whale, the comet, the turning stars — keep no accounts and sort no one; and that there is a hard mercy in that, the only justice some men are ever shown — to be, for a few weeks under a poison sky that was not really poison, exactly as small and exactly as significant as every other soul beneath it.

### Chapter — The Revolution

In the autumn of 1910, the year the great comet crossed the sky, the country the Méndez had come from a lifetime before the border ever reached them began to tear itself apart — and the tearing reached even the Santa Cruz flats, first in the newspapers and then in the people; and it found Don Aurelio first, and undid him in a way the boy had never seen.

The old man could not read, but he had the news read to him — in the cantina, in the lane, by whichever grandchild was nearest — and what he heard was this: that Porfirio Díaz, who had held Mexico in his fist for more than thirty years, the iron old man of *order and progress* and foreign money, had been driven out; and that behind him the whole country had come open — Madero, and then the generals, Pancho Villa riding in the north and Emiliano Zapata rising in the south under a banner that read *Tierra y Libertad,* land and liberty: the two words, the old man said, going very still at his own kitchen table, that his own father had died wanting in a paneled courthouse in San Francisco sixty years before.

He was a Californio. He had never once set foot in Mexico; neither had his father; the Méndez had been on this coast since before there was a Mexican republic to belong to, since the King of Spain, and the border had washed over them while they stood still on their own ground. And yet the old man wept — Cirilo found him on the back step one evening with the wet tracks shining in the cracks of his face — wept not for Mexico the government, which had signed the treaty that failed them and then collapsed three nations away as if to prove there had never been safety anywhere, but for those two burning words: *Tierra y Libertad,* the thing the poor had been wanting on both sides of that line for a hundred years and dying for and not getting, the land and the liberty the Méndez had been handed for one bright generation and stripped of by process, that the campesinos of Morelos were now burning haciendas to demand and would not get either, most of them, any more than the Méndez had. *They are us, mijo,* the old man said, the only thing he said the whole evening. *Down there. Burning. They are us, and they will lose the way we lost, because it is the same wheel. But God — look at them want it. Look at them want it out loud.*

And the war that had nothing to do with him reached up, in the end, and made his own climb harder. For as the Revolution dragged on the country to the north grew afraid of it, and the fear went looking, the way fear does, for the nearest face to land on. When Villa, cornered and furious, crossed the line in 1916 and shot up a New Mexico town called Columbus, and the Army sent ten thousand men down into Mexico after him and could not catch him; and when, the next year, the Yankees pulled from the wire a German telegram offering Mexico the return of Texas and Arizona if she would only turn and make war on the United States — the suspicion curdled into a thing a brown man could feel at a ballpark gate. On top of the old cold arithmetic that had kept him out of the high leagues for sixteen years — too old, too foreign to sell, a name no marquee would carry — came now a new and cheaper one: that a man with his face might somehow, in his heart, be on the side of the bandits and the burning. He heard it once in a valley grandstand, a voice at his back during the anthem — *sing it like you mean it, Pancho* — and he stood with his cap over his heart and sang it like he meant it, which he did, in the complicated way he meant everything about this country, and did not turn around.

### Chapter — The Cousin

It came nearer than any newspaper the spring a starved boy walked up out of the Pajaro Valley with Dolores's own grandmother's village in his mouth.

Dolores's people were Watsonville people, and Sonora before that — the worn rosary she kept in the window had come north in a Sonoran grandmother's fist three generations gone — and the Revolution, which emptied whole Mexican states onto the roads north, sent its share into the Pajaro apple country over the hill, where the stoop-labor was always wanted and never fairly paid. And among them that spring came a boy of perhaps seventeen, Tomás Bórquez, his shoes worn to string, who had walked and ridden and walked again the whole length of the catastrophe out of a Sonora town whose name, when he finally said it at her table, stopped Dolores's hands dead over the masa — because it was her grandmother's town, the one the rosary came from; and when the two of them worked it back through the long patient memory of who-married-whom that the poor keep in place of deeds, the boy's blood and hers proved to be one blood, frayed apart by a border and four generations and now, by the blind accident of a war, knotted back together over a Watsonville kitchen table.

He had seen things he could not always say, and said some of them anyway in the evenings, because saying them was the only way to set them down: the *federales* and then the *villistas* and then the federales again through the same small town, each taking what the last had left; his father put against a wall, not for any side but over a mule the wrong soldiers wanted; the trains going north with people clinging to the roofs, and the ones who fell from them, and the ones who were never let on. He carried *Tierra y Libertad* the way the boy in the next bed carried the catechism — could recite the whole want of it — and he carried nothing else: not a shoe, not a country, not a father, only the want, and the long walk, and a Sonora surname that had turned out, three thousand miles from Sonora, to be family.

Dolores took him in without a word of discussion, because that was what you did, and because the rosary in her window and the boy at her table had come north out of the very same fist. And Cirilo, home between Coast League seasons, sat up late with this cousin-of-a-kind, his own age near enough, who had lived a death Cirilo himself had only ever been threatened with — and felt the strange doubled thing the whole flats felt in those years: that these were his people, pouring north off the freights, and that they were also strangers, Mexicans of a Mexico he had never seen and never would; and that the country he had spent his life trying to climb did not trouble with the difference and never would — would look at Cirilo Méndez, Californio, sixteen years deep in trying to make a Yankee league say his name, and at Tomás Bórquez, three weeks off a Sonora boxcar, and see, with one glance, one brown problem. He lay awake that night hearing the boy breathe on the floor by the stove. *Reach down, or reach up* — that, the old man had always said, was the one question the wheel ever truly asked a man, on the rare turn it carried him up near the top of it. Cirilo had a cousin off a Sonora freight asleep on his mother's floor, and a name he was still trying to make a country learn to say. He knew, lying there in the dark, which way he meant to reach.

### Chapter — The Tide Pools

At the lowest tides the sea drew back off the rocks below the cliffs and laid bare a whole country that lived its entire fierce life in the margin — the thin violent band between the worlds, covered and uncovered twice a day, neither sea nor land but the hard contested seam where the two ground against each other — and the boy went down among the pools and learned, without knowing he was learning it, the law of the people he came from.

Nothing in the tide pools had it easy and nothing in them quit. The anemones, soft as anything alive, met the twice-daily catastrophe of being left in the air by folding themselves shut over a swallowed mouthful of sea and simply waiting, green and patient, for the water to come back; the limpets clamped to their one rock so hard that the surf that shattered ships could not pry them, each one returning, every single tide, to the exact scar its own body had worn in the stone — its home, held against the whole Pacific, by main strength and refusal; the crabs went sideways into the cracks; the sculpins hid in the shallow warm; the sea stars endured the sun and the gulls; and the mussels and barnacles built their crowded cities in the very zone of maximum violence, where the waves hit hardest, because that was the zone no gentler creature could stand to compete for, and so it was theirs. The whole margin was a lesson in it: that the hardest, most exposed, most twice-daily-destroyed band of the whole coast was not empty but *crowded,* teeming, the most densely and ferociously alive zone of the entire shore — because the things that could take it had it all to themselves, and had learned, over uncountable tides, to be soft enough to fold and hard enough to clamp and quick enough to hide and stubborn enough to come home, every time, to the one scar in the rock that was theirs.

That was the flats. That was Beach Flats and the tannery yards and the field-labor shacks and every margin where his people lived — the contested seam, covered and uncovered by forces they did not control, the zone the comfortable could not have survived a week in and the poor had made teem with a fierce crowded life all their own. The tide took everything twice a day and gave it back twice a day and the limpet held its scar through all of it; and Cirilo, grown, on the worst of his exposed days, would think of the limpet clamped to its one worn place against the whole shattering ocean, returning and returning to the exact spot that was its own, and would set his feet on the rubber sixty feet six inches from the only home plate the country would let him have, and clamp down, and refuse to be pried off, and pitch — a margin creature doing the one thing the margin teaches, which is to hold, and to come back, and to be, against everything, still there when the water draws away.

### Chapter — The Ornament

To understand what Rebecca Whitfield walked away from — and why she could pick out, across a whole shrieking midway, the one unsalted true thing in a country full of counterfeits — you have to climb the hill to the house she was made in; because she was made there as deliberately as a chair is turned on a lathe, sanded and lacquered to a purpose, and the purpose was the very thing she would one day, at the cost of everything, refuse to be.

The house stood near the crown of Nob Hill, and it was new. Every stone of it was new, though it had been built at great expense to look four centuries old — the way the freshly rich have always built, in a borrowed grandeur that murmurs *we were always here* to a city that knows perfectly well they were not. Horace Whitfield had come up out of nothing, a clerk's son with a clerk's terror of the gutter he could still see from the top, and he had bought, with the first real money, the two things such a man buys to launder money into respectability: the house, and a wife. Margaret Vane had a name older than any fortune in California and a family that had run through everything but the name, and the marriage was, on both sides, a clean dry transaction in the costume of romance — he took the old name to drape over the new money, she took the new money to keep the old name out of the rooming houses — and the one cost neither of them thought to enter in the ledger, the cost that came due anyway and was paid in full and in private by the woman, was Margaret herself.

Rebecca's whole girlhood smelled of that house: beeswax and hothouse lilies and the cold mineral smell of too many rooms kept warm for no one, and under it, faint and particular, her mother's verbena. She would know that scent the rest of her life — would stop dead over it in a stranger's parlor at sixty and be a small girl again — because it was the smell of the last good hours she ever had with her mother, and of the lesson those hours taught, which she spent a decade not understanding and then understood all at once and never unlearned. For her mother was the warmest thing in that cold new house and also its quietest casualty. She was a soft, quick, unguarded woman who had delighted in her daughter plainly and looked *into* the child and not merely at her — and who was, under the warmth, an ornament going out: a wife bought to drape an old name over new money, dimming year by year in a transaction she had agreed to before she understood its price, until the bright girl Horace had married played the piano very well to an empty music room in the afternoons and grew, by degrees, almost transparent. Rebecca was too young to name what she watched. She only knew that her mother was the one person who truly saw her, and that her mother was, somehow, going out. The fever took her in a week — the careless kind that was not supposed to reach people who could afford doctors and reached her anyway — when Rebecca was eight. And the whole lesson, the one that made everything after possible, came in the last of those days, in the verbena-smelling dark, when her mother took her hand and said the only entirely honest thing she ever said to her daughter: *They will make you a pretty thing on a shelf, Rebecca, because it is what they paid for and all they know how to want. Do not let them. Marry no man who wants you for the shelf — I would rather you were poor and laughed at and your own than safe up here and put out slowly, like me. Promise me you'll keep the part of you that laughs.* And Rebecca, eight years old, promised, not understanding; and carried it, half-decoded, through all the long correct years that followed — and understood it, fully, only on a pleasure pier, when a poor brown boy cleared six iron bottles off a shelf without seeming to look, and she heard herself laugh — the real one, the surprised one, the one her mother had begged her to keep — and felt, with the lurch of a missed stair, that she had just met the first person since her mother who wanted, in her, the exact thing the house had spent every year since trying to sand off.

Because that was the other half of the making, the half Horace never suspected he was doing: in raising a daughter to be the next ornament, he raised a daughter who had watched, at terribly close range, precisely what the ornament's life cost — and who could therefore read, in any drawing room, the hook inside the gift faster than the people offering it. She knew the warm lit cages on the hill from the inside. She knew that the men in them, Chuck Ashby and all his kind, wanted a wife the way they wanted a good horse or a view, as a confirmation of themselves; and she had been bred and trained and very nearly finished to be exactly such a confirmation, the engagement to Chuck all but arranged, two new-and-old fortunes folding tidily together the way her own parents' had. And she would have done it. That was the part she never afterward hid from herself, the part that kept her honest: she had been a breath from climbing into the gilded quiet and pulling it shut and going out by degrees like her mother before her, because it was easy and expected and the alternative was unthinkable. What made it suddenly thinkable was not principle. It was a laugh — the discovery, on a summer pier, that there existed in the world a man who looked at her and did not see a confirmation of himself, who saw instead the part that laughed, the part her mother had died begging her to keep, and who stood at a ball-toss booth in a sweat-dark shirt and could not have bought her one single thing the hill knew how to measure. The hill had taught her the price of everything. It had taught her, without meaning to, to recognize the one thing on the whole coast that was not for sale. And once she had seen it she could not unsee it, and the cage that had been merely her future became unbearable in an afternoon — because a cage is endurable only so long as you have never stood outside it and felt the air.

### Chapter — The Margins

What the world remembers, when it remembers a marriage at all, is the wedding and the deathbed — the two days with the good clothes on. What a marriage actually *is,* though, is the thousands of ordinary days in between; and theirs had begun long before the church. She had walked out of her father's house and cast her lot with him the year she was twenty, and they had been together — in the eyes of the flats and of God, if not yet of the law — through fourteen hard years before Holy Cross ever made it official. Across all those days, the lean ones, it went like this.

They moved a great deal, because a ballplayer the reserve clause can sell goes where he is sold; and Rebecca learned to make a home out of a valise and a rented room in a strange town in under a day — to find the cheap market and the Catholic church and the one grocer who would extend a little credit to a brown man's family, to hang the single framed thing they owned (a tinted photograph of a mission chapel, bought off a peddler, that was almost certainly not the rancho's chapel at all) so that wherever they woke, they woke somewhere that was theirs. She had been raised to run a house with six servants. She learned instead to run a marriage with none, on a journeyman's pay, in towns that would not always serve her husband at the counter — and she did it, after the first hard year, not with the grim martyrdom of the convert to poverty but with a flat competent cheer that surprised everyone, herself most of all. The girl who had been an ornament turned out to have, underneath the ornament, an iron the Whitfields had never suspected and never deserved.

They were happy. That is the thing that does not make it into the kind of story the world tells about people like them — the world wants its dispossessed either nobly suffering or righteously enraged, and keeps no column in its ledger for *happy* — but they were, in the margins, on the cheap, in the rented rooms, more reliably and ordinarily happy than nearly anyone Rebecca had grown up among in the warm lit cages on the hill. They had their jokes, the long-running private ones no outsider could have decoded. They had a way of reading in the same lamplight, her head on his shoulder, that needed no words. They had the thing they had found in the redwood grove and never lost — the being-known — and it turned out that being known, by one other person, all the way down, is the rarest and most expensive thing in the world, and that they had somehow, two of the least-moneyed people in America, contrived to own it outright, free and clear: the one piece of property no court could ever take. He still could not, often, say the soft things; the silence was too old in him. But he learned other languages for it. On the cold nights he got up first and warmed her side of the bed before she climbed in, and never mentioned it; and she pretended, for years, not to notice, so that he would never stop. That was the marriage. That was the whole of it. And it was enough — it was more than the men on the hill, with everything, ever managed to buy.

### Chapter — A Rented Room

The world keeps the weddings and the funerals. Here is one of the nights in between, which is the part nobody writes down, and the part where a marriage actually lives.

A valley town, a Tuesday, the second summer. The room cost a dollar and held a bed, a chair, a washstand, and a window that looked at a brick wall; and Rebecca had made it theirs inside an hour the way she always did — the tinted chapel photograph propped on the sill, his other shirt washed and hung to dry, a jar of poppies she had picked walking up from the depot stuck in a water glass as though they were people who owned vases.

He came in late and dark from the park. He had pitched a two-hitter that afternoon, and a man in a good hat had watched three innings from behind the plate and folded his notebook and gone before the fourth — the way they always went, the scouts and the bird dogs, there and gone, writing down somebody younger and paler in the time it took to find their hats — and Cirilo had watched him leave, and pitched the rest of the game to nobody, and now he sat on the edge of the bed and said nothing, the silence coming off him like cold off a winter window. And Rebecca — who had given up a white house and six servants and a name for this room and this man and this exact silence — felt the old fury climb her throat. *Say something, you impossible man. I left a country for you. The least you could do is talk.* She nearly said it. And then she looked at him, beaten not by the game, which he had won, but by the man in the good hat who would not trouble to write his name in a little book, and she put it down — not the feeling, the throwing of it; she had learned that much from Dolores already.

"He left early," she said. Not a question.

"In the third," Cirilo said. "Before the spitter even got warm."

"Then he's a fool, and he wouldn't know an arm if it took his hat off." She crossed the little room. "And *you* are going to eat better tonight than any man who ever scribbled the wrong name in a little book, because I have" — she produced it from the washstand with a magician's flourish — "half a cold chicken, and a very serious tomato that a Portuguese lady at the market gave me for nearly nothing, because I admired her grandbaby for ten honest minutes." She laid it out on the chair between them like a banquet. "Sit. Eat. We will discuss the blindness of scouts at length, and at no point will we be sad, because I have decided against it."

And the thing happened that was the whole secret of them, the thing no court could take and no league would pay for. The corner of his mouth went, despite him. "The tomato," he said, in the grave voice he kept for their oldest joke, "is from the Portuguese lady."

"The tomato is from the Portuguese lady."

"Then it is a good tomato."

Which meant nothing to anyone alive but the two of them, and was the funniest thing either had heard all day. They ate the chicken with their fingers off a chair in a dollar room, and laughed low so the next room would not hear, at a joke about a tomato that had begun in a market in Stockton a year before and would run, between them, the rest of his life. That was the marriage. Not the silence — the chicken after the silence. He still could not, that night or most nights, say the soft thing straight; the muscle for it had been cut out of the men of his family a long way back. But later, in the dark, he said it to the ceiling, which was as near as he could come: "I'm glad you left the bluff." From him it was a sonnet. "I know," she said, and did not make him say more, because she had learned the size of his currency and decided, early, to be rich in it. And they slept, two of the poorest people in California, in a room with a brick view, having had between them an evening neither of them would ever be able to explain to anyone who counted evenings any other way.

### Chapter — The Letter

The letter came in the fourteenth year of her exile, forwarded twice, the expensive cream stock gone soft with handling; and Rebecca knew the hand before she had the envelope fully out of the box, and stood a long moment in a Sacramento boardinghouse hallway with it unopened in her fingers — because some letters you have been waiting fourteen years for, and dreading exactly as long.

It was from her father's lawyer, not her father, which told her most of what it had come to say before she read a word: that Horace Whitfield was dying — the heart, the doctors said, though Rebecca thought privately it was more likely the seawall finally giving way, the lifelong terror of the bottom drowning at last the man who had built his whole life as a levee against it — and that he wished, before the end, to see his daughter, and to discuss, the lawyer's careful phrasing ran, *certain provisions.* Provisions. Even dying, even reaching for her at the last, he could not help reaching through an attorney, in the language of estates, offering reconciliation and an inheritance in the same breath as though they were the same thing — as though they had ever, to him, been distinguishable. The hook was still in the gift. It would be in the gift in his coffin.

She went. Cirilo offered to come and she told him no, gently — this was the one country he could not cross with her — and she rode the train down alone to the great house on Nob Hill where she had once been an ornament, and was shown up the wide stair she had come down for the last time fourteen years before, into a darkened room that smelled of camphor and money, where what was left of Horace Whitfield lay propped on pillows, gone small.

She had braced for a monster the whole three hundred miles. She found an old frightened man instead, which is harder. The anger had needed him to be large, and he was not large; he was small and dying and reaching, in the only language he owned, which was the language of estates — and you cannot hate small and dying and reaching, not if the margins have taught you the first thing about what it costs to be a person.

He opened his eyes and found her. "You came," he said.

"You asked."

His hand moved on the counterpane toward her, and she took it — dry, light, a bundle of sticks — and they sat a while in the camphor dark, and she waited, because she could see him working at something, the jaw going, the lifelong machinery straining to turn over a gear it had never been built to turn.

"There are provisions," he managed at last. "The lawyer has it in hand. The house, the—"

"I didn't come for the provisions, Father."

"No." A long breath. "No. I—" And there it stalled, where it was always going to stall, because the words he needed had never been installed in him; his whole life had been a levee built so that he would never have to go looking for them. *I was wrong. I am sorry. I drove you out and I have been alone with it for fourteen years.* He could no more have said it than flown out the window. She watched him fail to say it, and understood, with a clarity that surprised her, that she was not going to make him — that a younger, angrier Rebecca would have sat here and waited him out and made the dying man crawl the whole distance, and that she was no longer that woman; the margins had cured her even of that.

So she let him off. "I know," she said. It was a lie, and a mercy, and very nearly the freest thing she had ever done, because it cost her nothing she still needed.

His eyes closed in something like relief. And then, looking not at her but at the ceiling, in the flat voice he kept for the facts of business — share prices, the weather, the only register in which Horace Whitfield had ever been able to come within reach of love — he said: "They tell me your husband is doing well. In his line."

It was the nearest thing to a blessing the old man had in stock, and it had cost him everything he had left. Rebecca, who had married a king and learned to read kings, took it for exactly what it was. "He is," she said. "He's the best there is, Father. The very best." And the old man nodded, slowly, as if a long column had finally been made to balance, and seemed satisfied, and said nothing else that mattered.

He left her the money. She took it — not for herself, for she had found, sitting in that camphor dark, that she did not need it, which was the freest she had ever felt; but for the boy who was coming, and for the glove, and because refusing it would have been one more way of staying in the fight, and she was, she found, all the way done with the fight. She buried him in the plot with her mother and the long clawed-up line of Whitfields. She did not weep and she did not gloat. And she rode the train back north to her real life with the dead world's money in her bag and the dead world's hold on her finally, fully gone — having understood, somewhere out over the gray Carquinez water, the thing she would say later was the true beginning of her freedom, more than the wedding, more even than the boy: that she had not, after all, been the one in the cage.

### Chapter — The Debut

The dream of his whole life came true on a gray afternoon in Brooklyn, in front of a crowd that did not know his name, and it tasted, to his great confusion and lasting shame, of ash; and the learning of why — why the thing he had wanted since the river stone arrived hollow when at last it arrived — was the hardest education of his career, harder than any hitter.

He had waited sixteen years for it. Class D to Class C to the Beachcombers to the Seals, every rung climbed against the current, every season passed over while lesser legible men got the train ticket east — and then, at last, absurdly late, in his thirties, the call to the major leagues, the actual majors, the thing his abuelo had pressed his face against for a lifetime and never reached. He walked out onto a big-league field for the first time and stood on a big-league mound and looked up, out of the old habit, for a bird that was not in that sky, and felt his heart going like a boy's. This was it. This was the cathedral. This was the thing.

And it was just a ballfield. That was the secret no one warns you of: that the summit, climbed, is only ground; that the cathedral, entered, is only a room. The grass was the same grass. The mound was the same dirt. The crowd, twenty thousand strong, did not rise for him the way a few hundred had risen on a Sunday in the flats, because the crowd did not know him and had no reason to — he was a midseason nobody, an old rookie with a foreign name, and they read their programs through his warmups. He pitched well, of course; he always pitched well; he gave them his whole arsenal, the lie and the dying ball, and got his outs the smart slow way, and a few of them noticed and most did not. And afterward, in the big-league clubhouse he had dreamed of his whole life, he understood the rest of the ash: that the salary the reserve clause allowed an aging unglamorous junk-baller did not cover a cold-water flat and a winter both; that he would be back at a tannery bench come November, a major-leaguer who could not make the rent, the dream and the poverty stacked impossibly in the same life. The bigs did not save you. The bigs were just a harder, colder, farther-from-home league that the papers happened to take seriously.

He wrote his abuelo that night — the old man who had wanted this for the family for ninety years — and could not bring himself to tell the truth, that the promised land was only Ohio-flat ordinary ground with a bigger gate; so he wrote instead *I made it, abuelo. I stood where you wanted a Méndez to stand.* Which was true, and was the kindest lie he ever told, and was also, he understood much later, the actual truth underneath the ash: that the arriving was hollow, yes — but the *standing where his abuelo had wanted a Méndez to stand,* the simple fact of a Californio boy from a tannery on a major-league mound at last, was not hollow at all; was, in fact, the whole point; was a thing his grandfather and his murdered people and the barefoot flats boys could hold, even if the boy who'd done it stood there in the gray Brooklyn light feeling nothing but tired and far from home. The dream was never going to feel like the dream. Dreams don't. But it counted anyway, for the ones who could not be there to see it.

### Chapter — The Winter Hides

He reached the major leagues at last, in his middle thirties, and it nearly starved him.

The call, when it finally came, came from Brooklyn — Uncle Robbie's Robins, the National League's most dependable heartbreak, a ballclub that knew about being second the exact way Cirilo did. It was the thing he had chased for the better part of two decades, and he stood in a big-league uniform in a cold-water Brooklyn flat and could not make the month meet itself. The reserve clause pinned an old rookie's pay to the floor, and Brooklyn rent ate the floor, and there was no other club he was permitted to ask, because his contract was owned, the way everything about him had always been owned.

They had two cold rooms on the third floor of a tenement that belonged to a widow named Aoife Doyle, and it was from Mrs. Doyle, more than from anyone, that Cirilo learned what the great roaring immigrant city actually was beneath its noise: a place where everyone had come from somewhere they could no longer go back to, and had buried something along the way, and was holding on by the fingernails to a ledge that the next bad winter might pry them off of. Aoife Doyle kept a gold-star flag in her front window for a son named Liam who lay in the French ground near a town she could not pronounce, and she ran her house like a top sergeant and her grief like a furnace she would let no one stand near, and she was, under all the iron of her, so kind that the kindness embarrassed her into rudeness. The month the baby was coming, when a ballplayer's wage somehow would not cover both a doctor and the rent, she let the rent go — and then barked at Rebecca for a week over nothing at all, and left a covered pot of stew outside their door with no note, and denied it to Rebecca's face. She had decided, somewhere in her ruined heart, that these two were hers to keep alive now, the way Liam was not, and she went about saving them with the ferocious, graceless tenderness of a woman who has learned the hard way that love is not a feeling you have but a series of unpleasant things you do for people while pretending to be put out about it.

So he did what his father had done before him. One winter night Rebecca saw his hands — the pitching hands, the good unmarked ones — come home raw and stained dark at the knuckles, the old tannery stain, and she understood before he said it.

"You went back to the hides," she said.

"A tannery in Newark takes on winter men. No questions. They don't care what a man's name is if his hands know the work." A laugh with nothing in it. "I pitch in the major leagues, Rebecca, and I can't make the rent on it. The club owns my contract and pays me what it pleases, and there's no other club I'm allowed to ask. They call it the reserve clause. My abuelo had a shorter word."

"Leather," she said.

"Bought and sold and never asked. Four generations, and the family business is the same. Only now the hide is mine."

She took his stained hands in her clean ones — the heiress's hands, that had chosen this — and did not let go. "Then we work the winter," she said. "We wait for the spring. We do not quit. Say it." And he said it, the family creed, worn smooth as a river stone: *We do not quit.*

### Chapter — Aoife

Aoife Doyle kept the boardinghouse in Brooklyn where Cirilo Méndez lodged in the lean years, and she came, in time, to be something to him that no landlady's ledger has a column for; and behind that plain fact lies the whole life of a woman the country used up and never thanked.

She was Galway-born, off the boat as a girl in the wake of a hunger that had eaten half the people she came from — she had watched, at seven, in a townland the maps no longer trouble to name, the particular green-black rot come over a field of lazy-beds overnight and take the only food there was; and she had watched her own people learn the thing the Méndez knew in a different language on a different coast: that there is a kind of poverty arranged by men and called the weather, and a kind of dying arranged by ledgers and called the market, and that the arranging is always done by people who will never go hungry and will always be sincerely sorry. She came up out of all that with a hard mouth and soft hands and an absolute, unsentimental theology of the kitchen — that the world is a place where the innocent starve by policy, and that the only argument worth making against it is a hot meal set in front of whoever is in front of you, asked no questions; and that this argument, though it never wins, must be made every single day or the other side wins by default.

She had married a longshoreman named Tom Doyle and buried him young — the docks took him, a parted cable and a half-second of bad luck, the way the docks took someone's Tom every month and the city did not pause — and then she had given her one son, Liam, to the war, to France, to the mud: gold-star, twenty years old, buried in ground she would never have the fare to visit. And she had answered these two thefts the way Dolores answered hers three thousand miles west, the two women never knowing the other existed, working the identical trade in different tongues: she kept feeding people. She filled her house with the half-broken — immigrants, drifters, a brown ballplayer the city did not know what to do with — and fed them past what they could pay, and was sharp with them and warm under the sharpness; and she alone in that whole eastern city looked at the silent Mexican with the disappearing pitch and saw, with the clean sight of one survivor recognizing another, not a curiosity but a *son* — somebody's son, far from a mother, carrying something heavy and carrying it well. She asked him nothing he did not offer; and somewhere in that long plain winter of broth and back talk she decided, the way she decided everything — with her hands, and without discussion — that this one, far from his own mother, was hers now to look after, for as long and as far as the looking-after might one day have to reach. She would not have called it anything at all. She put the kettle back on. There were others in the house who had to eat.

### Chapter — The Cold Rooms

The boardwalk gave them the romance; the cold rooms of the East gave them the marriage; and the marriage was the better thing, the truer thing, though no one writes the song about it, because the song is always about the carousel and never about the fourteen years of cold rooms afterward in which two people quietly proved that the carousel had not been a lie.

They had said, on the turning carousel, *we'll take the hour; they can have the world.* And then they had been given not an hour but fourteen years of cold rooms — and they had taken those too, every one of them, and made of them a marriage so real that Rebecca, looking back from the far end of her life, asked nothing more of it than to have had it: the narrow table, the mended sheets, the man who came in tired from a road trip or a tannery shift and looked at her, every single time, as if she were the brass ring he had finally caught. He could not give her the world he had taken her from.
