He gave her the cold rooms, and himself in them, completely, which she came to understand was the larger gift — for the world she had left was a gilded cage full of people who looked at each other and never in, and the cold rooms were poor and hard and free, and held the one thing the gilded cage had never contained, which was a marriage in which both people were actually, daily, seen. *This,* she had said on the carousel, *just this, going around.* And they had gotten it — not the carousel, but the going-around, the daily orbit of two people in a small cold room choosing each other again at every turn, for as long as they were given, which was not long enough, and was also, she insisted to the end, with the fierce certainty of a woman who had counted the cost and paid it gladly, more than most people ever get.

### Chapter — The Newark Tannery

The tannery in Newark took on winter men, no questions asked, and that was how Cirilo Méndez — twenty-eight games a season at his peak, a name the Coast had thrown silver dollars at — came to spend the off-seasons of his major-league career trimming another man's hides in New Jersey for a wage that embarrassed no one to name.

It was a bigger, meaner operation than Volkov's: a barn of a place on the Passaic, the river running whatever color they were tanning that week, the air a wall you walked into. The men who worked it were the bottom stratum of the great immigrant city — Poles and Slovaks and Italians, and a few Black men up from the South who had found the North's welcome warmer in the telling than in the having, and a Mexican ballplayer nobody recognized, because nobody in a Newark tannery in January was thinking about the Pacific Coast League. They did not know him and he did not tell them, and there was a deep, unexpected peace in that — in being, for three months a year, simply a good pair of hands on the beam, judged by nothing but the work; which was, he reflected once, scraping a hide in the steam with his own breath hanging white in the cold, the only place in the entire country where being a brown man with skilled hands was an unmixed advantage and not a problem to be managed. The foreman, a one-eyed Slovak named Hlavác who had lost the eye to a splash of lime years before and ran the floor with a profane tenderness, took to him because he was quick and quiet and did not complain, and gave him the better work — and never knew, the two winters the silent Mexican trimmed hides on the beam beside him, that the man working at his elbow had been, all along, one of the best pitchers alive in America.

And here was the thing Cirilo carried home from the Passaic each spring, turning it over on the long train west: the leather did not care. The hide on the beam did not know whether the hands working it had once belonged to a ranchero king, or a tannery wage-man, or a major-league pitcher; it yielded to skill and to nothing else, honestly, the same for every man — the one perfectly fair transaction in his entire unfair life. There was a rest in that he never found anywhere else, not even on the mound. His great-grandfather had tanned hides as a kind of kingship and his father as a kind of defeat, and Cirilo, in the steam of a Newark winter with everything still ahead of him that he could not yet see, tanned them as a kind of prayer — the only church, he sometimes thought, that had never checked his name at the door.

### Chapter — Hooper

It was Harry Hooper who finally put the name in front of the right man, and it was the war that made the right man listen; and between the two of them, after sixteen years, the door at last came open.

Hooper was the gentleman of the Red Sox outfield and one of the genuinely good men the game produced — Hollister-born, a Saint Mary's man, an engineer by training who played ball because he was better at it than at engineering and because he loved it; a man who would, in the fullness of time, reach the Hall of Fame and deserve it. He had grown up a county over from the flats, near Hollister, and had watched Coast League ball as a boy, and knew, the way everyone who followed Coast ball knew, that the best pitcher in the West was an aging Méndez the East had simply never troubled to call up. He had kept the arm in the back of his mind for years, the way a ballplayer keeps the memory of a pitch that once made him look foolish, with no particular occasion to do anything about it — for the machinery that decided who rose and who did not was a vast and settled thing, the word of one outfielder a pebble against a mountain, and in the ordinary run of years there was simply no gap in the wall to push an aging fog-league spitballer through.

And then the war made a gap. In 1917, with the rosters bled white and the clubs short of arms, a recommendation could suddenly land where a year before it would have fallen on deaf ground; and Hooper said the thing he had been carrying all that while — that there was an arm out west, an old one, but better than what they were going to find, and that he had watched it his whole life. The word took because the war had cleared the ground for it to take, and Hooper himself would likely have said as much: not that one man's word had moved a mountain, but that the mountain had shifted, for terrible reasons all its own, and a good man's word had happened to be there, ready, when it did. He did the one plain decent thing within his reach at the one moment it could matter — carried a name, and spoke it when speaking it could finally land. The story will hang no larger virtue on him than that, because the larger ones are not ours to assign to a real man whose heart we never saw; and the small true one is plenty, and is his.

### Chapter — The Call

It was the war that finally opened the door — which is to say it was a hundred thousand graves that finally opened the door — and Cirilo would never quite forgive himself for walking through it grateful.

In the winter of 1917 a telegram came to the cold Brooklyn rooms, and he stood in the doorway stamping snow off his boots with his face doing something it did not have a name for. Boston had bought his contract. The Red Sox. The major leagues wanted a thirty-five-year-old spitballer they could have for the price of the train. Rebecca rose, the joy breaking over her — and he stopped her, gently, because the truth had to come with it. They wanted him because the young arms were in France. Half of Barrow's staff was in a trench or under one. Sixteen years he had waited for the call, and it came at last only because the war had taken every young arm to France, and a cheap old foreigner who could still make a ball die was suddenly worth the price of the train.

She took his frozen face in both her hands. "It does not matter why the door opened," she said. "It matters that you walk through it. You have waited your whole life for a door to open at all." And it was Hooper who had put his name in — Harry Hooper, the Hollister boy who had played Soquel ball one ridge from his brothers — so that after everything, the man who finally remembered him was a kid from up the road.

He lifted the worn glove off the table, his father's leather, and turned it once. His abuelo would say it was too high, he told her; he'd say the family was never meant to climb this far. "Then we climb it for this one," Rebecca said, a hand on her belly, where their first child was already on the way, due with the new year. "Let the curse take it up with a baby."

And from three thousand miles west, faint, a letter in the old man's hand seemed to follow them onto the train going east: *Mijo. They call you to the very top now. Be careful up there. The air is thin where they let us stand — and they have never, not once, caught us when we fall.*

---

## _Siempre Segundo_ — Act III, novelized (full chapters)

*The companion novel renders the screenplay's beats as full prose. These are the Act III chapters, drafted; the earlier acts await the same treatment.*

### Chapter — The First Day of the Year

The boy came in the first hour of the first day of the year, which Cirilo would decide later was the only wholly good omen the world ever sent him; and like all good omens, it arrived disguised as terror.

It was so cold in the Brooklyn rooms that the water in the basin had skinned over with ice by the time the midwife wanted it warm, and Cirilo — who had stood sixty feet from men paid to take his head off and never once felt his hands shake — stood in the doorway of his own bedroom and shook like a boy.

It was Aoife Doyle who had gone for the midwife — barked Cirilo out of the way and down the stairs and into the street herself, an old woman in a shawl thrown over her nightdress, running on the ice to fetch and then browbeat a doctor into coming on credit, because she had decided weeks before that this baby would arrive on her watch and would, by God, arrive properly. She came back up and put the water on and took the place by the bed that a mother might have taken, had Rebecca had a mother, or a mother-in-law nearer than three thousand miles. And between the worst of Rebecca's cries she stood a moment at the window by the little gold-star flag she kept there for a son named Liam who lay in the French ground — an old woman with one boy gone into the earth, listening to another fight his way out of it, in the same cold hour, in the same small rooms, the two facts sitting side by side without canceling and without comforting, simply both true, the way the best and the worst of a life will sometimes arrive together and ask you to hold them both at once. Then she turned back from the window and rolled up her sleeves and went to work, because the living were still here and needed her, and the dead, she had learned, did not.

Rebecca had stopped being quiet hours before. He had not known there were sounds like that in her, the heiress, the girl who laughed sideways, the woman who had walked out of a Nob Hill drawing room and into this; and each one went through him like a thrown strike he could not catch.

And then, near three in the morning, a different sound: thin, furious, alive. The midwife said something he did not hear. Rebecca said something he did, because she had been saving it, had carried it up out of the pain on purpose to give to him. "First baby of the year, she says. Born on the one. Before anybody else's." She found his eyes across the lamplight, exhausted past the far side of exhaustion, and she was smiling. "First, Cirilo. He's first at something already."

He crossed the cold floor and took the small hands in his — both of them folded into one of his, the gesture his abuelo had made over him thirty-five years and three thousand miles ago, on a floor that smelled of lime and hide — and found he could not speak, so he said the only words the family had. "Already big," he managed. "Already strong."

He went to the chair by the window where the old glove always sat, his father's leather and his great-grandfather's tanning, soft now past softness, and carried it back and laid it in the crook of the cradle beside his son, the way another man in another family might have laid down the deed to a thousand acres. "There," he said. "Now you own the only thing we never lost."

Outside, over the black river, a few horns and a few drunk voices were still seeing the new year in. Cirilo Méndez stood in the cold with his wife and his son and the old glove his own family had made him, a thirty-six-year-old man with a tannery stain still under his nails and his first real shot at the top finally in front of him, and for the length of one held breath he was not second to any man alive.

### Chapter — The Monarchs

Every October the dead came back to Santa Cruz on orange wings, and no one who truly watched it could ever again quite believe a thing was finished just because the one who began it had died.

They came in their tens of thousands to the grove by the cliffs — monarchs, down out of a whole continent, to hang in clustered curtains from the branches all winter, so thick the limbs bent under them, so still on the cold mornings that you would take them for dead leaves until the sun reached the grove and the entire tree breathed open into a silent storm of moving orange. The coast people had watched it. The Mission fathers had watched it. The boy watched it, taken by his mother one winter morning to the grove, and asked the question every child asks — *where do they come from* — and got from her the answer that was also, though neither of them knew it that morning, the answer to his own whole life.

For here is the thing about the monarchs, the thing that undoes you once you know it: the butterfly hanging in that grove had never been there before. Could not have been. The ones that left the spring before were four generations dead — the journey out and back took more lifetimes than one butterfly was ever given — so that the creature returning to the exact grove, the exact tree, very nearly the exact branch its great-great-grandparents had left, was returning to a place it had never in its own life seen, steering by a map no one had taught it, carried home by a memory that lived in no single one of them but only in the line: the relay, the unbroken handing-forward of a thing not one of them lived to complete. Not one monarch made the whole journey. Not one ever had. And yet the journey was made, every year, perfectly, for longer than there had been a Santa Cruz to make it to — because the finishing had never been any single one's task. The task was only to fly your own leg of it, into the wind, as far as your one life reached, and lay the egg, and trust the ones who would never know your name to fly theirs.

*That is us, mijo,* Dolores might have said, had she owned the words for what she felt with her son's hand in hers before the orange storm. *That is the family. You will not see the end of it. Your abuelo did not see this morning. I will not see all of what you become. You will not see what your children's children make of the name. No one finishes it. You fly your part, into the wind, as far as your one life carries you, and you hand it forward, and you do not drop it. The dropping is the only failure. The rest — the falling short, the dying second, the not-arriving — that is not failure. That is only being one butterfly. Failure is dropping the thing before you have handed it on.* And the monarchs hung in their thousands in the winter grove, every one of them second to the journey, not one of them first, not one of them finishing, and together — only together, only as a line and never as a one — they made it home, every single year, on the orange wings of the dead.

### Chapter — The Christening

They christened the boy at a Brooklyn church with a name three thousand miles long and no one of his own blood to hold him at the font.

It should have been the rancho's chapel, the one Don Joaquín built, the one the family no longer owned. It should have been Holy Cross with the whole flats packed in, Dolores's hands and the old man's blessing and the brothers loud in the back. Instead it was a strange cold church in a strange cold borough, a busy Irish priest who mangled the surname twice and gave up, and a congregation of exactly three: Cirilo, Rebecca, and Aoife Doyle, who had appointed herself godmother by the simple expedient of arriving in her good black dress and daring anyone to say otherwise.

They named him Joaquín — Joaquín Méndez, for the great-great-grandfather who had wept signing the grant, the king of the lost kingdom, a name with a whole century of California folded into it. The Brooklyn priest could not pronounce it and the Brooklyn clerk misspelled it on the certificate, *Joachim Mendez,* anglicizing the boy in the first hour of his official existence exactly as the papers were anglicizing his father across the river. Cirilo looked at the misspelled certificate a long moment and did not correct it, because he had learned the lesson of his own life too well: they will name you what they can pronounce, and the only answer is to make the name mean something anyway, by what you do, until one day they are obliged to learn to say it right. He folded the certificate into the lining of the old glove, where it would still be when the boy was a grown man, soft with handling.

Don Aurelio's blessing came by telegram, because the old man was too frail to travel now and they both knew he was dying and neither said so. It was the longest telegram Cirilo ever received, and must have cost the family a week of bread to send, and it read, in the clipped telegraphic Spanish of a man counting every word at the going rate: HE IS FIRST CHILD OF THE YEAR STOP TELL HIM THAT WHEN HE IS OLD STOP TELL HIM HIS NAME IS A KING STOP TELL HIM WE WERE NOT FINISHED STOP. Rebecca kept it her whole long life. She would read it to the boy on his birthdays, every New Year's, all the years after, until he had it by heart and could say it back to her — *first child of the year, your name is a king, we were not finished* — three generations of refusal folded into a yellow telegraph form: the cheapest and the most expensive thing the family ever bought.

### Chapter — The Weeks Between

There were a few weeks, that winter, between the birth and the train to Boston, that were the closest thing to peace Cirilo Méndez was given in his whole driven life — and he did not recognize them as peace until they were over, which is the usual way of it.

The baby did not sleep, and so Cirilo did not sleep, and he found that he did not mind: that there was something in walking the two cold rooms at three in the morning with the small furious weight of his son against his shoulder that undid, a little, the held thing he had carried since the pier — the silence, the chain. He could not say the soft words to Rebecca; he had tried, across the years, and the words died somewhere below his throat where his father's fear had built its wall. But he found that he could say them to the baby, in the dark, in Spanish, the language his mother had kept alive in his own mouth the very same way — could say all of it, the whole flood: *mijo, mijo, you are the king's namesake and the first child of the year, and you will not be second, I swear it to you, I will make them say your name* — could pour out, to a creature who could not yet understand a word of it, the entire dammed reservoir of a lifetime's unsaid tenderness. And Rebecca, lying awake in the next room and hearing the low murmur of it come through the wall, learned more about the man she had married in those few weeks than in all the years before, and never told him she had heard — the way she never told him about the warmed bed — so that he would not stop.

He held the boy's hands and counted the fingers and turned them in the gray Brooklyn light, the way the old man had turned his, the way the men of his family had always turned a new child's hands toward the light. He did the small useless adoring arithmetic that new fathers do — whose nose, whose chin, how big will the hands get — and underneath it the plain urgency of his age: that he was thirty-six, and the door had finally come open, and a man does not get many springs at thirty-six; that whatever he was going to make them say, he would have to make them say it now, this season, while the arm still answered. He meant to spend what was left of it well. That was the only thing he had ever known how to do about anything — to work, while the working was still his to do — and he set himself to it that winter with the quiet ferocity of a man who has waited a very long time and can feel, at last, the waiting about to end.

On the last morning he held the baby a long while at the window and showed him the gray river and the gray city, and told him, in Spanish, that his father was going away to do a thing, and would come back, and would teach him to throw — *I will teach you the dying ball, mijo, while they still let us throw it; everything they give us has a clock on it; you will have to learn to throw fast* — and then he handed his son to Rebecca, and took up his valise and the old family glove, and went down to the train, thinking only that he was running late, and that the season was waiting, and that *first,* after a whole lifetime of *second,* was finally close enough to reach out and take.

### Chapter — A Different Sky

The East had no hawk in it. That was the first thing he noticed and the last thing he got used to, and he never entirely did.

He had scanned the sky his whole life without knowing he was doing it — the old habit, the boy in the dirt yard who felt a shadow and looked up — and in California there had always been something up there to find: the red-tail riding the thermal off the ridge, the harrier low over the meadow, the kite hovering, the high lone hunter that was his own namesake and his own nature. In the East he scanned and found a different congregation entirely, and it told him everything about where he had come to. Gulls, wheeling and squabbling over the harbor garbage and the ballpark trash, screaming, scavenging, beautiful and venal. Pigeons in their gray multitudes on the cornices and the statues, the city's feathered poor, eating what fell. English sparrows in the gutters, brawling in flocks. Crows working the alleys. These were the birds of the East: crowd birds, scavenger birds, birds of the multitude and the cast-off and the shared scrap — fitting birds, he came to think, for a place that ran on the crowd and the scrap, where a man was one of a million and the trick of survival was to be quick to the fallen thing before the next gull got it. There was nothing wrong with them. They were simply not his. Not one of them was a lone high hunter holding its station against the blue with the whole country open beneath it. The East did not grow that bird. The East had no use for it; the East sorted by the flock.

He looked for the hawk anyway, out of the body's old hope, the whole displaced time he was out there — at the Brooklyn rooftops, over the Boston grandstand between innings, out the cold flat window — and never found it. Once he thought he had: a shape riding high over the gray city on a held wing, lone and still in the way that had meant *home* to him since he was four years old, and his heart climbed toward it — and then it tilted and screamed and folded down toward the harbor after a scrap, and it was a gull, of course it was a gull, it was always going to be a gull. He turned from the window and did not look up again for a while; and Aoife Doyle, who missed nothing, set a cup of tea by his elbow and said nothing, because some homesicknesses have no cure but the going home, and she had buried enough of her own to know it.

### Chapter — The East

He had never left California until the want and the war drove him east at thirty-six, and so the East, when he finally reached it, struck him the way it strikes everyone born on the far coast: as a foreign country that happened to fly the same flag.

The train came down into Boston through three days of country he had no map for — the dry West, the flat middle, then the crowded green East, older and tighter and grayer than anything in his California: a land worn smooth by people over three hundred years, where his own coast still had the raw new-cut look even in its oldest corners. Boston itself was a stone city, close and vertical and cold in a way the dry cold of a Sierra winter had never prepared him for — a wet cold that got into the old tannery ache in his hands and stayed. And the people were courteous and closed, nothing like the loud democratic carnival of Rec Park; a reserve he mistook at first for hostility and came to understand was only the East being the East, holding everyone at the same arm's length — which was, he decided, its own strange mercy. In Boston he was held at arm's length like everybody else, and there was a cold equality in that he had never felt at home, where the distance they kept from him had always been a different and more pointed distance.

He found two rooms in a part of the city where the rents were what a journeyman's pay could bear, among the Irish and the Italians and the Jews — the great immigrant tide of the East that had come in through Boston and New York the way his own people had simply always *been there,* before the border crossed them — and there was an odd comfort in being, for once, only one more newcomer among newcomers, in a city so crowded with arrivals that no one had the energy to make a project of him. And the first time he walked into Fenway Park — six years old then, a brick bandbox with its strange high wall in left and its odd green angles — he stood a while in the empty stands in the gray morning, before anyone else had come: an old man at the end of a road that had run from a tannery floor in Santa Cruz through the bushes and the fog and the cold marginal Brooklyn years — where he had hung on at the bottom of the bigs by his fingernails, up and down, never quite sticking — to *this:* a real rotation on a real contender, the first true shot of his whole life. He thought of his abuelo, three thousand miles and a few months from death; of the silver dollars shining in the Rec Park dirt; of a girl's laugh on a pier. He did not weep — he had business to attend to, and the weeping could wait — but he let the whole impossible distance of it settle into him a moment, and then he went down to the clubhouse and found his locker, the dead boy's locker, and hung up his glove, and got to work.

### Chapter — Barrow

Ed Barrow managed the Boston club in 1918, and it was Ed Barrow who put Cirilo Méndez in the rotation; and the plain reason, the only one the record will support, was that there was a war on and he was short of arms.

Barrow was, by the long testimony of the men who played for and worked with him, a hard, shrewd baseball man — a builder, who would go on after Boston to assemble the great Yankee teams of the twenties. In 1918 the wartime *Work or Fight* order had stripped his roster, young men gone to France or the shipyards, and a manager short of pitching takes the pitching he can find; so Barrow bought the contract of a thirty-five-year-old Coast League spitballer for the price of a train ticket and handed him the ball, because the wet one still fell off the table a foot in front of the plate and there were not enough younger men left to throw anything at all. It was the same short-handed season in which Barrow made his most famous call — to play the young Ruth every day for his bat instead of saving him for the mound — and both judgments came of the same shortage and the same hard practical eye for what was in front of him.

What Barrow privately made of the old Mexican, the record does not say, and this story will not invent it. What the record will bear is enough: that a manager who needed innings gave the ball to a man the rest of the game had spent years declining to call, and went on handing it to him, in the biggest games of the year, because he kept getting outs. The door did not have to be opened by a saint; it only had to be opened. In the summer of 1918 a hard man short of pitching opened it — for reasons of his own that had nothing to do with Cirilo Méndez and everything to do with a war — and that, in the end, was enough.

### Chapter — Graybeards

He reported to Boston in the spring of a year the whole country was trying not to look at directly. The war had been on long enough to have eaten the part of every town that did the dying, and *work or fight* had boiled the rosters down to a thin gruel of old men and boys and the few young stars the army had not yet reached. Ed Barrow, who managed the Red Sox the way a man drives a wagon down a grade with the brake smoking, took one look at the thirty-six-year-old spitballer he had bought for the price of a train ticket, said "God help me, I'm starting graybeards" more to himself than to anyone, and put him in the rotation — because there was no one else, and because the graybeard could still make a baseball die in the air a foot in front of the plate.

The clubhouse was the strangest mix the war had thrown together — graybeards and boys and the handful of stars the army had not yet reached. The biggest of them was a twenty-three-year-old named Ruth, then in the middle of becoming the most famous ballplayer who ever lived: enormous, loud, prodigious in appetite and in power, hitting baseballs distances that made grown sportswriters revise their arithmetic. He and the spare gray spitballer at the far end of the bench were opposites in every visible way, and they dressed in the same room for a single season — the young thunderclap about to end the dead-ball era, and the old man who could still, for one more legal summer, make a baseball die in the air the obsolete way. What passed between the two of them the record does not keep, and so neither will this.

And there was Hooper. Harry Hooper, the gentleman of the outfield, who had grown up one county over from the Beachcombers, near Hollister, and who had carried Cirilo's name in his pocket for years before he finally said it to the right man. They did not talk much about Santa Cruz. They did not have to. Twice a week, two boys off the same sliver of the same coast looked at each other across a major-league clubhouse three thousand miles from it, and that was conversation enough.

What the city could not do was say his name. The Boston papers tried for about a week and then gave up and made him a new one, the way the country had been making his people new names for seventy years — too foreign to be spoken, so corrected into something the tongue could hold. They called him El Cid.

He took the ball each time and did not correct them, because he had learned the first hard law of his life at the bottom of the bushes and never unlearned it: you do not own your name any more than you own your arm. They will call you what they please. The only answer is the pitch.

### Chapter — Halloran

Danny Halloran of the *Boston Post* gave Cirilo Méndez the name that made him and erased him in the same four letters, and he did it — as so much of the harm in the world is done — not out of malice but out of a deadline.

Halloran was a decent newspaperman of the rococo school, an Irishman two generations off the boat who had clawed up out of the same harbors of contempt that had drowned better men than him — *No Irish Need Apply* was a phrase his own father had read in shop windows — and who had, with the short memory of the recently admitted, pulled the ladder up behind him and learned to look down at the browner, newer, lower men the way the Brahmins had once looked down at him. He liked Cirilo. That was the trouble with him, the thing that made him a man and not a cartoon: he genuinely liked the quiet old-timer with the disappearing ball, admired the arm, was moved despite himself by the lateness of it all. And on a September night, on deadline, needing something with music in it to sit atop a column about a thirty-six-year-old Mexican shutting out the league, he reached — the way a tired man reaches for the nearest bright thing — and pulled down *El Cid.*

He knew what it did. That was the part he buried. He knew, being a literate man, that the Cid was a *Spanish* hero, Castilian, a Christian sword raised against the Moors — that to call a Mexican by that name was to perform, in two syllables, the exact erasure the whole country was already performing on the man: to reach past the brown, past the Indian, past the conquered Mexico of his actual blood, and hand him instead a clean white Spanish crown — a conqueror's name for a conquered man, the fog made flesh in a headline. *Spanish,* not Mexican. *El Cid,* not Cirilo. He knew it scanned because it lied, and he ran it anyway, because it was beautiful and it was late and the desk was screaming for the page; and it caught, the way the easy lie always catches, and ran in every paper in the league inside a week, and became the name the country agreed to love him by — on the strict and unspoken condition that it was not quite his.

There is a coda to Halloran that he never knew was a coda. Years on, an old man, he wrote a column about the great forgotten players, and put Méndez in it, and wrote — meaning it as the highest tribute he had — *we called him El Cid, and he carried it like a king.* And he never saw the thing the line confessed: that *we called him,* that the naming had been theirs and not his; that a man who is carried like a king under a name not his own is not a king but a hostage, dressed in the crown of the people who took his country and applauded for how well the captured wear the colors of the captor. The honest words were right there, on the same page, in the same hand — and the man who wrote them went to his grave never reading them.

### Chapter — Work or Fight

He owed his whole season to the war, and the war made him pay for it in the only coin a man like Cirilo had ever been let keep, which was his conscience.

In the spring of 1918 the Provost Marshal handed down the order the newspapers called *Work or Fight:* every man of draft age in a nonessential occupation must take up war work or take up a rifle — and baseball, it was ruled, to the genuine shock of men who had believed themselves national treasures, was nonessential. The young ones went. They went all spring and all summer, off the rosters and onto the troopships, and the clubs scrambled to fill the holes with whatever they could find: old men and boys and 4-Fs, and the occasional thirty-six-year-old Mexican spitballer the East had spent sixteen years declining to call. Cirilo had prayed his whole life for the door to open. It had opened, finally, because a generation of young men were being fed into the guns of France, and their empty lockers were his locker now, and he dressed in front of one every day and could not always meet his own eyes while he did it.

There was a kid — there is always a kid — a nineteen-year-old infielder named Pohlman, up from the bushes to fill a hole, who idolized Cirilo with the pure uncomplicated worship of the young for the old and good, and who got his draft notice in July and came to say goodbye to the old man before he shipped, shy and proud and terrified under the brave face, and asked Cirilo, because he had no father living and Cirilo was the nearest thing the clubhouse offered, whether he thought a fellow ought to be scared. And Cirilo, who had been scared every day of his life and had learned the one thing there is to learn about it, told him the truth: *Yes. Be scared. Scared is just your body telling you the thing you're about to do matters. The brave ones are not the ones who don't feel it. The brave ones are the ones who feel it and go anyway. You've been brave since the notice came, son. The going is only the last part.* Pohlman went, scared and brave and going anyway, exactly as he had been told — and was dead in the Argonne by October, one more of the numberless young that the autumn of 1918 fed into its guns. Cirilo had given him the only true thing he had to give, which was leave to be afraid and go regardless; and what the war did with the boy after that was, like most of what the world did to the people Cirilo loved, no longer in the old pitcher's hands.

He thought about it all season, under the cheering. Every game he won was a game some younger and whiter and luckier man was not at home to win, because that man was in a trench. He took the season anyway — *you take it anyway,* the family creed, the only creed they had — but he took it the way he took everything at the end, with the gratitude and the shame so tangled he had long since stopped trying to pull them apart, and had learned instead simply to carry them both, one in each hand, like a man carrying water across a long dry country: spilling a little of each with every step, and arriving, always, with just enough.

### Chapter — The Summer of the Dying Ball

He was, that summer, the best he had ever been — which is a cruel thing to become at thirty-six, in a body that has trimmed hides all winter to make the rent.

And the spitball, the wet one his abuelo had given him, the warning folded inside the gift — fell off the table that whole last legal summer like a thing that had given up on flight. The American League, which would outlaw the pitch forever in two more years, had no answer for an old man who threw it with a young man's arm and a cardsharp's mind. He led them all. The most wins. The fewest runs to cross the plate behind him. By the early, war-shortened end of the season, the perennial almosts of the country had themselves a pennant, and the man who had been second his entire life stood, for the first time, one short series from first at the only thing the whole bleeding country had stopped to watch.

### Chapter — Schang

The second great battery of his life Cirilo had to earn the way the bushes had never quite made him earn the first — across a whole season of a professional's suspicion, at thirty-six, in the one league that had spent his prime pretending he was not there.

Wally Schang was the Red Sox catcher that season, a hard, capable professional, and he had a whole job of work figuring out what to do with the old spitballer Barrow had dropped on him off the Coast — a pitcher who shook off the fastball, who wanted the target set a hair off the corner, who won by deception in an age that had mostly forgotten the trick of it. Over the summer the two of them built the thing a battery is — the catcher calling, the pitcher answering — until by the big games of the autumn they could run a hitter through a whole at-bat with no visible sign passing between them. And the stuff it was built of — the wet one that died in the air, the change thrown with a fastball's arm, the reading of a hitter's feet — was everything a ruined drunk named Mulqueen had spat into Cirilo twenty years and three thousand miles before, in the California bushes, handed forward now into a major-league crouch by a man who had never heard Mulqueen's name and never would.

Schang caught the finest baseball Cirilo Méndez ever threw. What the real catcher carried away from that one strange wartime season the record does not keep, and this story will not invent it for him; what can be said is only the shape of the thing — that the battery is the one clean marriage the game gives a man, and that Cirilo had two of them, Mulqueen in the fog at the beginning and a major-league professional in the clear at the end, and was faithful to both, and known by them, through a piece of leather, from the inside, as fully as a man is ever known by anyone.

### Chapter — The Duel

The game that finally made his name in the East — the one the Boston writers could not explain away, the one that turned "the old Mexican they picked up off the Coast" into El Cid in print inside a week — was a thing of almost unbearable beauty; and it was beautiful in the way that only baseball can be, which is by means of almost nothing happening, for a very long time, at an unbearable pitch of meaning.

It was a gray afternoon at Fenway, the Red Sox against the Senators, and the Senators had Walter Johnson — the Big Train, the fastest arm the game had yet produced or even imagined, a man who beat you with pure speed the way weather beats you, without deception and without apology, because he had never needed any. And Barrow gave the ball to Cirilo Méndez. The era's purest power against the old man's purest lie; the golden gift against the brown craft; the thing the country hands its favored sons against the thing the unfavored have to invent in the dark in order to survive. Everyone in the park who knew anything knew the whole story of the matchup before a single pitch was thrown.

For nine innings nothing scored, and the not-scoring was the most thrilling thing those people had seen in years. Johnson blew the Red Sox down with speed, the ball a rumor, the bats a half-beat late all day. And Cirilo got his outs the other way — the slow way, the lying way, the way Don Aurelio had spat into him off a bucket thirty years before. He read their feet. He changed the church every pitch, never the same height twice, spending the fastball only to make the soft stuff land; he ran the dying spitball in fat and waist-high and watched it fall off the table while a Senator swung over the ghost of it; he climbed out of a seventh-inning hole, two on and nobody down, with three straight change-ups that left three grown men flailing at air and muttering all the way back to the bench. The crowd came to it the way a crowd does, slowly and then all at once: the dawning understanding that the old brown junk-baller was matching the Big Train pitch for pitch, zero for zero, that the slow lying craft was holding the great golden fastball *even* — which was, though not one fan in those stands would have put it so, a public proof, on the one fair square in all of America, of the exact thing the country had denied him all his life. That what he had was as good as what they had. Precisely as good. Out in the open, on the scoreboard, where it could not be argued down.

It broke in the eighth, and it broke the way the dead-ball game always broke — on craft, not on power. Hooper worked a walk, stole second clean, took third on a ground ball to the right side, and came home on a sacrifice fly so shallow the Senators very nearly caught him at the plate: one run, the only run, manufactured out of patience and nerve and small honest baseball, the kind of run that says the game was never about the long ball, never was, the game is about doing the next small right thing under pressure until the pressure breaks the other man first. One to nothing. Cirilo set the Senators down in the ninth — a ground ball, a strikeout on the dying spitter, a soft fly that Hooper gathered against the green — and it was over. He had beaten Walter Johnson. One to nothing. And the Boston crowd that had walked into the park that afternoon not wanting to love him stood, at the end of it, every last one of them, and roared.

He did not whoop. He did not throw his arms up. He took the ball back from Hooper's glove and looked once at the flat gray New England sky, out of a habit older than the East, scanning it for a bird that was not there and was never going to be there; and he felt the thing rise in him that was not triumph but something stranger and far heavier — vindication, and beneath the vindication, grief. *I was this good the whole time.* Sixteen years in the fog. Sixteen years of doing exactly this for a few hundred souls and an old man on a bucket, and only now, at thirty-six, in the clear hard Eastern light, had anyone with a printing press been made to write it down. Halloran was writing it down that very minute, up in the box, reaching past the brown and the Mexican and the sixteen squandered years for something with music in it — finding *El Cid,* getting the man entirely wrong in exactly the right meter. And Cirilo walked off the mound a made man at last, finally, too late, feeling the vindication and the grief of how long it had taken; and underneath both of them, coldest and oldest, the deepest knowledge of his people stirring at the very bottom of all that cheering: that for a Méndez, to be finally and fully *seen* was not the safe arrival it looked like from the stands. The fog had hidden him. The fog had kept him. And the clear roaring adoring afternoon had just, for the first time in his whole life, found him completely.

### Chapter — Cobb

Detroit came to Boston that summer, and Detroit meant Cobb — and Cobb is worth a page here, because the country's long love of him is a matter of record, and the things the country was willing to forgive him are also a matter of record, and the two of them set side by side say something plainer than any sermon could.

Ty Cobb was, by the record, the finest hitter the game had yet produced — the highest career average anyone would ever post, a baserunner who turned a single into a threat. He was also, by the record, a violent man: he played with sharpened spikes and went into bases high, carried a vicious temper into every city in the league, and more than once turned it on people who were in no position to answer — including, in the documented cases the newspapers of the time set down, Black men a country in those years would not protect. In the most notorious instance, in New York in 1912, he climbed into the stands and beat a heckler who, it emerged afterward, had lost most of one hand in a press-room accident; he was suspended for it; and his Detroit teammates, to a man, refused to take the field in protest of the suspension — the first players' strike in the game's history, called not for wages or for safety but in defense of Cobb. The strike was settled within days, Cobb returned, and he batted, that season, near .400, and the cheering did not miss a beat.

None of that is invented; all of it is in the record, and the record is the point. Cirilo Méndez faced him the ordinary way, from sixty feet six inches, and got him out the ordinary way, with craft — the dying ball and the changed eye level and the patience the old man had drilled into him off a bucket — and that, the work done right on the one fair square, was the single answer the game ever reliably handed a man like Cirilo. It was not a small thing. On that ground, for those two hours, he was nobody's lesser, and the scoreboard said so in figures no umpire could revise; and if it was the only justice the game was built to give him, it was at least a justice it could not take back.

### Chapter — A Tuesday in July

What the record book will not tell you — what no record book ever tells you — is what it actually *felt* like, that summer, to watch Cirilo Méndez pitch a game. So here is one: an ordinary Tuesday in July, Boston against Detroit, ten thousand in the stands, the war news bad and the heat coming up off the infield in sheets.

He did not look like much warming up; he never had. A spare, gray-templed man of thirty-six with a tannery stoop he had never entirely lost, throwing easy on the side to a kid catcher who plainly could not believe he had drawn the assignment of handling the old Mexican. But Ruth wandered over to watch — Ruth always wandered over — and Hooper drifted in off the outfield grass, and by the time the first Detroit hitter dug in there were three future Hall of Famers and an aging Coast League nobody on a baseball field — and for the next two hours the nobody was going to out-pitch the lot of them.

He worked as the old man had taught him a quarter-century before, and anyone who knew what to look for could read the whole catechism in it. The leadoff man stood deep in the box, back near the catcher, buying himself time — so Cirilo busted him up and in with the fastball where the fear lived and had him jamming a weak roller to short before he had taken a full breath. The second man crowded the plate, so Cirilo tied him in a knot inside and then showed him the wet one — the dying ball, the spitter, legal for one more summer — and the man swung over the top of it by half a foot. The cleanup man was the dangerous one, a big free-swinger with his hands choked white around the bat, and Cirilo looked at those white knuckles and gave him nothing, nothing, three pitches off the plate that all looked exactly like strikes leaving his hand and all died somewhere in the last ten feet; and the big man fished at the third one and dragged it foul off his own shin and cursed, and then took the real strike three straight down the middle while he was still busy hating the spitter — because Cirilo had shown him the same eye level twice and then, the once that mattered, did not.

In the seventh, with the dying ball doing its work and the Detroit bench gone quiet and ugly, the bleachers began it — low at first, then all together, the way a congregation finds a hymn — *EL CID, EL CID, EL CID* — ten thousand Boston throats chanting the name of an eight-hundred-year-dead Castilian at a tanner's son from Santa Cruz, not one of them hearing the joke folded inside it. And Cirilo stood on the mound in the hammering July sun, taking the ball back from his catcher, not correcting them, his face giving away nothing — while somewhere under the iron of him a boy who had once swept these same strangers' planks for nickels stood very still and let the sound break over him and thought, with a clarity that frightened him: *they are saying a name. It is not my name. But they are on their feet, and they are saying a name, and I made them do it. It is the closest they will ever come, and it is not nothing, and God forgive me, after all these years, I will take it.*

He struck out the side in the eighth. Ruth, in the dugout, told anyone who would listen that the old man threw a ball that committed suicide on its way to the plate. It was, the *Post* said the next morning under Halloran's byline, a clinic — and they spelled the invented name correctly and the real one not at all. Cirilo clipped it out anyway and kept it, because his son would want it someday, and because a man takes what he is given, and is grateful, and hates that he is grateful, and is grateful still.

### Chapter — The Strikeout

The box score flattens it to a single letter, K. What it actually was: one at-bat in the Series, bases loaded, two out, the Cubs' Fred Merkle digging in — and a single at-bat against a dangerous hitter is a whole chess game played at the speed of nerve, and Cirilo Méndez played it the way Capablanca, the young Cuban who was even then becoming the chess champion of the world, played a board.

He had been reading Merkle all afternoon, the way the old man on the bucket had taught him to read a hitter before the hitter knows he is being read: the feet (Merkle crowded the plate, greedy for the outside corner, which told you he feared nothing inside and therefore should be made to); the hands (high and tense — a man hunting a fastball, a man who would not wait); the eyes (which lied, as good hitters' eyes always lie). Schang crouched, and the two of them ran Merkle through the whole grammar in five pitches. Pitch one: the fastball, but in, under the hands, off the plate — not to be hit, to be *felt,* to move Merkle's greedy feet back an inch and plant the idea that inside was in play. Merkle took it for a ball and did not like it. Pitch two: the change-up, the same furious arm, the same delivery exactly, and Merkle's whole body committed to the fastball that did not come and his swing died a foot out in front of the slow ball floating past — strike, and a flush of anger up the back of his neck, because nothing shames a slugger like swinging at air. Pitch three: waste the curve low, let him think, let the anger work; ball two. Pitch four: the fastball again, in again, closer, and Merkle fouled it off his fists, hard, a half-second late — the inside pitch confirmed now in his fear, his hands a fraction quicker to protect against it, which was the whole trap, the entire five-pitch construction, because a hitter guarding the inside fastball is a hitter who has stopped looking down and away. And pitch five was the dying ball. The wet one. It came in fat and waist-high and full of false promise, exactly where Merkle's furious guarded hunting eyes wanted it, and he swung from his heels — and it fell off the table at the last instant like a thing that had given up the ghost, and Merkle's bat went through the empty air a hand's width above it, and Schang's mitt took it at the shin, and the umpire's arm went up, and the inning was over, and the bases-loaded threat was dead, killed not by speed but by a five-pitch sentence the old Mexican had been composing since the man first dug in.

He walked off the mound without expression, the way he always did, and only Schang, jogging in beside him, and Don Aurelio, three thousand miles west in his good chair, would have fully understood what had just happened — that they had just watched a man be out-thought, publicly, sixty feet six inches at a time, by someone the country had insisted all his life was the lesser man. The crowd saw a strikeout. The two who could read saw the truth: that the lying was a kind of genius, and that the genius had just, on the one fair square, in front of everyone, won.

### Chapter — The Babe and the Old Man

The young man at the other end of that 1918 clubhouse is worth a word of his own, because his story and Cirilo's began in nearly the same place — and the place is a matter of record, not of invention.

George Herman Ruth was twenty-three that summer and in the middle of becoming the most famous athlete the country would ever produce. He had come up from nothing, and the nothing is documented: a Baltimore waterfront boyhood so far beyond managing that his family handed him over, at seven, to the Xaverian Brothers at St. Mary's Industrial School — a reform school and an orphanage in all but name — where the Brothers raised him and put a baseball in his hands, the first thing he had ever been good at and the only home, he said for the rest of his life, he had ever truly had. A boy nobody wanted, in other words, who clawed his way up on a single gift — which is, stripped of everything else, the same bare sentence a man could write about the tanner's son at the far end of the same bench.

And there the records diverge, and the divergence needs no narrator to sharpen it. Ruth caught the influenza that year — caught it, in fact, as the great second wave came on, and was gravely sick with it, and sweated through it, and recovered, and went on to play seventeen more seasons and to become a figure the country raised statues to. The pandemic he survived was the same one then gathering, unnamed and unhurried, over every crowded city in the land. That is as far as the record will carry us here, and it is far enough; the rest of what that autumn held, the story will reach in its own time, and will not borrow a real young man's sickbed to foretell it.

### Chapter — The Women in the Stands

Baseball is a thing done by men and paid for by women; and the green diamonds are only the lit half of it, the other half kept in the stands and the kitchens by the women who carried the cost of the men's one bright dream. So, before the Series, the women — watching, the way they always watched: with a double sight the men in the game never had, seeing the beauty and the bill at once.

Dolores never saw him play. That is the first thing. The woman who held the whole family up over the water so that her son could have an arm to throw with never once, in her life, sat in a stand and watched him throw — could not afford the fare east, and would not have left the others or the graves on the hill if she could, and so experienced her son's entire glorious career as a thing read aloud to her from newspapers by her grandchildren, in a language she could not read, about games she would never see, played by a boy whose face she had to keep in memory because she would not get another look at it. She followed him by faith, the way she did everything — tended his distant fame on her Sundays the way she tended the graves — and that, the mother in the kitchen three thousand miles from the only stand that would have mattered, was the truest seat in the house, and the one no camera ever finds.

And Rebecca learned to watch. She had not known the game — bluff girls were taught the piano, not the squeeze play — and she set herself to learn it the way you learn the language of a country you have married into, because she understood, early and without resentment, that she had not married a man who *played* baseball; she had married a man who *was* baseball, whose truest self lived out on that fair square and nowhere else, and that to love him she had to learn to love, or at least to read, the thing that had his whole soul. So she learned. She learned to read a count and a defense and the slow chess of it; learned to see, in the thing that looked to the untrained like nothing happening, the enormous something her husband was doing; learned to sit in the stands among people who did not want her there beside a man they did not want there and watch, with the fierce educated attention of a woman guarding the one thing that was hers, the long beautiful lie he threw for a living. She gave him that. Of all the gifts Rebecca gave Cirilo Méndez, the one he prized most and said least about was that she had taught herself to genuinely see the game — so that on the rare nights he could bear to talk about the work, there was one person in the cold East who actually understood it, who could say *you were tipping the change in the fourth* and be right, and be his, and be watching not a brown curiosity or a meal ticket but the actual man, doing the actual thing, that no one else in the cold East could fully read.

### Chapter — Comiskey

The strangest World Series anyone had ever seen opened in September — September, weeks early, the season guillotined by the war — and not even in the Cubs' own park, but in Comiskey, borrowed across town because it held more bodies, though in the end the bodies did not come. The country was too busy dying in France and in the troop camps to give its whole heart to a ballgame, and the stands at the first Series Cirilo Méndez ever pitched in held perhaps half the roar he had been promised, his whole life, the big time would be.

It opened with Ruth. Of course it opened with Ruth — the boy pitched the first game and threw a shutout, nine innings of the kind of thing that made men who had watched baseball for forty years go quiet — and Cirilo watched from the bench and felt the complicated thing he always felt watching the Babe: the pure clean joy of seeing the game played at the absolute edge of what a body can do, laid over the old buried ache of knowing that the boy doing it would be remembered for a hundred years, and the old man watching would be remembered, if at all, under a name a sportswriter had invented because his own was too much trouble. Both things. He had stopped, by then, expecting the two feelings to resolve. He had a locker and a uniform and a turn in the rotation coming, and that was more than the man in the derby had ever meant him to have, and he meant to use it.

It was a subdued and shadowed Series, played under the shadow of the thing no one would name yet — the players bound for the army, the flag draped over everything. In the seventh-inning stretch of the opener the band struck up a tune, a wartime favorite called "The Star-Spangled Banner," and the crowd, almost without meaning to, rose for it — the first time anyone present could remember a whole ballpark standing for that song; and Cirilo stood on the top step with his cap over his heart and sang the words he had been taught in a schoolroom that did not want him and on a boardwalk that swept him out at season's end, and meant them, in the complicated way he meant everything about this country: meant them as a wish more than a fact, the way the powerless always mean an anthem — singing not the country that was, but the one he had decided, against all the evidence of his life, to keep believing was still somehow possible. Then he put his cap back on, and waited his turn. It was coming. It had been sixteen years coming. And he was, at last, going to take it.

### Chapter — A Man in a Good Coat

Chuck Ashby came to the Series the way he came to everything: in the best seat money could rent, with a wife on his arm who was poised and handsome and was not Rebecca.

He had done, by 1918, exactly what he had always been going to do. He had inherited the chair at the club and the name on the building and the trick of regarding the world as a thing arranged for his convenience. He had not thought about the bottle boy in years — not really, not in any way that cost him sleep — so it was a peculiar shock to lift his field glasses over the rail of his box and find, on the highest mound in the country, the beach attendant. EL CID, the bunting said. And in the cheaper seats, in a plain coat with a baby on her hip, the only woman who had ever looked at Chuck Ashby and priced him correctly.

His wife asked whether they were friends of his. "I knew them," he said, a beat too long. "A long time ago."

Then he sat, in the most expensive seat in the park, and was given a clear and unobstructed and entirely unwanted view of the one thing his money had never been able to buy: a woman who'd had every other choice in the world, on her feet, weeping, screaming a Mexican's invented name, in love in the open with a man who had nothing the world counted and wanted nothing it could give her instead. The whole park was standing. Chuck Ashby did not stand. He had finally caught up to the bottle boy, and it had cost him eighteen years and a fortune to arrive at the precise spot from which he could watch — and not look away from — everything he had won instead of the thing that mattered.

And beside him his wife watched him watch, and understood all of it — because Eleanor Ashby, née Vane, married eleven years to the chair at the club, was nobody's fool and never had been. She had known about the other woman since before the wedding; you did not marry into a thing like the Ashbys without first reading the whole ledger, and Rebecca Whitfield's name had been an entry in it, in the column of the permanently unfinished. Eleanor had married Chuck with her eyes open, traded one loneliness for a comfortable and well-appointed version of itself, and spent eleven years in the gilded quiet learning the one lesson the cage teaches its long-term residents: that the people who win the way her husband had won are very often the emptiest people in any room, and that they know it, in the small hours, and that the knowing is the whole of their punishment and is never enough. She looked at the brown man on the mound and the plain-coated woman weeping for him in the cheap seats — two people with nothing who had somehow contrived to have everything — and felt no jealousy at all, only a clean, cold, almost scientific recognition, the kind one prisoner feels watching another prisoner pardoned. *So that is what it looks like,* she thought. *I had always wondered.* And she laid her gloved hand over her husband's on the rail — not to comfort him, for she did not especially love him and he had never given her cause to, but out of a strange fellow-feeling, one lifer to another. Chuck, who did not understand the gesture and never would, let it lie there; and the two of them sat in the best seats in the house and did not stand, and watched.

### Chapter — The Strike

There was an hour, in that World Series, that the record books note in a single sentence and the country forgot inside a week, and that Cirilo Méndez never forgot at all — because it was the one hour of his life he watched other men discover the thing he had known since the bushes: that they did not own themselves. And watched them try, just once, to do something about it.

It came out, between games, that the owners had quietly cut the players' share of the Series money to a fraction of what the men had been promised — diverted the gate to themselves and to a list of other clubs by a feat of accounting no player had been consulted on or could contest, the reserve clause being, as ever, a one-way street. And the players of both clubs, the Red Sox and the Cubs, broke and bound for the war and playing the first World Series anyone could remember for something close to nothing, did a thing that had never been done: they refused to take the field. They sat in the clubhouse under the Fenway stands with the crowd filling the seats above them and the band playing and the officials going purple, and for one hour the most powerless men in American labor — owned men, sold men, men whose contracts were traded like hides — held in their dirty hands a sliver of actual leverage: the only leverage labor has ever really had, which is the simple refusal to perform.

They lost, of course. They were always going to lose; men with families and no savings and the draft breathing on them cannot hold out long against men who have everything. But the *way* they were made to lose was the thing Cirilo carried. The owners did not negotiate. They sent in a former player, an official now, to stand in the clubhouse doorway and remind the men — gently, regretfully, with the flag all but audible behind him — that there were wounded soldiers in the stands, that the country was at war, that the public would never forgive ballplayers who struck for money while boys were dying in France. And he was right, and the men knew he was right, and they filed out and played, for the cut wages, having been beaten not with a club but with a flag — which is how the men who own things in this country have always preferred to win. Cirilo dressed in silence and went out and pitched. He understood the doorway and the flag better than anyone in that room. He had been managed by that exact instrument his whole life — *be patient, be grateful, do not make trouble, think how far you have come, think of the others worse off than you* — the soft, suffocating decency that asks the powerless to be the ones who keep the peace, always, at their own expense, forever. He threw a shutout. The owners kept the money. And somewhere in him the small cold clarity he had carried since a man in a derby decided his life in a single sentence turned over once more and settled, and he thought: *they will let us win the game. They will never, ever, let us win the argument. Remember it, on the chance you ever forget: they will hand you the trophy a hundred times before they will hand you the receipt.*

### Chapter — The Last Out

It came down, the way the dead-ball game so often did, to a single pitch in the failing autumn light; but to understand the pitch you have to understand the afternoon that built to it, because it was the best and most complete game Cirilo Méndez ever threw.

Not the wild brilliance of a young arm — the deep, slow mastery of an old one. Every pitch was a sentence; every hitter he read down to his shoes and sent back to the bench shaking his head. The dying ball fell off the table; the change came in with a fastball's fury and nothing behind it; the eye level was never twice the same. He had nothing left to prove to anyone in those stands and everything to settle with himself, and inning by inning he settled it, until the war-thinned crowd that had walked in not caring about an old Mexican was on its feet for him by the seventh without quite knowing why.

He was thirty-six, and he had waited sixteen years for exactly this, and now that it was finally here he found he was not afraid of it at all. He was, for once in his whole driven life, completely and perfectly at home: sixty feet six inches from a man with a bat, on the one fair square, the whole roaring country reduced to that single clean line, and nothing in the world to do but read the next man and throw.

And then the ninth. Two out, the tying run aboard, a full count — one strike from the first championship of his life and his club's and his family's whole stolen century. Barrow came halfway to the mound, the way a manager does when the season is one pitch from won or lost and he cannot make himself sit still for it, read the catcher's face and then Cirilo's, and stopped where he stood.

*You want me to bring the kid?* the look asked. *You've thrown a lot of pitches, old man.*

Cirilo shook his head. Once. "I've been second my whole life, skip," he said. "Not today."

Barrow looked at him a long moment, and nodded once, and walked back to the dugout, and left the old man his game.

In the box, Rebecca had their son up on her hip — alone with the baby, three thousand miles from anyone of Cirilo's blood, for the abuelo was far too frail now to have crossed the country and sat that very hour, Cirilo knew, in his good chair in Santa Cruz, the family law going round in him like a rosary: *we are not allowed to be first.* Had the old man been in these stands he would not have been watching the batter. He would have been watching his grandson's hands, and he would have been afraid of them.

Cirilo looked in. The stitched leather, an old friend. He did not wind up big; he never had. He threw the best thing he owned, and the world went silent the way it only does in memory, and the ball died in the air a foot in front of the plate, and the bat went through the place it had been.

Strike three.

And the park came apart. They reached him at the mound the way the sea reaches a rock and lifted him up — the tannery boy, the bottle boy, the man with the wrong name in the papers — up onto their shoulders and over the whole roaring country, a champion, *first*, for the first time in his life and the first time in his family's stolen century. He found Rebecca in the noise and held the ball up toward the boy like a man showing a coin he had crossed an ocean to earn. And no one in that roaring park knew — not Rebecca, weeping, holding the baby up to see — that the only soul alive who had foreseen this exact afternoon was an old man at the far western edge of the country, who would have closed his eyes rather than watch it, because he alone understood what being first had always cost this family, and he had been right to.

### Chapter — The Hour That Was His

There is an hour the story almost never grants its winners, because the story is in a hurry to reach the next thing, and the next thing for Cirilo Méndez was terrible and the story knew it and hurried; but he had the hour, and he deserves to be seen having it, because it was the only one of its kind in his whole life, and he spent it exactly as the man he was.

They had carried him off the field and set him down at last, and the clubhouse under the Fenway stands had filled with the particular roar of men who have won the whole thing — the young ones whooping, somebody upending a bucket, Ruth's enormous laugh going off like a foghorn over all of it, the smell of cheap champagne cutting through the wintergreen and the wet wool — and in the middle of it Cirilo Méndez sat on a stool in front of his locker with his soaked cap in his hands and was, for the length of that hour, completely and unspeakably still. He let the roar and the next thing and the long tomorrow all fall away onto the shelf at the back where he kept whatever could not be helped, and gave himself, just this once, leave to feel the one thing. Because the one thing had been sixteen years coming, and he did not know — though some weary part of him may have suspected — that it would never come again; that this hour in the loud bright room was not the first of many but the only one, the entire dividend, paid all at once, on a life of being told no.

What it felt like was not what he had spent sixteen years imagining. He had thought, lying awake in fog-league boardinghouses, that being first would feel like winning an argument — vindication, loud and final, the door he had been refused at last broken down. It did not feel like that. It felt, of all the things he had not expected, like *quiet*: like the strange flat calm that comes when a thing you have braced against your whole life finally happens, and is over, and cannot be undone. He had done it. He had stood on the highest mound in the country and thrown the dying ball his grandfather gave him exactly where it would do the most good, and the country that had spent a hundred years telling his family to stay second had, for one afternoon, on the one fair square, been made to watch a Méndez be first — and the watching could not now be unwatched. It had happened. It was in the record the East kept, the record that counted, in ink, for good. And the strange completion of it, the thing that held him still on the stool while the boys came apart around him, was the understanding that the door he had wanted broken down his whole life had never, in the end, been the point: that he had broken it and found behind it not the room he had imagined but only this — a quiet, and a tiredness, and the faces of the people who were not there.

For they were the ones he thought of, in the hour that was his. Not the roaring teammates, good men though they were — Hooper gripping the back of his neck without a word, Schang pressing the game ball into his hands, even Ruth bellowing his clubhouse name across the room — but the absent ones, the ones the hour was really for and who would never see it. His mother, three thousand miles west in a kitchen, who had held the whole family up over the water for forty unrecorded years so that this arm could exist, and who would learn of the greatest afternoon of her son's life the way she had learned of all of them: read aloud, haltingly, by a grandchild, off a paper she could not read herself, about a game she would never see, in the language of the country that took her land. The old man in the good chair, ninety and more, who alone of them all had foreseen this exact afternoon and would have closed his eyes against it, because he alone understood the price. Edward Purse, keeping another man's books in Oakland, who had written him that summer in the beautiful copperplate hand — *somebody has to make them practice saying it; you go on and make them practice* — and who would read of this in the colored paper and know, better than any man alive, both how much it was and how little. And the other Méndez, the Black Diamond, the better arm in the deeper dark, who had beaten the big leagues to their faces in Havana and been let into nothing, and who was the man Cirilo might have been with one shade more sun in his skin. He held the stitched-leather ball in his two hands in the roaring room and thought of every one of them, the whole true ledger — the kept-off and the held-up and the never-recorded — and he understood that he had not done this alone and was not first alone; that he was only the one of them the door had happened to open for, by the hair-fine accident of a word, and that he carried all of them up onto those shoulders with him, the ones who could not be carried, the ones the country had decided not to see.

Then someone said the city was out in the streets and would not go home until they had him — tens of thousands of them, in the wet autumn dark — and Cirilo Méndez stood up off the stool. He wrapped the ball in his soaked cap and put it in his coat against his heart, beside the old porter's letter, and went to the door — out toward the only embrace the city of Boston would ever offer a Méndez. And he went into the noise a champion, first, with the leather over his heart, and let the whole roaring grateful city close around him.

### Chapter — The Influenza

The thing that is about to kill him deserves a look full in the face, because it killed somewhere between fifty and a hundred million people that year — more than the war that took all the headlines — and because it did its killing in a way that should sound, a century on, exactly and terribly familiar.

It came in waves, and the wave that mattered was the second one, the autumn of 1918, and it was the cruelest plague the modern world had yet seen for a reason that ran against every comfortable assumption: it killed the *strong.* The ordinary flu takes the very old and the very young, the weak at the edges; this one took the people in their prime, the twenty-year-olds and the thirty-year-olds, the soldiers and the ballplayers and the young mothers — turned their own powerful immune systems against them, drowned the strongest lungs in the country in their own fluid inside of days, so that a man could be magnificent at breakfast and blue-lipped and gone by the weekend. The cities put on masks and closed the churches and the theaters and the schools; the death carts came; gravediggers fell behind; and the thing spread the way such things always spread, on crowds and on denial and on the refusal of the powerful to cancel the things that made them money — Philadelphia held its great war-bond parade against the doctors' pleading and buried the marchers by the thousand a fortnight later. And it came to Boston early and hard, because Boston had Camp Devens just up the road, a crowded wartime cantonment that became one of the great seeding grounds of the American catastrophe, men packed in by the tens of thousands and the virus moving through them like fire through chaparral.

It did its killing in a way that ought to sound familiar a century on — for a pandemic is a mirror a country holds up to itself, and what it shows, every time, is whom that country has quietly decided is spendable. The strong young poor died first and most, then as in every plague, because the strong young poor are the ones packed into the camps and the streetcars and the tenements and the crowds, the ones who cannot stay home, the ones whose labor the powerful require to keep moving even as the doctors beg for stillness. Cirilo Méndez caught it the way the spendable always catch it: in a crowd he was required to be in, celebrating a thing the powerful wanted celebrated, at the exact moment of his only triumph. The flu did not single him out. That is the horror and the lesson both. It took him the way it took the numberless others, indifferently, by the simple arithmetic of who has to be in the crowd — and the only thing that made his death a story rather than a statistic was that he had, for one bright autumn, climbed high enough that someone bothered to write it down.

### Chapter — What the Crowd Carried

They carried him through the streets that night, and the streets killed him.

It was the first time the city of Boston had ever opened its arms to a Méndez, and it did the thing wholeheartedly, the way a city does when it has decided you are briefly its own: forty thousand people shoulder to shoulder in the wet autumn dark, breathing the same air, passing the champion overhead hand to hand, his son held up over the crowd in a knit cap. For one night Cirilo Méndez was not second to any man in America, and the whole town loved him out loud, and the whole town was already sick and did not know it.

That was the cruelty the century had saved for him, and it was a tidy one: the embrace was the infection. The same packed, joyful, unmasked crowd that finally lifted the brown man onto its shoulders was the breath that carried the thing through the city in the days that followed — the camps already overflowing, Devens forty miles off counting its dead by the hundred, October closing its hand over the country until it became, before it was through, the deadliest month the nation would ever know. They had spent seventy years refusing to embrace his people, and the one time they did, in the one city, on the one night, it was the embrace that did what the refusal never had.

He went down four days later. He had survived the bushes and the reserve clause and the man in the derby and sixteen years of being told no, and the air of a celebration thrown in his own honor took him in under a week.

### Chapter — The Room

The influenza did in four days what sixteen years of *no* had not managed.

The flat was small and clean and full of the wrong quiet. On the sill the championship ball sat beside a guttering candle and the rosary that had come all the way from the beginning of the family, from a chapel on land they no longer owned. Rebecca sat at the edge of the bed, dry-eyed past the far side of crying, their son asleep against her.
